# 抱朴子内外篇

From the *Imperially Commissioned Complete Library of the Four Treasuries*, *Baopuzi Inner Chapters*, Scroll Two, by Ge Hong of the Jin Dynasty, "Resolving Difficulties," Chapter Seven

Someone asked: The august heavens are supremely divine, bestowing fate with perfect impartiality—why then do pine and cypress, mere commoners, receive the gift of deathless longevity, while the great sages Zhou and Kong enjoy no enduring span of life?

Baopuzi replied: The length or brevity of life is truly determined by the celestial configurations one encounters at the moment of conception, when qi coalesces and the embryo forms. The Dao of Heaven acts without deliberate action, allowing things to follow their natural course—it shows no partiality, no favor, no distinction between this and that. If one’s fate is bound to the star of life, that person will inevitably be drawn to the Dao of immortality; those drawn to the Dao of immortality will, if they seek it, surely attain it. If one’s fate is bound to the star of death, that person will not believe in the Dao of immortality and will not cultivate its practices. Whether one delights in goodness or not is decided by what one is endowed with; alteration, bestowal, or deprivation are not within Heaven’s power. It is like gold and iron being smelted in the furnace or pottery and tiles being shaped in the kiln: though the furnace and kiln bring them into form, the sharpness or dullness of tools, the uprightness or crookedness of jars, depend on what they encounter—this is no longer the work of the furnace or kiln.

The questioner objected: All that a skilled craftsman creates comes from his own hands. How can the divine intelligence of Heaven be incapable of anything? Yet you claim that human lives each encounter their own configurations, and are not shaped by the vast azure. This I find deeply perplexing—I cannot assent to it.

Baopuzi answered: When the primordial chaos split and the clear and turbid separated, some rose and moved, others descended and stilled—even Heaven and Earth do not know why this is so. The myriad things respond to qi, each following its own nature, and together with Heaven and Earth, each becomes a distinct entity. The only difference lies in their sequence of formation and the scale of their bodies. Because Heaven and Earth are vast, we perceive the myriad things as small; because the myriad things are small, we perceive Heaven and Earth as vast. Moreover, though the abdomen and back enclose the five viscera, they do not create them; though skin and flesh envelop blood and qi, they do not produce them. Though Heaven and Earth contain the myriad things, they do not bring them into being. It is like grasses and trees relying on mountains and forests to sprout and flourish, yet the mountains and forests do not act upon them; fish and turtles depending on lakes and marshes to propagate, yet the lakes and marshes do not intervene. The common folk, seeing the vastness of Heaven and Earth and the smallness of the myriad things, say that Heaven and Earth are the parents of the myriad things, and the myriad things are their descendants. Yet the lice born upon me—did I create them? Thus, though lice cannot arise without me, I am not their parent, nor are they my descendants. Gnats breed in vinegar, lingzhi and glutinous rice grow on trees and rocks, beetles thrive in filth and sludge, and emerald vines flourish on pine branches—none of these four are crafted by those things. The myriad things teem within Heaven and Earth—how is this different?

Behold! Heaven possesses the sun and moon, the cycles of cold and heat; man possesses sight and hearing, breath and movement. By drawing distant analogies and extending near observations, one infers from this to that. Man cannot comprehend his own body—why he ages or grows young, why he feels pain or itches—just as Heaven cannot comprehend its own body, the reasons for its waxing and waning, its calamities and auspices. Man cannot ensure his ears and eyes remain ever sharp, his nourishing and defending *qi* unceasing; thus, Heaven cannot ensure the sun and moon avoid eclipses or the four seasons maintain their order. From this reasoning, matters of premature death or longevity truly do not lie with Heaven and Earth, nor are the secrets of immortality their doing.

That which gives me life is my father; that which bears me is my mother. Yet even they cannot guarantee my form will be perfectly proportioned, my countenance fair and lovely, my nature and principles balanced and harmonious, my wisdom lofty and far-reaching. They cannot multiply my strength, prolong my years, or extend my lifespan. Instead, some are stunted and frail, some dark and ugly; some are deaf, blind, stubborn, or perverse; some are crippled, toiling, or lame. What they obtain is not what they desire; what they desire is not what they obtain. How much less can Heaven and Earth, so vast and distant, achieve this? Even parents are far removed—how can I, possessing this body, ensure it remains forever robust and unaging, perpetually healthy and free from illness, its joy and anger never misplaced, its plans and reflections without regret or remorse? Thus, those who bestow *qi* and shape form are my parents; those who receive and possess them are my own body. Beyond these, nothing is more intimately connected, nothing exerts greater control. If even these two cannot alter or benefit me, how can Heaven and Earth claim any part in it?

If all beings were truly the creations of Heaven and Earth, they should all be good without evil, complete without flaw, and all living things should fulfill their destinies without the sorrow of spring’s fading or autumn’s decay. Yet Heaven cannot grant Confucius or Mencius the blessing of transcending the world, proving that what they received was natural, not apportioned by Heaven and Earth. Sagehood is the pinnacle of virtue; if Heaven could bestow such perfect virtue, why would it not allow their desires to be fully realized, their achievements established, their positions supreme, their lifespans exceeding a century? This is no proof of Heaven’s deliberate action. The death of a sage is not Heaven’s killing, just as the birth of a sage is not Heaven’s creation. The worthy are not necessarily long-lived, nor are the foolish doomed to die young; goodness does not always bring fortune, nor does evil always invite disaster. Birth has no fixed term, death no constant measure. Men of towering virtue and wisdom may wither unfulfilled, while the mediocre Dou Gong lived to two hundred; Bo Niu was stricken with illness, Zixia lost his sight; the villainous Robber Zhi lived to a white-haired old age, while the wicked Zhuang Qiao sported a head of yellow hair. In this, Heaven’s non-action is manifest.

Some say, Confucius declared, "Since ancient times, all must die," while Laozi spoke of the learnability of divine transcendence. The words of sages are trustworthy and verifiable, but the teachings of Daoists are fanciful and difficult to apply. The *Baopuzi* states: Confucius is the sage of the Confucians, Laozi the sage of the Dao. Confucian teachings are near and easy to perceive, hence many follow them; the intent of the Dao is distant and hard to grasp, hence few attain it. The Dao is the source of the ten thousand differences; Confucianism is but a stream of the great purity. Before the Three Sovereigns, the Dao governed; since the rise of emperors and kings, Confucianism has prevailed. Discussants all know to esteem the simplicity of high antiquity and disdain the decadence of later ages—why then do they revere Confucius while belittling Laozi? This is like admiring the blossoms at the tips of branches while failing to recognize the root from which they grow. How is this different from prizing luminous pearls while despising the deep pools, cherishing the *He* jade while loathing Mount Jing? They do not know that the deep pools are the source of luminous pearls, and Mount Jing the origin of the *He* jade.

Moreover, nurturing one’s nature is but a remnant of the Dao; ordering the world is but a minor aspect of Confucianism.

Thus, the value of Confucianism lies in its power to transform customs and reform manners—not merely in its rites of bowing and yielding or its measured steps. The reverence for the Dao rests in its silent transformation, its influence extending far beyond the single matter of nurturing life.

If Confucianism and the Dao truly had an order of precedence, then Confucius could not be exclusively trusted, nor could Laozi be employed in isolation. Confucius himself paid respectful homage to Boyang, wishing to be compared to Lao Peng, and admitted that while he understood the ways of fish and birds, he did not comprehend the dragon—a metaphor by which he acknowledged Laozi as the dragon, an expression of heartfelt admiration, not empty words. This is no different from his words to Yan Hui: *"Gazing at it, it appears ahead; suddenly, it is behind. Boring into it, it grows ever harder; looking up to it, it grows ever higher."*

Some ask: *"Confucius personally met Laozi yet did not follow his teachings—why?"* The *Baopuzi* replies: *"This makes it all the clearer that what one is endowed with is a mandate of nature, and what one esteems is an unchanging disposition. Confucius recognized the profound subtlety of Laozi’s teachings and valued their rarity, yet he could not draw from the wellspring of pure emptiness at its source. The Great Ancestor emerges beyond form and enters within the Perfect Dao. What he sought to learn was confined to matters of the common world—how could he have entreated the methods of immortality? His heart was wholly absorbed in teaching and transformation, with no thought for esoteric arts. Though sagely in worldly affairs, Confucius was not one to abide in stillness, profound silence, self-preservation, or non-action. Thus, Laozi admonished him: *'A good merchant hides his treasures as if empty; a noble man of great virtue appears foolish. Shed your pride, your excess of desires, your overbearing demeanor, your reckless ambitions—these bring no benefit to your person.'* From this, we may know that Confucius was not free from worldly attachments and was no practitioner of immortality’s Way.

Ever restless, ever anxious, his sole concern was to set the age aright—lamenting the phoenix’s silence above, sighing over the gourd’s unsold fate below. How could one so impassioned in his desire to wield the whip of governance abandon the work of ordering the world to pursue the roundabout path of nurturing life?"

Some ask: *"Between the endeavors of Confucianism and the Dao, which is more difficult, which easier?"* The *Baopuzi* answers: *"Confucianism is difficulty within ease; the Dao is ease within difficulty. To renounce social ties, forsake wife and children, reject fame and honor, forgo profit and rank, turn from the dazzling before one’s eyes, and silence the clamor in one’s ears—to remain serene and withdrawn, content in solitude, guarding one’s integrity, unmoved by slander, unelated by praise, indifferent to high station, unashamed of lowly estate—this is the difficulty of the Dao. Yet one need not attend to condolences or visits, bear no burden of care for others, exhaust neither mind in the Seven Classics nor thought in calculations and calendrics, suffer no toil of astrological reckoning, nor servitude to literary arts. Freed from the weight of worldly burdens, harmony naturally flourishes; in non-action and non-concern, one knows no fear or dread—this is the ease of the Dao. Thus, it is ease within difficulty.

The Confucian, by contrast, follows established laws and models, his conduct governed by rules, his speech and silence timed to the moment, his teachers found in every household, his books clarified by commentaries and annotations—this is the ease of Confucianism. Yet to probe the profound and reach the distant, to weave together the ancient canons, to master the *He* and *Luo* texts, to encompass the vast learning of the Hundred Schools, to cultivate virtue in humble lanes, to devote one’s resolve to serving the ruler, to strain one’s spirit toward celestial patterns, to apply one’s thought to wind and clouds—if one matter remains unknown, one’s work is incomplete; if a single word is amiss, praise and blame are misapplied. Every step becomes a model for the world; every utterance is transmitted across the land—this is the difficulty of Confucianism. Thus, it is difficulty within ease.

Weighing the two, the Confucian path is largely difficult, while the Daoist way is succinct and easy. I have already suffered its difficulties—why not abandon it and follow what is easy? Yet those who mock me in this world are as numerous as shoulder-to-shoulder crowds, while those who share my aspirations remain unseen. If like-minded souls exist, they must belong to a future age—for I do not consider them rare.

Some say: *'Among the eminent scholars and erudite masters I have encountered—those who have plumbed principles to their depths, exhausted the nature of things, and scrutinized the boundaries of being and non-being—many have there been. Yet none have spoken of extending life or attaining immortality. Though your brilliance may rival the sun and moon, and your thoughts surpass ten thousand men, you suddenly discourse on the Way of long life—how can I believe it?'* The *Baopuzi* replies: *'Am I a man of mediocre talent, shallow insight, and scant learning, who would dare claim unique understanding surpassing all others? Yet have I not sought what is manifest and found it in the obscure? Have I not obtained with ease what others deem difficult?'* "

Those who dispute minor proofs will recognize their greater efficacy; those who witness what has already come to pass will understand what has yet to be tested.

Moreover, those who disbelieve in the existence of immortals in this world generally possess only the talents of common scholars, the skills of officeholders, or the knowledge gleaned from books that aid instruction. They judge human affairs by what is near and easily discerned, and when confronted with the vulgar delusions of the multitude, they claim: *"What others doubt, I alone can decide; what has not yet shown its first signs, I can foresee."* Thus, they believe they have exhausted the nature of all things, penetrated all that is hidden and obscure, and obtained all that can be known. If I say there are no immortals, then immortals must not exist—so firmly entrenched is this conviction.

I have often observed mediocre scholars who stubbornly reject and disbelieve in such matters. Their failing lies in possessing some intelligence yet being crippled by partiality and constraint, shackled by their own limitations, unwilling to consider what lies beyond. They are utterly benighted, unable to distinguish beans from wheat. To use the narrow vision of a pipe to block what their own intelligence cannot grasp—how is this different from using a single-rope well bucket to draw water from a hundred-fathom depth, then blaming the well for having no water when they fail to recognize the inadequacy of their tool?

The common folk hear the roar of fierce winds and raging fires and call it winter thunder; they see drifting clouds moving west and claim the moon is racing east. Though others may tell them otherwise, they refuse to understand, placing such faith in their own judgments. Indeed, in matters of sound, none fail to trust their own ears—yet how often are they deceived?

Those who observe form place unwavering trust in their own eyes—yet when what they hear and see is affirmed as true but proves false, are their ears and eyes truly beyond doubt? How much more so with the mind’s conjectures, which discern neither form nor sound, and whose obscurity far exceeds that of sight and hearing. To take what one’s own heart grasps as certain and then, on that basis, dismiss the most distant matters of the world—claiming that tales of divine transcendents are mere fabrications—is this not folly?

The *Baopuzi* says: Beauty and ugliness are fixed, yet love and hatred arise from differing sentiments, so the two eyes do not see for one another. The refined and the vulgar have long been distinct, yet likes and dislikes vary, so the two ears do not hear for one another. Truth and falsehood have their essence, yet inclinations and aversions clash, so the two hearts do not counsel one another. There are those who take the ugly for the beautiful, the turbid for the clear, the loss for the gain—these three divergences are glaringly evident, so plain to see. Yet even in such obvious cases, no unity of perception can be achieved. How much less, then, with the subtleties of divine transcendence, where expecting all to believe is without any plausible basis? If every person were made to understand, what value would there be in those of profound insight?

To wait for the common folk to cease their reckless talk is to wait for the Yellow River to run clear—it would not be long by comparison. My silence is not absolute; I hope only to guide those who may ascend or descend in understanding. As for the unyielding, even the ancients could do nothing with them.

The *Baopuzi* says: The difficulty of clarifying the highest truths and the skepticism toward divine transcendence are of long standing. [...] The rustic of Ma’s wilds, the ancient Duke, could not dissuade the Rong and Di from their desires—truth had its limits, and good words went unheeded. The *zhangfu* cap found no market among the Man and Yue; the red shoes were not worn by the Rong and Yi. How could one force them? To point at jade and call it stone is not because the jade is false, but because only a He Shi can recognize it.

To see a dragon and call it a snake is not because the dragon lacks numinosity, but because only Cai Mo can distinguish it. The reason the Dao is prized is that it cannot be increased by addition nor diminished by subtraction. The reason virtue is prized is that one hears slander without gloom and sees praise without delight. Those who firmly believe the world has no immortals, while I alone contend that they truly exist—this dispute grows ever more protracted, and their obstinacy ever more entrenched. Is this not a futile prolongation of this endless strife, offering no remedy for their incomprehension? Should one truly follow the principle of the interlocking rings?

*Releasing Doubts*, Chapter Eight

Someone asked: The ways of humanity are manifold, and seeking immortality is supremely difficult. If one does not abandon worldly affairs, how can one simultaneously attend to literary arts, the concerns of sorrow and joy, and the Way of ruler and minister?

The *Baopuzi* replied: The Dao is not burdensome; what is rare is the few who act upon it. The only concern is whether one’s resolve is firmly established and one’s faith unwavering—why fear the neglect of human affairs? Those of great talent can cultivate both without difficulty. They inwardly treasure the Way of nurturing life and outwardly harmonize with the world. In governing the self, they achieve long cultivation; in governing the state, they bring great peace. They use the Six Classics to instruct the common people and transmit esoteric arts to those of like mind. If they wish to remain briefly, they stay to assist the times; if they wish to ascend, they soar into the clouds and lightly depart—these are the superior scholars. Those who rely on their own abilities but cannot achieve both may set aside worldly affairs and focus solely on cultivating the Dao—these are the next best. In ancient times, the Yellow Emperor bore the burden of the Four Seas yet did not hinder his ascent at Mount Dinghu. Peng Zu served as a grandee for eight hundred years before traveling west to the Flowing Sands. Boyang was a chronicler, Maofeng a potter, Fang Hui a scholar, Lü Wang a grand preceptor, Qiu Sheng served in Yin, Ma Tui was an official in Jin, Lord Fan conquered Yue and sailed the seas, Qin Gao held a tablet in Song, Kang Changsheng humbled himself to hold the whip, and Duke Zhuang concealed his talents as a minor scribe. Many ancients attained the Dao while aiding the world, cultivating it in court and market—surely they had surplus energy. Why must one retreat to the mountains and forests, completely abandoning the affairs of the people, before achieving success?

There are also those whose hearts find peace in stillness, whose natures abhor clamor, who delight in freedom and loathe honor and office. They wear coarse ropes and tattered clothes, eat wild herbs, hold the plow, and enjoy their three joys. They abide by constancy, await their end, do not scheme for a fleeting life, and do not fear swift death. They decline appointments worth a thousand gold and scorn the nobility of high ministers. Even without cultivation, they are already like this—how much less would they be willing to serve the world if they were to add the supreme Dao to their practice? Each follows their own aspirations; one cannot generalize.

The *Baopuzi* said: The world says that a single good word is worth a thousand gold—yet this refers only to the gains and losses of state or the right and wrong of personal conduct. As for teaching others the secret of long life or transmitting the method of immortality, this is not merely a good word like those of ordinary people. How could it be worth only a thousand gold? If someone on the verge of death from illness could be saved and restored to health, none would fail to call it a profound kindness and a great favor. Now, if one follows the immortal classics and ingests the Nine Elixirs of liquid gold and jade, the entire world could be made immortal—its beneficence would not be merely the merit of saving one person. The virtue of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi is truly immeasurable, yet none recognize it, dismissing it as absurd and preposterous—how lamentable!

The *Baopuzi* said: To seek immortality, one must grasp its essentials. The essentials lie in treasuring essence, practicing qi, and ingesting the great elixir—this is sufficient, and no more is needed. Yet these three matters again have shallow and deep levels. Without a named teacher or enduring hardship, one cannot master them hastily. Though it is said to practice qi, there are several methods of doing so. Though it is said to practice the bedchamber arts, there are nearly a hundred techniques therein. Though it is said to ingest elixirs, there are roughly a thousand formulas for doing so. When first teaching others, one begins with the shallow. Only when their resolve is unwavering and their diligence evident does one reveal the deeper methods.

Thus, practicing qi can cure a hundred illnesses, enter epidemics, subdue tigers and snakes, stanch wounds and bleeding, allow one to dwell in water or walk upon it, ward off hunger and thirst, or extend lifespan. Its great essential is embryonic breathing. Those who attain embryonic breathing can refrain from exhaling and inhaling through the nose and mouth, as if within the womb—then the Dao is achieved. When first learning to practice qi, one draws qi through the nose and holds it, silently counting to one hundred twenty before gently exhaling through the mouth. Both inhalation and exhalation should be inaudible to oneself. One should always inhale more and exhale less. A test is to place a goose feather on the nose and mouth—if the feather does not move when exhaling, this is the sign. Gradually increase the count over time, and eventually one can reach a thousand. At a thousand, the old become young again, and one day’s practice reverses a day’s aging.

One should practice qi during the time of living qi, not during the time of dead qi. Thus it is said: "The immortal ingests the six qi." In a day and night, there are twelve hours. From midnight to noon are the six hours of living qi; from noon to midnight are the six hours of dead qi. Practicing qi during dead qi is of no benefit. Those who use qi can exhale upon water, causing it to flow backward for several paces; exhale upon fire, causing it to extinguish; exhale upon tigers and wolves, causing them to crouch and be unable to move; exhale upon snakes and vipers, causing them to coil and be unable to depart. If another is wounded by blades or weapons, exhaling upon them stanches the blood. If one hears of someone struck by poison, even if unseen, one can exhale from afar, directing the breath—men exhale upon their left hand, women upon their right—and though the person may be a hundred miles away, they will immediately recover. For sudden afflictions or acute illnesses, merely swallowing the qi of three nines also brings instant relief. Yet human nature is often restless, and few can remain tranquil to cultivate this Way.

Moreover, in practicing qi, one should not eat too much raw vegetables or rich, fresh foods, as they make the qi strong and difficult to contain. One must also refrain from anger, for much anger disorders the qi, preventing its overflow and sometimes causing coughing—thus few can succeed in this practice. My great-grandfather, the Immortal Duke, whenever deeply drunk or in the height of summer’s heat, would enter the depths of deep pools and remain for a day before emerging—this was precisely because he could close his breath and practice embryonic breathing.

The bedchamber arts comprise over ten schools: some repair injuries and damages, some attack and cure various illnesses, some gather yin to replenish yang, and some increase years and extend life. Their great essential lies in the single matter of returning essence to replenish the brain.

This method is one passed down orally from one true adept to another, never committed to writing. Even if one consumes famous medicines yet remains ignorant of this essential teaching, one still cannot attain longevity. Moreover, one must not completely abstain from the union of yin and yang, for doing so leads to stagnation and obstruction, giving rise to pent-up resentment, loneliness, illness, and shortened life. Yet yielding to unbridled desire also diminishes one’s years and vitality. Only by attaining the harmony of regulated release can one avoid harm. Without the oral transmission of its techniques, not a single practitioner will fail to injure or even destroy themselves through this practice.

The likes of Xuansuzi, Rongchenggong, and Pengzu recorded only its coarse aspects, never committing its most essential teachings to paper. Those who aspire to immortality should diligently seek and practice it. I have recorded these words as I received them from my teacher, Master Zheng, to show future believers in the Dao—this is no groundless speculation. Yet I myself have not fully mastered its secrets.

Some Daoist practitioners of one narrow path may wish to focus solely on the arts of sexual union in pursuit of immortality, neglecting the great medicine of golden elixir—this is utter folly. As the *Baopuzi* states, the Daoist texts that derive from the Yellow Emperor and Laozi are but a small fraction. Most are the work of later enthusiasts, each adding their own interpretations, so that the volumes now pile up like mountains. The ancients were simple and often lacked literary talent; their discussions of natural principles were incomplete, their evidence unclear. They concealed their essential teachings, making them difficult to understand, and even when explained, the explanations were not profound enough to fully expound their meaning, open the way for the frustrated, encourage the determined, instruct beginners, or reveal the subtle paths of the mysterious and wondrous, the sources of fortune and misfortune. Merely reciting these texts ten thousand times will yield nothing. Even if one seeks broad learning, one should carefully select the finest works and focus on them, for texts that do not convey the essential Way are not worth poring over.

Some later learners fail to distinguish the depth of their authors. Taking anything labeled as Daoist teachings, they collect them indiscriminately until their boxes and baskets overflow, exhausting their minds in search of meaning—like seeking phoenix eggs in a swallow’s nest or trying to catch eels at the bottom of a well. No matter how diligent their efforts, they seek what cannot be found, gaining nothing of practical use. They waste their days and months in vain toil, reaping not even the slightest benefit. In public life, they fail in their duties; in private, they achieve no longevity. Thus, people inevitably point and say, “They practiced the Dao so diligently yet did not transcend the world—surely there is no method for avoiding death!” But they do not realize that such seekers of immortality are like those who stand by a river, admiring the fish but lacking a net—it is not that the water holds no fish.

The *Five Thousand Characters* may have come from Laozi, yet they are but general and cursory discussions. They never fully present a coherent method that can be verified. Merely reciting this scripture without grasping its essential Way is labor in vain—how much more so for those who fall short of even that?

As for Wenzi, Zhuangzi, Guanling Yinxin, and their like, though their florid writings trace back to the Yellow Emperor and Laozi, expounding the great principles of the mysterious and empty, they never reveal the ultimate teachings. Some even equate life and death, calling them no different, treating survival as forced labor and demise as rest—such views are already a thousand *li* removed from the path of immortality. How can they be worth indulging? Yet their allegories and parables may still offer some value for minor uses, filling gaps in times of need. Alas, that later generations of glib, unscrupulous, and immoral men have made Laozi and Zhuangzi their dens of refuge—is this not lamentable?

Some may ask: “In an age ruled by a sagely and enlightened sovereign who treasures only the worthy, if those who study immortality refuse to enter office and instead cultivate the Dao, who will assist in governance?” The *Baopuzi* replies: To turn one’s back on a sagely ruler and dwell in the mountains was why Chaofu and Xu You were esteemed. To encounter a virtuous age and withdraw from the world was why Zhuang Bo was honored. The Yellow Emperor ruled all under heaven in perfect order, yet Guangchengzi did not serve him. Tang Yao governed the four seas in great peace, yet Woquan did not assist him. Yet neither the transformative power of their virtue nor the supply of talented men suffered for it.

When Tang overthrew the Xia, Wu Guang bore a stone and cast himself into the river; when King Wu destroyed the Shang, Boyi and Shuqi refused to eat in the Western Mountains. When Duke Huan of Qi rose to power, Shao Ji reclined in a humble lane; when Wei Wen flourished, Gan Mu let his hair loose at his gate. The Four Elders withdrew to Shangluo, yet this did not hinder the great achievements of the Han; Zhou Dang stood aloof in the forests and marshes, yet this did no harm to the penal reforms of Emperor Wen. For such men, neither favor nor wealth could sway their hearts, nor could extreme riches alter their resolve. They washed their tassels in the Canglang’s waters, neither submitting nor suffering disgrace. They took fragrant groves as their pavilions, towering peaks as their mansions, emerald orchids as their cushions, green leaves as their curtains. They wore coarse cloth in place of court robes, ate wild greens instead of fine fare. Without tilling the soil themselves, they did not fill their bellies; without their wives’ weaving, they did not clothe their bodies. Such men have appeared from time to time over a thousand years—how much more so when they further abandon their kin to their clans, forsake their households without a glance, spurn glory as if it were dust, banish desire from their hearts, scale towering peaks alone, befriend echoes in the famed mountains, turn inward to the formless realm, and return to the utmost stillness? Within the eight directions, they guard against none, yet you fear the ruler will lack ministers—is this not excessive worry?

Some may say: “Those who study immortality merely purify themselves while neglecting the great bonds of society; they turn their backs on the sovereign and display disloyal disrespect. I fear they will fail to achieve long life and instead incur punishment.” The *Baopuzi* replies: The northerners Shi Hu and Shan Juan, along with Zizhou, were all men of great talent, yet they lived in seclusion, roamed freely, and nurtured their vast integrity. Neither the grand transformations of the age nor the great Way suffered loss for it. How much less should those who study immortality necessarily possess the talent to govern or the usefulness to stand in court? Gaining them would not add even a drop of dew’s benefit; losing them would not cause the slightest harm. Today, the nine provinces are united, and even those from distant lands come to serve. The virtuous and capable are abundant, yet there are idle officials awaiting posts and long-serving ones sighing over delayed promotions. Those of high talent chafe under seniority constraints. The flourishing of worthy men is unmatched—how could the realm lack even a single commoner?

In former times, Prince Jin abandoned his duty to attend the royal meals and relinquished his position as heir apparent, yet King Ling did not reproach him for disloyalty. Yin Sheng resigned his official sash and departed from his post, yet the Zhou did not condemn him for disloyalty. Why? They truly understood that such men did not slight the world or despise the ruler; their aspirations simply differed from those of ordinary men and could not be altered. A ruler of the Dao harbors tolerance and forgiveness, knowing that human hearts cannot be the same and that each person’s path in life differs. He does not compel or forbid, thereby honoring and magnifying the highest principles. Above, he bears no grudging narrowness; below, he enjoys the utmost joy of those who follow their hearts. Thus, his radiant reputation shines in all directions, and even the greedy, hearing of it, feel shame.

I have heard that when the balmy winds rise, fur robes and stoves are put away; when the world’s path is level, extraordinary men withdraw. Now that the ravages of war are settled, oxen are left to rest and horses released; beacon fires are extinguished, and the shadows of shields and spears are stilled. The mighty bows *Fanruo* and *Luque* are sheathed and cooked. Zhang Liang left the dark tent to return to the lanes; Xin Pi doffed his armor to take up fishing. How much more so for those who study immortality—of whom there is not even one in ten thousand—what harm could the state suffer in sparing them?

Yet their practice lies in few thoughts and scant desires; their work lies in preserving the body and prolonging life. They do not engage in the ugliness of competition nor bear the burden of offending custom—what crime is there in this? Moreover, the peaks of Hua and Heng are vast, the ocean’s expanse boundless. Their heights do not await the arrival of soaring dust; their depths do not rely on the inflow of rainwater. A handful of earth cannot diminish their loftiness; a ladle of water cannot lessen their breadth. In a generation, there are but a few immortals—how could they disrupt the world’s affairs?

Some may say: “If the Dao of immortality can truly be sought and attained, why do the Five Classics not record it? Why do Zhou and Confucius not speak of it? Why do the sages not transcend the world? Why do the supremely wise not live forever? If Zhou and Confucius did not know of it, they cannot be called sages; if they knew but did not study it, then there is no Dao of immortality.” The *Baopuzi* replies: Human lives correspond to the stars, each with its destined station—this has already been explained in the preceding chapter. You might as well wear a basin on your head and look up, failing to see the radiance of the seven luminaries, or briefly crane your neck toward a great river, unaware of the wonders in its depths.

There are countless things the Five Classics do not record; there are many matters Zhou and Confucius did not speak of. Let me briefly mention a fraction for you. Though you may laugh uncontrollably and your feelings may be hard to open, let me at least allow you to hear a rough outline.

Heaven and earth are the greatest of all things. The nine sages together composed the *Book of Changes*, which suffices to encompass yin and yang—nothing more can be added. Now, ask a master of the *Changes*: What are the degrees of the celestial sphere? How vast are the four seas? How far apart are the cosmos? Where does the upper limit end? Where does the lower limit rest? What propels their turning and movement? Why do the sun and moon vary in speed? What causes the nine paths’ excesses, the alternation of dawn and dusk, the lengthening and shortening of days? How do the seven stars take turns governing? How do the five planets wax and wane? What causes the halos, eclipses, conjunctions, and transgressions of the four and seven? Where do comets and broom stars emerge? What are the anomalies of celestial arrows and vapors? What are the omens of solar halos and old stars? Why does the pole star not move while the guardian star still drifts east? Why does the sun’s light extend beyond its form, while the moon’s light is cold within? Why does the Milky Way, when viewed from above, embody the nature of moisture flowing downward? Why do the tides ebb and flow with great and small variations? How do the five notes and six pitches convey emotions of joy and anger? How do clouds move and qi rise, bearing signs of fortune and misfortune? What are the *changqiang*, *you shi*, *xun*, and *shizhong*? What are the *sizhen* and *wucan*? What are the *tiangou* and *guixie*? Some bring success through the son, others ruin through the proper way. Even the *Changes* at its most profound cannot address these matters.

Next, ask the masters of the *Spring and Autumn*, the Four Books, the *Book of Songs*, and the Three Ritual Classics—none will be able to answer. They will all say: “These are entirely unrecorded in the orthodox classics; only Wu Xian, Gan Gong, Shi Shen, and Hai Zhong’s Xi Meng recorded them in the *Seven Luminaries*.”

Shall I then ask: Are these six schools’ works to be considered teachings of the classics? They will answer: No. I shall further ask: Are Gan and Shi and their like sages? They too will answer: No. Yet if one is born beneath heaven and grows old upon the earth, seeking these things in the Five Classics yields nothing; searching in the books of Zhou and Confucius brings no result. Should we then dismiss them all as false and delusory?

The world is vast beyond measure, and even what meets the eye cannot be fully grasped—how much less the mysteries within mysteries, the subtleties beyond all subtlety? Again, I ask the common folk: Are there not lands where chariots ride clouds and jade is born from the earth? Peoples whose livers and hearts never decay? Those who dwell in nests or caves, who have but one eye or three heads, or whose bodies are like horses’ manes or dogs’ hooves? Beings with elongated arms crossing their thighs, or yellow pools where no men exist? Those with pierced chests or laboring mouths, who build granaries upon stone or rise from sand dunes? Do not dragons and women, Nü Wa and Gong Gong, emerge from the earth, piercing the heavens? Do not falling tiles take flight, and grand words resound? Do mountains shift and altars move? Do entire armies transform in a single morning—nobles into cranes, commoners into sand? Do not the ugly lean upon withered trees, while the Two Fu embrace pillars, and the lodger guards against collapse? The two-headed snake, the bow of *chun* wood, the unburning timber, the unextinguished fire, the birds of Changshu, the eyeless beasts, the headless bodies, the formless trunks? Does not Jingwei fill the sea, do not trees yield their places in turn, does not fire-washed cloth exist, the knife that cuts jade? Does not Yanmei spit flames, does not grinding mud strain water, does not withered wood revive when watered, does not form change upon mountains? The nine-headed *Xiu*, the *Bifang* bird, the *Ren Yu*, the *Shao Qian*’s exorcism, the *Bo Shuai*’s service to the sage minister, the *Su Shuang*’s frost over the western Qiang, the rise of the Xianbei through riding turtles, the strength of Linyi through divine records, the rule of Yongshu through floating corpses, the divine marriage of Emperor Jian, the flying *Zongmu*—do not these transform the world in the peaks of Jing? Did not the Five Ding lure the serpent to topple the mountains, while within, wings beat across the three seas? Were not the golden slips and jade characters revealed beside Yu’s Well? The true mechanism, the balanced scale, the cleaving within patterned stone—are not these marvels numbered in the thousands? Yet the Five Classics do not record them, and Zhou and Confucius do not speak of them. Should we then declare they never occurred?

Consider further: The southerners who enter pillars and exit through their ears; those who halt bandits by stopping water with their elbows and shoot arrows with their arms; Bohun who treads cliffs of ten thousand *ren* yet stands on tiptoe; Lü Liang who walks singing over abysses; Duke Song who carved mulberry leaves to deceive the eye; Gongshu Ban whose wooden kites soared; Li Zhu who discerned the tip of a hair at a hundred paces; Ben and Huo who displayed their strength with ten thousand *jun*; the man of Yue who revived the dead with a needle; Shu Hai who leapt over a thousand paces; the craftsman of Ying who swung his axe at the whitewash on his nose—did not Zhong Du bare his body in freezing weather? None of these could Zhou or Confucius perform. Can we then claim they do not exist?

If the sage truly has limits, then it is no wonder he did not attain immortality. And if he did not attain immortality, this does no harm to his sageliness. The sage may simply not have mastered such arts—why make this the basis of attack? The sage may choose not to follow the same path, letting nature take its course. He has a body but does not claim it as his own; he has life but does not strive to preserve it. He entrusts life and death to heaven, accepts long and short as fate—so why should it be strange that he did not study immortality?

**On the Meaning of the Dao, Ninth**

The Master of Embracing Simplicity said: The Dao encompasses heaven and embraces earth, yet in its origin it has no name. To speak of its nonbeing, even echoes and shadows are more substantial than it. To speak of its being, even the myriad things are as nothing before it. Fu Xi could not reckon its multitude, Li Zhu could not perceive its likeness, Wu Zha and Jin Ye could not fathom its sounds. Within the six directions, the *huanxi* and *huanzhu* beasts run swiftly yet cannot trace its signs. Beyond the cosmos, in nearness it flows through autumn down yet remains inexhaustible; in farness it pervades the great void yet is not filled.

The sound’s sound, the echo’s echo, the form’s form, the shadow’s shadow—what is square attains stillness through it, what is round attains motion; what descends attains prostration, what ascends attains reverence. To forcibly name it as the Dao is already to lose its truth, let alone dissecting it a thousand times, dividing it a hundredfold, or splitting it into ten thousand parts—its nature thereby reduced to boundlessness, how far then does it stray from the Dao!

The common folk cannot recognize the original root of the Great Beginning and instead cultivate its decadent branches. Those who can remain serene and joyful, unstained and unshaken, nurture their minds with desirelessness and preserve their spirits with purity; they sweep away temptations and return to rectitude, dispel thoughts of hard-to-attain things, cast off burdens that harm the true, temper the aberrations of joy and anger, and extinguish the roots of love and aversion. Then, without seeking blessings, blessings come; without averting misfortune, misfortune departs.

Why is this so? Because destiny lies within, not dependent on externals; the Dao abides here, with no need to await that. Alas! Ordinary people cannot preserve the true: lacking the restraint of barriers, they are swayed by desires and attachments, galloping in dissipation, lost without return. Their emotions stir in response to things, their wisdom overflows in engagement with affairs; seduced by what can be desired, the heavenly principle is extinguished. Deluded by what they see and hear, their purity is corrupted. Their minds are enslaved by extravagance, their spirits muddled by turbulence. Thus, they bring upon themselves calamities of ruin and misfortunes of irretrievable collapse. Yet all they do is slaughter fattened beasts, offer rich libations, strike gongs and drums, sing and dance, prostrate themselves, and kowtow, sitting in stillness and emptiness to beg for blessings, hoping for their fulfillment—until death, they never awaken. How pitiful!

Moreover, their vital essence is exhausted by vexations, their constructive and defensive *qi* depleted by toil. Their form and *qi* are scorched and worn, their heavenly harmony eroded; labor and rest are imbalanced, and their heads are shattered in supplication. Illness arises in the marrow, yet they pray for healing; they lie in wind and dampness, apologizing to spirits, or disrupt their diet and blame demons. Such a frail body brings these afflictions upon itself—what aid can heaven, earth, or the gods provide? What use are their exhaustive sacrifices of livestock?

For blessings are not obtained through obsequious entreaty.

Disasters are not averted through solemn sacrifices. If fate could be prolonged by repeated prayers and illness dispelled by lavish offerings, then the wealthy would surely live forever and the noble would never fall ill. Yet the gods do not accept sacrifices from those outside their lineage, nor do demons partake of wanton rites. The lanes of the poor cannot attract the golden chariots of the highborn, nor can the gates of commoners stir the six-horse teams of the noble. All share the same human form, yet their stations are worlds apart—how much more so with the celestial gods, distant and exalted, whose nature differs entirely? Their majesty is absolute; surely they cannot be moved by the stinking wine and meager dishes of the vulgar, nor by the bowing and scraping of the common folk. This much is clear.

Those who are disloyal and unfilial commit the gravest of sins. Even if they offer bribes of a thousand gold or feasts of the finest cattle to a wise ruler, they cannot erase their infamy or absolve their guilt before the state. If men cannot absolve men, how much less can one hope to gain long life or cure grave illness through such means? The gods are of a different order, upright and just; to expect their partial favor is delusion. A ruler ashamed of his own failings or a minister who endures disgrace can still reward the good without bribes and punish the wicked without bias, adhering strictly to law without favor or prejudice. How much more so with the gods, who surpass men immeasurably? They cannot be swayed by clever words or bribes in disguise. The lesson is plain.

King Ling of Chu personally performed as a shaman, sparing no sacrifice, yet could not repel the Wu army’s invasion. The Prince of Guangling in Han exhausted his treasury in devotion to Li Ban, yet could not escape the punishment for rebellion. Emperor Wen, though he revered the gods with unstinting rites, could not avoid his death at Five Oaks. Sun Zhu, despite his noble rank and the enfeoffment of Hua Xiang, could not prolong his allotted span. It was not that their sacrifices were meager or their jades and silks insufficient; their sincerity was not lacking, nor was their reverence shallow. They suffered mountains of loss yet gained not a hair’s benefit. Is this not a case of neglecting what lies near while chasing what is far?

When the Fifth Duke eradicated sorcerous ways, he lived long and prospered. When Lujiang of Song abolished mountain sacrifices, his blessings and fortune endured. When Wen Weng destroyed the shrine of the Water Spirit, he enjoyed personal safety and the people’s peace. When the Martial Emperor of Wei banned wanton rites, great fortune followed. These past events are no falsehood—they serve as mirrors for the future.

Virtue alone is fragrant; those without care live long. Frugality preserves life, while excess invites early death. To follow the natural course is to age in health; external forces are of no avail. If one’s nurturing is imbalanced and depletion unchecked, a hundred ailments take root, and the constructive and defensive *qi* wither away. What use then are the grand sacrifices of the three beasts?

The customs of the day are rife with deceit, each fraud begetting another, growing ever more entrenched. The people, unable to cure their illnesses or awaken from their delusions, abandon the remedies of medicine and stone, clinging instead to the folly of prayers and sacrifices. They beseech without end, consult oracles without wearying. The petty sorcerers and shamans spin tales of calamity and possession, of illness and crisis—nothing is beyond their fabrication. At their behest, the people exhaust their wealth: the rich deplete their stores, the poor borrow at usurious rates, selling their fields and homes until their coffers are empty. If by chance they recover, they credit the gods; if they die, they blame the demons’ refusal to pardon. Should they survive by luck, their fortunes are spent, leaving them to freeze and starve. Some turn to banditry or burglary, meeting their end at the tip of an arrow or under the blade of the executioner, their bodies disgraced by punishment—all due to these very practices.

Some squander all their possessions on sacrificial expenses, their grain and silk consumed by greedy shamans. When the shamans die, there is no money left for coffins or shrouds, and their corpses rot in the open—a pitiable end. How deep is the blindness of the foolish people!

Wanton rites and sorcerous deceit are forbidden by ritual and law, yet the common folk remain obstinate in their delusion. It falls to the ruler to sharpen the laws: let no offense, great or small, escape the death penalty. Offer bounties for the capture of shamans who refuse to cease, and execute them without mercy, displaying their bodies in the marketplace. In time, such practices will surely be eradicated. This is no small kindness—it will cut off the source of hunger and cold, stifle the sprouts of theft and banditry.

In times past, there were men like Zhang Jiao, Liu Gen, Wang Xin, and Li Shen, who claimed to be a thousand years old, peddling petty tricks—appearing and vanishing at will, changing their forms and faces—to deceive the common folk. They gathered the foolish, forming bands of rebels. Their aims were not to prolong life or cure illness, but to amass treacherous followers and stir up rebellion. Soon enough, they met their just deserts, some even bringing ruin to the innocent. Others swindled the people for wealth, amassing mountains of money and silk, surpassing even the nobility in riches. They indulged in extravagance and debauchery, feasting on delicacies, their chambers filled with concubines, their halls lined with musicians, their service manned by assassins and desperadoes. Their power overshadowed local lords, their might intimidated officials, and fugitives found refuge in their dens. All this stemmed from the failure of authorities to suppress them—truly a cause for lament.

Though we common folk may see the truth, without office, what can we do? Those who govern fear that banning such practices might invite divine retribution. Even if some officials harbor doubts, they hesitate to act, deeming it a matter of secondary importance, not urgent enough to risk their careers. Their foolish wives and stubborn children cling to these beliefs, and their underlings, fearing to offend, echo their warnings. Thus, hesitation prevails, and none dare act—how maddening!

I have personally known several men who never worshipped the gods or offered prayers, yet lived long lives, attained high rank, and saw their descendants flourish in wealth and nobility.

For my part, I have no business with such matters, observing only the seasonal sacrifices to my ancestors. I have traveled by land and water for ten thousand *li*, passing by no fewer than a hundred shrines and temples along the way. Yet on every journey, I have paid them no heed, and my carriages have never overturned, nor have I encountered storms while crossing rivers. Though I have often faced epidemics, I have always found the aid of medicine. Though I have braved arrows and blades, I have suffered no wounds. This only confirms the powerlessness of gods and demons.

Among the hundred-odd sorcerous sects, all but the Way of Li indulge in blood sacrifices. The latter, though it spares lives, still demands lavish offerings for its rituals—no expense is spared in procuring the finest and freshest provisions. Feeding dozens of people, their kitchen costs are no small matter.

Moreover, these practices are not purely simple or frugal—all such sects rightfully belong on the list of those to be banned and eradicated.

Someone asked, "When did the Way of Li originate?" I replied: During the reign of Emperor Da of Wu, there was a man from Shu named Li A who dwelt in caves and ate nothing. He lived for generations, and those who saw him called him the "Eight-Hundred-Year Elder." People often came to consult him, but Li A never spoke; he merely observed their countenance. If their expression was joyful, all matters would be auspicious. If their face bore sorrow, all matters required caution. If Li A smiled, great fortune would follow; if he sighed in warning, deep misfortune lay ahead. Such omens never once failed.

Later, one day, he suddenly vanished without a trace. Afterward, a man named Li Kuan arrived in Wu from Shu. Fluent in the Shu dialect, he could chant incantations over water to cure illnesses, and many were healed. Thus, people far and near unanimously declared Kuan to be Li A and called him "Li Eight-Hundred," though in truth he was not. From high ministers to commoners, none failed to flock to his door. Later, he grew arrogant and noble, no longer granting regular audiences. Visitors could only bow at his outer gate before departing. Such was his strangeness and eccentricity.

Officials evading service and commoners alike took Kuan as their master, numbering nearly a thousand. Yet those who advanced to the inner chambers and attained high mastery learned nothing more than chanting over water, the Three Section Talismans, and the circulation of solar and lunar *qi*—daily practices devoid of the essential methods for cultivating the body, ingesting divine medicines, or attaining longevity and immortality. Breath ingestion and grain avoidance could sustain one for a hundred days or so, but no longer—such was the shallowness of his arts.

Many of my acquaintances who had met Kuan attested that he grew frail and decrepit with age, coughing and sighing, his eyes dim, ears deaf, teeth fallen, and hair white. Gradually, his mind dulled, and he forgot his own children and grandchildren, no different from an ordinary man. Yet the people insisted that Kuan feigned infirmity to deceive them—could this truly be so?

Once, Wu suffered a great epidemic, and over half the population perished. The chamber where Kuan practiced his Way was called a "lodge." During the outbreak, Kuan himself contracted a febrile illness. Claiming to enter the lodge for purification and fasting, he died within it. Yet his followers still insisted he had transformed his form and achieved *shijie*—a corpse-liberated immortal—not truly dead.

The distinction of the divine immortals' methods lies precisely in their transcendence of aging and death. Now, Kuan aged and died—this alone proves he had not attained the Dao. What further doubt could there be? If, by the standards of immortal arts, one ought to achieve *shijie*, why not remain among the people for a century or two, living without death, before departing? The Dao of immortality is not absent in this world—Kuan was simply not the one to attain it.

I have discussed Kuan’s case in such detail because his disciples, passing down their teachings to one another, have spread throughout the lands south of the Yangtze, numbering in the thousands. Unaware of the shallowness of Kuan’s methods and unworthy of their adherence, they cling to them in hopes of transcending the world. My aim is to awaken people to this delusion and dispel their stubborn confusion.

In the world, things that seem true but are false are countless. Let me briefly recount a few stories to enlighten those who fail to understand.

Long ago in Runan, a man set snares in the fields to trap roe deer. After leaving, he still worried about retrieving his catch, though he neglected to tend to the traps. A fisherman passing by saw them and, placing a salted fish in one of the snares, departed. When the owner returned and found the fish, he was astonished, believing it to be a divine omen. Afraid to take it home, he spread the word among the villagers. Together, they built a shrine and established a temple, naming it the "Lord Bao." Over time, more devotees gathered—red pillars, painted beams, and unceasing bells and drums. If someone’s illness happened to improve, they claimed it was the work of the divine. Travelers passing by never failed to make offerings. After seven or eight years, the fisherman returned to the temple and asked about its origins. When the villagers explained, he said, "This is merely my salted fish. What divinity is there?" And so the worship ceased.

Another tale: In Nandun, a man named Zhang Zhu was plowing a white field when he found a plum sapling in his path. Feeling pity for it, he wanted to take it home but, unable to dig it up at the moment, left it there, covering its roots with damp soil and placing it in a hollow mulberry tree before forgetting about it. Later, Zhang took a distant post and was away for some time. Meanwhile, the villagers noticed the plum tree suddenly growing in the mulberry and called it a miracle. A man with an eye ailment rested beneath the tree and prayed, "Lord Li, if you heal my eyes, I will offer you a pig in thanks." When his eyes happened to improve, he slaughtered the pig and made his offering. Word spread, growing ever more exaggerated, until people claimed the tree could restore sight to the blind. From near and far, they came in droves to seek blessings—carriages and horses filled the roads, and wine and meat flowed like rivers. This continued for several years until Zhang returned from his post. Seeing the commotion, he said, "This is merely the plum sapling I once planted. What divinity is there?" He then cut down the tree, and the worship ended.

Moreover, in Runan, there was a Peng family tomb near a major road, with a stone statue standing at its entrance. An old peasant woman, returning from the market with several cakes she had bought, passed by the tomb as the day grew hot. She placed the cakes on the statue’s head to rest beneath the trees, then hurried off and forgot to retrieve them. Travelers who saw the cakes on the stone statue’s head found it strange and asked about it. Someone claimed, "This stone statue has divine power to heal illnesses, and those who recover offer cakes in thanks." The story spread, and people began saying, "Those with headaches should rub the statue’s head, and those with stomachaches should rub its belly—even rubbing oneself after touching the statue brings healing." From a thousand *li* away, people came to seek treatment from the stone statue. At first, they offered only chickens and pigs, but later, they brought oxen and sheep, setting up tents and pavilions with a constant stream of visitors. This continued for several years until the old woman who had forgotten the cakes heard about it. She explained the truth to others, and no one returned to worship the statue.

Furthermore, west of Luoyang, an ancient tomb had collapsed, filling with water. The tomb contained much lime, and the lime-infused water was effective for treating sores. One summer, a traveler suffering from sores, exhausted by the heat, saw the clear water in the tomb and bathed in it. His sores unexpectedly healed. Word spread, and all who were ill came to wash themselves. Some even drank the water to treat internal ailments. The people living near the tomb built a shrine there and sold the water. Those who came to buy it also made offerings at the shrine, with wine and meat flowing endlessly. As demand grew, the water was depleted, so the sellers secretly added other water at night to supplement it. Travelers from afar, unable to visit, sent messengers or entrusted others to buy the water for them. Thus, the sellers grew wealthy. Some claimed there was no divinity at work, and officials eventually prohibited the practice, filling in the tomb to stop it.

In Xinggu, a prefectural governor named Ma had a relative or old friend who sought his aid. Ma instructed the man to stay outside the city and pretended he was a divine Daoist healer, curing illnesses instantly with his touch. He also sent eloquent men to spread rumors, claiming he could restore sight to the blind and enable the lame to walk. Soon, people from all directions flocked to him like a marketplace, and his wealth in money and silk piled up like a mountain. He ordered all who sought healing—even if they were not immediately cured—to tell others they had recovered. This way, they would eventually heal; if they claimed otherwise, they would never recover. The method was infallible, and none dared doubt it. Thus, when later visitors asked earlier ones about their condition, they all said they had been cured. Within ten days, Ma’s relative amassed great wealth.

Most people, though small-minded and foolish, dismiss methods for extending life and achieving longevity as empty fictions, yet readily believe in malevolent spirits and omens, dancing and praying to so-called gods—all of which are merely Ma’s deceptions to swindle people. I record these several cases as a warning to those who remain unaware.

Someone asked, "Why are there those who, though ignorant of the Way, techniques, or arts, still live in peace and enjoy long lives?"

The Master Embracing Simplicity replied: "Such people may possess hidden virtues or good deeds that bring them blessings, or their fate may simply be long, allowing them to age slowly and die late. Some may also be fortunate enough to avoid disasters by chance—like game left behind in a hunt or grass and trees spared by a passing wildfire. To protect oneself and ward off harm, one should cultivate the arts of guarding the body, wear talismans of celestial patterns, and carry a sword. Ritual sacrifices and prayers are of no benefit; one should rely on one’s own inviolability, not on the forbearance of spirits. However, contemplating the mysterious and holding to unity, embracing the light and encircling the body, can repel evil and dispel misfortune, though it cannot extend life or cure bodily illnesses. Those who follow nature without techniques may still live out their natural years, yet they will have no means to resist violent spirits or great epidemics. Storing armor and stockpiling rain gear is like preparing for war or rain—if neither comes, their absence makes no difference. But when arrows and stones fill the air like mist and smoke, the unarmored will suffer; when torrential rains or blizzards strike, those standing in the open will struggle. One cannot dismiss the vast forces of yin and yang because of the insignificance of shepherd’s purse or wheat, lest later learners be misled into believing techniques are useless."

**On Clarifying the Origin (Chapter Ten)**

Someone asked about the precedence of Confucianism and Daoism.

The Master Embracing Simplicity answered: "The Dao is the root of Confucianism, and Confucianism is the branch of the Dao. The arts of yin and yang are full of taboos, making people timid and fearful. Confucianism is broad but lacks essentials, laborious yet unproductive. Mohism is frugal but hard to follow. Legalism is strict but unkind, harming benevolence and righteousness. Only the teachings of the Daoist school enable people to focus their spirit, move in harmony with non-action, encompass the strengths of Confucianism and Mohism, and summarize the essentials of Legalism. They adapt to changing times, respond to the transformations of things, are concise and easy to understand, require little effort yet yield great results, and aim to preserve the simplicity of the great origin and uphold the source of true integrity. Yet Ban Gu, following Sima Qian, placed the Yellow Emperor and Laozi before the Six Classics, calling Qian’s arrangement mistaken. Qian’s vast knowledge and comprehensive synthesis allowed him to sift through matters, verify the good and bad of events, and assess the uprightness of ancient figures. His evaluations truly stem from nature, and his praises and criticisms are all measured with care."

The ultimate principle neither falsely praises nor indiscriminately condemns, nor does it echo conventions merely to conform. Liu Xiang, a master scholar of his generation, called it a faithful record, yet Ban Gu’s judgments cannot be hastily accepted as correct. Ban Gu was indeed a pure Confucian, unversed in the intent of the Dao, and thus, constrained by his habitual learning, he could not strike the proper balance.

What is meant by the Dao? Is it merely the matter of nurturing life? The *Yi Jing* states: "The Dao of Heaven is established as yin and yang; the Dao of Earth is established as softness and hardness; the Dao of Man is established as benevolence and righteousness." It further says: "The *Yi* contains the four Dao of the sage." Yet if the right person is not present, the Dao does not manifest in vain. It also says: "In a flourishing and peaceful world, it is called having the Dao; in a perilous state with a chaotic ruler, it is called lacking the Dao." Again: "To sit and discourse on the Dao is the role of the Three Dukes." In a state possessing the Dao, the poor and lowly feel shame.

In all discussions of the Dao, from the Two Modes above to the myriad things below, none do not proceed from it. Yet the Yellow Emperor and Laozi grasped its root, while the Confucians and Mohists managed its branches. Those today who are called masters of the Dao must broadly comprehend past and present, observe the heavens above and examine the earth below, traverse changes and cross boundaries, fathom the course of rise and fall, and grasp the essence of order and chaos. With minds free of confusion and answers to every question, why must they cultivate the methods of longevity or admire the martial prowess of Master Red Pine and Qiao? Yet the pedantic scholars, with their narrow views and arbitrary judgments, upon hearing of those who dwell in mountains and forests and follow the teachings of Bo Yang, deride and mock them, saying, "Those are but minor arts, not worth considering."

Alas!

Those who clutch fireflies within cramped walls never witness the radiance of heaven’s light; those who consort with gudgeons and shrimp in shallow pools never know the vastness of the four seas. They weigh the depth of rivers and streams yet do not realize that what spews them forth is Kunlun; they prize the harvest of millet and panicled millet yet do not perceive that what brings forth their luxuriance is fertile soil.

Now, if one merely extols Confucian arts yet does not know that what perfects them arises from the Dao—this Dao is what tempers the hundred schools, casts the Two Modes, gestates the myriad beings, and ferments the constant norms. In the world, the shallow and near are many, while the deep and far are few; the few cannot prevail over the many—this has been so for ages. Thus, though Sima Qian was profound, he was not praised; though Ban Gu was limited, he was not censured. Yet things are valued for their rarity and disdained for their abundance—how could human affairs be otherwise? Weeds blanket the plains while magic mushrooms seldom appear; thorns and brambles overrun the wilds while fragrant trees bloom but rarely. Sands and pebbles are countless, yet pearls and jades are scarce. Hawks and falcons swarm the skies, but phoenixes seldom take flight; vipers and lizards fill the marshes, yet dragons are rarely seen. That Ban Gu had many partisans is only fitting.

The Dao, when applied inwardly, governs the self; when extended outwardly, it orders the state. It ensures the Seven Governors follow their courses, the Two Qi harmonize, and the four seasons do not err in their cycles of cold and warmth. Wind and rain do not turn violent to become calamities for creatures. The Jade Candle heralds the signs of ascending peace; clear sweet wine manifests the tokens of virtue’s harmony. The burning wheel and rainbow lie dormant, their omens quelled; clouds of decay and Shangyang birds fold their wings. Splendor shines on high, auspicious grain gathers in abundance, pestilence does not spread, and disasters do not arise. Moats and ramparts are not built, shields and spears are not wielded. Without deliberation, all is fitting; without covenant, trust is firm; without binding, all is secure; without scheming, all is accomplished; without reward, all are encouraged; without punishment, all is solemn. What is sought is obtained without seeking; what is forbidden ceases without prohibition. Those above are not deemed burdensome; those ahead are not resented as troubles. Before a command is issued, customs shift; before an order is given, mores transform—such is the governance of the Dao.

Thus, when the Dao flourishes, the Three August Ones and Five Emperors ruled with folded arms, and all was more than sufficient.

When the Dao declines, then Shun and Yu gallop in vain and all is insufficient. Only when there is sufficiency does non-action bring forth beautiful transformation; only when there is insufficiency do punishments grow harsh and villainy multiply. The common folk grieve below, the august spirits rage above. Some see floods surge wildly; some endure scorching droughts that parch the earth; some witness mountains and valleys shift their forms; some hear winter thunder and summer snow. Some see blood flow like drifting masts, corpses pile like city walls; some see trenches swallow tens of thousands, bones split and children exchanged. The higher the walls, the craftier the siege engines; the deeper the moats, the more ingenious the ladders. The clearer the laws, the more thieves and bandits; the more frequent the covenants, the worse the rebellions. Like fish and turtles stirred in the depths by stormy waves, like birds flustered in the marshes by fine nets, like beasts harried in the forests by packs of wolves, like minnows scorched in the cauldron by fierce flames—so it is when the Dao declines.

Then rulers and ministers swap places; fathers and sons turn blades upon each other. Only then is loyalty and righteousness named in perilous states; only then do filial sons win praise in ruined households. Plagues arise, and shamans and physicians grow esteemed; the Dao and virtue wane, and the Confucians and Mohists gain weight. From this, the sequence of Confucianism and Daoism may be determined.

Someone asked: "In former times, Master Red Pine, Wang Qiao, Qin Gao, Laozi, Peng Zu, Wu Cheng, and Yu Hua—all true persons—served in the world and did not flee to seclusion. Yet since the middle ages, those who pursue the Dao all vanish without trace into hidden valleys. Why is this?"

Master Baopu replied: "In ancient times, simplicity reigned and crafty falsity had not yet sprouted. Those who clearly trusted the Dao studied it diligently; those who did not trust it remained silent. Slanderous words were not spoken; malicious thoughts did not lodge in the breast."

Thus, true persons moved unhurried among the people, never hastening toward transcendence. In later ages, moral decay and artifice grew ever deeper; the transformative influence of the pure and tranquil waned, while the factions of perverse customs multiplied. Those who did not trust the Dao delighted in slander, calling the truly upright "deviant" and "errant," dismissing divine transcendents as "deluded" and "absurd." Some accused them of "bewitching the masses," others of "disrupting the social order." Therefore, the loftiest scholars felt shame in dwelling among them.

In former times, the perspicacious perceived the first signs of decline and fled at the slightest hint of danger, departing by night without waiting for dawn. They acted at the first glimpse of peril, not lingering until the day’s end. Thus, when Zhao plotted against Mingdu, Confucius turned his carriage back; when no sweet wine was offered, Musheng departed at once. If even Hua Yuan left such a place, how much more should the wise? Their pursuits and roots were fundamentally different—what attachment could they have to remaining there?

When the deep pools dry and the marshes drain, the jiao-dragons no longer swim. When nests topple and eggs are gathered, the phoenixes no longer alight. When one dwells in a house where slander is spoken, the soaring gulls do not descend. When common plants are pruned in spring, the sacred mushrooms and lingzhi do not flourish. As vulgar customs vilify the upright and heap scorn upon them, how could those who possess the Dao not spread their wings and soar beyond? Would they not rather conceal their tracks in the boundless expanse of the unseen?

The mountains and forests are not the Dao itself, yet those who seek the Dao inevitably enter the mountains and forests. Truly, they wish to distance themselves from the foul stench of the world and draw near to this purity. Those who enter the nine chambers to refine their thoughts and preserve the true unity to summon the spirits do not delight in clamor or defilement. Those who compound the great elixir of golden cinnabar and refine the flying essence of the eight minerals particularly abhor the chatter of fools and the perceptions of the vulgar. When such disturbances arise, the numinous spirits do not descend, and the divine medicines do not form—this is no minor prohibition.

If one remains among people, shallow critics may slander them, officials may impose punishments or bestow favors, and relatives or old acquaintances may entangle them with celebrations or mourning. How much better to retreat to secluded valleys, entirely free from such verminous nuisances! Those who wander alone in the distant wilds find their joy in the towering peaks—is this not the reason?

Some say: "The highest scholars attain the Dao amid the three armies, the middling scholars attain it in the cities, and the lowest scholars attain it in the mountains and forests." This refers to those who have already completed the divine medicine but do not yet wish to ascend to heaven. Though they may be among the three armies, blades cannot harm them; though they may be in the cities, human calamities cannot touch them. The lowest scholars have not yet reached this stage, so they remain in the mountains and forests. It does not mean that those of the highest rank, when first learning the Dao, should dwell among the three armies or in the cities to attain it.

Thus, the teachings of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi could endure to this day without fading.

Someone asked: "The Dao is the source, while Confucianism is but its later stream—I have already heard this teaching. But what, then, are the minor differences of the present age?"

Gezhi Zibao (The Master Who Embraces Simplicity) said: The teachings of rising and descending, bowing and looking up; the rituals of three thousand refinements; the arts of attack and defense, advance and retreat; the discipline of lightening the body and esteeming righteousness; the matters of joy and sorrow, rites and music; and the strategies for governing the world and aiding the common people—these are the concerns of the Confucians. External things are discarded, the world is forsaken, scheming is cleansed away, wealth and nobility are forgotten, dissuasion and encouragement are cut off, and one does not grieve over poverty, nor rejoice in prosperity; neither does one sorrow in adversity nor delight in praise—these are the practices of the Daoists.

Confucians perform sacrifices to pray for blessings, while Daoists walk in rectitude to ward off evil. What Confucians cherish is power and profit; what Daoists treasure is desirelessness. Confucians are ever eager for fame and gain, while Daoists embrace the One and cultivate solitude. What Confucians study are the ledgers of mutual scrutiny; what Daoists practice are the teachings of dispelling emotions and admonitions.

As for the Dao: in its becoming, it excels in self-cultivation to accomplish its tasks; in its dwelling, it excels in claiming what others do not contend for; in its governance, it excels in severing misfortune before it arises; in its bestowal, it excels in aiding beings without claiming virtue; in its movement, it excels in observing the people to apply its mind; in its stillness, it excels in abiding cautiously without distress. This is why it is the sovereign of the hundred schools and the ancestor of benevolence and righteousness. The minor differences in principle are thus compared—yet the fundamental rise and fall of its essence remains unchanged.

Someone said: The Confucians are Zhou and Kong; their texts are the Six Classics. They are the means by which the world is governed and rectitude preserved, the standards for establishing oneself and conducting affairs. Their use is far-reaching, their enterprise noble, their affairs grand, their words beautiful—they are the unchanging institutions for states and families. As for those who practice the Dao, they do not cultivate ritual teachings, nor regard great human relations. They consort with foxes and raccoons in the marshes, pair with apes and monkeys in the forests, solitary and drifting, taking trees and stones as neighbors. This is like the folly of the eastward-running rat or the sweetness of the sunflower—deluded and self-indulgent.

Gezhi Zibao replied: I do not esteem the display of flowers or the galloping of ornate words; I have long been sated with correcting the ignorant and rescuing the deluded. Truly, I do not wish to contend with you over the merits of principles or debate gains and losses at the tip of the tongue. Yet seeing a child fall into a well is not the intent of the benevolent; watching a blind man strike a pillar is not the heart of universal love. Thus, I briefly set forth an outline, roughly holding up one corner.

Those who embody the Dao to craft things and treasure virtue to attain longevity follow the teachings of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi. The Yellow Emperor governed the world and brought about great peace, yet he also ascended as an immortal—how can he be deemed inferior to Yao and Shun? Laozi both synthesized and administered teachings, yet he also attained long vision—how can he be considered lesser than Zhou or Kong?
