Thus, Confucius sighed in admiration, yet I have never heard him utter words of disparagement. That later generations of mediocre people, failing to enter its gate, practice Confucianism and Mohism while slandering Daoism—how is this different from descendants cursing their ancestors? They do not recognize their own origins; this is truly excessive.

The hands of a dwarf cannot topple Mount Song or Hua; the legs of a pygmy cannot fathom the vast sea. Yet I often see vulgar, tree-stump-clinging Confucians, narrowly absorbed in their studies, ignorant of broad principles, instructing the stubborn and commanding the foolish, adorning evil words to falsely interrogate Daoists. When they speak of the dregs of糟粕, it is as if they glimpse a swift horse passing a crack; when they wade into the abyss of the spirit, they drown and lose themselves. Like the sparrow flapping its short wings to ride the waves of the sunlit sea, or the fly straining its weak body to cross the peaks of the sun-monkey—tasks beyond their capacity, only hastening their distress. Yet they croak within their narrow confines, their ears unversed in the vast, their eyes unpenetrating of the distant. Aspiring to stand on tiptoe to encompass the Three Luminaries, drumming their bellies to stir lightning—how can their dullness not be obscured?

Only by ascending the distant mystery of the celestial sphere does one know the depths of the well and valley; only by beholding the brilliance of the great light in the heavens does one recognize the paltriness of the wren’s nest. I was not born with knowledge, nor did I believe in it from youth. At first, I too was as ignorant as you. Yet after observing the vastness of its profound secrets, I regret not escaping my ignorance sooner.

The affairs of the Five Classics are illuminated by commentaries and explanations, yet even beginners may not understand them—how much more so the golden tablets and jade slips of the divine immortals’ scriptures, the essential words that are often not written down? Only after ascending the altar and swearing blood oaths are the oral instructions transmitted. If one is not the right person, even if offered land split asunder, halls filled with gold and jade, they will not be casually shown.

Though one may obtain the texts, without a teacher’s guidance, it is like looking up without seeing the head or looking down without knowing the feet—how could you, sir, be thoroughly familiar with this? Those who attain immortality may ascend to the Great Clarity, soar in the Purple Empyrean, reach the Mystic Isles, or dwell in the paulownia of the Celestial Hook’s music, feasting on nine-fold fungi. They carry pine and cassia beyond the inverted shadows, banquet in the Jasper Chambers of the Eternal Sun—why should they consort with foxes and raccoons or pair with apes and monkeys? This is what is called "acting without knowledge." Such is the Dao.

Those who roam freely amidst the rainbow’s arc, soaring through the cinnabar skies, traversing the vastness of the Six Voids—all is shaped by their intent. Though cast aside and left to drift, they feel no sorrow.

Gathered in herds, adorned in embroidered silks—yet when weighed against joy, how does this compare to the solitary flight of the untethered unicorn? The auspicious light parts from its mate, yet its fortune abounds.

**Elixirs of Immortality, Chapter Eleven**

The *Baopuzi* states: The *Divine Farmer’s Four Classics* says, "Superior elixirs bring bodily peace, prolong life, and enable ascent as celestial spirits, roaming above and below, commanding the myriad spirits, growing feathers and wings, summoning traveling kitchens, and arriving in an instant." It further says, "The Five Fungi, cinnabar, jade tablets, malachite, realgar, orpiment, mica, *Taiyi*’s leftover grain—each may be taken alone, all granting flight and longevity." It also says, "Middle-grade elixirs nurture nature; lower-grade ones dispel illness. They render poisons powerless, keep fierce beasts at bay, ward off malign qi, and repel all malevolent spirits." The *Filial Classics’ Divine Concordance* states: "Pepper and ginger repel dampness; calamus sharpens hearing; sesame prolongs life; *weixi* averts weapons—all are the utmost words of the supreme sages, the true records of esoteric arts."

The texts are clear and luminous, yet the world’s people ultimately do not believe—how lamentable!

Among elixirs of immortality, cinnabar ranks highest, followed by gold, then silver, then the various fungi, then the Five Jades, then mica, then luminous pearls, then realgar, then *Taiyi*’s leftover grain, then *shizhonghuangzi*, then *shigui*, then quartz, then *shinao*, then sulfur, then *shidan*, then malachite, then pine and cypress resin, *fuling*, rehmannia, *maimendong*, sesame, *chonglou*, coptis, *shiwei*, paper mulberry fruit, and *xiangchai* (also called *chunlu*). Some call it *xianrenzhang*, others *Queen Mother of the West’s Staff*, or *tianjing*, or *que lao*, or *digua*, or *gouqi*.

*Tianmendong* is also called *dimendong*, *yanmendong*, *dianji*, *yinyangshi*, or *guansong*. That which grows in high places has short roots, a sweet taste, and fragrant qi—this is superior. That which grows by water or in lowlands has fine leaves resembling *yun*, yellowish bark, long roots, a bitter taste, and foul qi—this is inferior. It may still be consumed, but it tends to induce qi descent, though its benefits are slower. After a hundred days of consumption, one becomes robust, surpassing even those who take *zhu* or *huangjing*. Upon entering the mountains, it may be steamed or boiled and eaten to sate hunger, granting strength and enabling grain abstention. It may also be made into powder or its juice extracted for wine—consuming the powder is especially fine. The people of Chu call *tianmendong* *baibu*, yet there exists another *baibu* herb whose roots resemble a hundred clustered together, though their sprouts differ slightly. True *baibu* resembles *baxie* and is used only to treat coughs and repel lice—it is unsuitable for consumption and must not be mistaken. For example, *huangjing* is also called *baiji*, but it is not the *baiji* used to make paste. According to the *Materia Medica*, many herbs share names with others; only the meticulous and learned can distinguish them—one must be thorough.

*Huangjing* is also called *yuzhu*, *jiuqiong*, or *chuizhu*. Consuming its flowers surpasses its fruit; consuming its fruit surpasses its roots. However, flowers are difficult to gather in quantity—ten *hu* of fresh flowers yield only five or six *dou* when dried. A daily dose of three *ge* requires great effort to prepare. Consuming *huangjing* for a mere decade yields substantial benefits. Both it and *zhu* enable grain abstention, but *zhu* nourishes strength, allowing one to bear heavy loads and traverse perilous paths. Yet it does not match *huangjing* in sweetness and ease of consumption, which can sustain the elderly and young during famines. Those unable to distinguish them mistake it for dried rice.

The Five Fungi comprise stone fungi, wood fungi, herb fungi, flesh fungi, and mushroom fungi—each with a hundred or so varieties.

**Stone Fungi** resemble living creatures, growing on the shores of distant seas, famous mountains, and island cliffs where stones accumulate. They take the form of creatures with heads, tails, and four limbs—those resembling living beings are superior. They cling to large stones, favoring lofty peaks and perilous cliffs, often inverted or suspended. Red ones resemble coral, white ones like sliced fat, black ones like lacquer, green ones like kingfisher feathers, and yellow ones like purple gold—all are luminous and translucent like solid ice. On dark nights, their glow is visible from three hundred paces. Large ones weigh over ten *jin*; small ones, three or four *jin*. Without prolonged refinement, utmost essence, and the talismans of *Laozi’s* *Five Treasures of Lingbao*, one cannot encounter them. Upon seeing any fungus, first place the *Kaishan Jihai Fu* upon it to prevent it from vanishing or transforming. Then, on an auspicious day, perform a ritual with wine and dried meat, pray, and gather it by approaching from the sun’s direction, stepping with *Yubu*, and holding one’s breath. If one obtains a stone-simulacrum fungus, pound it thirty-six thousand times and consume a *fangcunbi* daily. Finishing one *jin* grants a thousand years; ten *jin*, ten thousand years. It may also be shared with others.

**Jade-Fat Fungi** grow on jade-rich mountains, often in precarious places where jade sap flows. After ten thousand years, it congeals into fungi, sometimes resembling birds or beasts, though their forms and colors vary. Most resemble mountain *xuan* and water *cang* jade, lustrous as crystal. Ground into powder and mixed with *wuxincao* juice, it soon turns to liquid. Drinking one *sheng* grants a thousand years.

**Seven Luminaries and Nine Radiance Fungi** are also stones, growing on high cliffs near water. They resemble plates or bowls, no larger than a *chi* in diameter, with stems rising three or four *cun*. Those with seven holes are called *Seven Luminaries*; those with nine, *Nine Radiance*. Their lights resemble stars, visible at night from over a hundred paces. Their glow is distinct—scatterable but not combinable. Gather them at the autumn equinox, pound them, and consume a *fangcunbi*. Upon entering the mouth, one’s body harmonizes with the five flavors, sweet and delicious. Consuming one *jin* grants a thousand years, bestowing a luminous body. In dark places, one sees as if by moonlight, enabling night vision.

**Stone Toad Fungi** grow in the stone chambers of Shaoshi Mountain. Within the chambers lies a deep valley, impossible to cross. Tossing a stone into it, the sound echoes for half a day. Ten-odd *zhang* outside the chamber stands a stone pillar topped with a stone canopy, about a *zhang* tall and wide. The *toad* fungus emerges from the stone chamber, resting beneath the canopy, which never overflows.

The stone chamber’s entrance is carved with tadpole script, reading: *"One who consumes a *dou* of Stone Toad Fungi shall live ten thousand years."* Daoist adepts have long pondered this place, yet none can reach it. The only method is to affix a bowl to the tip of sturdy bamboo or wood to catch it—but none have succeeded. Judging by the inscription, there must have been those in ancient times who obtained it.

**Stone Cassia Fungi** grow in the stone caves of renowned mountains, resembling cassia trees but with stone-like fruit. About a *chi* tall and a *chi* in diameter, they glow and taste pungent, with branches. Pounding and consuming one *jin* grants a thousand years.

**Stone Yellow Seeds** are found everywhere, but the Qin River Mountains yield the most. When embedded in large stones, the stone remains perpetually moist, never dry. Striking the stone dozens of times reveals them, nestled within like an egg in its shell. They must be consumed immediately—if left, they harden into stone and become unfit for ingestion. The proper method is to drink them before they solidify; once hardened, they should be ground into powder. Breaking open a stone may yield as much as a *sheng* or as little as a few *ge*, which can be consumed at once. Even if one cannot obtain much, continuous consumption—totaling three *sheng*—grants a thousand years. The only hindrance is their scarcity.

**Stone Brain Fungi** grow within talc, resembling Stone Yellow Seeds but not always present. Breaking open a thousand or so large talc stones may yield one. When first split, it glows with five colors within the stone and moves of its own accord. Consuming one *sheng* grants a thousand years.

**Stone Sulfur Fungi** are found on the Five Peaks, with Mount Ji yielding the most. According to local legend, Xu You consumed them here to attain longevity, freeing him from worldly concerns and allowing him to decline Yao’s abdication. Known as *Sulfur Elixir*, it is the red essence of stone sulfur, seeping from cliffs. The moist form can be rolled into pills, while the hardened form can be ground into powder. There are one hundred and twenty varieties of stone fungi, as recorded in the *Taiyi Jade Formula* and *Changyu Inner Records*, though they cannot all be enumerated here.

**Wood Fungi** include *fuling*, formed when pine resin seeps into the earth and transforms over a thousand years. After ten thousand years, a small plant resembling a lotus flower grows atop it, called *Wood Might-Joy Fungi*. It glows at night, feels slippery to the touch, and does not burn when lit. Carrying it repels weapons: place a chicken wearing it among eleven others in a coop, then shoot twelve arrows from twelve paces away—the other chickens will be struck, but the one with the fungus remains unharmed. Harvest it from the *Gate of Life*, dry it in the shade for a hundred days, then grind and consume a *fangcunbi* three times daily. Consuming one entire fungus grants three thousand years.

**Thousand-Year Cypress** has roots resembling a seated person, seven *cun* long. When carved, it bleeds. Applying its sap to the soles of the feet allows one to walk on water without sinking. Rubbing it inside the nostrils lets one enter water, which parts to allow dwelling at the riverbed. Applying it to the body grants the form of a dragon—desired visions appear upon wiping it away. It also cures illnesses: scrape and consume a *daogui* for internal ailments, or apply it to external swellings and pains, which vanish instantly. For example, if the left foot is afflicted, scrape the left foot of a figurine. Mixed with *jusheng* and shaped into a candle, its light reveals buried treasures—gold and jade—by turning green and bending downward. Digging with a spade uncovers them. Consuming ten *jin* of its powder grants a thousand years.

**Flying Node Fungi** grow within the bark of three-thousand-year-old pine trees, resembling coiled dragons. The largest weigh ten *jin*. Consuming ten *jin* of its powder grants five hundred years.

**Fan Peach Fungi** resemble ascending dragons, with flowers and leaves like red gauze and fruit like kingfishers. No taller than five *chi*, they grow on the shady sides of famous mountains near east-flowing springs. Harvested at the *Start of Summer*, their powder grants five thousand years when consumed entirely.

**Triple-Union Fungi** are red and luminous. When struck, their branches and leaves chime like metal or stone, yet snap back together if broken. **Wood Channel Fungi** grow parasitically on large trees, resembling nine-stemmed lotus clusters, sweet and pungent. **Jian Wood Fungi** bear fruit in *Duguang*, their bark like *yingshe* vines and fruit like *luan* birds. Consuming any of these three grants ascent to heaven in broad daylight.

**Yellow Reed Seeds**, **Prevention Wood Blossoms**, and **Mysterious Liquid Blossoms** grow on Mount Tai in Yaoxiang and Fenghao. Consuming them grants a thousand years.

**Yellow Cedar Root Fungi** grow beneath yellow cedar trees, resembling three-*hu* vessels, connected to the main trunk by fine roots twenty to thirty *zhang* away, like threads. Consuming its powder entirely grants earthly immortality.

There are one hundred and twenty such varieties, each illustrated in accompanying diagrams.

**Herb Fungi** include:
- **Solitary Sway Fungi**, which move without wind. Their stems are finger-thick, red as cinnabar, with leaves like amaranth. The main root resembles a *dou*, with smaller roots like chicken eggs, twelve in total, encircling the main root like the twelve earthly branches. Each is a *zhang* apart, connected by fine roots like white hair. They grow atop high mountains and deep valleys, where no other plants thrive. Consuming the main root’s powder entirely grants a thousand years; consuming a smaller root grants a hundred years and can be shared with others. Carrying the main root grants a dragon’s form—turning it left reveals it.
- **Ox-Horn Fungi**, found on Tiger Longevity Mountain and Wu Slope, resemble scallions but grow singularly like ox horns, three to four *chi* long and blue. Consuming a *fangcunbi* three times daily for a hundred days grants a thousand years.
- **Dragon Immortal Fungi**, resembling ascending dragons with leaf-scales and roots like coiled dragons. Consuming one branch grants a thousand years.
- **Hemp Mother Fungi**, resembling hemp but with red stems and purple flowers.
- **Pearl Fungi**, with yellow flowers, red leaves, and purple fruit like plums, twenty-four hanging in strands like pearls.
- **White Talisman Fungi**, four to five *chi* tall, resembling plum trees, blooming in heavy snow and fruiting in late winter.
- **Vermilion Grass Fungi**, with nine bends, each bearing three leaves—each leaf representing three generations.

The **Five-Virtue Fungi** resemble towering halls, their square stems bearing leaves of five colors, each distinct and unmingled. Above, they form an inverted canopy, and within, sweet dew gathers while purple qi rises several *chi* high.

The **Dragon-Chariot Fungi** invariably sprout in pairs during mid-spring, with three nodes and twelve branches. Their roots resemble seated figures.

These herbal fungi number one hundred and twenty kinds in all. When dried in the shade and consumed, they enable one to endure as long as heaven and earth—some granting a thousand years, others two thousand.

The **Flesh Fungi** include:
- The **Ten-Thousand-Year Toad**, which bears horns on its head and eight red characters inscribed beneath its chin. Heavy in body, it is captured at noon on the fifth day of the fifth month, then dried in the shade for a hundred days. Drawing on the ground with its left foot summons flowing water, while wearing its left hand on the body repels all weapons. Should an enemy shoot at one, the bow, crossbow, and arrows reverse course to strike the assailant.
- The **Thousand-Year Bat**, white as snow, roosts upside down due to the weight of its brain.

When these two creatures are dried in the shade, ground into powder, and consumed, they grant a lifespan of forty thousand years.

The **Thousand-Year Spirit-Turtle** displays all five colors. The male’s forehead bears two bony ridges resembling horns; bathing it in sheep’s blood allows one to scrape off its shell, which is then roasted over fire, ground, and consumed in *fangcunbi* doses thrice daily. Consuming one entire shell grants a thousand years.

Encountering in the mountains small figures riding carriages or horses, seven or eight *cun* tall, signifies **Flesh Fungi**. Capturing and consuming them bestows immortality.

The **Wind-Born Beast** resembles a leopard, blue in color and the size of a wildcat, dwelling in the great forests of the Southern Sea. To capture it, hunters gather cartloads of firewood and set it ablaze. When the wood is consumed, the beast remains unscathed amid the ashes, its fur unburned and impervious to blades or piercing. Striking it feels like beating a leather sack; only after forging it with an iron hammer for thousands of blows does it die. Upon death, it gapes toward the wind, and in moments revives and flees. Plugging its nostrils with *Acorus* from a stone causes it to die. Its brain, mixed with chrysanthemum and consumed—ten *jin* in total—grants five hundred years.

The **Thousand-Year Swallow** nests with its entrance facing north, its plumage mostly white with a forked tail. When dug up, dried in the shade, and ground into powder, consuming one grants five hundred years.

These, too, number one hundred and twenty kinds, all classified as **Flesh Fungi**.

The **Mushroom Fungi** grow in deep mountains, beneath great trees, or beside springs. Their forms vary—some like palaces, others like carriages or horses, dragons or tigers, human figures, or flying birds—shifting in color without constancy. They, too, number one hundred and twenty kinds, each depicted in illustrations. To gather them, one must perform the **Yu Step**, cut them with a bone knife, dry them in the shade, grind them into powder, and consume a *fangcunbi* dose. The superior varieties confer several thousand years, the lesser a thousand.

To seek fungal herbs in famous mountains, one must enter during the third or ninth month—when the mountains open to yield divine medicines. Avoid inauspicious days; instead, choose times of **Heavenly Assistance** or the **Three Auspicious** for added protection. On reaching the mountain, select a **Six Yin Day** during the **Hall of Light** hour. Carry a **Lingbao Talisman**, lead a white dog, hold a white chicken, and bring a *dou* of white salt along with an **Opening Mountain Talisman** and memorial, placing them on a large stone. Hold a bunch of **Wu Tang Grass** (or flowers) to enter the mountain, for the mountain spirits delight in this and will grant success.

When gathering or consuming fungi, choose days when the **Celestial Stems and Earthly Branches** align in mutual generation for optimal harmony.

These fungi abound in famous mountains, yet ordinary Daoists—lacking single-minded devotion, deficient in virtue, and ignorant of mountain-entry techniques—may possess the illustrations but fail to recognize the fungi, thus never obtaining them. Mountains, regardless of size, are guarded by **Guai Spirits**, who do not bestow fungi upon the unworthy. Even if one treads upon them, they remain invisible.

**Mica** has five varieties, though most cannot distinguish them. The method: hold it toward the sun, carefully observe its hues, and discern its nature. Viewing it in the shade reveals no mixed colors.

- When all five colors are present but green dominates, it is **Yunying** (Cloud Essence), best consumed in spring.
- When all five colors are present but red dominates, it is **Yunzhu** (Cloud Pearl), best consumed in summer.
- When all five colors are present but white dominates, it is **Yunye** (Cloud Liquid), best consumed in autumn.
- When all five colors are present but black dominates, it is **Yunmu** (Cloud Mother), best consumed in winter.
- When only green and yellow appear, it is **Yunsha** (Cloud Sand), best consumed in late summer.
- Pure white and lustrous mica is **Linshi** (Phosphorus Stone), suitable for consumption in all seasons.

Methods for refining the **Five Clouds**:
- Infuse with cassia, scallion, or jade to transform into water.
- Steep in dew within an iron vessel, then simmer with **Xuan Water** to produce liquid.
- Seal with saltpeter in a tube and bury to create water.
- Knead into curd.
- Soak in autumn dew for a hundred days, then pound in a leather pouch to make powder.
- Mix with **Wudian Grass** and *Ailanthus* blood for consumption.

Consuming it for a year cures all illnesses; for three years, an old man reverts to youth; for five years, one commands **Guai Spirits**, walks through fire unburned, enters water unsoaked, and treads thorns unharmed, meeting immortals. Other substances char when buried, but the **Five Clouds** remain unburned even in raging fires and never decay, thus granting longevity.

After ten years of consumption, clouds perpetually shroud one’s head. Consuming the mother (Yunmu) begets the child (other varieties)—a natural principle.

When held toward the sun, if a murky, pure black hue appears, it is unfit for consumption, causing illness, strangury, sores, and other afflictions. Even when prepared as water or powder, it must first be soaked for a hundred days in water from thatched roofs, east-flowing streams, or dew, then washed to remove impurities before use.

**Wei Shuqing** of Zhongshan consumed it for years, eventually riding clouds. He sealed the formula in a jade box, and after ascending, his son **Shi** and the Han envoy **Liang Bo** obtained it, followed the method, and likewise attained immortality.

**Realgar** must come from Wudu Mountain, pure and unadulterated, its red hue like a rooster’s comb, radiant and gleaming. Only this is suitable for immortality elixirs. Pure yellow realgar lacking red luster is unfit for immortality but may be used in medicinal compounds.

Methods of consumption:
- Steaming or boiling.
- Mixing with wine.
- Dissolving with saltpeter into water, then solidifying.
- Wrapping in **Xuan Intestines**, steaming beneath red earth.
- Blending with pine resin.
- Refining with three substances to draw it out like cloth, white as ice.

Consumption grants longevity, cures all diseases, expels the **Three Corpses**, erases scars, restores black hair, and regrows fallen teeth. After a thousand days, **Jade Maidens** attend, ready to serve and summon traveling kitchens. The Jade Maidens always bear a yellow jade mark on their noses, the size of millet—a sign of authenticity.

Those lacking this mark are but demons testing men. Jade, too, is an immortality elixir, though difficult to obtain. The *Scripture of Jade* states: "He who consumes gold attains longevity like gold; he who consumes jade attains longevity like jade." It further says: "He who ingests the **Mysterious Perfection** knows no end to his life." The **Mysterious Perfection** is another name for jade. Its consumption enables the body to become light and ascend—yet this is not merely earthbound immortality. However, the Way is slow to manifest; only after consuming one or two hundred *jin* does one begin to perceive its effects.

Jade may be dissolved into water using **Black Rice Wine** or **Sanguisorba Wine**, or melted into a paste with scallion broth. It may also be ingested as pills or burned into powder. After a year of consumption, one becomes impervious to water, fire, blades, and all poisons. Yet one must not use jade that has already been fashioned into vessels, for it harms without benefit—only unworked jade (*pu* jade) is suitable.

The finest jade comes from Khotan, particularly the white variety. Next in quality is the jade from the borders of Xuyanting in Nanyang, as well as that from the waters of Lurong in Rinan. **Master Red Pine** soaked jade in **Mysterious Worm** blood to transform it into liquid, which he then consumed—thus enabling him to ride the smoke and ascend or descend at will.

While jade shavings, whether taken dry or dissolved in water, grant immortality, they are inferior to gold in that they frequently induce **cold-food powder**-like symptoms, such as outbreaks of sores. To prevent this, those who consume jade shavings should take, every ten days, a *daorui* each of realgar and cinnabar. Afterward, they should bathe, wash their hair, and walk against the wind in cold water to avert the outbreaks.

Dong Junyi once gave **Jade Elixir** to a blind man, whose sight was restored within ten days. There was also Wu Yanzhi, who was determined to consume jade. Though he obtained the *Scripture of Jade* and its methods, he did not fully grasp its regulations and prohibitions. He gathered a vast quantity of ritual tablets, rings, discs, and even the fittings of swords, intending to refine and ingest them. After I explained that these were unsuitable, he sighed and said, "This matter cannot be approached without precision—not only is it futile, but it may even invite disaster."

Silver, while inferior to gold and jade, may still confer earthbound immortality.

The method of ingestion involves dissolving it in wheat broth, or consuming it with wine infused with *zhu* grass, or refining it with dragon fat. However, after three doses, it becomes as large as a pellet—something beyond the means of impoverished Daoist practitioners. Pearls measuring over an inch in diameter may also be consumed, granting longevity. Soaked in yogurt or broth, they dissolve like liquid mercury; alternatively, they may be dissolved in *fushi* water or *ke* water, then wrapped in *tongshe* yellow and combined to form strands three or four feet long. Ingested as pills, they enable fasting and confer immortality.

Pure lacquer that does not adhere may be consumed to commune with spirits and attain longevity. The method of ingestion involves either mixing it with ten large crabs (also called "the gutless lord") or combining it with mica-infused water or jade-infused water. After consumption, the nine worms are expelled, and foul blood departs through the nose. Within a year, the Six Jia spirits will provide divine sustenance. Cinnamon may be steamed with scallion juice to produce a liquid, or consumed with bamboo sap, or mixed with *xianzhi* stone brain (also called *xuan*). After seven years of ingestion, one may walk upon water and achieve immortality.

*Jusheng* (also called sesame) prevents aging, resists wind and dampness, and replenishes vitality in the elderly. Peach resin, soaked in mulberry ash water and consumed, cures all illnesses; prolonged ingestion lightens the body and grants luminosity, so that in dark nights, one shines like the moon. Excessive consumption enables fasting. The red fruit of the *ning* (or *chu*) tree, ingested for a year, restores youth to the elderly and grants clear vision, revealing spirits. The Daoist Liang Xu, at seventy, consumed it and grew younger, reaching 140 years of age. He could write at night and outrun horses, later ascending to Green Dragon Mountain. *Huaizi* seeds should be sealed in a new jar with mud for over twenty days until their outer skin rots, then washed to resemble large soybeans. Daily consumption nourishes the brain, prevents graying hair, and grants longevity. Among the three hundred-odd herbs in the *Xuan Zhong Man Fang*, such as *Chu feilian*, *zexie*, *dihuang*, and *huanglian*, all extend lifespan and may be consumed individually.

The *Lingfei Powder*, *Weiyang Pill*, *Zhiming Pill*, and *Yangxue Pill* all preserve youth and reverse aging. In the mountains of Lixian County, Nanyang, there is a Sweet Valley Brook, whose sweetness arises from the surrounding sweet chrysanthemums. Their blossoms fall into the water over generations, altering its taste. The residents near this valley do not dig wells but drink solely from the Sweet Valley Brook. None fail to live long lives—some reach 140 or 150 years, while the youngest live to eighty or ninety, with no premature deaths. This is due to the power of the chrysanthemums.

Thus, when the Minister of Ceremonies Wang Chang, the Grand Commandant Liu Kuan, and the Grand Tutor Yuan Wei served as governors of Nanyang, they each ordered Lixian County to deliver forty *hu* of Sweet Valley water monthly for their consumption. Though these officials suffered from wind impediment and dim vision, their conditions improved. However, they did not reap the full benefits as did the valley’s residents, who had consumed the water since birth.

Chrysanthemums resemble *yiyi* flowers but are distinguished by taste—chrysanthemums are sweet, while *yiyi* are bitter, as the saying goes, "bitter as *yiyi*." True chrysanthemums exist everywhere but are rare. They mostly grow near water, with the greatest abundance in Goushi and Lixian. The *Xian Fang* refers to the "Essence of the Sun" and "Ever-Full," both names for a single chrysanthemum with differing roots, stems, flowers, and fruits. The descriptions are elegant, yet recent attempts at consumption have yielded no effect, precisely because true chrysanthemums were not obtained. If even Sweet Valley water, imbued with chrysanthemum essence, grants longevity to its residents, how much more so with superior herbs?

My late grandfather, the Vice Minister of Ceremonies, once served as magistrate of Linhang County. He recounted that the Liao family in the county had lived long lives for generations—some reaching a hundred, others eighty or ninety. After they relocated, their descendants suffered early deaths. When others occupied their former residence, longevity returned over generations. This suggested the house itself was the cause, though its reason was unknown. Suspecting the well water was unusually red, they excavated around it and unearthed several dozen *hu* of cinnabar buried by ancients a few feet away. The cinnabar’s essence had gradually seeped into the well, so drinking its water conferred longevity. How much more so if one refined and ingested cinnabar directly?

I have also heard of Zhao Qu from Shangdang, who suffered from leprosy for years. Despite numerous treatments, his condition worsened, and death seemed imminent. His family, despairing, abandoned him in a mountain cave. Qu, lamenting his misfortune, wept day and night. After a month, an immortal passed by the cave, took pity on him, and inquired about his plight. Qu, recognizing the immortal’s extraordinary nature, kowtowed and pleaded for mercy. The immortal gave him a pouch of medicine with instructions for consumption. After a hundred days, Qu’s sores healed, his complexion became radiant, and his skin smooth as jade. The immortal revisited him, and Qu expressed gratitude for the gift of life, begging for the formula. The immortal replied, "This is merely pine resin. This mountain abounds with it. Refine and consume it to attain immortality." Qu returned home, where his family, initially mistaking him for a ghost, were astonished. He then consumed pine resin continuously, his body growing lighter, his strength increasing a hundredfold. He could scale perilous heights and traverse rugged terrain all day without fatigue. At 170, his teeth remained intact, his hair unwhite. One night, he suddenly saw a light in his room, bright as a mirror. When he asked his attendants, they saw nothing. Over time, the light expanded until the entire room shone as if by daylight. Another night, he saw two tiny maidens, two or three inches tall, with fully formed faces and bodies, playing around his nose and mouth. After a year, the maidens grew larger and stood beside him. He often heard the sounds of zithers and lutes, laughing joyfully to himself. After over three hundred years among mortals, his appearance remained that of a youth. He then entered Baodu Mountain—undoubtedly an earthbound immortal.

At the time, news of Qu’s consumption of pine resin spread, and many rushed to gather it. The industrious transported it by cart and donkey, filling entire rooms. Yet most who consumed it stopped after a month, perceiving no significant benefit. Such perseverance is rare.

During the reign of Emperor Cheng of Han, hunters in the Zhongnan Mountains encountered a person without clothing, their body covered in black hair. The hunters pursued them, but the figure leapt over gullies and valleys as if flying, impossible to overtake. The hunters then secretly observed their location, surrounded them, and captured them—only to discover it was a woman. When questioned, she said, "I was originally a palace woman of Qin."

Hearing that rebels from east of the Pass had arrived, the King of Qin went out to surrender. His palaces were set ablaze, and in terror, she fled into the mountains. Starving and with nothing to eat, she nearly died of hunger when an old man taught her to consume pine needles and pine nuts. At first, they tasted bitter, but gradually she grew accustomed to them, and thus she neither hungered nor thirsted, felt no cold in winter nor heat in summer. Calculating the years, this woman must have been a palace woman of Qin King Ziying, living until the reign of Emperor Cheng—over three hundred years. When brought back and fed grains, she first retched at the smell, vomiting for days before recovering. After about two years, her body hair fell away, and she grew old and died. Had she not been captured by men, she would surely have become an immortal.

The Wen family of Nanyang recounted that during the great chaos at the end of the Han, their ancestor fled into the mountains, starving and near death. Someone taught him to eat *zhu* (atractylodes), and thereafter he never knew hunger. After several decades, he returned to his hometown, his complexion youthful and his strength greater than before. He claimed that in the mountains, his body felt light, as if he could leap and bound over heights and perils, walking on ice and snow without feeling cold. He often saw a high cliff where several people sat—some playing board games, others reading. Leaning forward to observe, the Wen ancestor overheard them ask, "Has this one reached the upper ranks yet?" One replied, "Not yet."

*Zhu* is also called *shanji* (mountain thistle) or *shanjing* (mountain essence). The *Divine Elixir Classic* states: "To attain longevity, one must constantly consume mountain essence." Formerly, the Eight Immortals each consumed a single substance to achieve earthly immortality, living for several hundred years before combining the divine elixir and golden liquor to ascend to the Great Clarity. If one were to combine and consume all eight substances, their power would not be realized, for the properties of these medicines counteract and restrain one another.

Han Zhong consumed calamus for thirteen years, his body growing hair, and he could recite ten thousand words after reading them once. He never felt cold in winter. The calamus must grow on stone, be at least an inch long with nine or more nodes, and have purple flowers—this is especially effective. Zhao Tuo’s son consumed cinnamon for twenty years, and hair grew on the soles of his feet; he could walk five hundred *li* in a day and lift a thousand *jin*. Yi Menzi consumed schisandra for sixteen years, his complexion like jade, and he could enter water without getting wet or fire without being scorched. Chu Wenzi consumed rehmannia for eight years and could see at night as if by light, his hands strong enough to draw a crossbow. Lin Ziming consumed *zhu* for eleven years, and his ears grew five inches long; his body became light as flight, able to leap over ravines of twenty *zhang* or more. Du Ziwei consumed asparagus for eighty years, kept eighty concubines, and fathered one hundred thirty children; he could walk three hundred *li* in a day. Ren Ziji consumed poria for eighteen years, and immortals and jade maidens came to follow him. He could conceal and reveal himself at will, no longer ate grains, and his scars vanished, his face and body glowing like jade. Lingyang Zizhong consumed *yuanzhi* (polygala) for twenty years and fathered thirty-seven children; he never forgot what he read, whether sitting or standing.

The *Immortals’ Classic* states: "Though one may consume the leaves of herbs and trees and live for several hundred years, if they neglect the divine elixir, they will ultimately fail to become immortals." From this, it is clear that herbs and plants merely extend life—they are not medicines of eternal life. Until one can refine the elixir, one may consume them to sustain oneself.

Someone asked whether there is a proper order for consuming medicinal substances. Master Baopu replied: "According to the *Regimen for Consuming Medicines* by Master Zhonghuang: Take medicines for treating illness before meals, and those for nourishing life after meals. As for the golden elixir and great medicine, there is no need to discuss propriety."

A single word on the palace:
*Gengzi, Gengwu, Xinhai, Xinwei, Bingchen, Bingxu, Dinghai, Dingsi, Wuyin, Wushen, Jimao, Jiyou.*

Three-word *zheng*:
*Jiachen, Jiaxu; Yihai, Yisi; Bingyin, Bingshen; Dingyou, Dingmao; Wuwu, Wuzi; Jiwei, Jichou.*

Five-word *yu*:
*Jiayin, Jiashen; Yimao, Yiyou; Bingzi, Bingwu; Dingwei, Dingchou; Renchen, Renxu; Guisi, Guihai.*

Seven-word *shang*:
*Jiazi, Jiawu; Yichou, Yiwei; Gengchen, Gengxu; Xinhai, Xinsi; Renshen, Renyin; Guimao, Guiyou.*

Nine-word *jue*:
*Wuchen, Wuxu; Jihai, Jisi; Gengyin, Gengshen; Xinmao, Xinyou; Renzu, Renzi; Guichou, Guiwei.*

Yu’s Stepping Method:
Step forward, left foot crosses over;
Right foot passes left, left foot joins right.
Next, step forward, right foot crosses over;
Right foot joins left, then step forward, right foot.
Right foot passes left, left foot joins right;
Right foot joins left.
Thus, three steps complete—
Twenty-one *chi* and nine footprints in all.

Minor divine formulas are to be consumed after meals.
I consulted Master Zheng on this matter.
Master Zheng said: "This is easily understood.
When using medicine to attack illness, it is proper to take it before eating,
for the interior is empty, allowing the medicine’s power and momentum to act with ease.
If taken after eating, the medicine will merely attack the grain and its force will be exhausted.
If one takes medicine before eating to nourish life,
the medicine’s power will not yet have acted when it is driven downward by the grain,
unable to remain and thus of no benefit."

Someone asked: "When people take medicine to nourish life,
are there indeed proper correspondences?"
Master Baopu replied: "According to the *Jade Screen Record* and the *Kaiming Sutra*,
one may know the location of a person’s life in a given year through the five tones and six correspondences.
*Zi* and *wu* belong to *geng*;
*mao* and *you* belong to *ji*;
*yin* and *shen* belong to *wu*;
*chou* and *wei* belong to *xin*;
*chen* and *xu* belong to *bing*;
*si* and *hai* belong to *ding*.
One-word matches belong to *gong* and earth;
three-word matches belong to *zheng* and fire;
five-word matches belong to *yu* and water."

Seven-word matches belong to *shang* and metal;
nine-word matches belong to *jiao* and wood.
If one’s life corresponds to earth, one should not take green-colored medicines;
if to metal, one should not take red-colored medicines;
if to wood, one should not take white-colored medicines;
if to water, one should not take yellow-colored medicines;
if to fire, one should not take black-colored medicines—
this follows the principle of the Five Phases, wherein wood overcomes earth, earth overcomes water, water overcomes fire, fire overcomes metal, and metal overcomes wood.

Refine three *jin* of cinnabar and six *jin* of white honey, blend them, dry in the sun, and simmer until pliable enough to form pills. Take ten pills the size of sesame seeds at dawn. Within a year, white hair will turn black again, fallen teeth will regrow, and the body will become lustrous. With prolonged use, an old man will revert to youth, and with constant use, one will attain immortality without dying.

For the minor elixir of yellow gold: smelt the gold in clear wine, removing and reinserting it two hundred times until it boils. Knead it between the fingers until it becomes like clay; if it does not boil or fails to emerge between the fingers as clay, return it to the wine and smelt it repeatedly without count. When complete, take a pill the size of a bullet. Alternatively, form one pill into smaller ones and take them for thirty days—then cold and heat will no longer afflict you, and divine beings and jade maidens will descend. Silver may also be ingested using the same method as gold. Taking these two substances allows one to dwell in famous mountains or stone chambers; after a year, one will become weightless and ascend. Those who take them in the mortal world are called earthbound immortals—do not recklessly transmit this.

For the dual elixirs of smelted yellow gold: the *Pig-Borne Leather* method. Take three *jin* of lard, one *dou* of pure bitter wine, and five *liang* of yellow gold. Place the gold in a vessel and simmer it over an earthen stove, dipping the gold into the lard one hundred times in and out. Bitter wine may also be consumed: one *jin* grants a lifespan encompassing heaven and earth; half a *jin* grants two thousand years; five *liang* grant one thousand two hundred years. Take as much as desired, but prepare it on a day when the celestial stems and earthly branches are in harmony. The divine efficacy is assured—do not transmit this to others, for if you do, the medicine will fail to form and lose its potency.

To expel the Three Worms, one should take cinnabar. The method for ingesting cinnabar: grind one *jin* of cinnabar, mix it with three *sheng* of pure bitter wine and two *sheng* of pure lacquer (one version adds two *sheng* of honey). Combine the three substances thoroughly, then simmer over a fire until pliable enough to form pills. Take three pills the size of sesame seeds, twice daily. After forty days, the hundred diseases of the abdomen will be cured, and the Three Worms expelled. After a hundred days, the muscles and bones will become firm and strong. After a thousand days, the Director of Destinies will erase your name from the register of the dead, and you will endure as long as heaven and earth, gaze as far as the sun and moon. Your form will transform, your countenance will change, and you will become unfathomable. At midday, you will cast no shadow, for you will possess a radiance of your own.

*Baopuzi, Inner Chapters, Scroll Two*
*(Subsection: Masters, Daoist Works, Baopuzi Inner and Outer Chapters)*

*The Imperial Four Treasuries Edition*
*Baopuzi, Inner Chapters, Scroll Three*
*By Ge Hong of the Jin Dynasty*

**Disputation and Inquiry, Chapter Twelve**

Someone asked: If immortality must be attainable, then the sages would have already cultivated it. Yet neither the Duke of Zhou nor Confucius pursued it—this proves that no such Way exists.

Baopuzi replied: The sage need not be an immortal, nor must the immortal be a sage. The sage receives his mandate when the Dao of long life is not at hand; his sole desire is to eliminate cruelty, expel bandits, level dangers, pacify violence, establish rites, compose music, enact laws, transmit teachings, reform perverse customs, correct degenerate mores, rescue imperiled rulers, and sustain failing states. He carves the *Odes* and *Documents*, compiles the *River Chart* and *Luo Writing*, authors classics and proclamations, harmonizes *Ya* and *Song*, instructs the young, and responds to summons from the feudal lords. Like smoke unfurling without pause, his mat never warms—his affairs press upon him, his tasks exhaust the years without end. How then could he close his ears, cover his eyes, turn his gaze inward, reverse his hearing, practice breath regulation, engage in Daoist gymnastics, maintain prolonged purity, enter water to pan for gold, ascend mountains to gather herbs, count his breaths, contemplate the spirits, abstain from grains, or purify his intestines?

As for immortals, they require only unwavering resolve, perfect faith, and unrelenting diligence. One who can embrace tranquility and stillness may attain it—no extraordinary talent is needed. Those lofty transcendents who descend into the world become but heavy burdens to Daoist practitioners.

To obtain and ingest the Great Elixir, to master the essentials of guarding the One and nurturing the spirit, is to achieve long life and enduring vision. How could this compare to the boundless endeavors of the sages as commonly understood?

Moreover, the so-called "sages" of the world are sages of governing the age, not sages of attaining the Dao. The sages of attaining the Dao are Huang-Lao; the sages of governing the age are the Duke of Zhou and Confucius. The Yellow Emperor first governed the world, then ascended as an immortal—this was merely a rare case of one possessing both talents.

Of the ancient emperors and kings who carved inscriptions on Mount Tai, seventy-two can be verified through reading, while the rest have eroded beyond count. Yet only the Yellow Emperor’s ascent to immortality is recorded—its authenticity is thus clearly established.

The world deems those who excel in what others cannot as "sages." Thus, one unmatched in *weiqi* is called a "chess sage"—hence Yan Ziqing and Ma Suiming are still known today as chess sages. One peerless in clerical script is called a "calligraphy sage"—hence Wei Xie and Zhang Mo are still known today as calligraphy sages. One exceptionally skilled in carving is called a "wood sage"—hence Zhang Wei and Ma Zhong are still known today as wood sages.

Therefore, Mencius called Boyi the sage of purity and Liuxia Hui the sage of harmony.

Yi Yin is the sage of governance. Let me expound and argue that sagacity is not confined to a single endeavor. Ban Di was the sage of machinery, Fu Bian and Bian Que the sages of healing illness, Zi Wei and Gan Jun the sages of astrological divination, Shi Su and Xin Liao the sages of milfoil and turtle-shell divination, Xia Yu and Du Hui the sages of physical strength, Jing Ke and Nie Zheng the sages of courage, Fei Lian and Kua Fu the sages of swiftness, Zi Ye and Yanzhou the sages of musical discernment, Sun Wu, Wu Qi, Han Xin, and Bai Qi the sages of military strategy. Sagacity is the supreme designation for human affairs—it is not limited to literature alone.

Zhuang Zhou said: "Bandits possess five aspects of the sage’s Way. To conjecture and know what others conceal is *ming*; to enter first without hesitation is *courage*; to withdraw last without fear is *righteousness*; to discern what is fitting is *wisdom*; to distribute spoils equally is *benevolence*. Without these, none could become the greatest bandit under heaven."

Some may say: "The sage’s Way cannot be divided into branches or scattered into leaves; it must be grasped in its entirety to be called sagacity." I reply: Among Confucius’ disciples, seventy-two attained mastery, each embodying a single facet of the sage—thus sagacity admits of division. It is also said: "Yan Yuan possessed the whole but in miniature"—thus sagacity admits of degrees. The *Yi* states: "There are four aspects of the sage’s Way: in speech, one esteems its eloquence; in action, one esteems its adaptability; in crafting implements, one esteems their symbolic resonance; in divination, one esteems its omens." This is clear proof that the sage’s Way may be divided. Why, then, should those who excel in the Dao and attain immortality alone be denied the title of "sage of the Dao"? If there were no sages of the Dao, could Zhou and Kong be called sages of governance? Since sagacity is not singular, why demand that one person embody all its forms?

The immortal scriptures hold that those who attain transcendence are all born under the influence of the qi of immortality, naturally endowed with it. Even in the womb, they already harbor a predisposition toward the Dao. Once they gain consciousness, their hearts incline toward its practice, and they must encounter an enlightened master to receive its methods—otherwise, they will neither believe nor seek, and even if they seek, they will not obtain.

The *Jade Key Record* states that the origins of fate and the variations in human fortune—whether long or short—are determined at the moment of conception, when one receives the essence of the celestial lodges. Those aligned with the lodge of sagacity become sages; with the lodge of worthiness, worthies; with the lodge of literature, scholars; with the lodge of martiality, warriors; with the lodge of nobility, the noble; with the lodge of wealth, the wealthy; with the lodge of lowliness, the base; with the lodge of poverty, the poor; with the lodge of longevity, the long-lived; with the lodge of immortality, immortals. There are also lodges of divine immortals and sages, lodges of world-governing sages, lodges combining both, lodges of nobility without wealth, of wealth without nobility, of combined wealth and nobility, of wealth followed by poverty, of nobility followed by lowliness, of combined poverty and lowliness, of wealth and nobility unfulfilled, of loyalty and filial piety, of malevolence and wickedness—such variations cannot be exhaustively listed. The broad distinctions are as follows: a person’s life is fundamentally predestined, as Zhang Chezi’s teachings affirm.

If one is not fated to receive the mandate of immortality, one will not harbor a heart inclined toward transcendence. No one seeks what their heart does not desire, nor obtains what they do not seek. From antiquity to the present, there have been those of towering talent and profound understanding who disbelieved in immortals, and those of ordinary ability who studied and attained transcendence. Some, though discerning in many matters, may be blind to immortality; others, though less perceptive, may grasp its principles—is this not the work of heavenly mandate?

Daoist lineages treasure and guard their transcendent arts, selecting disciples with utmost care and testing them over long years before imparting their essential secrets. How much less would they reveal these to the world at large, who neither believe nor seek? Why compel them to speak? Unable to transform others into believers, they would only invite ridicule and slander. Thus, those who attain the Dao differ from worldly people in their paths, their dwellings, their words, and their conduct. They keep a thousand *li* distant, fearing even that is insufficient to escape the assaults of gossip; they sever all traces, fearing even that is insufficient to avoid the shame of slander. Neither rank nor wealth can sway them—why would they ever proclaim to vulgar scholars, "I possess the arts of immortality"? This is why Zhou and Kong had no means to know the Way of transcendence.

Moreover, Zhou and Kong were merely men of great talent and profound learning. Even in minor skills, they were often unversed. Had they been made to juggle balls, wield swords, leap over blades, toss daggers, walk tightropes, scale poles, pluck plates, climb tables, or cling to sheer cliffs a myriad *ren* high; to swim the unfathomable depths of Lüliang; to lift a thousand *jun* with their hands or tread upon whirlwinds; to grapple with caged tigers and leopards or catch flying arrows—even ordinary people could perform such feats, yet Zhou and Kong could not. How much less could they surpass these?

What others contemplate—the orientation of fleas and lice, the colors behind walls, the grasses beneath trees, the texts in chests, the treasures buried in the earth, the birds and beasts in dense forests and deep marshes, the fish and turtles in vast abysses and torrential pools—had Zhou and Kong been made to discern their hues, distinguish their names, enumerate their quantities, and verify their presence or absence, they might not have known them all. How much less could they comprehend what lies beyond?

Sages grow hungry without food, thirsty without drink; they feel heat when scorched, cold when chilled, pain when struck, and injury when cut. With time, they age; with harm, they sicken; with the severing of *qi*, they die. In these ways, they differ little from ordinary people—their distinctions are few. What sets them apart lies only in their superior talent, far-reaching thought, eloquence, refined writing, complete virtue, pure conduct, rigorous instruction, and vast learning. How could they excel in every endeavor? Having already composed the *Dian* and *Mo* to pacify the realm and govern the people, why demand that they also master the Dao of transcendence and attain deathless longevity? To impose such expectations on sages is excessive.

I have heard that profound words offend the ears of the vulgar, and truthful speech contradicts the crowd. Those who read this text will surely accuse me of slandering the sages. How could I? I merely exhaust the principles of things. When principles are exhausted and affairs fully explored, it may seem like disparagement of Zhou and Kong. Yet the world claims that sages descend from heaven as divine beings, omniscient and omnipotent—this is an extreme exaggeration.

Those who revere the sages’ names dare not examine their deeds, claiming that what sages cannot do, no one else can, and what sages do not know, no one else knows. Is this not laughable? Let us now compare their actual deeds to awaken understanding.

The birds of Mount Wan sang their songs of life and death, yet Confucius did not know them—could one then say that Yan Hui could only partially comprehend them? When Confucius heard the weeping of a woman on Mount Tai and asked her cause, he learned that a tiger had devoured three of her kin, yet he did not understand why she had not moved away. Only upon questioning did he realize. When he saw hunters catching fledglings, he did not grasp their intent until asked. When he wished to bury his mother but did not know his father’s grave, he required guidance. Once the grave was set, its collapse again escaped his notice, and only after his disciples informed him did he weep in sorrow. When he suspected Yan Yuan of stealing food, he feigned a desire to sacrifice to his ancestors to test him, casting dust to expose the deception. When a stable burned, he did not know whether men or horses were harmed. Later, hearing that Yan Yuan had died, he declared it so. Though he wandered through seventy states, he could not foresee that none would employ him. Restless and hurried, his mat never warmed, yet he did not know the people of Kuang would surround him until he took that road. He asked Laozi about ancient rites but found some beyond his understanding. He inquired of Tanzi about the offices of birds but could not recognize them all. Traveling without knowing the ford, he sent others to ask, unaware that those he questioned would mock him and refuse to show the way—had he known, would he not have refrained from asking? When he alighted from his carriage to chase a man singing of the phoenix, he did not realize the man would not stay. Meeting Nanzi, he did not foresee it would bring no benefit. Such instances are too numerous to list, yet why should it be strange that he did not know the methods of transcendence?

Common scholars claim that what sages cannot do, no one else can. Yet Dan of the Marsh lived in water, Liang Mu transformed through fire, Zibo Zhan endured extreme cold, Zuo Ci escaped death through martial arts, Gan Shi fasted for years, Fan Ye remained unharmed when struck, the Turtle Decree revived the dead, Shao Qian grasped a hundred demons, Chang Fang shrank the earth’s veins, Zhong Fu took the form of a morning duck, and Zhang Kai summoned clouds and mist with his breath. Never have we heard that Zhou or Kong could perform such feats.

Some say, "Zhou and Kong could have done these things but chose not to." I reply: If one does not seek evidence in clear records but instead points to empty words, I could just as well claim that Zhou and Kong could spread their wings and soar to the eight directions, summon clouds and rain, or move mountains and shift wells—but they chose not to. Without written records as proof, what limits are there?

If this is the case, I could also say that Zhou and Kong both ascended as immortals, but they concealed this method lest the world know that deathlessness is attainable. If all abandoned their duties to pursue the Dao—forsaking official posts, risking peril, and venturing into the depths—then families would have no descendants, states no ministers or officials, loyalty and filial piety would vanish, and the great order would collapse. Thus, Zhou and Kong practiced in secret, revealing only the appearance of death while inwardly mastering the arts of transcendence. If so, how could you refute me? It may well be true.

The *Lingbao Sutra* contains the *Zhengji*, *Pingheng*, and *Feixian Shouzhi*—three scrolls of immortal arts. When the King of Wu quarried stone to build his palace, he found within the rock a book of purple script on golden tablets. Unable to read it, he sent an envoy to ask Confucius, who deceived him, saying, "The King of Wu, in leisure, received a book delivered by a red sparrow to his hall. Unsure of its meaning, he seeks your counsel." Upon seeing it, Confucius declared, "This is the *Lingbao* method, the Way of long life. Yu of Xia practiced it, dwelling in the watery realm, his years equaling heaven and earth, his court held in the Purple Palace. When Yu was about to transcend, he sealed it in a stone casket on a sacred mountain, and now the red sparrow has brought it—surely it is a gift from heaven." By this reasoning, Yu of Xia did not die, and Confucius knew of it. How can we be certain he did not secretly cultivate this Way?

Even if sages did not practice such arts, one cannot say they lacked their effects. People’s likes and dislikes vary as widely as their faces—is this not true? What aligns with their inclinations, though small, they will pursue; what does not resonate with their spirit, though great, they will not learn. Some love bitterness and despise sweetness, while others crave profit and abandon righteousness—such cases are countless.

The greatest treasure of sages is rank; what draws people together is wealth. Riches and nobility are what all desire, yet in ancient times, some declined the abdication of imperial power, spurned the riches of the four seas, scorned the offices of the Three Dukes and Nine Ministers, and rejected jade and silk, choosing instead the lofty purity of mountains and forests, content with the humble life of fishing. Such examples are too many to count.

It is said that men and women, food and drink, are humanity’s greatest desires. Thus, some cannot be dissuaded from their love of beauty, nor can they forget their cravings for fine flavors. Yet in the past, there were those who abandoned grain, forsook delicacies, took no wives or concubines, wandered alone in enlightenment, spoke words of insight, delighted in their own reflection, rinsed their mouths with flowing water, and savored plain tastes—such cases are too numerous to recall.

Human nature cherishes rosy cheeks and graceful forms, yet the Yellow Emperor delighted in the homely Momu, and the Marquis of Chen loved the repulsive Dunqia. No nose dislikes fragrance, so why are liuhuang, yujin, orchids, musk, suhe, xuandan, sujiao, jiangli, jieche, spring orchids, and autumn orchids prized like jade and jasper? Yet the women of the sea follow men who reek of rancid odors, never tiring of them. No mouth dislikes sweetness, yet King Wen of Zhou preferred the unpalatable *kuai* to the rich flavors of the grand sacrifice. No ear dislikes music, yet Emperor Ming of Wei favored the sound of hammer and chisel over the harmony of strings and bamboo. Each person has their own inclinations—how can one demand that this be the same as that?

Zhou and Kong themselves did not believe in the Dao of transcendence. The sun and moon do not illuminate all things; sages do not know all things. To claim that what sages do not do, the world cannot do, is like blaming the three luminaries for not illuminating the inside of an overturned basin.

**Chapter Thirteen: On the Utmost**

Someone asked: "Were the immortals of old all those who attained transcendence through study, or were they specially endowed with exceptional qi?"

The Master Who Embraces Simplicity replied: "What kind of question is this? None of them did not carry their satchels to follow teachers, accumulating merit through diligent effort. They endured frost and braved peril, combed by wind and washed by rain, personally sweeping and toiling. Only after proving their sincerity and conduct were they tested with hardship and adversity. Only those whose nature was steadfast and true, whose hearts held no resentment or doubt, were permitted to ascend the hall and enter the inner chamber. Some grew weary and halted midway; some became resentful and retreated; some were lured by glory and profit, returning to the ways of the common world; some were swayed by heterodox teachings, losing their resolve for detachment. Some expected success by evening after practicing in the morning; some sat in cultivation yet stood hoping for results. As for those who beheld wealth and beauty without their hearts wavering, or heard worldly words without their resolve faltering—among ten thousand, one such person is rare."

Thus, those who strive are as numerous as ox hairs, while those who attain are as rare as unicorn horns. The archer who draws the bow exerts effort in loosing the arrow; the traveler who crosses the great river preserves his life only upon reaching the far shore. A well that does not reach the spring is as good as undug; a single step not taken is as good as never having set forth. The toils of the long road cannot be traversed in a single day’s watch, nor can the heights of the clouds be scaled by one basket of earth. Yet those who climb perilous peaks falter when near the summit, their strength failing; those who walk the Way fall ill when on the verge of completion, their resolve unfulfilled. A thousand granaries of grain are not the yield of one planting; a tree of a thousand feet does not grow in ten days. The unfathomable abyss begins with a trickle; the wealth of Tao Zhu was amassed over hundreds and thousands. Thus, when others retreat, one advances—this is how Yin Xi attained the utmost Dao. Reverent to the end as at the beginning—this is how Xian Men rode the clouds and dragons. If my resolve is truly steadfast, what matter who they are?

The Master Embracing Simplicity said: The common folk, unable to sustain life, instead devote themselves to destroying it. Finite things cannot supply infinite depletion; the flow of rivers cannot fill a bottomless vessel. Most people, earning little yet spending much, still cannot make ends meet—how much less so for those with no income at all yet endless expenditures? None, young or old, are free from illness; it is only a matter of severity. Each receives qi in varying measure: those with much exhaust it slowly, while those with little deplete it swiftly. Those who know the Way replenish and restore themselves, first returning to their original state before seeking further benefits. If one were to consume elixirs for ten days, flesh would soar and bones ascend; if one practiced Daoist arts with each new moon, wings would sprout and feathers align—then the world would have no one who disbelieved in the Way.

The trouble lies in this: the meager benefits of a single ladle are not yet firm, yet the costs of bushels and tons are already guarded against; the roots’ hold is not yet deep, yet the poison of frost and snow assails them. Not recognizing that the fault lies within, one instead claims the Way is of no benefit—thus discarding pills and powders, abandoning breathing exercises. Therefore, it is not attaining long life that is difficult, but hearing the Way; not hearing the Way that is difficult, but practicing it; not practicing it that is difficult, but persisting to the end. A skilled craftsman can provide another with compass and square, but cannot ensure their mastery; a wise teacher can impart medical texts, but cannot guarantee their application. Cultivation is like sowing grain; completion is like harvesting it. Though the field be fertile and the water abundant, if one misses the season, neglects plowing and weeding, the crops will wither in the fields, unharvested and uncut. Though the acres be vast, there will be no yield.

Ordinary people not only fail to recognize benefit as benefit, but also fail to recognize harm as harm. Harm is easy to know and swift; benefit is difficult to know and slow. If one does not grasp what is easy, how can one comprehend what is difficult? Harm is like the consumption of lamp oil—unseen, yet suddenly exhausted; benefit is like the growth of seedlings—unnoticed, yet suddenly lush. Therefore, in cultivating the body and nurturing nature, one must attend carefully to the smallest details. One cannot dismiss minor benefits as insignificant and neglect them, nor overlook minor harms as harmless and fail to guard against them. All great things arise from the accumulation of small things; a hundred million begins with one. If one cherishes the beginning and completes the end with diligence, then one has guarded the Way.

Someone asked: In ancient times, were there those who attained long life without any practice, merely by chance? The Master Embracing Simplicity replied: There were not. Some followed renowned teachers, accumulating merit and enduring hardship, and were thus granted completed elixirs; others received secret formulas and practiced them in seclusion, their deeds untouched by the world, their words unburdened by俗. Records preserve only their names, not the means by which they attained immortality—thus, such accounts are incomplete.

Of old, the Yellow Emperor, born speaking, commanded a hundred spirits—one might call him naturally endowed by Heaven. Yet even he could not attain the Way by sitting idly. Thus, he ascended Wangwu Mountain to receive the cinnabar scriptures, journeyed to Dinghu to ascend on a flying pearl, climbed Kongtong to consult Guangcheng, and served Da Kui at Juci. He traveled to Mount Tai to honor Zhonghuang, entered Jingu to consult Hua Zi, discussed the cultivation of the Way with the two Xuan Su maidens, sought the precision of astronomy from Shan Ji and Li Mu, studied divination from Feng Hou, learned pulse diagnosis from Lei Qi, mastered warfare from the Five Tones’ defense, exposed hidden evils through the *Baize* records, surveyed geography through the *Qingniao* writings, and mended wounds with the arts of metallurgy. Thus, he encompassed all secrets, exhausted the Way, and fulfilled the truth, ascending on a dragon to the highest reaches, peerless with Heaven and Earth.

Yet the *Scriptures of Divine Immortals* all state that the Yellow Emperor and Laozi served the Great One, the Primordial Lord, to receive essential instructions. How much less could those inferior to these two lords attain immortality and transcend the world without such guidance? I have never heard of it.

Someone asked: If the Yellow Emperor truly attained immortality, why is there a tomb at Mount Qiao? The Master Embracing Simplicity replied: According to the *Classic of Mount Jing* and the *Record of the Dragon’s Return*, after the Yellow Emperor consumed the divine elixir, a dragon came to welcome him. His ministers, overwhelmed with longing, could not bear to part. Some took his mat and staff to establish a temple and make offerings; others took his robes and crown to bury and guard. The *Biographies of Divine Immortals* states that the Yellow Emperor chose his day of departure seventy days in advance. Seventy days later, he returned and was buried at Mount Qiao. Suddenly, the mountain collapsed, the tomb was empty, and only his sword and sandals remained. Though these accounts differ, they all affirm his attainment of immortality.

Those who speak of the Yellow Emperor’s immortality appear frequently in Daoist texts and the sayings of the Hundred Schools, yet the Confucians, unwilling to entertain strange or extraordinary paths, focus solely on ritual and teaching. The affairs of divine immortals cannot instruct the common folk, so they claim his death to restrain the people’s hearts. Zhu Ba and Luan Ba rendered great service to the people, who built temples and made offerings to them in life. In ancient times, when men of great virtue passed away, their ministers carved their achievements on enduring monuments. Even today, when officials and people long for departed rulers and elders, they erect steles to praise their virtue—this is akin to the Yellow Emperor having temples and tombs. How does this prove he must have died?

Someone asked: Peng Zu lived eight hundred years, An Qi three thousand—these are lives surpassing others. If there truly is a Way of immortality, why did they not attain it? Is it not that their allotted lifespan and received qi had a fixed span, and they merely happened to live longer? In principle, it cannot be extended, so they could not escape decay and death.

The Master Embracing Simplicity said: ...

Gezhi Ziyang responded: According to the *Pengzu Jing*, he served under Emperor Ku, assisted Yao, passed through the Xia dynasty, and became a grandee in Yin. The Yin king sent women to learn the arts of the bedchamber from him, and when they proved effective, sought to kill Pengzu to suppress his Way. Pengzu sensed this and fled, departing at the age of seven or eight hundred—this was not death. The *Huangshi Gong Ji* records that more than seventy years after Pengzu’s departure, his disciples saw him west of the Flowing Sands—clearly, he did not die. Moreover, Pengzu’s disciples—Qingyi Wugong, Heixue Gong, Xiumei Gong, Baitu Gong, Zililou Gong, Taizu Jun, and Gaoqiuzi Bukenlai—seven or eight in all, each lived for several hundred years in Yin before ascending as immortals. How could Pengzu himself have died? Furthermore, Liu Xiang’s *Liexian Zhuan* also states that Pengzu was an immortal.

As for Master Anqi, he sold medicines by the sea in Langye. Transmitted accounts say he had already lived a thousand years by the time Qin Shihuang summoned him for conversation, which lasted three days and nights. His words were lofty, his defenses profound, his learning vast and well-evidenced. Shihuang, astonished, bestowed upon him gold and jade worth tens of millions. Anqi accepted them, placing them at Fuxiang Pavilion, and left a pair of red jade shoes as repayment, along with a letter saying: “Seek me again on Penglai Mountain after several thousand years.” Thus, he was already a thousand years old when he met Shihuang—clearly, he did not die. Moreover, Shihuang was harsh and cruel, a man who ruled the world and would not have believed in immortals, nor would he have tolerated answers that displeased him. Yet when he questioned Anqi about the matter of longevity, Anqi’s responses were fitting, and Shihuang, awakening to the truth, came to believe that immortality must exist in the world. He bestowed generous gifts and earnestly desired to learn the art of deathlessness, but lacked an enlightened teacher. Deceived by the likes of Lu Ao and Xu Fu, he failed to attain it. Had Anqi’s words lacked substance, within three days and nights they would have been exhausted, and Shihuang would surely have boiled, roasted, or slaughtered him, sparing him no calamity of the cauldron. How then could he have received such lavish gifts?

Someone asked: If there are those who consume medicinal substances, practice qi circulation, and perform daoyin exercises, yet still cannot escape death, why is this?

Gezhi Ziyang answered: Those who do not obtain the golden elixir but only ingest herbal medicines or cultivate minor arts may prolong their years and delay death, but they cannot attain immortality. Some know only how to take herbs but not the essential techniques of rejuvenation—thus, they will never achieve long life. Others fail to understand how to wear spirit talismans, observe prohibitions, or guard the true unity within; they may only prevent illness, ward off wind and dampness, and avoid minor ailments. But if suddenly afflicted by malevolent ghosts, fierce demons, mountain spirits, water toxins, or other harms, they will die. Some do not know the methods for entering mountains, provoking mountain spirits to bring misfortune—demons test them, beasts injure them, ravine toxins entangle them, snakes and vipers sting them—leading to many deaths, each with its own cause. Others begin cultivation late in life, having already inflicted deep harm upon themselves, making recovery difficult. Even if they attempt to mend what is broken, the benefits are not yet consolidated when illness strikes again. How can such destructive practices lead to longevity?

Some who take up the Way in old age attain immortality, while others who begin young fail—why is this? The former, though aged, received abundant qi at birth; with abundant qi, their injuries are shallow and easily healed, so they succeed. The latter, though young, received little qi at birth; with scant qi, their injuries run deep and are hard to heal, so they fail. Consider the hibiscus and willow: when cut and replanted, they revive whether upright or inverted, whether planted vertically or horizontally—no plant revives more easily. Yet if buried too shallowly or not long enough, if scraped or stripped, shaken or uprooted, even if nourished with rich soil and bathed in spring rains, they still wither and perish. Their roots are not firm, they cannot sprout new buds, their vital fluids fail to coalesce, and their life force cannot take form. The human body is easily harmed and difficult to nurture—far more so than these two plants. Yet the forces that assault it—scraping, stripping, shaking, uprooting—are even more severe. With little to sustain it and much to destroy it, death is only natural.

Those who exhale the old and inhale the new rely on qi to grow, but when qi is greatly depleted, growth becomes difficult. Those who consume medicines rely on blood to replenish it, but when blood is nearly exhausted, replenishment is hard. Running and panting, coughing and choking, laboring the body and exhausting the spirit—these are signs of qi depletion. A dull complexion, withered skin, parched lips, pale veins, shriveled pores—these are signs of blood exhaustion. When these two signs decline externally, the spiritual root withers within. In such cases, even superior medicines cannot save them.

Those who cultivate the Way yet fail, who manage their lives yet die—though they possess qi and blood, the root of what makes them qi and blood has already perished, leaving only its branches and streams. Like embers cast into water, the fire is extinguished, yet smoke does not immediately vanish; like a severed tree, its leaves may yet live. These are not without smoke or leaves, but what makes them smoke or leaves has already perished.

People assume illness begins when symptoms appear, and death when breath ceases. They fear only wind, cold, heat, and dampness, unaware that these cannot harm the robust. Only those with weak bodies and scant qi cannot endure them and thus succumb. How to compare this? Suppose several people of the same age and similar health venture into the desert, braving a frigid night. Snow falls upon them, ice forms beneath, cold winds lash their limbs, and the night is filled with coughs and frozen spittle. Among them, some may fall ill from the cold, but not all—this is not because the cold favors some, but because some bodies cannot endure it. Likewise, when all eat the same food, some may fall ill from it—not because the food is inherently toxic, but because their constitutions differ. When drinking from the same vessel, some may become drunk while others remain sober—not because the wine’s potency varies, but because their tolerance differs. When exposed to scorching heat, some may die of heatstroke—not because the sun discriminates, but because their resilience differs.

Taking the same medicine, some may alone grow drowsy, restless, and distressed—not because the drug’s potency harbors favor or spite. Thus, when a fierce wind rushes into the forest, the withered boughs are the first to snap; when a great wave crashes against the cliff, the rock embracing a fissure is the first to crumble; when wildfire sweeps the plain, the parched grasses are the first to burn; when a dragon bowl falls to the ground, the brittle ones alone shatter. From this we see that those without the bodily vessel of the Dao, already ailing in their fundament, are merely provoked by wind, cold, heat, or damp. If one can ensure that the upright qi does not decline and that form and spirit guard each other, nothing can harm them.

Generally, those who pursue the Dao fear beginning too late, not too early. Those who rely on their youthful vigor and robust strength overtax themselves, accumulating a hundred ailments until their lives hang by a thread, like morning dew. Even with potent medicine, merely consuming herbs and plants may restore them to the state of ordinary people, but cannot extend their allotted span. Thus the immortal classics say: “In nurturing life, not causing harm is the foundation”—this is the essential teaching. Shennong declared: “If the hundred diseases are not cured, how can one attain long life?” Truly so.
