Someone asked: “Is what you call harm not found in excess and desire?” Baopuzi replied: “How could it be only that? Yet the essence of long life lies in the Way of Returning to Youth. The superior practitioner knows this and can thereby extend years and dispel illness; the next best does not harm himself. If one, though still young and strong, knows the Way of Returning to Youth, takes the Yin Elixir to replenish the brain, and gathers Jade Fluid from the long valleys, even without consuming medicinal substances, they will not fail to reach three hundred years—though they may not attain immortality. Those who do not grasp the method are like one who fills an ice cup with boiling water or wraps fire in a feather bundle. Moreover, straining to think beyond one’s capacity harms; forcing oneself to lift beyond one’s strength harms.”

Grief and sorrow wear one down; excessive joy and delight harm as well. Relentless pursuit of desires injures; prolonged talking and laughter injure. Sleeping and resting at improper times injure; drawing bows and pulling crossbows injure. Deep intoxication and vomiting injure; eating one’s fill and lying down immediately injure. Leaping and running until breathless injure; exultant shouts and bitter weeping injure. Lack of union between Yin and Yang injures. When accumulated injuries reach their limit, early death follows—early death is not the Way.

Thus, the methods of nurturing life are these: do not spit far, do not walk swiftly, do not strain the ears, do not gaze intently for long, do not sit to the point of exhaustion, do not lie down until fatigued. Dress before the cold becomes extreme, remove clothing before the heat becomes unbearable. Do not eat only when ravenously hungry, and do not eat to excess; do not drink only when parched, and do not drink too much. For overeating leads to stagnation and accumulations, while overdrinking forms phlegm and obstructions. Avoid extreme exertion and extreme indolence; do not rise late, do not sweat profusely, do not sleep excessively. Do not gallop on horseback or race in carriages, do not strain the eyes gazing into the distance, do not consume too much raw or cold food. Do not drink wine in the wind, do not bathe too frequently, do not harbor grand ambitions or distant aspirations, do not pursue strange and elaborate contrivances. In winter, do not seek extreme cold; in summer, do not pursue excessive coolness. Do not sleep exposed beneath the stars, do not sleep with shoulders bared in the night. Extreme cold, extreme heat, great winds, and thick fog—all should be avoided.

The five flavors entering the mouth should not be taken in excess: too much sourness harms the spleen, too much bitterness harms the lungs, too much pungency harms the liver, too much saltiness harms the heart, and too much sweetness harms the kidneys—this is the natural principle of the Five Phases. What is called "injury" is not immediately apparent; it means that over time, one’s lifespan is diminished.

Therefore, those skilled in nurturing life rise and retire according to the seasons’ early and late hours, maintain regularity in their daily activities with perfect harmony, regulate and strengthen sinews and bones through proper postures, ward off illness and repel evil with techniques of swallowing and exhaling, circulate and harmonize the constructive and defensive qi with methods of supplementation and drainage, and balance labor and rest with the essentials of giving and taking. They restrain anger to preserve Yin qi and suppress joy to nourish Yang qi. Only then do they first take herbal medicines to remedy deficiencies, and later ingest the Golden Elixir to secure boundless longevity. The principle of eternal life is fully contained herein.

If one resolves to follow their inclinations, considering themselves enlightened and accepting of fate, dismissing heterodox teachings, indulging emotions and exerting strength without cultivating longevity—when such a person hears these words, they pass by like the wind.

The lightning’s passage before one’s eyes cannot be described in full—yet some, though their bodies wither in dissipation and their qi expires amid silken pleasures, remain content. How then could one speak to them of the arts of nurturing life? Not only will they refuse to accept it; they will call it heresy. To expect their belief would be like offering a mirror to the blind or music to the deaf.

**Chapter Fourteen: Earnest Seeking**
The Master of Embracing Simplicity said: The greatest virtue of Heaven and Earth is the generation of life, the cherishing of all things. Thus, the most guarded and revered secrets of the Daoist tradition are none other than the methods of attaining longevity. For this reason, blood-oath covenants are sworn before transmission, and the warnings of heavenly punishment are invoked. Even the former masters dared not lightly impart these teachings to others; only when a seeker’s devotion is most earnest should one carefully select the most sincere to instruct. How much less should one casually offer them to those who neither desire nor seek them, or who seek without wholeheartedness?

Those who are not destined to attain immortality, even if they witness immortals thronging the world, will still insist that such beings are of a different kind, that they belong to another realm, or call them transformations of demons and sprites. Some may claim it was mere chance encounter. Who among them would acknowledge that immortality is the result of cultivation? If they do not believe, even if Red Pine or Wang Qiao were to speak to them directly, they would still point and call it heresy.

Yet even among those who recognize and believe, many falter in their inability to seek diligently for a true master. Those who comprehend the essentials and attain the genuine Dao are indeed rare, and cannot be found in haste. Still, those who know of it say only that such masters are few—not that they have vanished from the world. For those with the destiny for immortality, if they seek broadly and sincerely, they will inevitably encounter them. Yet while some seek and do not find, none have ever found without seeking.

In the world, there are countless deceivers who usurp the title of Daoist to swindle money. Yet even these will not admit to complete ignorance; they may feign some superficial understanding, falsely claim mastery, or adorn themselves with tricks to deceive. The gullible, unable to discern truth from falsehood, flock to them with questions, only to be misled. These frauds then restrict their followers, preventing them from seeking further, and declare, “The Dao is exhausted here.” How lamentable and infuriating that so many earnest seekers are thus led astray!

Occasionally, one may encounter those who understand the refinement of the Five Clouds, the transmutation of the Eight Stones, the compounding of the Nine Elixirs, the alchemy of cinnabar and gold, the congealing of jade-like frost and snow in the divine crucible, or the gathering of lingzhi on Mount Song. Yet the many will slander them, saying, “Only Red Pine and Wang Qiao knew these methods. For modern men to claim such knowledge is sheer delusion.” The shallow-minded, failing to recognize the deceit in such words, abandon their far-reaching quests in despair. How sorrowful and sigh-worthy this is!

Time flies like an arrow, swift as the wind; youth vanishes in an instant, and old age arrives with terrible speed. Nothing in the world compares to its haste. A lifespan of a hundred years is but thirty thousand days.

Those who guard against weakness know nothing, while those who await decline and old age find all joy and pleasure abandoned. From the confusion of childhood to the senility of dotage, several decades pass in peril and distress, with sickness and sorrow ever at hand. Of the years one dwells in the world, barely half remain. Even if one lives a full century, the days of laughter and peace scarcely exceed fifty or sixty years—no more than six or seven thousand days of fleeting delight, sorrow, and senility. In the blink of an eye, they are gone. How much rarer still is one who attains a full hundred years? Reflecting earnestly, one can only pity those summer insects and morning mushrooms.

Truly, this is the utmost sorrow of those who do not know the Dao. A common saying goes: "Each day a person lives is a day lost, like driving cattle or sheep to the slaughter—every step brings death nearer." Though the comparison is crude, it holds truth. The enlightened do not grieve over death not because they do not seek life, but because they know no way to escape it. Thus, they say, "Rejoice in Heaven and know fate, and so have no sorrow." It is not that they do not desire long life. The Duke of Zhou offered himself in place of King Wu, and Confucius dragged his staff in lament—even sages do not wish for swift death.

Yet common folk, seeing Zhuangzi’s metaphor of the great dream, rush to espouse the doctrine of equating life and death. These are cunning sophists, feigning detachment with words that defy restraint—precisely the kind Confucius sought to refute and punish. Observe those who speak thus: when struck by illness, they hastily seek acupuncture and moxibustion; when faced with mortal danger, they fear death intensely. Yet the prevailing custom dismisses truth, turning from the classics to the works of the Masters. Those who do not spout clever, paradoxical arguments are deemed rustic and unrefined, lacking the "bone and marrow" of Lao-Zhuang thought. Thus, the vulgar drift into baseless doctrines, unable to return to reason.

Laozi made long life and enduring vision his pursuit, while Zhuangzi prized wagging his tail in the mud over being a sacrificial ox in embroidered robes, or starving while begging grain from the Marquis of He. From this, we know he could not truly equate life and death. Later scholars, failing to examine the truth, cling to a single phrase—is this not folly?

Moreover, to descend into the Nine Springs, where the endless night begins, to become food for ants and worms, and finally merge with the dust—this thought sears the heart with dread. If one truly seeks life, how can one not abandon trivial matters and cultivate the wondrous arts? Those who do not believe may do as they please. But those who do believe yet fail to cast off worldly attachments, who cannot focus solely on nurturing life but dabble in cultivation only in the spare moments between worldly affairs—even if they attempt it, they often begin too late and rarely succeed.

What most people covet is power, profit, and desire. Yet if one’s body is not whole, even high office, vast wealth, mountains of gold and jade, or countless beauties are not truly one’s own. Thus, the superior person first attends to the matter of long life. Once long life is secured, all desires can be fulfilled at will. If one has not yet ascended to the realm of the immortals, one may at least become an earthbound immortal, dwelling among men like Pengzu or Laozi, living for centuries without losing the joys of human existence. Then, one may gradually ascend—this, too, is a grand achievement. Yet one must find a worthy teacher; without sufficient devotion, success is impossible.

In former times, the Empress Dowager of Han studied the *Book of Documents* under Xiahou Sheng, rewarding him with a hundred catties of gold and countless treasures. Upon his death, she bestowed two million coins upon his family and mourned him for a hundred days. When Emperor Cheng was heir apparent, he studied the *Analects* under Zhang Yu. After ascending the throne, he enfeoffed Yu as Marquis Within the Pass, granted him a fief of a thousand households, and appointed him Grand Master of Splendid Happiness, bestowing a hundred catties of gold. Later, when Yu was promoted to Chancellor and advanced to the rank of Marquis of An, he begged retirement in his old age. The emperor granted him a carriage drawn by four horses and a hundred catties of gold, along with tens of thousands of coins. When Yu fell ill, the emperor personally visited and knelt at his bedside. When Emperor Zhang was heir apparent, he studied the *Classic of Filial Piety* under Huan Rong. After ascending the throne, he appointed Rong Grand Master over the Masses. The emperor visited Rong’s residence, allowing him to sit facing east with a table and staff, while officials and hundreds of Rong’s disciples attended. The emperor personally held the texts and lectured. He enfeoffed Rong as Marquis Within the Pass with a fief of five thousand households. When Rong fell ill, the emperor visited his home, dismounting at the alley entrance and hurrying forward with the scrolls in his arms, like a disciple. When Rong passed, the emperor donned mourning garb.

None of these men achieved merit through conquering cities, battling in the wilds, breaking enemy formations, or expanding the realm—through deeds of valor or exploits in distant lands. They merely transmitted the teachings of a single classic, expounding its phrases, yet were honored so grandly. These were but empty words about the dead. Even emperors, in their exalted position, humbled themselves to serve such teachers. Yet in the world today, those who wish to cultivate the Dao of long life refuse to humble themselves before a worthy master, trampling upon propriety in their haste to seek the essentials. How can they possibly succeed?

Scholars may bow in reverence and run errands in service—what benefit do such trifling acts bring to their teacher? Yet without them, their hearts remain unyielding. If their hearts remain unyielding, even when instructed, they will not apply themselves fully. If they do not apply themselves fully, how can they possibly obtain the secret essentials in their entirety? At best, they may be shown superficial techniques—how could these suffice to achieve the work of immortality?

There are also those whose fair complexion and pleasing appearance inspire trust, yet their devotion to the Dao is not rooted in the heart. Their spirits seek only to extract something. Outwardly, they feign deep reverence, but within days, their negligence becomes apparent. Should they encounter a discerning teacher, he may wish to observe their intentions closely. Testing them with prolonged delay and withholding instruction to gauge their resolve, such people will reveal their true nature—deception and falsity. Even if taught, they will not receive the full transmission, nor will they speak with sincerity. If their words are not sincere, teaching them is of no benefit.

Chen Anshi was but thirteen years old, a mere retainer in Guan Shuben’s household. Having first attained the immortal Dao, Shuben—though seventy years of age with a head of white hair—bowed morning and night to Anshi, saying: “The Dao is noble, and virtue is esteemed; he who first attains the Way is the teacher. I dare not grow weary in upholding the rites of a disciple.” Thus, Anshi revealed to him the essential methods, and Shuben, too, ascended as an immortal.

Man receives his spirit first from Heaven and Earth, then his qi and blood from his parents. Yet without a enlightened teacher to instruct him in the Dao of transcending the world, he has no means to escape death. A chisel may leave embers in the stone, but the years of one’s life have already crumbled. By this reasoning, the grace of an enlightened teacher truly surpasses that of Heaven and Earth and outweighs that of parents. How can one not revere them? How can one not seek them?

Ge Hong said: The ancients were plain and valued deeds over words. Thus, those who governed did not esteem rhetorical flourish, and those who cultivated the Dao did not exalt eloquent discourse. As customs declined and shallowed, external adornments grew ever more elaborate. The barriers of formal learning piled high like mountains at the gates of the Confucians, while the inner scriptures were likewise scattered and neglected among the masters of esoteric arts. Beginners could not be immediately entrusted with essential teachings, and human nature, moreover, finds greater delight in abundance of beginnings and ends. Thus, later adepts of the Dao—such as Gan Ji, Rong Song, and Gui Bo—each composed works numbering in the thousands of chapters. Yet these were largely admonitions and exhortations, unwilling to reveal the great guiding principles and ultimate aims.

The true oral instructions for attaining the Real might be transmitted only by word of mouth or written on no more than a foot of silk, concealed within the folds of one’s collar. Without long and arduous training under a master, without enduring trials and tests, one could not obtain them. Even among the motley disciples, each was taught according to the depth of their devotion, the length of their suffering, the reach of their intelligence, and the capacity of their talents. Over a thousand years, only a few ever exhausted the secrets hidden in their master’s pillows or sleeves.

Some were merely given prepared elixirs to share, sufficient to grant them immortality, yet the masters never transmitted the written methods. Thus, among the Daoist adepts of the world, not one in ten thousand knows the art of the Golden Elixir. Yet the shallow and narrow-minded assume that the methods of immortality must lie in voluminous texts or in the rituals of sacrifice and prostration. Long life and mastery of destiny do not come from offerings and prayers.

In the Qin and Han dynasties, great sacrifices were made to Taiyi, the Five Spirits, Chenbao, the Eight Immortals, and others, expending cattle, sheep, grain, silk, and coin by the millions—all to no avail. How much less can a common man, whose virtue is incomplete and whose body is untended, hope to extend his years by offering the three sacrificial animals, wine, and delicacies, beseeching the gods with prayers? Such delusion is profound.

Some well-meaning enthusiasts, though earnest in their desire for the Dao, fail to diligently seek out renowned masters or compound rare elixirs. Instead, they spend their days reciting and expounding thousands of useless volumes, gaining nothing in old age. They conclude that immortality is but a myth, or they prostrate themselves in empty rooms, slaughter animals, burn incense, and pray for blessings—yet the sick are not healed, deaths follow one after another, fortunes are squandered, and not a single effect is achieved. They never repent, blaming only their own lack of devotion. Had they applied such diligence to seeking a master, had they spent such wealth on purchasing elixirs and secret arts, they would surely have attained transcendence and long life, escaping the world. What difference is there between this and plowing a stony field in old age, hoping for a thousand granaries’ harvest? Though their labor is great, they do not grasp the proper means. It is like setting out for Chu but taking the road to Yan: though the horse may be swift, it will not arrive, not because it does not run fast, but because it has lost the Way.

Some are credulous by nature and too ready to trust others. Their intelligence is insufficient to discern truth from falsehood, to gauge depth from shallowness. Their learning is narrow, and they cannot appraise things. When they chance upon someone who praises himself—saying, “I possess secret texts”—they cling to him and serve him. Yet many of these are vulgar men or mere children who falsely assume the name of a Daoist adept, their reputations exceeding their true worth due to boasting and deceit. Greedy and impure, they seek only profit. When asked for teachings, they force out vague sounds, nodding and bowing, raising and lowering their voices as if guarding profound secrets that cannot be revealed. If pressed, they smile and promise to disclose them later. Thus, students cannot bear to leave, believing their devotion insufficient and their gifts too meager. Their faith grows ever more reverent; they offer pearls and treasures, perform the labors of servants, carry heavy burdens over great distances, endure hardships and dangers, hoping to prove themselves through accumulated toil and suffering, to gain some rare insight. Yet the years pass in vain, their parents’ support squandered, their wives and children neglected. They tread frost and ice, following their master year after year, wasting their resources and strength, achieving nothing in the end.

At first, the master may have deceived them; later, he may feel shame, but his body is empty, his knowledge exhausted, his abilities too meager to teach. What method can he use to perfect others? I have seen no fewer than ten or twenty such men. Some style themselves with lofty names, lingering in the world for ages; others claim to be three or four hundred years old, merely changing their names to pose as sages among men. Many have followers who serve them. I do not deign to record their names.

I have traveled among the common folk, where the unlearned cannot distinguish truth from falsehood. They praise and exalt such men, amplifying their deceptions. The cunning and false gain empty fame, and their influence spreads, for none can discern the truth. Even the wise may occasionally let down their guard, relying only on their ears, and thus mislead later students. Such cases never fail to evoke sighs.

I have seen these charlatans deceive the world to gain power and profit, and though their punishment may be slow, Heaven’s net is wide but not leaky. Yet it is the earnest seekers they mislead who are most pitiable.

People of the world often chase empty reputations, rarely examining the facts. When they hear that so-and-so has many disciples—sometimes a hundred or more—they assume there must be something extraordinary about him. They rush to join, gathering in crowds to serve him, wasting their time and effort in reverence of such ignorant men. They do not investigate further, nor do they realize that the master’s disciples may have gained wealth or eloquence through their service, yet after years of devotion, they have not attained the Dao. Foolish men do not understand that such a master is unworthy of service, nor do they awaken to this truth. How tragic!

To search for a foot-tall mound in a mountain’s expanse, to seek a tree that reaches the sky, to strain for a whale that swallows boats from a cow’s hoofprint—though one may spend a lifetime at it, how can one succeed? Alas! Future students, though they must make seeking a master their duty, must also take care to choose wisely.

The narrow-minded, whose conduct is shallow, whose virtue is thin, whose merits and deeds are few, cannot perfect others in the Dao, nor can they repay the profound debt owed to their teachers. Reflect deeply on this, lest your efforts be in vain.

Ge Hong said: These Daoists of empty reputation are skilled in deception, misleading students. Many are short on talent and ashamed of their ignorance. Outwardly, they pretend to have studied enough, never willing to seek instruction from those greater than themselves. Like fools, they stand facing a wall, not merely silent but resenting and slandering those who truly possess the Dao, fearing that their own reputations will be eclipsed.

Such men—do they truly seek the methods of longevity? Their aim is only to gather disciples and exploit their wealth and strength to gratify their own desires. Yet they do not realize how Heaven discerns high and low; their end will surely bring calamity.

The poor must not falsely claim to be rich, nor the lowly pretend to be noble. How much less should those who possess no true attainment in the Dao’s arts yet keep disciples and students on empty pretenses? Even ordinary people should not harbor envy toward the good—how much more should Daoists, whose very lives depend on loyalty, trust, and open-heartedness? How can they allow such arrogance to fester in their breasts?

Men cannot perceive the divine, yet the divine perceives them with ease. What difference is there between this and standing outside a gauze curtain, unable to see what lies within the chamber, yet acting with such haughty indifference, assuming no one sees them? It is no different from the thief who, hearing the clamor of the bell he strikes, covers his own ears to avoid detection—yet the deaf and blind remain within him.

Those who covet fame, monopolize glory, and gather followers for show, seeking reputation abroad and profit at home, resenting anyone greater than themselves—such rivalry exceeds even the worldly struggle for power and influence. They wield their lips and tongues like blades, using slander and praise to form factions. Outwardly, they feign closeness; inwardly, they are distant. Their faces show harmony, but their actions betray division. They speak of shared purpose in the open, yet secretly harbor the venom of wasps and scorpions. This is what both Heaven and men despise, the very writ that summons disaster.

Even those who study the Five Classics should not be ashamed to seek instruction from inferiors to cultivate virtue and refine their learning, daily advancing in brightness. As for archery, charioteering, basic skills, simple calculations, humble tasks of farming and weaving, or minor arts of measurement—all require a teacher to master their principles. How much more so for the methods of nurturing longevity, which extend years and preserve life? This is no different from rescuing someone from the brink of death. How can one cling to the shame of seeking instruction, enduring lifelong ignorance, never changing even in old age, never repenting even at death? Such are the truly benighted among Heaven’s people.

Those who witness this should feel shame and dread on their behalf, yet the perpetrators themselves remain oblivious, heedless of their own reflection. Even Confucian scholars must steadfastly guard simplicity, outwardly embracing plainness, knowing as if not knowing, possessing as if not possessing, so that common fools cannot exhaust their praise. When questioned, they do not answer at once but respond only after yielding in deference. How is it that those who claim to be Daoists so stubbornly pretend to know what they do not, to possess what they lack, vainly flaunting themselves to pursue ill-gotten gains?

Those who stray and do not turn back only sink deeper, as if some force compels them. One can only assume they feel no shame in their conduct. I do not speak these words lightly but out of genuine concern, for I cannot remain silent in the face of such affliction.

Those who pity and ponder cannot bear to witness an infant falling into a well, yet they refuse to heed bitter counsel when peril is nigh—this is as plain as a diagnosis without taking the pulse. As the *Baopuzi* states: If a man condemned to death could be saved, none would begrudge the toil or humiliation, nor shrink from humble entreaties, for the merit of preserving life is assured. Now, the rabble of miscellaneous Daoists, failing to attain the Great Elixir and the supreme method, will surely never achieve longevity—this is self-evident. Though they may cure illnesses with effects akin to raising the dead, though they may fast for years without hunger, summon spirits at will, vanish or appear at whim, gaze a thousand *li* afar, discern others’ rise and fall, dispel hidden afflictions, and foresee fortune or misfortune before it sprouts, all this avails nothing for their own lifespan. Yet they still feel shame in seeking instruction, disdain serving those who have gone before, cling to a moment’s pride, and willingly embrace eternal mediocrity—such are those blind to the weight of matters.

The ancients said: "Life’s benefit to me is vast indeed." In terms of its worth, though one were enthroned as emperor, it could not compare to this method. In terms of its weight, though one owned all under heaven, it could not equal this art. Thus arose the parable of the dying king who envied the living rat. Now, governing a state to bring peace, or cultivating the body to attain life—these do not come of themselves but are achieved through deliberate means.

Those who cling to the hollow fame of brief deficiency and feel shame at the temporary toil of seeking a teacher—though they claim not to be foolish, I do not believe it. Suppose a man were spared certain death only to face execution: he would still rejoice in shedding a heavy burden for a lighter one, in escaping searing agony to preserve his breath, and would endure such suffering as greater than rebirth. Yet people remain unashamed of their ignorance only because they do not know the day of their death. If they truly knew it, and could delay mutilation by even a short time, they would surely do so. How much more, then, should they willingly perform menial tasks—sweeping floors, holding towels, exerting themselves for those greater than themselves—to be taught the Way of not dying? What hardship is there in this that the blind fear it?

If a man were ashamed to run swiftly to escape a wildfire, or too proud to flee the wind only to drown in deep waters, the world would surely call him witless. Yet all know to mock those who do not avoid disaster, while none wonder why they do not fear calamity. Why is this?

The *Baopuzi* says: Many have written books on the Dao in the past, yet none have avoided the pursuit of florid, clever words to embellish empty profundities. None have thoroughly examined the steps to long life, or sharply diagnosed the ailments of the Way, as diligently as I have. My true aim is to guide the lost back to the path, to recover what was lost in the east at the mulberry groves, to draw the rope for those fallen into the well before they sink entirely. But those who nurture perilous illness and reject bitter medicine—what can be done for them?

Who is without fault? To err and then correct it is like the sun and moon eclipsed yet shining again, like Yan Hui. I also wish to ensure that future seekers of the Way of life choose their teachers wisely. Thus, I offer my most sincere counsel without adornment or extravagant words. My words are direct, my brush unflinching, my pain at the thought of long life thwarted, and my restraint of deceit—what more is there to seek?

The *Baopuzi* says: I deeply consider that those who study the arts of the Dao and cultivate life often follow teachers unworthy of the task, achieving nothing in the end. This leads later aspirants, seeing their failure to attain long life, to conclude that immortality is impossible. Those who wish to transcend life but refuse to discipline the body or restrain the self in pursuit of the wondrous and subtle will advance only to lose the means of seeking office, and retreat without the merit of delaying old age. They harm themselves within and discourage others without.

Attaining immortality through study is as certain as sowing millet or grain—its success is plain to see. Yet no one reaps fine harvests without plowing, nor gains long life and transcendence without diligence.

**Miscellaneous Responses, Chapter Fifteen**

Someone asked: "Can abstaining from grain lead to long life? Among all methods of restraint, which is the best?"

The *Baopuzi* replied: Abstaining from grain can only reduce the expense of food; it cannot alone grant long life. Those who have fasted for long periods say they suffer fewer illnesses than when eating grain. Consuming *Atractylodes* or *Polygonatum*, or *Yu yu liang*, twice daily over time increases strength, allowing one to bear burdens and travel far without fatigue. Those who take mineral elixirs and maintain them for ten or five years, or swallow qi, consume talismans, or drink divine water, merely stave off hunger—their bodies cannot endure labor.

Though Daoist texts say, "To attain long life, the intestines must be pure; to achieve immortality, the intestines must be free of residue," and claim, "Those who eat grass run swiftly but are foolish; those who eat meat are strong but fierce; those who eat grain are wise but short-lived; those who eat qi are spiritually enlightened and do not die"—these are but the biased views of qi-practitioners and cannot be relied upon alone.

If one wishes to take the golden elixir or great medicine, it is best to fast for a hundred days first. If unable, one may take it directly, though attaining immortality will be slightly delayed—there is no great harm. In times of famine or hardship, those who know this method of abstaining from grain can avoid starvation in the mountains and forests. Otherwise, there is no urgent need to fast, as the benefits are not substantial. Moreover, those who abruptly cut off meat may still crave rich, fresh flavors. For those who have not yet left the worldly life, abandoned their families, or dwelled in mountain caves, it is better not to force abstinence from grain. Instead, one should regulate hunger and satiety.

There are now over a hundred methods: some involve taking dozens of mineral elixir pills to avoid hunger for forty or fifty days. Refining pine, cypress, or *Atractylodes* can also sustain one, though not as effectively as great medicines, and the effects last no more than ten years. Some methods allow one to fast for a hundred or two hundred days, while others require daily consumption to stave off hunger. Some involve eating a lavish meal first, then taking medicine to preserve the food, preventing digestion and allowing one to fast for three years. To return to eating grain, one must take *Malva* seeds with lard to purge the undigested food, which will pass unchanged.

In Luoyang, the Daoist Dong Wei and others at the White Society never ate. Chen Zixu served and studied under them for a long time before obtaining their method. It involves grinding over ten ingredients—such as licorice, *Saposhnikovia*, and *Gleditsia* pods—into a powder. One first takes a square-inch spoonful, then swallows twelve stone pellets the size of sparrow eggs, which suffice for a hundred days. When the time comes, one takes the powder again, and strength and complexion remain unchanged. To return to eating grain, one must drink a *Malva* seed decoction to expel the stones before eating.

There is also the method of using "Red Dragon’s Blood" and "Blue Dragon’s Paste" with cinnabar, malachite, and water to soften stones, which can then be eaten. If not consumed immediately, they dissolve completely. Eating these stones to satiety strengthens the body.

Another method is the "Stone-Drawing Powder": a square-inch spoonful is added to a *dou* of white stones and boiled in water until tender, like taro, to replace grain. Zhang Taixuan and his family, along with dozens of disciples, lived in Linlü Mountain and ate stones this way for over ten years, remaining robust. However, this requires white stones, unlike the "Red Dragon’s Blood" and "Blue Dragon’s Paste" methods, which can use any stone but require firewood for boiling.

Some use talismans, some water, some both. Others eat nine jujubes daily with one or two *sheng* of wine. Some consume the twelve *shi* of qi, starting at midnight, counting down from nine nines to five fives. In spring, they face east to ingest the green qi of Jupiter into the liver; in summer, they take the red qi of Mars into the heart; in the three months of each season, they consume the yellow qi of Saturn into the spleen; in autumn, they take the white qi of Venus into the lungs; in winter, they ingest the black qi of Mercury into the kidneys.

The Daoist Qie Yuanjie of Mount Song consumed the essence of the six *wu* days, which was highly effective. For example, during the ten-day period of *jiazi*, if the essence of *wuchen* is present, one faces the direction of *chen* and swallows qi for the entire ten days. When the next *jia* day arrives, one turns to the *wu* of that period.

The method of Gan Shi involves summoning the Jade Maidens of the Six *Jia* and Six *Ding*, each with their own names, and reciting incantations over water before drinking it—this can even render cattle and horses free from hunger. Alternatively, one may meditate on the spirit within the spleen, named Yellow Skirt Child, and by closing the mouth, ingest internal *qi*. All these methods yield genuine results. I have personally witnessed many who practiced grain avoidance for two or three years, their bodies light, complexions radiant, and resistant to wind, cold, heat, and dampness. Most were not stout.

Though I have not seen those who abstained from grain for decades, it is certain that ordinary people cannot survive beyond ten days without food. Yet those who have practiced this for years remain unharmed—how could one doubt its long-term efficacy? Those who attempt grain avoidance often fear they cannot endure, but when questioned, practitioners all report initial weakness followed by gradual strength, improving month by month and year by year. Thus, it is indeed sustainable.

Those who attain longevity and the Dao invariably do so through consuming elixirs and ingesting *qi*—this is no falsehood. However, those who take elixirs or abstain from grain typically experience extreme emaciation at first. Those who use talismanic water or solely ingest *qi* grow fatigued and thin within forty days, but thereafter regain vigor.

Master Zheng said, "By nature, I did not drink much wine. Once, while dwelling in Copper Mountain, I abstained from grain for over two years and could drink several *dou* of wine without intoxication. From this, one may infer that fasting enhances resistance to toxins, which in turn makes one less susceptible to illness." I then asked how he obtained wine in the mountains. Master Zheng replied, "First, brew fine cloud-liquid without pressing or straining. Then, mix it with powdered cinnamon, aconite, licorice, and five or six other ingredients, form them into pills, and dry them in the sun. Dropping one pill the size of a chicken’s egg into a *dou* of water instantly produces fine wine."

There is also the Yellow Emperor’s method for Cloud-Liquid Springs: combine sprouted rice with seven or eight medicinal ingredients, and add one *sheng* to a vessel of water—like the water infused with thousand-year-old vinegar—never depleting, its flavor always excellent and unchanging, greatly benefiting those who drink it.

Though talismanic water and grain avoidance may initially cause emaciation, they are advisable when combined with proper knowledge. In times of sudden famine, when there is no time to prepare elixirs, talismanic water is the superior choice.

There was a man named Feng who solely ingested *qi* and abstained from grain for three years. I observed him climbing mountains while carrying a load of over one *hu*, walking all day without fatigue. Occasionally, he drew his bow but spoke little, and when he did, he refused to raise his voice. When asked why, he said, "Abstaining from grain depletes essence and expends *qi*—speaking loudly is the greatest taboo."

I have also repeatedly seen shallow Daoists who, seeking to feign marvels and gain fame for fasting, in truth know nothing of the Way. They merely pretend to avoid porridge and rice while secretly consuming wine by the *dou* at midday, along with dried meats, preserved fruits, dates, chestnuts, and eggs—never ceasing to eat. Some devour large quantities of meat, swallowing the juices and spitting out the dregs, consuming dozens of *jin* in a day. This is nothing more than crafting fine cuisine.

Ordinary wine drinkers who consume dried meats and forgo grain can endure half a year or even a full year without distress—yet this cannot be called grain avoidance.

In Wu, the Daoist Shi Chun practiced *qi*-ingestion to treat illnesses. He would wait until the patient recovered—sometimes a hundred days, sometimes ten—before eating. When Emperor Jing of Wu heard of this, he remarked, "This cannot last; he will surely die of hunger." He summoned Chun, locked him up, and had him closely guarded. Chun requested only three or four *sheng* of water daily. Over a year passed, and his complexion grew even more radiant, his strength undiminished. When the emperor asked if he could endure for a long time, Chun replied, "There is no limit—I could go for decades, fearing only old age, not hunger." The emperor then released him.

Judging by Chun’s case, it is clear that grain avoidance cannot extend lifespan. Even today, some practice Chun’s method.

When asked about the method to resist cold, *Baopuzi* said: "On the day of the Beginning of Winter, consume the talismans of the Six *Bing* and Six *Ding*, or close the mouth and circulate the *qi* of the Five Fires one thousand two hundred times—this will prevent cold for the entire twelfth month. Alternatively, drink Solar Wine, take the Purple Quartz Rice-Lacquer Powder, or first consume one Male Pill followed by two Female Pills (in another version, first Female then Male)—these can also endure a day and night without cold."

The Female Pill uses orpiment, azurite, *bin*-stone, and magnetite.
The Male Pill uses realgar, cinnabar, and chalcanthite.

However, these offer no benefit to longevity.

When asked about the method to resist heat, *Baopuzi* said: "On the day of the Beginning of Summer, consume the talismans of the Six *Ren* and Six *Gui*, or circulate the *qi* of the Six *Gui*, or take the Black Water (or Ice) Pills, or the Flying Frost Powder. However, the latter requires the bark of trees from Mount Xiaoqiu and the blood of a black snake traveling north at noon on the fifth day of the fifth month—few have succeeded in preparing it. Only Fang Bozi and Wang Zhongdu could wear heavy furs in summer, stand in the midday sun surrounded by twelve braziers, and neither complain of heat nor sweat—undoubtedly, they used this method."

When asked about the method to repel weapons, *Baopuzi* said: "I heard that Emperor Wen of Wu once received essential teachings from Master Jie, who said that merely writing the characters for the Northern Dipper and the sun and moon would render one unafraid of blades. The emperor tested this on dozens of his attendants, who led charges and plunged into battle, yet none were harmed for life."

Master Zheng said, "Simply reciting the names of the Five Weapons also proves effective. The sword is named Great Chamber and governed by the Void Star; the halberd is named Crooked Tension and governed by the Root Star; the arrow is named Wandering and governed by Mars; the blade is named Great Wound and governed by the Horn Star; the crossbow is named Distant Gaze and governed by the Spread Star; the shield is named Great General and governed by the Three Stars."

Before battle, one should quietly recite these names. Alternatively, on the fifth day of the fifth month, create the Red Spirit Talisman and wear it over the heart; or on the day of *bingwu* at noon, make the Swallow Lord and Dragon-Tiger Double-Pouch Talismans, replacing the yearly talisman annually, the monthly talisman monthly, and the daily talisman daily. One may also wear the Talisman of the Messenger of Military Trust of the Queen Mother of the West, the Talisman of Mars and the Vermilion Bird, the Talisman of the Southern Culmen and Melting Metal, or the Talismans of Repelling Blades and Zhurong. Alternatively, apply the Jade Tablet Powder, bathe in forbidden onion broth, or use the male chaste tree to create the Six Yin Spirit Generals Talisman, pointing it toward the enemy. Or, during a lunar eclipse, carve the blood of a three-thousand-year-old toad (which has eight characters under its throat) onto the blade one wields. One may also carry the Martial Might Talisman, the Firefly Fire Pill, or when blades clash, tread on the *kui* and *fang* stars while invoking the Lords of the Four Directions—all of which have proven effective. Even today, some possess the method to repel weapons.

When asked about the method of vanishing, *Baopuzi* said: "The divine Way has five postures, but their number is lost to obscurity."

Yet these methods offer no benefit to the affairs of longevity and destiny. If practiced idly among men without cause, they invite only rumors of the uncanny and strange—such practices are not to be recklessly pursued. They may, however, be prepared for times of war and peril, employed in extremity to avert disaster.

Master Zheng said: "Wearing the Great *Lü* Talisman for ten days, one may vanish by turning left or appear by turning right." Alternatively, one may apply the Jade *Yan* Pill to the body, use the Snake’s Foot Powder, carry the Mother of Separation Grass, or break the Green Dragon Grass to subdue the Six *Ding* Spirits. One may enter a bamboo field and grasp the Great Pivot’s soil, or construct a Stone Chamber of the River Dragon and vanish beneath the cloud canopy’s shade. One may lie concealed in a clear, cold abyss to traverse the paths of the Dark Gate, ride the Heavenly One’s horse to wander the Purple Chamber, ascend the Heavenly One’s Bright Hall, or enter the Golden Coffer of the Jade Maiden. One may stand beneath the Three Canopies with the back toward the *Fu* star and face the *Guan* star, or cast off one’s headscarf and remove one’s shoes—[text missing]—boiling gall, wearing talismans, residing in guarded seclusion, using the clear liquid of cassia and platycodon, the Six *Jia* Parents’ adhesive, the *Pi Ce* glue, the Horse Mud Pill, the Wooden *Xu*’s essence, or the Mugwort of the Metal *Shang*. These may transform one into a child, an old man, a bird, a beast, grass, or a tree, or into the Six Domestic Animals. One may merge with wood to become wood, with stone to become stone, with water to become water, or with fire to become fire—this is what is meant by shifting form and altering appearance, though it does not allow for prolonged vanishing.

Someone asked: "Cao Cao of Wei once captured Zuo Yuanfang and bound him in shackles, yet he escaped naturally. By what method was this achieved?"

*Baopuzi* replied: "I cannot claim precise knowledge of what Master Zuo employed, but having perused various esoteric texts, I find methods such as taking adlay seeds thrice monthly with the Three-Five Yin Elixir, or using paired teeth and the Yang Embryo. Alternatively, one might leap and cast off restraints on the seventh day of the seventh month while facing east, or wear the single garment of the Dragon’s Child on a stone on the fifth day of the fifth month. One might don the robe pierced by lightning on the Summer Solstice, or use the twenty-one-character talisman of celestial patterns, or the blood of one’s father who has departed, or the Jade Child’s Remnant Grain. Combining the Mountain Lord’s Eye, the River Earl’s Remnant Grain, and the dregs of floating clouds to anoint oneself—all these may bring about self-liberation. Yet Master Zuo’s transformations were boundless and not necessarily confined to such methods. When employing the Six *Jia* to alter one’s true form, it cannot be grasped."

Someone asked: "Can one who practices the Dao avoid illness?"

*Baopuzi* said: "Those who fully embody the principles of nurturing life both ingest divine medicines and practice *qi*-circulation without cease, conducting daily *daoyin* to invigorate the constructive and protective energies, ensuring no blockages. They further employ the arts of the bedchamber with moderation, regulate their diet, avoid wind and dampness, and guard against what they cannot endure—thus they may avoid illness. Yet those who dwell among men often find their resolve divided, their practice inconsistent, and their diligence lacking, so they cannot escape minor ailments.

If one merely harbors faith in the Dao without engaging in practices that benefit the self, and if one’s destiny lies under the baleful stars of *gu* and *xu*, with the body vulnerable to harm, then the Three Corpses will exploit one’s weakness. On perilous days and inauspicious months, when one enters the realm of fatal illness, they summon malevolent *qi* and recklessly invite demons and specters to wreak havoc. When the Six Calamities converge and the Three Punishments align, disaster is assured. Those still in their prime may develop various illnesses, while those already afflicted will see their conditions worsen. Thus, the ancients who first pursued the Dao invariably studied medicine as well, to remedy immediate misfortunes.

Yet common Daoists, ignorant of this principle, rely on what they have heard—methods largely unrelated to healing. Unable to withdraw from the world and seclude themselves to focus solely on inner practices, they lack means to repel illness. When sickness strikes, they are even less capable than ordinary people who specialize in herbal remedies. They are like those who, attempting to advance, cannot master the steps of Handan, and in retreat, lose even the fundamentals of Shouling—utterly bereft.

I have seen the *Golden Coffer* and *Green Satchel* compiled by Dai Ba and Hua Tuo, the *Yellow Silk Prescriptions* of Cui Zhongshu, and the miscellaneous formulas of a hundred schools—some five hundred scrolls in all. Gan, Hu, Lü, Fu, Zhou Shi, Gan Tangtong, Ruan Nanhe, and others each compiled collections of emergency remedies for sudden afflictions, ranging from one hundred and ten to ninety-four, eighty-five, or forty-six entries. The world regards these as exhaustive and precise, beyond improvement.

Yet upon close examination, I find them woefully incomplete, lacking remedies for many urgent conditions. They are disorganized, jumbled, and without clear structure, making it difficult to locate and apply treatments swiftly. For acute and violent illnesses, they prescribe costly medicines—dozens of varieties—accessible only to the wealthy residing in the capital, who can afford to stockpile them. Moreover, they often recommend acupuncture, yet fail to clarify the precise locations and measurements, merely listing the names of points and channels. Without prior study of the *Mingtang*, *Liuzhu*, and diagrams of the body’s pathways, how can one comprehend them?

I have compiled a work in one hundred scrolls titled *The Jade Bureau Prescriptions*, organizing ailments by name in a coherent sequence, avoiding confusion. Ninety-three scrolls consist of straightforward, easily applied remedies, readily available even in remote villages. A glance suffices to find treatments for all urgent illnesses. With this book in one’s home, physicians become unnecessary. Most doctors inherit their profession as a family trade, possessing fame without true skill, cultivating empty reputations for profit. The poor and reclusive cannot afford their services, and those who do summon them often suffer from their errors. Far better to master the essentials oneself than to rely on ignorant physicians. Yet physicians are not always available, and even when found, they may refuse immediate aid. By the time they deign to act, the illness has penetrated the interstices, becoming a deep-seated malady in the vital organs—beyond cure. In cases of sudden, severe illness, traveling far to seek help often results in needless death."

Someone asked: "Can one foresee future fortune or misfortune, safety or peril, and thus make informed decisions in advance? Is this the mark of one who has attained the Dao?"

*Baopuzi* replied: "Observing celestial patterns above and earthly principles below, divining the winds and *qi*, casting milfoil stalks, calculating the Three *Qi*, pacing the Nine Palaces, consulting the Eight Trigrams, examining the positions of flying and hidden stars, diagnosing omens in phenomena, and interpreting fortune and misfortune through cracks in tortoise shells—these are all inferior arts, laborious and unreliable. To perceive all under heaven without stepping beyond one’s curtains—*that* is the work of one who has entered the divine.

Alternatively, one may summon the celestial texts of the Three Sovereigns to invoke the Lords of Life and Peril, the Five Marchmounts, the spirits of the paths and crossroads, and the Six *Ding* deities, causing them to appear and answer questions on all matters. Thus, fortune and misfortune become as clear as if held in the palm, and nothing, however distant or obscure, remains unknown.

Or one may summon the Six Yin Jade Maidens, a process requiring sixty days to complete. Once accomplished, they may be commanded at will. One may also summon the Eight Historians, who are the essences of the Eight Trigrams."

Or this is sufficient to foresee what has yet to take shape.

Alternatively, one may ingest *ge* flower, autumn *ma* pollen, or a *daogui* of powder the size of a square *cun* spoon. Suddenly, as if overcome by sleep, one hears voices speaking of matters one was unaware of—fortune and misfortune are instantly determined.

Or one may use a bright mirror of nine *cun* or larger to gaze upon oneself, focusing one’s thoughts for seven days and nights. On the seventh night, one will behold divine immortals—whether male or female, old or young. After a single revelation, the mind will inherently know matters from a thousand *li* away as they unfold.

The mirror may be used singly or in pairs, called the Sun and Moon Mirrors. If four are used, they are called the Four Compasses, each placed to the front, back, left, and right when gazing. With the Four Compasses, the spirits that appear are numerous—some soaring freely, others riding dragons or tigers, their crowns and robes resplendent in colors unseen in the mortal world. All have their own scriptures and illustrations. To cultivate this Dao, one must first silently recite the names, titles, and ranks of the spirits one seeks to summon, memorizing their attire and regalia. Without this, one may forget their identities upon sudden arrival, or become startled and harmed by them.

The practice demands stillness and seclusion, preferably in quiet groves or secluded forests, where external forms do not catch the eye and extraneous sounds do not reach the ear. Only then will the Dao surely come to fruition.

The Three Lads and Nine Maidens, the Longevity Lord with Nine Heads and a Serpent’s Body, and the One Hundred Twenty Officials—though they may appear, one must not gaze upon them too closely. If they question or rebuke you, do not respond. If attendants, radiant warriors, armored soldiers, or those riding dragons and tigers arrive with clamorous drums and pipes, do not lift your eyes or speak to them. Instead, concentrate solely on visualizing the true form of Lord Lao. Once the true form of Lord Lao appears, rise and bow twice.

The true form of Lord Lao is thus: his surname is Li, his given name Dan, and his courtesy name Boyang. He stands nine *chi* tall, with a yellow complexion, a prominent nose, and elegant brows five *cun* long. His ears measure seven *cun*, and his forehead bears three lines extending from top to bottom. His feet display the Eight Trigrams. He rests upon a bed of divine tortoise, with halls of gold and jade, steps of white silver, and robes of five-colored clouds. He wears a layered crown and wields a sword of sharp steel. One hundred twenty youths attend him: twelve Azure Dragons to his left, twenty-six White Tigers to his right, twenty-four Vermilion Birds before him, and seventy-two Dark Warriors behind. Twelve *Qiongqi* beasts precede him, and thirty-six *Pixie* beasts follow. Thunder and lightning blaze above, radiant and dazzling. This description comes from the immortal scriptures.

Upon beholding Lord Lao, one’s years and lifespan extend, and the mind becomes as bright as the sun and moon, knowing all things without effort.

Someone asked about the Dao of strengthening teeth. The Master Who Embraces Simplicity replied: "One who can nourish them with the Flowery Pond and steep them in sweet liquor, brushing them three hundred times each dawn, will ensure they never loosen. Alternatively, holding *dihuang* decoction in the mouth, or *xuandan* soup, snake fat pills, *yushi* pills, or the Nine Thorns Powder, will secure those already loose and cure those afflicted with worms. Taking the Spirit-Flight Powder can even regrow teeth that have fallen out."

Someone asked about the Dao of sharpening hearing. The Master Who Embraces Simplicity replied: "One who can practice Dragon Leading, Tiger Guiding, Bear Stretching, Tortoise Swallowing, Swallow Flying, Serpent Bending, and Bird Extending—bowing to heaven and earth—ensuring the red and yellow light never leaves the inner chamber, and performing these twelve hundred times, will preserve hearing without loss. For those already deaf, fumigating with *xuanxun*, or plugging the ears with a pouch of thorn tips, sheep dung, cassia bark, and sparrow cinnamon, or using *langdu* and *zhige*, or inserting a mixture of aconite and scallion sap into the ears, or irrigating them with steamed carp brain, will restore hearing."

Someone asked about the Dao of brightening the eyes. The Master Who Embraces Simplicity replied: "One who can draw the ascending *jing* of the Triple Burner (or the outer *jing*), summon the Great Fire in the south, wash the eyes with bright water, and warm them with sunlight, or burn the *Bing-Ding* Penetrating Vision Talisman and wash the eyes with wine, as the ancients did to read at night—such methods will suffice. Or one may boil *wujingzi* in vinegar until soft, dry them in the sun, grind them into powder, and take a square *cun* spoonful thrice daily. Consuming a whole *dou* enables night vision. Or one may use dog gallbladder to decoct *qingyang*, *banjiu*, *shijueming*, and *chongwei*, or the Hundred Flowers Powder, or steep *jishe* fragrance and *huanglian* in milk and apply it as drops. All eye ailments will be cured, and vision will become twice as sharp as before."

Someone asked about the Dao of ascending steep heights, traversing perilous paths, and journeying without exhaustion. The Master Who Embraces Simplicity replied: "Only by ingesting the great elixir can one achieve a light body, mighty strength, and endure labor without fatigue. For those newly entering mountains and forests whose bodies are not yet fully robust, it is fitting to use Cloud Pearl Powder, Hundred Flowers Elixir, *xuanzi* soup to wash the feet, Tiger Gallbladder Pills, Vermilion Bright Wine, Heavenly Male Crane Fat Pills, Flying Knife Decoction, Autumn *Mang*, *Cheqian*, and *Zefang* Powder. After ten days of use, not only will one traverse great distances without exhaustion, but one’s pace will become three times faster than usual.

If one can master the art of *Qiao*-riding, one may roam the world unbound by mountains and rivers. There are three methods of *Qiao*-riding: Dragon *Qiao*, Tiger *Qiao*, and Deer-Reel *Qiao*. Alternatively, one may ingest talismans and concentrate the mind. To travel a thousand *li*, focus for one hour; to travel twelve thousand *li* in a day and night, focus for all twelve hours. Beyond this, one must renew the focus as before. Or one may craft a flying chariot from jujube wood, binding it with oxhide straps and drawing its mechanism with reversed swords. Or one may visualize five serpents, six dragons, and three oxen intersecting at the *gang* position and ride upon them, ascending forty *li* to reach the Great Clarity. Within the Great Clarity, the *qi* is so pure it can sustain a person.

The master says: when a kite soars higher, it merely stretches its wings straight without flapping, yet advances effortlessly—this is because it gradually rides the pure *qi*. When a dragon first ascends through the clouds, it rises forty *li* before moving on its own. These words come from immortals and have been passed down among the common folk, yet they are truly beyond the ken of ordinary people."

Moreover, to ride the *qiao*, one must first observe a prolonged fast, abstaining from meat and blood offerings for a full year before attempting to mount these three *qiao*. Even if one ingests talismans and visualizes the five dragons, the *qiao* of greatest distance, the others will not exceed a thousand *li*. Their ascent, descent, departure, and pause all follow their own methods—one must not act arbitrarily. Should one violate these prohibitions, recklessly riding the *qiao* risks the peril of a fall.

Some ask: The *Laozi* chapters, the *Tortoise-Shell Scriptures*, and other texts all record that in the years of Metal and Wood following great warfare, plagues will surely arise, sparing but one in ten thousand. May I inquire about the Way to evade pestilence? The *Baopuzi* replies: The immortals’ secret method for entering plague-stricken regions involves visualizing oneself as five jades—these jades correspond to the colors of the four seasons: green in spring, red in summer, yellow in the fourth month (or sixth month), white in autumn, and black in winter. One must also visualize wearing a golden headband and one’s heart as a blazing flame, large as a *dou*, so that nothing may inspire fear.

Another method: visualize one’s hair spreading to cover the body, with a great star fixed at the end of each strand. Further, visualize the Seven Stars of the Northern Dipper, with the *Kui* inverted over one’s head and the *gang* pointing forward. Then visualize the *qi* of the five viscera issuing from the eyes and enveloping the body like mist—liver *qi* green, lung *qi* white, spleen *qi* yellow, kidney *qi* black, and heart *qi* red—so that the five colors intermingle. Thus, one may share a bed with the plague-stricken.

Alternatively, one may perform the *Yu* step and invoke the Jade Maiden of the Straight Day, or hold the breath while visualizing a force of a hundred and twenty warriors, each wielding a thousand-*jin* hammer, to guard oneself. Other efficacious methods include the *Shegong* pellet, the Red Chariot Envoy pellet, the Marshal pellet, the *Xuchangqing* powder (a medicinal herb), the Jade Casket Essence powder, the Green Ox Daoist’s Fumigation pellet, the *Cui* (or *Que*) Script Yellow (or *Xing*) powder, the Herb Jade Wine, the *Huangting* pellet, the Imperial Talisman, the *Laozi* Neck Talisman, the Red *Hu* Talisman, and the Peach Blossom Talisman.

**Yellow and White (Chapter Sixteen)**
The *Baopuzi* states: The *Divine Immortals’ Scriptures* contain twenty-five scrolls and over a thousand methods for the *Yellow and White*. The *Yellow* refers to gold...

White refers to silver. The ancients guarded this Dao in secrecy and were reluctant to name it directly, hence its euphemistic designation. Some texts title the scrolls *Gengxin*, for *Geng* and *Xin* also pertain to metal. Yet most methods remain deeply concealed, difficult to fathom, and few are clearly explicable. The world often doubts this art as fantastical and false, no different from those who disbelieve in divine immortals.

In my youth, I studied the Nine Elixirs and the *Scripture of Gold and Silver Liquids* under Master Zheng, and subsequently received five scrolls of the *Central Scripture of the Yellow and White*. Master Zheng recounted that he and Master Zuo had once tested these methods together in the mountains of Lujiang, and all succeeded. Yet the rigor of purification, taboos, and hardships were no less than those required for the golden elixir and divine immortals’ medicines.

Vulgar folk often mock my pursuit of arcane arts, accusing me of striving to connect what cannot be connected in the world. Why would I do so? If my aim were merely to dazzle future generations with marvels and ornate prose, my *Outer Chapters* and miscellaneous writings—over two hundred scrolls—would suffice to convey my intent. I would have no need for this. Moreover, these *Inner Chapters* are plain speech, devoid of embellishment.

I am also aware that discussing such matters, most people dismiss them as impractical and irrelevant, far less compelling than debating mundane principles that align with common sentiment. Yet I cannot desist from these pursuits. Though I know they will not find favor in the world’s eyes, I persist in writing of them because I have witnessed their efficacy, and the masters from whom I received transmission did not speak falsely.

I am poor and lack resources, beset by misfortune and relentless hardship. Even the roads are perilous, and medicinal ingredients are unattainable, leaving me no opportunity to practice these arts. Now, when I tell others I know how to transmute gold and silver, yet suffer hunger and cold myself, it is no different from one who cannot walk selling remedies for lameness—how could anyone believe me? Yet one cannot dismiss all possibilities on the basis of a single setback. My diligent recording of these methods in writing is for the sake of future scholars who cherish the strange and prize truth, that they may see my work and grasp the full intent of the Dao.

The arts of transformation know no bounds. The human body is visible, yet there are methods to conceal it; spirits are hidden, yet there are ways to reveal them. Those who master such arts are many. Water and fire reside in the heavens, yet we draw them forth with burning mirrors. Lead is white by nature, yet it is reddened to make cinnabar; cinnabar is red by nature, yet it is whitened to make lead. Clouds, rain, frost, and snow are all qi of Heaven and Earth, yet they can be replicated with medicines, indistinguishable from the real. Even among creatures that fly and crawl, whose forms are fixed by creation, sudden transformations abound—countless kinds, beyond enumeration.

Humans, the most spiritually endowed of beings, may alter their forms: men and women become cranes, stones, tigers, apes, grains of sand, or soft-shelled turtles—such cases are not rare. Mountains become abysses, and deep valleys rise into ridges—these, too, are transformations of great things. Transformation is the natural way of Heaven and Earth; why should gold and silver alone be exempt from being wrought from other substances?

Consider the fire obtained from a yang-burning mirror or the water drawn from a fang-zhu vessel—are they different from ordinary fire and water? When a snake becomes a dragon, or when thatch is refined into ointment, they are no different from those that arise naturally. Yet the origins and causes of such transformations stem from natural resonance, and only those who exhaust principle and fulfill their nature can comprehend their guiding principles. Only those who trace beginnings to ends can grasp their true conditions.

Those of narrow vision and shallow understanding, confined by their own limitations, dismiss the profound and unfathomable as unfathomable, and relegate divine transformations to the realm of the absurd. Because the Zhou and Confucius did not speak of such things, or because the ancient texts do not record them, they dismiss them entirely—is this not ignorance?

Moreover, when common folk see that Liu Xiang failed to transmute gold, they conclude that such a Dao does not exist in the world. This is like a farmer who, after a flood or drought ruins his harvest, declares that the five grains cannot be cultivated. Wu Dawen, the Prefect of Chengdu, was a learned and erudite man, yet he, too, dismissed most extraordinary matters as implausible. Later, a Daoist taught him the methods of the Yellow and White. Wu tested them: melting lead in an iron vessel and adding a powdered medicine transformed it into silver; further refining it with another medicine turned it into gold.

After mastering these methods, Wu studied the arts of thorough vision. Within a hundred days, as he lay abed at night, he could see the celestial patterns and his four neighbors as clearly as if there were no walls or fences. His concubine, Yaohua, who had died, appeared to him and spoke as in life. When he sacrificed at the temple, he heard the temple deity respond to his bow, and the altar seemed to move with a sound. Wu then sighed, saying, “Truly, there is nothing that does not exist in the world. Though the Five Classics do not record such things, one cannot dismiss them out of hand.”

Yet those unversed in such arts, upon hearing of them, how could they not be astonished and incredulous?

There is also the case of Huan Junshan, who recounted that Cheng Wei, a Yellow Gate Gentleman of the Han, was fond of the arts of the Yellow and White. He married the daughter of a master of these methods. Once, while accompanying the imperial procession, Cheng found himself without proper attire and grew anxious. His wife said, “Let me procure two bolts of silk.” Immediately, the silk appeared before them. Cheng consulted the *Hongbao* in his pillow and attempted to transmute gold, but failed. His wife came to observe and found him fanning the coals, heating a crucible filled with mercury. She said, “Let me show you something.” She took a small amount of medicine from her pouch and added it to the crucible. After the time it takes to eat a meal, she opened it, and the mercury had turned to silver.

Cheng was astonished and asked, “This Dao was near at hand all along, yet why did you not tell me sooner?” His wife replied, “One must be destined to receive it.” From then on, Cheng entreated her day and night, selling fields and houses to provide fine food and clothing, yet she still refused to teach him. Cheng then conspired with a companion to beat and torture her, but she only told him, “The Dao must be transmitted to the right person. If you encounter such a person on the road, you may teach them. But if their mouth speaks one thing while their heart holds another, even if you cut them to pieces, the Dao will not emerge.”

Cheng persisted in pressuring her, and she eventually went mad, stripping naked and running about, smearing herself with mud, until she died.

Recently, Hua Lingsi, the Prefect of Lijiang, was a man of great talent, learning, and broad knowledge, yet he, too, dismissed most extraordinary matters as implausible. Later, a Daoist taught him the methods of the Yellow and White. Hua tested them: melting lead in an iron vessel and adding a powdered medicine transformed it into silver; further refining it with another medicine turned it into gold. After learning from this Daoist, Hua mastered the arts of thorough vision. Within a hundred days, as he lay abed at night, he could see the celestial patterns and his four neighbors as clearly as if there were no walls or fences. His concubine, Yaohua, who had died, appeared to him and spoke as in life. When he sacrificed at the temple, he heard the temple deity respond to his bow, and the altar seemed to move with a sound. Hua then sighed, saying, “Truly, there is nothing that does not exist in the world. Though the Five Classics do not record such things, one cannot dismiss them out of hand.”

Moreover, the arts of the Yellow and White, like the compounding of divine elixirs, both require a hundred days or more of fasting and purification. One must also find a secluded place, comprehend the meaning of the alchemical texts, and only then may proceed—these arts are not for those of impure conduct, nor for the dull-witted or those who seldom engage with the numerological arts. Some methods may require oral instructions, which should all be transmitted by a master. It is also fitting to enter deep mountains, to a place of purity, and not let vulgar fools know of it. Yet Liu Xiang practiced these arts within the palace, employing palace women to attend to the work—surely they were not fasting and purifying themselves, nor could they sever all worldly affairs to prevent comings and goings. How could such a practice ever succeed?

The *New Discourses* of Huan Tan states: Shi Zixin, seeing that he had been appointed as an official in the Chancellor’s office, ordered the construction of a workshop, mobilizing clerks, officers, and even palace slaves and maids to assist him in making gold. When it failed, the Chancellor himself admitted his efforts were insufficient and reported to Empress Dowager Fu that gold was no longer profitable. Yet when he heard that gold, once refined, could be used to make longevity elixirs, he eagerly desired it. He then reassigned Shi Zixin to the Northern Palace of the Weiyang Palace, where attendants were assigned to serve him. How could such divine methods be practiced within the palace, allowing common people to mingle and work together? Even dyeing and bleaching silk in the secular world is not to be witnessed by outsiders—if seen, the process is ruined—how much more so for the transformations of the Yellow and White?

In all matters, great or small, one must grasp the essentials. Without proper method, even brewing wine, making soy sauce, vinegar, or stews may fail—how much more so for greater undertakings? I once consulted Master Zheng, saying: “The Old Master said, ‘Do not value rare and precious goods.’ In the age of perfect governance, gold is cast into the mountains and jade discarded in the valleys. I wonder why the ancients prized gold and silver yet left behind their methods.”

Master Zheng replied: “What the Old Master spoke of refers to those who sift through sand, split rocks, drain mountains, and dredge abysses, traveling thousands of miles without fear of being crushed or drowned, seeking rare treasures to hinder the people’s seasons, never knowing contentment, adorning the useless, and even those who aspire to the Dao and seek long life—such men are no different from merchants. They lack integrity, plunge into peril, chase profits without regard for life or limb, and do not cultivate frugal desires. As for the true person who makes gold, it is for the purpose of ingesting it to attain immortality, not to become wealthy. Thus, the scriptures say: ‘Gold can be made, and the world can be transcended.’ Silver may also be ingested, though it is not as effective as gold.”

I objected: “Why not ingest the gold and silver of the world instead of transmuting them? If one makes them, are they not false? And if false, can they be deceptive?”

Master Zheng replied: “The gold and silver of the world are indeed good, but Daoists are generally poor. As the saying goes, ‘There are no fat immortals or wealthy Daoists.’ If a master has ten or five disciples, how can they obtain enough gold and silver to supply them? And they cannot travel far to gather them, so it is fitting to make them. Moreover, the gold transmuted from medicines is the essence of all ingredients, superior to the natural kind. The *Immortals’ Scripture* says, ‘The essence of cinnabar generates gold’—this is the method of making gold from elixirs. Thus, in the mountains where cinnabar is found, gold often lies beneath. When gold is successfully made, it is a true substance, its interior and exterior indistinguishable, unchanging even after a hundred refinements. Hence, the method states it can be used to make nails, demonstrating its hardness and strength. This is the attainment of the natural Dao. If one can achieve this, how can it be called deception?

Deception refers to coating iron with malachite to turn it red like copper, or transforming silver with egg white to make it yellow like gold—these are changes only on the surface, not within. The *Immortals’ Scripture* records methods for cultivating lingzhi mushrooms with the Five Stones and Five Woods, and the mushrooms thus grown are no different from the natural ones, both granting long life. This is akin to making gold. The pheasant transforms into a clam, the sparrow into a mussel—these are the same as their natural counterparts. Thus, the scripture says: ‘The flowing pearl, after nine transformations, the father does not tell the son.’ It also says: ‘Cinnabar made into gold and ingested ascends to immortality—this is the way of the superior adept. Eating lingzhi, practicing breath control, and swallowing qi grants long life—this is the way of the middling adept. Consuming herbs and plants to live a thousand years—this is the way of the lesser adept.’

It further states: ‘Gold and silver can be made with their natural properties. Long life can be learned and attained.’ The *Jade Tablet Record* says: ‘All under heaven may attain long life—it is only hesitation that prevents success.’ Mercury congealed into gold can be used to make nails.”

The *Bronze Pillar Scripture* states: “Cinnabar can be transmuted into gold; the ‘river chariot’ can be made into silver. Once established, it can be accomplished—once accomplished, it becomes the true elixir. Mastering this Way, one may attain an immortal body.” Huangshanzi said: “Heaven and earth possess gold, and I can make it. Two yellows and one red—once established, there is no doubt.” The *Tortoise-Shell Script* declares: “My life is in my hands, not in Heaven’s. When the returning elixir is perfected and gold is made, it endures for ten thousand myriad years. How could the ancients have deceived me?”

Yet the trouble lies in this: those who know this Way are often poor, and the medicines may be of low value but grow in distant lands, unattainable in times of chaos. Take, for instance, salt from the Qiang tribes and bittern from the marshes—both are worthless things, not even a coin in times of peace. Now, though their price is unrestricted, none can be bought. As for Qiangli’s chalcanthite, though one might offer ten thousand coins for a single catty, it cannot be obtained. Thus, those who merely know the method are no different from those who do not—how lamentable!

Those who possess the method may suffer hunger and cold, lacking the means to compound it, while the wealthy and noble remain ignorant of the method. Even if they knew it, none would believe it. Suppose some did believe—being already rich in gold and silver, who would willingly spend their visible wealth to purchase the medicinal substances, fearing the regret of casting aside what is secure to chase what flies away? Thus, none are willing to do so. Moreover, calculating the cost of the medicines against the value of what is obtained reveals even greater profit, yet the additional requirements of fasting, purification, and hardship deter them from attempting it.

Furthermore, without the oral instructions of a renowned master, one must not lightly undertake this practice. Even in medicine, the ingredients are quite ordinary, yet their commonly used and effective formulas are kept secret. Consider the following: glue from the secluded women of the rear palace, sealed with the lord’s clay; cinnabar pills; *muhuzi*; golden *shangzhi*; flying *jun* root; hidden dragon liver; white horse sweat; floating cloud dregs; dragon’s offspring; cinnabar-clad night-shining bones; hundred-flower nectar; winter *zou* purification—all are nearby substances. Yet without oral instructions, they remain incomprehensible. How much more so, then, for the arts of the yellow and the white?

Those capable of practicing these arts today do not merely keep them secret because of their high value.

This Dao, once accomplished, enables one to attain longevity—longevity being the ultimate of the Way. Thus did the ancients hold it in such esteem. Now, among the substances named in alchemical formulas, some may share names with common drugs yet are not the same. For instance, the *Heavenly Maid of the River* is not a woman; *Master Bright of Mount Lingyang* is not a man; *Yu’s Remnant Grain* is not rice; *Yao’s Brew* is not water. Yet when ordinary people see formulas calling for *dragon’s gall*, *tiger’s paw*, *chicken’s head*, *duck’s foot*, *horse’s hoof*, *dog’s blood*, *rat’s tail*, or *ox’s knee*, they assume these are merely substances of blood and qi. When they see *broken cups*, *upturned basins*, *cauldrons*, *dikes*, *great dikes*, *arrow shafts*, or *heavenly hooks*, they take them for vessels of iron or clay.
