When they see *Hu the Royal Envoy*, *Yigu the Spinster*, *Wild Man of Culture*, *Guardian of the Fields*, *Dai Wen*, *Yu the Bathing One*, or *Xu Changqing*, they suppose these are merely human names. Given such superficial understanding, how could they grasp the profound and secret formulas? No wonder Liu Xiang failed in his attempt to transmute gold.

Yet once one grasps the essentials, the practice does not require the talents of sages or great worthies—any person may undertake it. Was Liu Xiang an ordinary man? His failure lay solely in not receiving the oral instructions. Now, I shall record its essentials and demonstrate its efficacy to bequeath them to like-minded seekers of future generations.

First, obtain *realgar* from Wudu, its cinnabar hue bright as a rooster’s comb, lustrous and free of impurities. The quantity may be chosen at will, but it must not be less than five *jin*.

Grind the realgar to a fine powder and mix it with ox bile, then boil until dry. Take a red clay crucible with a capacity of one *dou*, first line its interior with a three-*fen* layer of *rong* salt and chalcanthite powder. Then add a five-*fen* layer of realgar powder, followed by another layer of *rong* salt, repeating this pattern until the crucible is filled. Add crushed charcoal the size of jujube pits to a depth of two *cun*. Seal the crucible’s exterior with a mixture of earthworm and mole cricket soil and *rong* salt, forming a three-*cun*-thick layer to prevent leakage. Cover with another crucible, similarly sealed, and allow to air-dry for one month. Then, using horse dung as fuel, gently heat for three days and nights. Upon cooling and opening, strike the crucible to release the copper, which will flow like molten bronze or iron. Cast this copper into tubes, then fill them with cinnabar water. Again, heat with horse dung for thirty days, open the furnace, and strike to obtain the refined metal. Use this metal to fashion another tube, fill it with cinnabar water, and heat with horse dung for another thirty days. Upon opening, grind the contents, mixing two parts of the refined metal with one part raw cinnabar and powdered mercury. The mixture will immediately congeal into lustrous yellow gold, hard enough to withstand hammering.

**Method for Preparing Cinnabar Water:**
Take one *jin* of cinnabar and place it in a fresh bamboo tube. Add two *liang* each of chalcanthite and nitre. Seal the tube’s openings with lacquer and bone glue, ensuring it is thoroughly dry before immersing it in strong vinegar buried three *chi* deep in the earth. After thirty days, it will transform into a red, bitter-tasting liquid.

**Master Jinlou’s Method for Transmuting Gold (Learned from Qinglinzi):**
Fashion tin into squares six *cun* wide and one *cun* two *fen* thick. Mix red salt with lye to form a paste, coating the tin to a uniform thickness of one *fen*. Layer the coated tin in a red clay crucible, using four *jin* of red salt for every ten *jin* of tin. Seal the crucible tightly and heat with horse dung for thirty days. Upon opening, the tin will resemble ash, with bean-sized golden granules within. Place these in an earthen jar, refine with charcoal ten times, and they will fully transmute into gold. From ten *jin* of tin, twenty *liang* of gold may be obtained. Only crucibles from Changsha, Guiyang, Yuzhang, and Nanhai are suitable, as the locals frequently use them for cooking.

**Method for Preparing Red Salt:**
Take one *jin* each of cold salt, cold water stone, cold feather stone, and white *bin*, and place them in an iron vessel. Heat with charcoal until melted and turned red, then remove for use.

**Master Jiaoli’s Method for Transmuting Gold (Learned from Jiqiuzi):**
Place two parts *bin* water stone in an iron vessel and heat with charcoal until boiling. Add mercury in any quantity, stirring until well combined. After six or seven boils, pour onto the ground to form white silver. Then, in a crucible, combine one part each of cinnabar water and azurite water with two parts realgar water. Heat over a crucible fire, stirring until well mixed, then add more charcoal and boil. Introduce the white silver into this mixture, again in any quantity, and boil six or seven times. Pour onto the ground, and upon solidifying, it will become high-quality purple-gold.

**Method for Preparing Realgar Water:**
Place one *jin* of realgar in a fresh bamboo tube, add two *liang* of nitre, seal as before, and bury in strong vinegar three *chi* deep for twenty days. It will dissolve into a liquid. The methods for preparing azurite water and *bin* stone water are identical, though each must be stored in separate tubes.

**Method for Transmuting Gold (Attributed to a Child):**
Fashion a large iron tube, one *chi* two *cun* in diameter and height, and a smaller iron tube, six *cun* in diameter, polished smooth. Grind together one *jin* each of red halloysite, nitre, mica, ochre, and calcite, along with half a *jin* of sulfur and four *liang* of malachite. Sift the mixture finely and knead with vinegar to coat the interior of the small tube to a thickness of two *cun*. Add one *jin* of mercury, half a *jin* of cinnabar, and half a *jin* of *liangfei*.

**Method for Preparing *Liangfei*:**
Take ten *jin* of lead and melt in an iron basin over a furnace. Add three *liang* of mercury, stirring until fully amalgamated. Scoop out the early-formed amalgam with an iron ladle—this is *liangfei*.

Place the *liangfei* in the small tube, cover with mica, and seal with an iron lid. Position the tube inside the furnace, then melt lead and pour it into the large tube until it submerges the small tube, leaving half a *cun* of the small tube exposed. Heat vigorously for three days and nights to produce *zifen*. Melt ten *jin* of lead in an iron vessel for twenty days, then transfer to a copper vessel. Once the lead melts, add the *zifen* in portions the size of a square *cun* spoon, stirring to transmute it into gold. To produce silver, place mercury in an iron vessel, add *zifen* to a depth of three *cun*, heat until combined, and pour into water to form silver.

**Master Wuchengzi’s Method:**
Fashion an iron tube nine *cun* long and five *cun* in diameter. Grind three *jin* of realgar and mix with equal parts earthworm, mole cricket, and *rang* soil to form a paste. Coat the interior of the tube to a diameter of three *cun*, leaving the opening four *cun* wide. Add two *ge* of cinnabar water, then heat over horse dung until thoroughly dry. Place the tube inside a copper cylinder, seal with a copper lid, and reinforce with yellow sand. Cover with a layer of *rang* soil, ensuring no leaks. Position the assembly in a furnace surrounded by three *cun* of charcoal, heating until the tube’s opening glows red. Upon cooling, the realgar will have entered the copper cylinder. Repeat the process until all three *jin* of realgar’s essence collects in the cylinder. Mix this with an equal part of yellow sand and construct a furnace of any size. To use, place the furnace in a charcoal fire until red-hot, then add mercury. When the mercury becomes active, introduce lead, and golden streams will rise from the sides to converge in the center. Pour onto the ground to form gold. Each furnace can produce one thousand five hundred *jin* of gold before its power is exhausted.

This gold, when steeped in wine made from *Vitex negundo* and red millet for one hundred days, becomes malleable. Consuming a pill the size of a small bean three times daily, one *jin* in total, will dispel the three corpses, cure all diseases, restore sight to the blind, hearing to the deaf, and return the elderly to the vitality of a thirty-year-old. It renders one impervious to fire, repels all evil and poisons, and protects against cold winds, heat, and dampness. Consuming three *jin* enables walking on water, traversing mountains, and summoning spirits for protection. Lifespan becomes boundless as heaven and earth. Rubbing a pill with *Ailanthus* blood and *zhucao* juice restores vision to see demons and subterranean objects, and allows writing in darkness. Coating a pill with white sheep’s blood and casting it into water will immediately summon fish and dragons for capture.

Coating one pill with black sheep’s blood and another with cinnabar-red rooster’s blood, then hanging them above the city gate, will prevent epidemics for a *li* around. Applying it to the foreheads of cattle, sheep, and all livestock will shield them from plagues and diseases, and tigers and leopards will not dare approach. Coating a pill with tiger’s gall and snake’s fat, then casting it toward the enemy’s army from the direction of the monthly *jian* earth, will cause the troops to descend into chaos, slaying one another without cause before fleeing in disarray.

Coating a pill with ox’s blood and casting it into a well will make the well boil; casting it into flowing water will cause the current to reverse for a hundred paces. Coating a pill with white dog’s blood and placing it in a site of sixfold *yin* before casting it into a shrine or temple will summon the demonic spirits, who may then be commanded. Coating a pill with rabbit’s blood and placing it in a site of sixfold *yin* will cause the Jade Maidens of the traveling kitchen to arrive instantly, sufficient to serve sixty or seventy people.

Coating a pill with carp’s gall and carrying it into water will part the waters to a depth of a *zhang*, allowing one to breathe and move beneath the surface. Wearing it will keep one’s clothes dry even in pouring rain. Boiling a pill with purple amaranth and swallowing its juice will stave off hunger for a hundred days. Boiling a pill with lodestone and placing it in one’s topknot will render one immune to blades and arrows in battle; if shot at, the arrows will turn away of their own accord.

Mixing a pill with earth from the days of the Six *Ding* and Six *Ren* and applying it to a person’s philtrum will render them invisible. Holding a pill and facing north to exhale will extinguish fire. On *geng* or *xin* days, at the hours of *shen* or *you*, facing west and casting a pill toward the earth will cause trees to wither that very day. Casting a pill while performing the *Yu* step will instantly kill tigers, wolves, vipers, and venomous snakes. Inscribing stone with a pill will cause the writing to penetrate the stone; inscribing metal will cause it to penetrate the metal; inscribing wood will cause it to penetrate the wood, sinking into the grain so deeply that no amount of scraping can remove it.

For those who have died suddenly but not yet spent a night in death, administering a pill with water from the direction of the monthly *jian* earth into their throat, then spraying water onto their face, will restore them to life. Coating a pill with fox’s blood or crane’s blood and placing it beneath one’s fingernail will allow one to point at anything and transform it at will—mountains will shift, trees will uproot, and people will witness these changes, though in truth nothing has moved.

In all endeavors of *huangbai* alchemy, one must first invoke the Supreme Unity, the Primordial Maiden, and Laozi, performing the same offerings and rituals as in the method for refining the Nine Elixirs. Incense of five fragrances must burn without cease. Moreover, once the gold is completed, three *jin* must first be cast into deep water, and ten *jin* into the marketplace, before one may freely use the remainder as one wishes.

**The Inner Chapters of the Master Embracing Simplicity, Scroll Four**
*By Ge Hong of the Jin Dynasty*
**Chapter Seventeen: On Ascending Mountains and Crossing Rivers**

Someone asked about the Dao of ascending mountains. The Master Embracing Simplicity said: All who seek the Dao, compound medicines, or take refuge in the mountains to avoid chaos must enter the mountains. Yet those who do not know the proper method of entering often encounter misfortune. Hence the saying: "Beneath Mount Taihua, white bones lie scattered like kindling." This refers to those who know but one thing and cannot be broadly prepared. Though they harbor the will to live, they instead hasten their deaths.

Mountains, great or small, all possess spirits. The larger the mountain, the greater its spirit; the smaller, the lesser. Those who enter without technique will surely suffer harm—afflicted by illness, pierced by thorns, or stricken with terror and unease. Some see lights and shadows, hear strange sounds, or witness great trees topple without wind, rocks fall without cause, striking and killing people. Others are driven mad, wandering in confusion until they plunge into ravines, or are beset by tigers, wolves, and venomous creatures. One must not enter the mountains lightly.

One should enter in the third or ninth month, for these are the months when mountains "open." One must also choose an auspicious day and hour within these months. If matters are urgent and one cannot wait, one may simply select an auspicious day and hour.

All who enter the mountains must first purify themselves for seven days, avoiding defilement. They should carry the *Ascending Mountain Talisman* and, upon departing, perform the *Threefold and Fivefold Circumambulation Rite* around the body. Moreover, the Five Peaks have years of calamity, just as the Nine Provinces experience cycles of prosperity and decline. When a region receives the *Flying Talisman* of baleful *qi*, its rulers and officials should not act—this is also true for dwellings and mountains.

One must also strictly avoid entering the Eastern Peak in the first or second month of a *jia-yi* or *yin-mao* year; the Southern Peak in the fourth or fifth month of a *bing-ding* or *si-wu* year; the Western Peak in the seventh or eighth month of a *geng-xin* or *shen-you* year; the Central Peak in the fourth-season months of a *wu-ji* year; and the Northern Peak in the tenth or eleventh month of a *ren-gui* or *hai-zi* year. One must not enter Mount Taihua, Mount Huo, Mount Heng, Mount Tai, or Mount Songgao in these years, for the same prohibitions apply to all directions of these peaks.

All aged creatures possess essences that can assume human form to delude the eyes and test people, yet they cannot alter their true form in a mirror. Thus, ancient Daoist masters entering the mountains always carried a bright mirror, nine *cun* or larger, suspended behind their backs. Then, no old specters would dare approach. If something comes to test a person, one should glance at the mirror: if it is an immortal or a benevolent mountain spirit, its reflection will appear human; if it is a bird, beast, or malevolent specter, its true form will be revealed.

Moreover, when old specters depart, they always retreat backward. One may turn the mirror to face them and observe their reflection: if they lack heels, they are old specters; if they have heels, they are mountain spirits.

Formerly, Zhang Gai (or *He*) and Gao Ou (or *Hao*) were both cultivating their thoughts in a stone chamber on Mount Yuntai when suddenly a man in a yellow linen robe and hemp headscarf approached and said, "Are you weary, Daoists, from your toils in these secluded mountains?" The two glanced at their mirror and saw it was a deer. They asked, "You are an old deer of these mountains—how dare you feign human form?" Before the words were finished, the visitor turned into a deer and fled.

At an inn beneath Mount Linlü, there was a demon that often caused travelers to die or fall ill. Nightly, dozens of figures in yellow, white, or black robes—some male, some female—would appear. Later, a man named Xi Bo (or *Zhi*) encountered them while lodging there. He sat with bright lamps burning and recited scriptures. At midnight, over ten figures came and sat opposite him, playing *chupu* and gambling. Xi secretly shone his mirror upon them and saw they were a pack of dogs. He stood, holding a candle, and pretended to accidentally singe their robes with the flame, filling the air with the stench of scorched fur. Grasping a small knife, he seized one and stabbed it. It first cried out in a human voice, then died as a dog. The rest fled, and the hauntings ceased—all thanks to the power of the mirror.

Superior adepts entering the mountains carry the *Inner Writings of the Three Sovereigns* and the *True Forms of the Five Peaks*, summoning the mountain spirits and consulting the *Demon Registers* of stone, earth, and mountain officials. Then, the spirits of trees and stones, the essences of mountains and rivers, dare not test them. Lesser adepts establish the *Seventy-Two Talismans of the Spirit Guardians* to control the hundred evils, or use the *Seal of the Vermilion Official* and the *Twelve Seals of Prime Origin* to seal the four directions of their dwelling—then no evil will dare approach. Still lesser adepts carry the *Eightfold Numinous Staff* and wear the *Jade Talisman of Laozi*, by which they may command the mountain spirits—how could they dare bring harm?

I have heard my teacher, Master Zheng, speak thus, though I cannot fully grasp these matters. My master often told his disciples: "Those who seek the Dao as if grieving over poverty or lamenting a lowly station—how could they not attain it? The only danger is a lack of steadfast resolve, pursuing the near and forgetting the far. At first, they are delighted, leaning forward eagerly, but soon they forget as if it were nothing. The benefits of a hair’s breadth are not secured, while the losses of a mountain’s weight never cease—how then can they exhaust the subtleties of the ultimate words or achieve the loftiest peaks of the Dao?"

The Master Embracing Simplicity said: The great taboos for entering mountains are as follows—
- First month: *wu* day
- Second month: *hai* day
- Third month: *shen* day
- Fourth month: *xu* day
- Fifth month: *wei* (or *xu*) day
- Sixth month: *mao* day
- Seventh month: *jia-zi* day
- Eighth month: *shen-zi* day
- Ninth month: *yin* day
- Tenth month: *chen-wei* day
- Eleventh month: *ji-chou* day
- Twelfth month: *yin* day

Auspicious days for entering mountains are: *jia-zi*, *jia-yin*, *yi-hai*, *ji-si*, *yi-mao*, *bing-xu*, *bing-wu*, *bing-chen*, and *si* days—these are greatly auspicious.

The Master Embracing Simplicity said: According to the *Secret Records of the Nine Heavens* and the *Taiyi Hidden Stems*, when entering mountains in a long month, avoid the 3rd, 11th, 15th, 18th, 24th, 26th, and 30th days. In a short month, avoid the 1st, 5th, 13th, 16th, 26th, and 28th days. Those who enter on these days will surely be tested by mountain spirits, their aims unfulfilled, their works unaccomplished. Not only Daoists, but all who enter on these days will suffer harm, encountering tigers, wolves, and venomous creatures.

The Master Embracing Simplicity said: The conditions of Heaven and Earth, the auspicious and inauspicious aspects of yin and yang—how vast and obscure they are!

The intricacies of these matters are indeed difficult to detail. I neither assert their certainty nor dare guarantee their absence. Yet the Yellow Emperor and the Grand Duke both trusted and relied upon them; more recently, Yan Junping and Sima Qian both employed them as authoritative. The classics and traditions speak of "governing the calendar and illuminating the seasons," of days of firmness and softness. An ancient saying declares: "Auspicious days are only *wu*—this has long been so." Kings established the office of Grand Historian, and all appointments, establishments, and affairs—whether ancestral sacrifices, suburban rites, or offerings to Heaven and Earth—were conducted only after selecting propitious times. How foolish and ignorant it is, then, for even mediocre men who claim to transcend the vulgar to scorn the choice of good days in their actions!

Whenever I have observed those entering mountains without regard for favorable times and days, the consequences have proven real—one must not enter lightly.

According to the *Jade Lock Classic*: "Those who wish to enter famous mountains must know the secret arts of *dunjia*, yet these are not explained in detail for others." The *Lingbao Scripture* states: "When entering mountains, one should use *bao* days, *yi* days, or *zhuan* days—these are greatly auspicious. To use *zhi* days or *fa* days is certain death, for there is no second Way."

In my youth, I harbored the ambition to enter mountains, and for this reason I studied *dunjia* texts, of which there are over sixty volumes. The matters are too vast to master quickly, so I excerpted their essentials to create a ready reference for my satchel. Though I have not transmitted them in writing, I now discuss their general principles. Those of discernment who wish to enter mountains and practice these arts should seek out those who know them—such people are not entirely absent from the world.

The *Central Classic of Dunjia* states: "To seek the Dao, use *tiannei* days and *tiannei* hours to subdue demons and spirits, and apply talismanic writing. On *tianqin* days and *tianqin* hours, enter famous mountains, and all evil, tigers, wolves, venomous creatures, and bandits will not dare approach. Exit through *tiancang* and enter through *dihu*—all *gui* days are *tiancang*, and all *ji* days are *dihu*."

It further says: "To avoid the chaos of the world and vanish into famous mountains, free from worry, use the *dingmao* day of the *shangyuan* period, called the time of *yinde*—also known as *tianxin*. This is what is meant by 'sinking into obscurity in broad daylight,' where the sun and moon lose their light and humans and spirits cannot see you."

Again, it says: "To seek immortality and enter famous mountains, use *gui* days and *gui* hours—also called *tiangong* days—when one is certain to transcend the world."

It also states: "When going into mountain forests, take grass from the *Qinglong* with your left hand, break it in half, and place it beneath the *Fengxing*. Pass through the *Mingtang*, enter the *Taiyin*, and perform the *Yu* step while reciting the incantation: *'Nuogao, Great Yin General, hear this: your humble descendant, Wang Jia, does not open to outsiders. Let those who see Jia take him for bundled firewood; let those who do not see Jia take him for a non-human.'* Then place the grass you hold on the ground, take soil with your left hand, and apply it to your philtrum. Hold the grass in your right hand to shield yourself, extend your left hand forward, and perform the *Yu* step to the *gui* position. Hold your breath and proceed—humans and spirits will not see you."

All *jia* days are *Qinglong*, all *yi* days are *Fengxing*, all *bing* days are *Mingtang*, and all *ding* days are *Yinzhong*.

The *Jiji* hexagram of the *Book of Changes* begins with the first and second lines, where the traces do not match the nine-trace sequence, yet they remain interconnected. One step is seven *chi*; it is also said that one *chi* equals two *zhang* and one *chi* (or, in another version, one step is three *chi*). Glance at the nine traces.

The method of the *Yu* step: Stand upright with the right foot forward and the left foot behind. Next, advance the right foot again, then bring the left foot behind the right foot—this is one step. Then advance the right foot again, then the left foot, bringing the right foot to join the left—this is the second step. Next, advance the right foot again, bringing the left foot to join the right—this is the third step. Thus, the method of the *Yu* step is complete.

All arts under Heaven should employ the *Yu* step—this is not limited to these matters.

The Master Embracing Simplicity said: The *Lingbao Scripture* states that so-called *bao* days are those where the heavenly stems generate the earthly branches, such as *jiawu* or *yisi* days. *Jia* is Wood, *wu* is Fire; *yi* is also Wood, *si* is also Fire—Fire is born of Wood. *Yi* days are those where the earthly branches generate the heavenly stems, such as *renshen* or *guiyou* days. *Ren* is Water, *shen* is Metal; *gui* is also Water, *you* is also Metal—Water is born of Metal. So-called *zhi* days are those where the heavenly stems overcome the earthly branches, such as *wuzi* or *jihai* days. *Wu* is Earth, *zi* is Water; *ji* is also Earth, *hai* is also Water—by the principles of the Five Phases, Earth overcomes Water. *Fa* days are those where the earthly branches overcome the heavenly stems, such as *jiashen* or *yiyou* days. *Jia* is Wood, *shen* is Metal; *yi* is also Wood, *you* is also Metal—Metal overcomes Wood. All others follow this pattern; extend the principle, and all can be known.

The *Baopuzi* states: When entering famous mountains, choose an *jiazi* day or a *kai* or *chu* day, and hang five-inch squares of silk in the five colors on a large stone—whatever is sought will surely be obtained. It also says: When entering mountains, one should know the secret incantation of the Six *Jia*: "Lin bing dou zhe jie zhen lie qian xing"—nine characters in all. These should always be silently recited; there is nothing they cannot avert. This is the essential and unburdensome Way.

The *Baopuzi* states: In the mountains, the forms of mountain spirits resemble small children with a single foot, walking backward. They delight in assaulting people. If one enters the mountains and hears human sounds at night, loudly call out their name, *Qizhi*, and they will not dare to harm the person. Another name for them is *Nei*; one may also call them by this name. There is also a mountain spirit resembling a drum, red in color, with a single foot—its name is *Hui*. Another appears like a person, nine feet tall, clad in fur and wearing a bamboo hat—its name is *Jinlei*. Or it may resemble a dragon, five-colored with a red head—its name is *Feifei*. Upon seeing them, call them by their names, and they will not dare to cause harm.

The *Baopuzi* states: In the mountains, there are large trees that can speak—not that the trees themselves speak, but their spirit is named *Yunyang*. Calling its name brings good fortune. If one sees firelight in the mountains at night, it is merely the work of long-dead withered wood; do not be alarmed. If one sees a person of the *Fan* tribe in the mountains at night, it is the spirit of bronze or iron. Seeing a person from the Qin dynasty is the spirit of a hundred-year-old tree; do not be alarmed, for they cannot cause harm. Those who appear in mountains and waters to summon people are called *Sijiao*; calling their names brings good fortune. If one sees a large snake in the mountains wearing a cap and headscarf, its name is *Shengxiang*; calling its name brings good fortune. If one sees an official in the mountains or only hears a voice but does not see its form, and it ceaselessly calls out to people, throw a white stone at it, and it will cease. Another method: use reeds to make a *ru* or *mao* and stab it—this also brings good fortune. If a mountain spirit comes to call a person and begs for food without ceasing, throw white cogon grass at it, and it will die. If a mountain spirit constantly bewilders people and causes them to lose their way, throw a reed staff at it, and it will die.

In the mountains, on *yin* days, those who call themselves *Yu officials* are tigers; those who call themselves *Lords of the Road* are wolves; those who call themselves *Magistrates* are old raccoon dogs. On *mao* days, those who call themselves *Elder* are hares; those who call themselves *Eastern King Father* are elk; those who call themselves *Western Queen Mother* are deer. On *chen* days, those who call themselves *Rain Masters* are dragons; those who call themselves *River Earls* are fish; those who call themselves *Lord of the Gutless* are crabs. On *si* days, those who call themselves *The Solitary* are snakes in the earth-altar; those who call themselves *Time Lords* are toads. On *wu* days, those who call themselves *The Three Dukes* are horses; those who call themselves *Immortals* are old trees. On *wei* days, those who call themselves *Hosts* are sheep; those who call themselves *Officials* are river deer. On *shen* days, those who call themselves *Human Lords* are monkeys; those who call themselves *Nine Ministers* are apes. On *you* days, those who call themselves *Generals* are roosters; those who call themselves *Thief-Catchers* are pheasants. On *xu* days, those who call themselves by human surnames and given names are dogs; those who call themselves *Lord of Chengyang* are foxes. On *hai* days, those who call themselves *Women* are gold and jade; those who call themselves *Divine Lords* are pigs. On *zi* days, those who call themselves *Altar Lords* are rats; those who call themselves *Divine Persons* are bats. On *chou* days, those who call themselves *Scholars* are oxen. Simply knowing the names of these creatures prevents them from causing harm.

Someone asked: What is the method for dwelling in mountains and marshes to avoid snakes and vipers? The *Baopuzi* replied: In ancient times, the Round Mound abounded with large snakes and also produced fine medicines. When the Yellow Emperor was about to ascend it, Guangchengzi taught him to wear realgar, and all the snakes departed. Now, when entering mountain forests and grasslands, carry five ounces or more of Wudu realgar, the color of a rooster’s comb, and one will not fear snakes. If a snake bites a person, place a small amount of realgar powder into the wound, and it will immediately heal. Though there are many kinds of snakes, only the viper and the green-gold snake bite people with the greatest urgency; if untreated, they kill a person within a day. Those who do not know the methods of treatment and are bitten by these two snakes should cut the wound with a knife, scrape the flesh, and cast it to the ground—it will boil as if roasted by fire. When the flesh is entirely scorched, the person will survive. During the seventh and eighth months, when these snakes’ venom is at its peak, they cannot bite people, and their venom does not dissipate. Instead, they gnaw on large bamboo and small trees, which immediately wither and dry. Now, Daoists entering mountains only know grand methods but do not understand the way to avoid such dangers—this is no small matter.

Before entering the mountains, one should first practice at home. Learn the method of restraint: visualize the sun, moon, Vermilion Bird, Dark Warrior, Azure Dragon, and White Tiger to guard one’s body, then proceed. Upon reaching the mountain forests and grasslands, inhale three breaths from the left, hold them, and blow into the mountain grasses, imagining this breath as red, like clouds and mist filling dozens of miles. If there are companions, no matter how many, have them stand in rows and blow the breath upon them. Even if they tread upon snakes, the snakes will not dare to move, and they will scarcely encounter any. If one sees a snake, face the sun, inhale three breaths from the left, hold them, press the tongue against the roof of the mouth, pinch the *duguan* point with the hand, close the Heavenly Gate, and block the Earthly Door. Then press down on the snake’s head with an object, bind it with the hand, and draw a prison on the ground to contain it—it may also be captured and played with. Even if it coils around the neck, it will not dare to bite. If one does not know how to release the restraint, spit out the breath to blow upon it, and it will never escape the prison. If another person is bitten by a snake, inhale three breaths from the left and blow upon them, and they will immediately recover, feeling no more pain. If they are ten or more miles away, one may also perform the breath from afar, calling out their name. For a man, pray with the left hand; for a woman, pray with the right hand, and they too will recover.

Master Jie’s method: Upon dwelling in the mountains, visualize five-colored snakes, each with one head, then hold the breath and pierce them with bent green bamboo or small wooden boards. Walk the *Yubu* step to the left, visualize thousands of centipedes covering the body, and one will never encounter snakes. Alternatively, carry dried ginger and aconite behind the elbow, or burn the horns of oxen, sheep, or deer to fumigate the body, or carry Wang Fangping’s realgar pills, or place the dirt from a pig’s ear and musk pills under the toenails—all are effective. Also, musk and wild boars both eat snakes, so they are used to ward them off. Moreover, the *yunri* bird and the *yong* both eat snakes, so southerners entering the mountains carry the tails of *yong* and the beaks of *yunri* birds to avoid snakes. If a snake bites a person, scrape these two substances and apply them to the wound, and it will immediately heal. *Tan* is another name for the *zhen* bird. Also, southerners entering the mountains carry bamboo tubes containing live centipedes. Where there are snakes, the centipedes will move within the tube, so one should carefully inspect the grass and will surely see the snake. If a large snake, over a *zhang* long and a *wei* in girth, is encountered, the centipede can restrain it with its breath, and the snake will die.

When snakes see centipedes on the banks of rivers or lakes, even large snakes flee into deep pools at the bottom of valleys to escape. The centipede merely floats upon the water, restraining all who see it—there appears a dark green object, thin as a thread, descending straight into the water to where the snake lies. After a moment, the snake floats to the surface and dies. Thus, southerners grind centipedes into powder to treat snakebites, and the wounds heal immediately.

Someone asked, “In the mountains and valleys of Jiangnan, many poisonous creatures abound. Is there a way to ward them off?”
Baopuzi replied, “In the Central Plains, the high plateaus have clear and harmonious *qi*, and the famous mountains of the central states are entirely free of such creatures. Yet in the wilds of Wu and Chu, where heat and dampness stagnate, even Mount Heng and Mount Huo—though sacred peaks—teem with venomous stings. There is also the *duanhu*, also called *yu* or *shegong*, which lies in wait to shoot its shadow. It is truly a peril of water. Its form resembles a cicada or a three-legged cup, with wings to fly, no eyes but keen ears. In its mouth lies a transverse object like a crossbow. Upon hearing human voices, it aligns this object like a crossbow and shoots *qi* as arrows, striking through water. If it hits a person’s body, a sore erupts; if it strikes their shadow, the victim falls ill but does not immediately develop a sore. Those who do not know the cure will die. The illness resembles severe cold damage, and within ten days, all perish.

There are also sand lice, found both on land and in water. After fresh rain or before the heat of dusk, one must be cautious when wading through water, for they inevitably cling to people. Only during scorching days or when the grass is dry are they rare. They are as small as the tip of a hair; once they attach to a person, they burrow into the skin. Their presence feels like a thorn’s prick—minor contact causes great pain. One can remove them with a needle; they appear bright red like cinnabar, moving upon the fingernail. If left unremoved, the insect will bore to the bone, then circulate throughout the body. Its effects resemble those of the *shegong*, and both kill. When traveling through areas with these insects, upon returning to one’s lodging, one should thoroughly scorch the body with fire, causing the insects to fall to the ground.

If one carries the Eight Substances—musk pellets, *Dushi* pills, *Fangming* pills, *Yuhu* pills, rhinoceros horn pills, *Qixing* pills, and *jini*—they will repel sand lice and *duanhu*. If these medicines cannot be obtained at once, carrying fine musk alone is also effective. Grind equal parts realgar and garlic into a pellet the size of a chicken egg and carry it; this is also beneficial. If already afflicted, applying this medicine to the sore will heal it. Chewing and applying the juice of red amaranth also cures it. The roots of *wuqie*, *xuangou* grass, and *ge* vine—each of these three can be used alone. Pound them to extract one or two *sheng* of juice and consume it.

The *shegong* hibernates in mountain valleys during winter. When heavy snow falls, search for it where the snow does not accumulate, where the *qi* rises as if steaming. Dig no deeper than a *chi* into the earth to find it. Dry it in the shade, grind it into powder, and carry it. In summer, it will naturally repel *shegong*.

If a Daoist knows the *Yi* prohibition method and constantly maintains the *Dongbai* prohibitions, guarding the True One, then a hundred poisons dare not approach, and no medicines are needed.

Someone asked, “When Daoists dwell in mountains, nestling in cliffs and sheltered valleys, they need not rely on warm, layered robes. How do they dare not fear wind and dampness?”
Baopuzi said, “The *Jinbing* powder, *Sanyang* liquid, *Changxin* pills, *Xunhua* *naidong* decoction, *Duyao* ointment, *Yinyu* *xuanhua* powder, and *Qiudihuang* *xue* pills—taking any of these for no more than fifty days will enable one to resist wind and dampness for a decade. If one ingests the Golden Elixir and the Great Medicine, though not yet ascending to emptiness or becoming ethereally light, the body will be free from illness. Even when exposed to wind and dampness, they cannot harm it. These seven medicines are for those who have just begun to study the Dao.

Master Yao merely took the *Sanyang* liquid, then lay bare-chested upon the ice without shivering. These are the proven, secret methods of Master Jie and Liang Youdao, who slept upon stones and faced autumn and winter winds without harm.”

Someone asked about the way to cross rivers and seas to repel snakes and dragons.
Baopuzi said, “When Daoists have no choice but to traverse great rivers, they should first, by the water’s edge, break a chicken egg, mix a small amount of powder with fragrant herbs, stir it into the water, and bathe themselves. Then they need not fear wind, waves, or dragons. Also, wearing the *Donghai Xiaotong* talisman, the *Zhishui* talisman, and the *Penglai* ritual repels all harm in water.

There is also the *Liujia Sanjin* talisman and the water prohibition. Another method: when approaching a river, first recite the incantation: *Juanpeng, juanpeng* [or *gonggong, fengfeng*], *Xiang Bodao, qian bi jiaolong, wan zai xiaomie, tian qingming*. The *Jinjian Ji* states: On the *bingwu* day of the fifth month, at noon, pound five minerals—realgar, cinnabar, orpiment, *bin* stone, and *zengqing*—into powder. Bathe in the Golden Flower Pool, then place them in the Six-One Divine Furnace and smelt them with cassia wood. When the copper is refined, temper it with hard charcoal. Have a boy and girl tend the fire. Take the *yin* copper to forge the male sword and the *yang* copper to forge the female sword, each five *cun* and five *fen* long, corresponding to the number of earth. This subdues water spirits. Carrying them while traveling on water ensures that dragons, giant fish, and water spirits dare not approach.

To distinguish *yin* and *yang* copper: Have a boy and girl pour water over the copper when it is red-hot in the fire. The copper will split into two pieces—those with protrusions are *yang* copper, and those with indentations are *yin* copper. Engrave them with their names. When entering water, wear the male on the left and the female on the right. If merely boarding a boat without wading into the water, wear the male on *yang* days and the female on *yin* days. Also, writing the *Beidi* script from the *Tianwen Dazi* on silk and carrying it repels wind, waves, dragons, and water perils.”

Someone asked about the method to repel mountain spirits, river deities, and the hundred *gu* in temples.
Baopuzi said, “Daoists should constantly carry the *Tianshui* talisman, the *Shanghuang Zhushi* talisman, Laozi’s Left Contract, and guard the True One while visualizing the Three Generals of the Three Departments—then *gu* dare not approach. Alternatively, studying the *Lun Gu Lu* to know the names of all *gu* in the world, as well as the *Baize Tu* and *Jiuding Ji*, will cause the multitude of *gu* to retreat. Next, taking *chunzi* pills with red stone, *zengqing* *yeguang* powder, *congshi* pills, *wuyan* pills, swallowing white quartz, or the *Zhimu* powder will make one visible to *gu*, and the *gu* will fear them.

Baopuzi said, “There are the *Laojun Huangting Zhongtai* forty pills and the true secret talismans. When entering mountains and forests, on a *jiayin* day, write in cinnabar on white silk, place it on a table at night, face the Northern Dipper, and make offerings with a little wine and dried meat. State your name, bow twice, receive it, and place it in the collar of your robe. This repels mountain and river spirits, the hundred *gu*, myriad spirits, tigers, wolves, and poisonous creatures.”

Why must only Daoists, in times of chaos seeking refuge in mountains and forests, know these methods? <*Baopuzi Inner and Outer Chapters*, Inner Chapters, Scroll Four>

Baopuzi said, “The five talismans above are all the Lord Lao’s ‘Entering Mountains Talismans.’ Write them in cinnabar on peachwood boards, inscribing the characters so they fill the entire surface. Place them on doorways, the four directions, the four corners, and key locations along paths within fifty paces of one’s dwelling to repel mountain spirits, *gu* demons, and spectral beings. They may also be affixed to beams and pillars within the house. Whether one dwells in mountains and forests or enters them briefly, all may use them, and no creature will dare harm them. These three talismans may be inscribed together on a single board—this is not the method of the *Youshi*.”

Baopuzi said, “This talisman is the Lord Lao’s ‘Entering Mountains Talisman.’ It may be affixed to beams and pillars within the house. All who dwell in mountains and forests or enter them briefly should use it.”

Baopuzi said, “This is the ‘Entering Mountains Talisman to Repel Tigers and Wolves,’ bestowed by the immortal Chen Anshi. Write two talismans in cinnabar on silk, each distinct, and always carry four of each at one’s dwelling. When moving or crossing water, retrieve and take them along—this is a great divine secret. The ‘Mountain-Opening Talisman’ is named after the thousand-year-old *lüe* plant. When doors open, treasured books, ancient scripts, gold, and jade all appear—guard this secret. The method to the right is largely similar with minor differences.”

Baopuzi said, “This talisman is the Lord Lao’s divine seal against the hundred *gu*, serpents, vipers, tigers, and wolves. Carve it into a two-inch square of jujube heartwood, bow twice, and wear it—it is highly efficacious, the talisman of the immortal Chen Anshi.”

These three talismans may also be affixed together on the left and right, front and back of oxen, horses, and houses, as well as pigpens, to repel tigers and wolves.

Someone asked, “Formerly, I heard of Tan Chang, who could walk upon water or dwell within it for long periods. By what method did he achieve this?”

Baopuzi said, “Mix scallion sap with cassia and form pills the size of parasol seeds. Take seven pills three times daily for three years, and one will be able to walk upon water.”

Master Zheng said, “Simply practice breath-holding until one can sustain a thousand breaths. With time, one may dwell underwater for about a day. Carve a piece of true *tianxi* rhinoceros horn over three inches long into the shape of a fish and hold it in the mouth when entering water. The water will part three feet around, allowing one to breathe. True *tianxi* rhinoceros horn has a red vein like a thread running from base to tip. If a piece of this horn is filled with rice and placed among chickens, they will retreat in alarm before pecking within inches. Thus, southerners sometimes call *tianxi* rhinoceros horn ‘chicken-frightening rhinoceros.’ Placing this horn atop a grain pile prevents birds from alighting. On nights of thick mist and heavy dew, if placed in the central courtyard, it will remain entirely dry.

This rhinoceros dwells deep in the mountains. On dark, moonless nights, its horn shines brightly like a torch. When its horn is used to stir poisoned medicine into a decoction, white foam surges forth, and the poison’s potency vanishes entirely. If used to stir non-toxic substances, no foam appears. Thus, one may discern its efficacy.

When traveling in foreign lands where *gu* poison is prevalent, always stir food and drink with this horn before consuming. If one is struck by a poisoned arrow and near death, carve the horn’s pattern into the wound, and foam will emerge as the wound heals.

The reason *tianxi* rhinoceros horn can neutralize poison is that the beast feeds exclusively on toxic grasses and thorny, bristling plants, never consuming soft or smooth vegetation. Once a year, it sheds its horn among mountain rocks. If one finds it and carves a replacement from hardwood, matching its color, grain, and shape, the rhinoceros will not notice. The following year, it will shed its horn in the same place.

Other rhinoceros horns also repel evil and neutralize poison, but none match the marvel of *tianxi* rhinoceros horn.”

One may consume the *Six Wu Talismans* for a thousand days or compound the *Fengyi Water Immortal Pills* with red-spotted spiders and seven-layered water striders. Taking these allows one to dwell underwater. Applying them to the soles of the feet enables walking upon water. If mere head filth can make metal and iron float, how much more so these superior methods?

Someone asked, “Those who cultivate the Dao often dwell in mountains and forests, where tigers and wolves pose great harm. How may one repel them?”

Baopuzi said, “Ancients entering mountains all carried the seal of *Huangshen Yuezhang*. Four inches wide, it bore one hundred and twenty characters. Sealing mud with it and placing it at the four directions a hundred paces from one’s dwelling kept tigers and wolves from approaching. When encountering fresh tracks, impressing the seal in the direction of the tracks would drive the tiger away; impressing it against the direction would make the tiger return. Carrying this seal while traveling through mountains and forests also dispels fear of tigers and wolves.

Not only does it repel tigers and wolves, but if mountain, river, or earth spirits, or malevolent deities of shrines that consume blood offerings seek to bestow fortune or misfortune, sealing the mud to block their paths renders them powerless.

Formerly, at Stonewater, there was a great soft-shelled turtle dwelling in a deep pool, lending the pool the name ‘Turtle Pool.’ This creature could manifest as a *gu* demon, afflicting people with wandering illnesses. A Daoist of Wu named Dai Bing once observed it. Using the *Yuezhang* seal, he made hundreds of mud seals, boarded a boat, and cast them throughout the pool. After a long while, a great turtle over a *zhang* in length surfaced, motionless. It was struck dead, and the afflicted all recovered. Many smaller turtles also floated to the shore, dead.

When suddenly encountering a tiger in the mountains, perform the ‘Three-Five Prohibition,’ and the tiger will retreat. The method of the ‘Three-Five Prohibition’ must be transmitted orally, for brush cannot convey its intricacies.

Another method: visualize oneself as a vermilion bird, three *zhang* tall, standing atop the tiger’s head. Then hold the breath, and the tiger will depart. If lodging in the mountains at night, secretly take a hairpin from the head, hold the breath, and pierce the white tiger with it—there will be nothing to fear.

Another method: hold a knife in the left hand, hold the breath, and trace a square on the ground, reciting the incantation: ‘North of Mount Heng, south of Mount Tai, bandits do not rise, tigers and wolves do not prowl, city walls remain unbreached. Seal with a golden lock.’ Then lay the knife horizontally on the white tiger’s path—there will be nothing to fear for ten days.”

One method employs the Great Prohibition: swallow three hundred and sixty breaths, then inhale from the left and exhale to the right while shouting at the tiger—the tiger will not dare rise. Using this method to enter the mountains, one need not fear tigers. Alternatively, employ the Seven-Star Step, the Jade Spirit Talisman, the Eight Might Talismans, the Five Victorious Talismans, the Li Er Talisman, the Great Peace Talisman, the Central Yellow Hua-Gai Seal, or burn sulfur powder and ox or sheep horns. Or erect the Western Marchmount Lord’s Mountain-Prohibiting Talisman—all have proven efficacy.

These four talismans are omitted here.

This talisman is the Old Lord’s Mountain-Entering Talisman, as described below. It may also be affixed to doorways, beams, and pillars. Whether dwelling in mountains and forests or entering them briefly, all may use it.

**Earthly Perfection, Eighteenth**

The Master of Embracing Simplicity said: I have heard from my teacher that one who knows the One knows all things. To know the One is to know nothing that is not known. Not to know the One is to know nothing at all. The Dao arises from the One; its value lies in its singularity. Each resides in its own place, symbolizing Heaven, Earth, and Human—hence the Three Ones.

Heaven attains the One and becomes clear; Earth attains the One and becomes tranquil; Human attains the One and lives; spirits attain the One and become numinous. Metal sinks and feathers float; mountains stand and rivers flow. Gazing, one does not see it; listening, one does not hear it. Preserve it, and it is present; neglect it, and it vanishes. Face it, and fortune comes; turn from it, and misfortune follows. Safeguard it, and it endures without limit; lose it, and life decays, qi is exhausted.

The Old Lord said: Dimly, dimly—within it there is an image. Vaguely, vaguely—within it there is a thing. This is what is called the One.

Thus, the immortals’ scriptures say: If you desire long life, guard the One; when hungry, the One provides sustenance; when thirsty, the One offers drink. The One has a name and a form: for men, it is nine *fen* in length; for women, six *fen*. It may reside two *cun* and four *fen* below the navel in the lower cinnabar field, or in the Crimson Palace and Golden Gate beneath the heart—the middle cinnabar field. Or it may dwell between the eyebrows, one *cun* back as the Hall of Light, two *cun* as the Grotto Chamber, and three *cun* as the upper cinnabar field. These are what the Daoists revere, guarding them across generations and transmitting their names only by word of mouth.

The One can complete yin and generate yang, regulate cold and heat, spring and autumn. In spring, the One brings forth; in summer, it grows; in autumn, it harvests; in winter, it stores. Vast, it cannot be measured by the Six Directions; minute, it cannot be compared to the tip of a hair.

Of old, the Yellow Emperor traveled east to Green Mound, passing Wind Mountain, where he met the Purple Mansion Master and received the *Inner Writ of the Three Sovereigns* to summon the myriad spirits. To the south, he reached Round Ridge and the Yin Jian Tree, observing where the hundred grains ascend, gathering the flowers of the *ruo* and drinking the waters of the cinnabar reins. To the west, he met Master Central Yellow and received the *Nine Additions Formula*, then studied with Master Broad Completion to receive the *Scripture of Self-Completion*. To the north, he reached Vast Dyke and Mount Juci, where he met the Great Hidden Lord and the Yellow Canopy Youth, receiving the *Divine Mushroom Chart*. Returning to the royal chambers, he obtained the *Golden Instructions of the Divine Elixir*. Arriving at Mount Emei, he met the Heavenly Perfected August One in the Jade Hall and inquired about the Dao of the True One. The August One said: “You rule the Four Seas—do you still seek long life? Is this not greed?” Their dialogue cannot be fully recorded; I roughly present one corner.

The methods for long life and immortality lie solely in the golden elixir; to preserve form and repel evil, only the True One suffices. Thus, the ancients prized it above all.

The immortals’ scriptures say: The nine-times-cycled elixir, the *Golden Liquid Scripture*, and the *Guard the One Formula* are all stored within the Five Cities of Kunlun, sealed in jade caskets, inscribed on golden tablets, bound with purple clay, and stamped with the Central Seal.

I have heard from my teacher that the One resides in the Great Abyss at the North Pole. Before it lies the Hall of Light; behind it, the Crimson Palace. Towering canopy, golden towers, vaulted domes—left, the *Gang*; right, the *Kui*. Waves surge, skies soar; dark mushrooms blanket the cliffs, vermilion grasses twine the peaks. White jade soars; sun and moon hang their light. Passing through fire, crossing water, traversing the dark, treading the yellow—palaces and gates intertwine, curtains and jades gleam. Dragons and tigers stand guard; divine beings attend. It neither acts nor bestows; the One rests in its place. Neither slow nor swift, the One abides in its chamber. At ease, at leisure—the One does not depart. Guard the One, preserve the True, and you may commune with spirits. Reduce desires, moderate food—the One lingers. With blades at your neck, think of the One and live. To know the One is not difficult; difficulty lies in maintaining it without fail. Thus, one may endure endlessly, evade evil beasts on land, and repel dragons in water. One need not fear *wangliang* or venomous creatures; blades cannot strike, poisons cannot harm. This is the great outline of preserving the True One.

The Master of Embracing Simplicity said: I have heard from my teacher that among the Daoist arts and scriptures, methods of visualization and concentration to repel evil and protect the body number in the thousands—such as containing shadows, concealing forms, guarding form without life, the Nine Transformations, Twelve Changes, Twenty-Four Births, and visualizing the various spirits within the body to make them visible. The methods are innumerable, each with its efficacy.

Yet some require visualizing thousands of things for self-protection, often so cumbersome they exhaust the mind. If one knows the Dao of guarding the One, all such methods may be discarded. Thus, it is said: “To know the One is to complete all things.”

The oral instructions for receiving the True One are clearly recorded. They are transmitted on a day of royal phase, with the blood of a white sacrificial animal, using white silk and white silver as pledges. A golden contract is divided—careless transmission renders the spirit ineffective.

One who can guard the One, the One guards in return. Thus, blades find no edge to strike, and a hundred harms find no place to lodge. In ruins, one may prosper; in peril, one remains secure.

In demonic temples, mountain forests, plague-ridden lands, tombs, tiger and wolf lairs, or snake-infested places—guard the One without fail, and all evils flee far away. If one momentarily forgets to guard the One and is harmed by the hundred demons, or is oppressed in sleep, immediately go to the central courtyard, gaze at the Auxiliary Star, clench the fists, and guard the One—the demons will depart.

In dark or rainy weather, simply remain indoors, face north, and visualize the Auxiliary Star. If surrounded by enemy troops with no escape, swiftly enter the Yin of the Six Jia, prostrate yourself, and guard the One—then the five weapons cannot harm you.

One who can guard the One may travel ten thousand *li*, enter armies, cross great rivers—no need to select days or hours, choose auspicious times to begin work, move houses, or enter new dwellings. One need not consult geomancy, star calendars, or avoid the General of Great Yin, the Monthly Builder, baleful stars, or the taboos of one’s fate—one need never fear calamity again.

The Way of the Former Worthies has been tested through the ages and proven true. The *Baopuzi* states: "The Dao of the Mysterious One is also an essential method. It wards off all harm and shares the same efficacy as the Upright One. My *Inner Chapters*, the first of which is titled *Changxuan* (Unfettered Mystery), refer precisely to this. Guarding the Mysterious One is even easier than guarding the Upright One. The Upright One has a name, a style, a fixed stature, and prescribed attire, whereas the Mysterious One is merely perceived at first and sought at noon—this is what is meant by 'knowing the white, guarding the black,' so that one cannot die even if one wished to. However, one must first purify and fast for a hundred days before one may await and seek it. It is obtained in no more than three or four days, and once obtained and guarded, it will never depart.

When guarding the Mysterious One, one also visualizes one’s body dividing into three persons. Once these three are seen, they may be further multiplied to several dozen, all identical to one’s own body and clearly manifest. Each has its own oral instructions—this is the Dao of dividing the form. It is how Master Zuo and Jizi Xun, as well as the Transcendent Lord of Wei, were able to appear in dozens of places in a single day. There was once a host who sat with his guest, while another host greeted the guest at the gate, and yet another host fished by the water. The guest could not tell which was the true host.

The Master said: 'Guarding the One while cultivating the Bright Mirror—when the Mirror’s Dao is accomplished, one may divide the form into dozens of persons, all identical in clothing and appearance.'

The *Baopuzi* states: 'The Master said: "If one wishes for long life, one must diligently ingest the great elixir. If one wishes to commune with spirits, one must divide the Golden and Watery forms. Once the forms are divided, one may perceive the three *hun* and seven *po* within one’s own body, and the spirits of heaven and earth may all be encountered. The gods of mountains and rivers may all be commanded."

The *Baopuzi* states: "Life is to be cherished; death is to be feared. Yet those who cultivate long life, nurture their nature, and ward off death have never succeeded without beginning with diligence and culminating in enduring vision. Once the Dao is accomplished, there is little left to do. Before its accomplishment, there is nothing one does not do: gathering herbs and plants, toiling in mountains and marshes, decocting and preparing medicines—all require physical effort. One ascends perilous heights, crosses dangerous waters, and labors day and night without rest. Without the utmost resolve, one cannot endure.

When one wishes to refine the Golden Elixir and ascend to heaven, the ingredients for the great medicine all require funds that cannot be swiftly obtained. One must rely on farming, herding, trade, or commerce to accumulate resources over years of toil before the elixir may be compounded. Even on the day of compounding, one must maintain purity, fast, and sever worldly affairs. The difficulties are manifold, and one must further apply oneself to spirit visualization, guarding the One, repelling evil, and protecting the body—ever as a ruler governs a state or a general faces an enemy. Only then may one attain the merit of long life.

Only those of keen intelligence and great wisdom, capable of bearing the weight of worldly affairs and aiding the common good, may undertake this practice and be certain of success. Mediocre and ordinary people, though they may have the aspiration and fondness for it, cannot persevere to the end. Thus, the body of one person is a microcosm of a kingdom."

The chest and abdomen are like the palace halls, the four limbs like the suburban borders, the divisions of bones and joints like the hundred officials. The spirit is like the ruler, blood like the ministers, and qi like the people. Thus, one who knows how to govern the body can govern the state. Loving the people secures the state; nourishing qi preserves the body. When the people scatter, the state perishes; when qi is exhausted, the body dies. The dead cannot be revived, nor the lost restored. Therefore, the perfected person eliminates dangers before they arise and treats illness before it takes form—healing in times of peace rather than chasing after what has already passed. The people are difficult to nurture yet easy to endanger; qi, though pure, is easily polluted. Thus, one must carefully assess authority and virtue to safeguard the altars of the state, and sever desires to fortify blood and qi. Only then may steadfast unity be preserved, the Three and Seven guarded, and the hundred harms repelled. Thus, lifespan is extended.

The *Baopuzi* states: "The master’s teaching says that ingesting the great elixir of gold cinnabar, though one has not yet departed the world, keeps all evil at bay. If one merely consumes herbs, plants, minor remedies, or the eight minerals, it may cure illness and extend life, but it is insufficient to avert calamities from without. One may be afflicted by demons, slighted by the spirits of great mountains, or invaded by spectral beings. Only by maintaining steadfast unity can one fear none of these. Next in efficacy is wearing divine talismans. If one fails to understand these two methods and seeks long life, it is perilous indeed. With four gates, yet three are closed."

Should thieves yet find entry, how much more so when all gates lie open?

*Far-Ranging Observations*, Scroll Nineteen

Someone asked: "I, a humble man, have been confined by the walls of Confucian teaching, knowing only the Five Classics, the Three Histories, the words of the Hundred Schools, and the vain, florid poetry and frivolous essays of no benefit. I have devoted my thoughts to these for many years. Born into an age of manifold calamities, where chaos knows no end and weapons never rest, where literary arts are scorned and only martial prowess is valued, I have consumed the days of my two heavens in bitter toil, exhausting my thoughts on attack and defense, seeking escape—yet official rank eludes me, and I cannot even avoid the toil of the fields. Moreover, this has drained my vital essence, wasted my years, and brought no benefit to my lifespan. Now, with gray hair at my temples and old age upon me, my original aspirations have withered. Just as I wish to turn back and seek the Dao of life, sudden crises press in from all sides, leaving me no direction to flee, like one crossing a great river without knowing where to ford.

You, Master, have exhaustively studied the ancient canons and also mastered the arcane and esoteric. I wonder: among the Daoist texts, how many scrolls are there in total? I wish to know their titles."

The *Baopuzi* replied: "I, too, once suffered from this same affliction. In the past, I was fortunate to meet a wise teacher, Master Zheng. My only regret is that I, his disciple, lacked the wisdom to penetrate the most profound and scale the loftiest heights. Though I served as one of his many disciples, sweeping and cleaning, my talent and insight were shallow, and I was still young and vigorous. My thoughts were not single-minded, and worldly attachments were not yet exhausted, so I could not gain much—this I still consider my greatest regret.

Master Zheng was over eighty at the time, his temples already streaked with white, yet within a few years, his hair turned black again, his complexion became full and radiant. He could draw a strong crossbow and shoot a hundred paces, walk hundreds of li in a day, and drink two *dou* of wine without becoming drunk. Whenever he climbed mountains, his body was light and agile, scaling perilous heights and crossing treacherous terrain—even young men could not keep up. His diet was no different from that of ordinary people, and I never saw him abstain from grain. I once asked Huang Zhang, a senior disciple who had followed him for a long time, and he told me: 'Master Zheng was once returning from Yuzhang when he encountered a great storm at Juegou浦. Hearing that there were many bandits ahead, his companions urged him to wait for reinforcements. Everyone’s provisions were scarce, so Master Zheng shared his rice with the group and did not eat again for fifty days—yet he did not hunger, nor did he show any sign of what he had done. I do not know by what means he accomplished this.'

When writing small characters by firelight, he surpassed even young men. He understood music and was skilled at playing the zither. When sitting in leisure, he could converse with several people at once, answering their questions without pause, his voice clear and his hearing sharp. He could direct those playing the *sheng* and *se* to adjust their pitch without the slightest error.

Later, I became one of Master Zheng’s disciples. When I requested to see his esoteric texts, he told me: 'The essential Dao fits on a foot of silk—this alone is enough to transcend the world; there is no need for more. Yet after broad study, one far surpasses those who have seen nothing. Moreover, understanding human nature allows one to teach simpler techniques to protect beginners who have not yet achieved mastery from various afflictions.'

He first gave me nearly a hundred scrolls of Daoist admonitions and precepts, showing them to me gradually. I had already seen many of them before, and for those I had seen, I often asked him about the points that puzzled me. Master Zheng said: 'You have the talent to discern truth from falsehood—you can be taught. However, though you know much, your knowledge is not yet refined. Your mind is set on external learning, and you cannot focus single-mindedly. You have not yet deeply immersed yourself in the classics or ventured far enough.'

From then on, he promised to show me finer texts. He also allowed me to gradually obtain shorter writings copied on silk and fine linen. Over the years, the total I had seen should have amounted to over two hundred scrolls, yet I could never obtain them all. His other disciples were all tasked with menial labor—gathering firewood, plowing fields—while I, frail and weak, was unfit for such work. Unable to prove myself in other ways, I often swept, dusted, arranged his bed and desk, ground ink, held the candle, and helped him transcribe old texts. For this, he treated me the same as his senior disciples.

He told me: 'Every scroll of miscellaneous Daoist texts contains valuable teachings. One should only assess their refinement or coarseness and choose what to practice—not memorize everything, lest one waste time and exhaust the mind. Once the Golden Elixir is achieved, none of these will be needed. Yet sometimes, one must teach in a way that begins with the basics to encourage and guide learners, giving them a foundation to aspire to.'

Master Zheng would not allow others to copy his books. One had to memorize their essence. Even if he lent them for a long time, no one dared to steal a single character. Master Zheng was originally a great Confucian scholar. In his later years, he took up the Dao, yet he never ceased teaching the *Book of Rites* and the *Book of Documents*. His bearing was noble and upright, his demeanor dignified and proper. Those who met him all treated him with reverence. Whenever they had questions, they waited for him to soften his expression before daring to speak.

The texts I had in my possession for long periods—sometimes a month—were sufficient to transcribe much, yet I dared not secretly copy them. Precisely because Master Zheng was wise and discerning, I feared that if he discovered it, I would lose more than I gained. Even at the outset of receiving his teachings, I dared not act recklessly but only made requests when I deemed it appropriate. Thus, though I knew of drinking from the river, I could never fill my belly.

Among his fifty-odd disciples, only I was taught the *Scripture of the Golden Elixir*, the *Inner Writings of the Three Sovereigns*, the *Five Agents Record in the Pillow*, and the like. The others did not even get to see the first line of these texts. Though I did not obtain all his books, I recorded their names. Now, I will tell them to you so that later generations who love these texts may seek them more broadly.

Daoist scriptures include:
*The Inner Writings of the Three Sovereigns* (Heaven, Earth, and Human, three scrolls),
*The Mysterious Writings* (Upper, Middle, and Lower, three scrolls),
*The Scripture of Mixed Perfection* (two scrolls),

All texts without a specified number of scrolls consist of one scroll each."

Following the secondary category are the various talismans, which include:

*The Nine Terraces Talisman* (nine scrolls),
*The Six Jia Spirit-Communing Talisman* (ten scrolls),
*The Six Yin Mobile Kitchen*,
*The Dragon Embryo Stone Chamber* (three scrolls),
*The Three Metals and Five Woods Defense Against Death Talisman*,
*The Combined Five Hundred Talismans for Military Fire Summoning and Governance*,
*The Jade Axe Talisman* (ten scrolls).

These are all major talismans. The remaining minor ones are too numerous to record in full.

The *Baopuzi* states: "Master Zheng said that talismans originate from Lord Lao and are all celestial patterns."

Lord Lao could commune with the divine, and all talismans were bestowed by the divine. Yet few prove efficacious when used by people today, for their origins are distant, and errors have multiplied through repeated transcription. Moreover, if one’s faith is not steadfast, even correct talismans will fail to work. This is like writing characters: a miswritten talisman not only brings no benefit but may cause harm. Even in writing, people know that frequent copying leads to errors—hence the saying, “After three transcriptions, ‘fish’ becomes ‘Lu’ and ‘empty’ becomes ‘tiger.’” The difference between *qi* 七 and *shi* 士 lies merely in the length of a stroke or the hook of a line. Yet in talismans, the characters are unreadable, and errors go unnoticed, so no one knows whether they are correct.

There are also those who, by their innate constitution and mastery of technique, find talismans uniquely effective—just as some people, when using musk, naturally emit fragrance, a quality that cannot be transmitted. Even so, one must obtain an uncorrupted talisman and use it with a sincere heart; the effects will simply be slower than for those who employ the true method with innate skill. Still, there is always some benefit.

Generally, Daoists seeking long life focus on elixirs; talismans and swords serve only to repel demons and ward off evil. As for the claim that using certain great talismans can lead to immortality, this cannot be taken as definitive. In the Wu dynasty, there was a man named Jie Xiang who could read talismanic scripts and discern whether they were correct. Someone tested him by taking miscellaneous talismans for curing illnesses and exorcising demons, removing their labels, and showing them to him. He identified each one and corrected the errors. Since then, no one else has possessed such knowledge.

Someone asked, “Of all the great elixirs for immortality, none surpasses the Golden Elixir. I have heard your teaching on this—may I ask about talismanic texts? Which are the most important?”

The *Baopuzi* replied: “I have heard Master Zheng say that of all Daoist texts, none are weightier than the *Writings of the Three Sovereigns* and the *True Forms of the Five Peaks*. In ancient times, immortals, perfected beings, and celestial officials held this Way in the highest secrecy; it could not be transmitted except to those with the name of immortals. After receiving it, one must wait forty years before passing it on, swearing a blood oath and pledging one’s life as surety. These texts are found in all the famous mountains and the Five Peaks, but they are hidden in stone chambers in secluded ravines. Only those destined for the Way may enter the mountains and meditate with utmost sincerity, whereupon the mountain spirits will open the mountain, allowing them to see the texts. For example, Fan Zhongli found them in the mountains, set up an altar, offered silk, and always made a copy before leaving.

One must keep these texts in a pure and clean place. Before any undertaking, one must first report to them as if serving a lord or father. The scripture says: ‘If a household possesses the *Writings of the Three Sovereigns*, it will repel evil demons, pestilence, epidemics, and all manner of calamities. If someone is gravely ill and near death, a believer with sincere devotion to the Way may hold this text, and the person will not die. For a woman in difficult labor on the verge of death, holding this text will ensure the child is born safely. A Daoist seeking long life who enters the mountains with this text will be protected from tigers, wolves, mountain spirits, the Five Poisons, and all evil beings. One may cross rivers and seas without fear of flood dragons or wind and waves. Mastering its methods allows one to transform and summon laborers without needing to select auspicious days or locations. The household will be free from misfortune. If building a new house or tomb, one may write the *Writings of the Earth Sovereign* dozens of times, spread them on the ground, and observe the next day: where the yellow color appears, one may begin construction, and the household will surely prosper. When others are burying their dead, one may write the *Writings of the Human Sovereign*, include one’s own name, secretly place it in the coffin, and keep it hidden from others—this will protect the household from flying calamities and theft. If someone plots against you, the plot will rebound upon them.

Furthermore, after purifying oneself for a hundred days, one may summon the Celestial Gods, the Controller of Destinies, the Year Star, the Daily Patrol, the spirits of the Five Peaks, the Four Waterways, and the deities of altars and temples. They will appear in human form, and one may ask them about fortune and misfortune, safety and danger, and the causes of illnesses and demonic afflictions. There are also eighteen characters that, when placed in one’s clothing, allow one to cross rivers and seas without fear of wind or waves.

The *True Forms of the Five Peaks* can repel soldiers and prevent harm from traitors; those who seek to harm you will instead suffer calamity. Daoists occasionally obtain this text, but if they do not practice benevolence, compassion, and sincerity, or if they are impure and unrighteous, disaster will strike, and their families will be destroyed. This text must not be taken lightly.

As for methods of transformation, the greatest is the *Five Phases Record* of Master Mo, originally in five scrolls. Formerly, when the Lord Liu was about to ascend as an immortal, he extracted the essentials and condensed it into one scroll. Its methods involve elixirs and talismans, enabling one to fly and travel freely through the earth and skies. Smiling turns one into a woman; frowning, into an old man; squatting, into a child; holding a staff, into a tree; planting objects, into fruit-bearing trees. Drawing on the ground creates rivers; gathering soil forms mountains. One may conjure traveling kitchens, summon clouds, and ignite fires—there is nothing it cannot do.

Next is the *Subtle Writ of the Jade Maiden Grotto*, also in one scroll. It allows one to transform into flying birds and running beasts, as well as gold, wood, jade, and stone. It can summon clouds and create fog for a hundred miles. One may cross great waters without boats or bridges, divide one’s form into a thousand people, and fly high on the wind, entering and exiting without obstruction. One may exhale qi of seven colors, see the eight directions and the earth’s depths, emit light for ten thousand *zhang* to illuminate a dark room—this, too, is a great art. However, one must tread the paths of the stars through dozens of twists and turns, which few can master. The *Hongbao* and *Wanbi* of Huainan cannot compare to this text.

There is also the *Seven Transformations of the White Tiger*: take the skin of a white tiger killed on the third day of the third month, fresh camel blood, tiger blood, purple silk shoes, and duckweed. Combine and plant them on the third day of the third month. The first growth resembles garlic chives and bears fruit; plant this fruit, and with each generation, it will transform. After seven plantings, use the fruit to alter one’s form and appearance, allowing flight or submersion at will. This method is roughly similar to those of Master Mo and the *Subtle Writ of the Jade Maiden Grotto*; beyond this, there is nothing worth discussing.

This overview is for those who love the Way, so they may know the names of these extraordinary texts.

Master Zheng was not merely versed in the Five Classics and the Way of immortality; he also mastered the Nine Palaces, the Three Marvels, astronomical calculations, and the *He* and *Luo* writings. There was nothing he did not study deeply. In the first year of the *Da’an* era, foreseeing the chaos of the end times and the coming turmoil in the south, he carried his satchel of elixirs and entered the mountains. His disciples scattered eastward to Mount Huo, and none knew his whereabouts.

*Dispelling Delusions, Chapter Twenty*

The *Baopuzi* states: “One who seeks luminous pearls will not find the night-shining jewel of the black dragon in any but the depths of Hepu.”

To seek fine jade, one will not obtain a foot-long bi-disc worth a city’s ransom unless from the caves of Mount Jing. To receive instruction in the Dao, if one does not find the right master but lingers in hesitation, hoping for some gain, or clings to the wrong teacher, one will ultimately achieve nothing—wasting effort, hindering progress. Though one may later regret it bitterly, it will be too late.

Even in mundane, simple matters, one cannot gain knowledge by mere sitting; how much less so with the affairs of immortals and transcendents? Though one be sagely or wise, there is no way to comprehend them alone. They cannot be attained through prolonged reflection, nor sought by groping blindly. One must find a teacher who is both profoundly learned and broadly versed—yet even then, it is like scooping water from the vast sea or felling trees in an endless forest. The difficulty lies not in the scarcity of resources, but in one’s own limited strength.

What tigers and leopards leave behind, raccoons and rats fight over. What Tao Zhu discards, Yuan Xian lacks. If one’s teacher is narrow in vision and shallow in learning, their knowledge will be inherently limited, their insights meager and unremarkable. What they treasure and keep secret is already imperfect; if they further dilute their coarse teachings to instruct others, how can any achievement be possible?

It is like borrowing grain from the gates of Boyi and Shuqi, or seeking warmth from the household of Qian Lou—what one obtains will be no more than acorns and coarse cloth, never a feast of prime beef or robes of brocade and fox fur.

Some cling to mediocre teachers and never awaken; others, though fortunate enough to encounter a true master, fail to seek diligently. Such losses, born of unawareness, cannot be recovered. Discerning the depth of others is truly no easy task—this is why the ancients found it so difficult.
