White stone resembles jade, and the treacherous resemble the worthy. The worthy conceal themselves further, seeming as if they possess nothing, while the treacherous flaunt their emptiness as if it were substance. Without the utmost clarity, how can one distinguish between them?

Those who persist with mediocre teachers do not do so because they recognize their ignorance and choose to remain; rather, they genuinely believe such instruction is sufficient. Those who encounter a true master yet fail to serve them do not do so because they grasp the master’s depth and cannot bring themselves to ask; rather, they genuinely believe there is no difference.

For one who truly knows the essential Dao is without desire for worldly things, does not chase after worldly praise—how then would they display themselves to the common crowd?

The shallow and superficial, in their multitude, are often boastful and self-aggrandizing. They adopt stern expressions and rare tones to adorn their falsehoods, which suffice to delude and bewilder later learners. Boldly uttering grand claims, they declare they have ascended famous mountains and met immortals. Those who hear such words in haste, unable to calmly examine and verify them, seldom detect the deception.

In the past, I frequently encountered miscellaneous wandering Daoists flocking to the gates of the wealthy, instructing their followers to fabricate empty reputations, claiming they had already lived four or five hundred years. When someone happened to inquire about their age, they would feign ignorance, smiling and nodding vaguely, saying, "I must be eighty or ninety now." They would then boast, "I once fasted in the Hua Yin Mountains for fifty years, then spent forty years in the Song Mountains and Mount Shaoshi, followed by sixty years on Mount Tai. Later, I spent fifty years on Mount Ji with so-and-so." They recounted their experiences in such a way that, if one were to tally the years, they would indeed appear to have lived several hundred years. As a result, households fond of such tales would gather like smoke and mist, crowding at their doors.

Some practitioners of arts might occasionally possess innate abilities to perceive spirits and deities, and could somewhat divine others' futures or past events, though in truth they could neither bring harm nor benefit. They were like divining stalks or tortoise shells—when people witnessed their minor accuracies, they would hail them as divine beings, believing them omniscient. Others might excel in talismanic water, incantations, or exorcisms, achieving efficacy in dispelling malevolence, yet remain ignorant of the Way of immortality. Some might practice miscellaneous arts and perceive spirits or anomalies, but such abilities did not prolong their lifespan. When questioned about the Way of the Golden Elixir, they were largely ignorant. Upon closer examination, one found that many deceived the world to amass wealth, stopping at nothing. These individuals, though differing in method from thieves who break in and steal, shared the same destination.

Those who rely on empty words are not as credible as those whose deeds provide tangible evidence. To warn later learners and expose such deceptions, one may examine verifiable falsehoods.

In the past, there was a man named Gu Qiang who practiced herbal remedies and somewhat followed the methods of Rong Cheng and the mysteries of plain silk. At over eighty years of age, he remained sharp and not overly frail. People of the time thus called him an immortal or the "Thousand-Year-Old Man." When Governor Ji of Yangzhou heard of him, he tested and welcomed him to Yidu. Upon arrival, Gu Qiang coughed and hesitated, as if his knowledge were vast but not fully expressed. Enthusiasts then amplified his reputation, and followers gathered like echoes responding to sound or shadows clinging to form, crowding around him. Offerings and tributes flowed incessantly, and his wealth rivaled even that of Luan Da, who was esteemed in the former Han. He regularly consumed asparagus root, which revealed that his body had never ingested the Golden Elixir or great medicines.

Gu Qiang had some acquaintance with books and was familiar with historical anecdotes. He claimed to be four thousand years old and spoke without shame of having seen Yao, Shun, Yu, and Tang, asserting they were exactly as described. The common saying that Yao had eight-colored eyebrows was incorrect—his eyebrows merely stood upright like the character "eight." Yao was tall and handsome, with a fine beard, and could drink over two *hu* of wine in a day. Later generations exaggerated this to a thousand goblets, but he could not have managed such a feat. I myself witnessed him thoroughly drunk on several occasions. Though a sage, as he aged, his governance declined compared to his younger years. When he saw the Four Assassins and the Eight Elemental Ministers, he relied on Shun.

Shun was merely a poor orphan from a humble family, yet possessed extraordinary talent. He plowed on Mount Li, fished in Lei Marsh, and made pottery by the Yellow River, but no one recognized his uniqueness. I saw that wherever he went, he transformed the people through virtue. His eyes had double pupils, and I knew this was the sign of a greatly noble countenance. I often encouraged and comforted him, saying, "Do not worry about not becoming wealthy and noble—your great virtue is nearing its culmination. The mandate of heaven will surely be passed to you. If not you, then who?" His father was utterly obstinate, and his brother was particularly wicked, constantly seeking to kill Shun. I once admonished them, saying, "This child will prosper your family and bring blessings to the world. You must not treat him carelessly." Soon after, he received the abdication, and I recalled my words as having been proven true.

I also said that when Confucius's mother was sixteen or seventeen, I divined that she would bear a noble son. When Zhongni was born, he was truly an extraordinary person—nine feet and six inches tall, his head resembling Yao's, his neck like Gao Yao's, and his eyebrows like Zichan's. From the waist down, he was three inches shorter than Yu. Though poor and orphaned, as a child he already loved ritual vessels. I knew he would surely accomplish great things. When he grew up, his lofty discourse astonished people, and learners from near and far followed him, with thousands enrolling to study. I delighted in his words and visited him several times, regretting only that my lack of learning prevented me from engaging in profound discussion with him.

He often urged me to study the *Book of Changes*, saying, "This is an excellent book. I privately cherish it, having worn out three sets of bamboo slips and broken three iron awls." Now I have finally grasped its meaning. In the fourteenth year of Duke Ai of Lu, a qilin was captured in the west and died. Confucius asked me about it, and I told him this was not an auspicious omen. Confucius wept sorrowfully. Later, he had an ill-omened dream and wished to see me. It was the fourth month, and the heat was oppressive, so I could not go. Soon after, I heard he had fallen ill for seven days before passing away. To this day, I vaguely recall his countenance.

I also said that when the First Emperor of Qin took me to Pengcheng and retrieved the tripods of the Zhou era, I told him, "These tripods are divine objects. They emerge when virtue is present and sink when the Way is lost. If you cultivate yourself, they will come of their own accord, but they cannot be obtained by force." The First Emperor was greatly displeased with me, yet when he tried to drag them out, they could not be moved. He then apologized, saying, "You truly are a man of far-reaching insight."

I also clearly recounted the affairs of Emperor Gaozu of Han and Xiang Yu—such matters are too numerous to record in full. People at the time recognized them as jokes, yet when ordinary people heard them, they believed his words. Moreover, Gu Qiang grew increasingly senile, forgetting things. Governor Ji of Yangzhou once gave him a jade cup. Later, Gu suddenly told Ji, "Master Anqi once bestowed this upon me." He later fell ill and died at Huang Zheng's home in Shouchun. Huang suspected he had transformed and departed, but after a year, he opened the coffin and found Gu's body still intact. All these were cases of fame without substance, causing the world to disbelieve in the existence of immortals, for such impostors confuse truth with falsehood.

Wu Wen, the Prefect of Chengdu, spoke of Cai Dan from Wuyuan, who loved the Dao but could not find a worthy teacher. He neglected his family and career, reciting day and night the *Yellow Court Classic*, the *Central Scripture of Supreme Clarity*, the *Observation of Heavenly Nodes*, and other non-essential texts from various schools, his mouth never ceasing. He believed the Dao was exhausted in these recitations, yet ultimately did not know how to apply them. He merely admired their florid rhetoric and, when meeting others, would teach them to simply read the texts a thousand times to grasp their meaning. After a long time, his family suffered hardship, their clothing and food depleted, yet he could not surpass others. Ashamed and resentful, with no way to justify himself, he abandoned his home, declaring he had achieved the immortal Dao.

Thus fleeing to a remote mountain range in another realm, he knew nothing of gathering herbs or plants that could sustain him without grain. He could only sell firewood to exchange for clothing and food. For three years he endured hunger, cold, and hardship, yet when recognized by others, he would feign ignorance. Eventually unable to bear it, he returned home—blackened, emaciated, his bones protruding, no longer resembling a man. His family asked where he had been, and he could not admit he had failed to attain immortal Dao. Instead, he deceived them, saying, “I have not yet ascended to heaven, but have become an earthbound immortal. My rank is still low, and I must serve the senior immortals. Gradually, I will be promoted.

“Previously, I was tasked by Lord Lao to tend several dragons. One was a mottled dragon, five-colored and the finest—it was Lord Lao’s usual mount. I neglected my duty, playing dice with the junior immortals, and lost track of this dragon. It vanished without a trace. For this offense, I was punished and sent to the foot of Kunlun Mountain to weed and hoe three or four *qing* of land, where the plants grew densely but were overrun with brambles. The labor was indescribably arduous. By law, I must serve ten years before being pardoned. Master Poxuan, Wang Qiao, and other immortals came to inspect my work. They pleaded on my behalf and even helped me, allowing me to return home temporarily to cultivate further and seek liberation. Thus, I will grow old and die here.

“When I first returned, people asked about Kunlun. How did I answer? Heaven is not measured by its height in miles—when you look up, it is no more than a dozen *zhang* away. There grows the *muhe* tree, four *zhang* and nine *chi* tall, its ears heavy as cartloads. There are trees of pearl and jade, *shatang*, *langgan*, and *bigui*, as well as jade plums, jade pears, and jade peaches. Their fruits resemble ordinary plums and peaches, but they are luminous, translucent, and hard. One must wash them with water from a jade well to soften them before eating. When the wind rises, the branches, flowers, and leaves of the pearl and jade trees strike one another, producing the five notes of music—clear and mournful, stirring the heart. I saw exiled immortals who had heard of this, and none could help but feel sorrowful and grieve.

“I also saw that atop Kunlun Mountain, each circuit has four hundred and forty gates, each gate four *li* wide. Within are five cities and twelve towers. Below lie green dragons, white tigers, giant serpents, and pythons stretching over a hundred *li*, their mouths and fangs as large as three-hundred-*hu* vats. There are wasps a *zhang* long, whose venom can kill an elephant. There are divine beasts—lions, *bixie*, celestial deer, *jiaoyang*, copper-headed, iron-browed creatures with long fangs and chisel teeth—thirty-six kinds in all. If one knows their names, no evil spirits or beasts in the world will dare harm them.

“The deities there include the Headless One, Lord Daojing, Master Xilu, Lord Zhonghuang, and Minister of Deer Gate, Zhang Yang, styled Ziyuan. They guard the jade palaces and do not carry Lord Lao’s bamboo tally. Those without matching tallies on their left and right cannot enter. Five rivers flow from the mountain’s edges, and Weak Water encircles it—feathers cannot float upon it, and birds cannot fly over it. Only immortals may cross. Above dwell divine birds and horses—*youchang*, *jiaoxian*, *tenghuang*, *jiguang*—all capable of human speech and immortal. Truly, it is a joyous and swift immortal realm. I regret I could not roam there freely.”

At the time, many who heard Cai Dan’s words believed them clearly. Moreover, in Puban of Hedong, there was a man named Xiang Fangdu who, along with his son, entered the mountains to study immortality. After ten years, they returned home. When his family asked why, Fangdu said, “After three years of focused cultivation in the mountains, an immortal came to welcome me. Together, we mounted a dragon and ascended to heaven. For a long while, I looked down, but the earth was already distant. The dragon flew swiftly, its head raised and tail lowered, making me feel perilously unstable on its back. When we reached heaven, we first passed the Purple Palace, where golden beds and jade tables gleamed brilliantly—truly a place of nobility.

“The immortals gave me only a cup of *liuxia* to drink, and I was no longer hungry or thirsty. Suddenly, I longed for home. When I appeared before the Heavenly Emperor, I lost my composure in reverence and was reprimanded, sent back to cultivate further. Only after accumulating more merit may I return.”

“In the past, King Liu An of Huainan ascended to heaven and met the Heavenly Emperor. He sat arrogantly, calling himself ‘the solitary one,’ and was demoted to guard the Heavenly Kitchen for three years. Who am I compared to him?” Thus, in Hedong, Fangdu was called the “Reprimanded Immortal.” Many such frauds exist in the world, their deceptions varied and numerous—one cannot fail to examine them closely.

These absurd tales are so blatant, yet some still do not recognize their falsity. How much less can they discern subtler deceptions? Fraudsters often embellish their lies with plausible details. Without keen discernment, who can swiftly expose them?

There are also those who falsely claim to be renowned Daoist masters of past eras, like Bai He, who was said to have lived eight thousand seven hundred years before appearing in the world. Then, suddenly, he vanished without a trace. In Luozhong, there was a Daoist well-versed in many arts, skilled in numerology and alchemy. He posed difficult questions to Bai He, who answered them all effortlessly, leaving no doubts. Thus, he was regarded as a man of profound knowledge—though no one knew his true age, they believed he was nearly a thousand years old. Later, he vanished without a trace.

Then, a man in Hebei claimed to be Bai He, and people from far and near flocked to serve him, bestowing gifts that made him wealthy. When Bai He’s disciples heard of his reappearance, they rejoiced and went to see him, only to confirm he was an imposter. The man fled.

The Five Classics and Four Divisions have become like straw dogs, discarded lees—mere traces left by feet, not the feet themselves. Books are the works of sages, not the sages themselves. Yet Confucians travel a thousand *li* with satchels to seek teachers. How much more should one diligently pursue the Way of long life, which true immortals hold in such esteem? Yet one must carefully distinguish truth from falsehood.

I fear that frauds like Gu Qiang, Cai Dan, Xiang Fangdu, and Bai He will never vanish from the world. Those who delight in such matters should heed this book and refine their judgment of good and bad.

The *Immortals’ Classic* states that the pupils of immortals’ eyes are square. In Luozhong, I saw Bai Zhongli, whose pupils were indeed square—truly an extraordinary man.

**Appendix:**
Nurturing the essence of the embryo and stabilizing the spirit are akin to guarding the primordial *qi*. One must merely refrain from obstructing the breath’s egress and ingress.

Those who regularly practice may inhale *qi* through the nose at the time of its generation, then exhale through the mouth, expelling two parts while retaining one. They may then forcefully swallow this *qi*, causing a muffled sound in the throat—this is not the *qi* of the primordial embryo, but merely the ingestion of coarse *qi*. Coarse *qi* resides in the abdomen, yet does not dwell with the primordial *qi*. Coarse *qi* is the *qi* of panting breath.

Though the primordial *qi* is scant and difficult to disperse, it does not rely on the ingress and egress of coarse *qi*. Moreover, one should not even wish to hear one’s own breathing—how much less should swallowing produce sound? When the inhaled *qi* is coarse, it injures the lungs, for the lungs are the canopy of the five viscera, and *qi* descends to reach them first.

Those who ingest the primordial *qi* do not follow the coarse *qi* in its exit and entry, thus transcending the moments of *qi*’s generation and depletion. Yet when one forces coarse *qi* into the viscera of primordial *qi*, it instead harms the person. All who exert strength do so through the *qi* of the multitude—meaning the *qi* of myriad things and the essences of food and drink. This *qi* of the multitude can only lift heavy objects, traverse great distances, or move the body; it cannot prolong life when retained, nor shorten it when expelled. Why, then, should one prohibit or restrict it?

The art of employing *qi* is merely the use of coarse *qi*, which can shift mountains, part rivers and seas, subdue tigers and leopards, and ward off bandits. Thus, the *qi* of the multitude is inferior to coarse *qi*, and coarse *qi* may be dispensed with, but the primordial *qi* must not be allowed to depart.

To preserve *qi* is to preserve the primordial *qi*, not the *qi* of the multitude or the coarse *qi*. When the primordial *qi* fills the viscera, coarse *qi* is naturally expelled, and one may move with coarse *qi* alone, without need of the *qi* of the multitude. Those who practice abstention fear the steaming of the five viscera by grain *qi*, hence their avoidance of it. Yet once one cultivates the art of *qi*, grain *qi* is naturally dispelled—even if one eats nine meals in a day, it cannot become harmful, and even if one fasts for an entire year, it cannot cause emaciation or distress. Thus, the Dao of *qi* is profound indeed!

Daoist exercises do not depend on establishing names, imitating forms, adorning appearances, or depicting images in diagrams. They are nameless and formless—whether stretching or bending, bowing or raising, walking or lying, leaning or standing, pacing or strolling slowly, chanting or resting—all are Daoist exercises. One need not perform them every morning; whenever the body feels unwell, one may practice them. In all cases, one should hold the breath, for holding the breath causes the *qi* to surge and circulate.

One need not wait to count breaths; when the *qi* feels nearly exhausted, first draw in a small amount through the nose, then exhale through the mouth. Since the *qi* has been held for some time, it will surge against the throat. If one does not first draw in more *qi* before exhaling through the mouth, the *qi* will become coarse and injure the lungs.

One should cease the practice once the illness is cured, and not induce sweating, for sweat leaves one vulnerable to wind and agitation. When practicing Daoist exercises, the joints may produce sounds—great movements yield great sounds, while small movements yield small sounds—this is the relaxation of sinews and the circulation of *qi*.

Daoist exercises treat illnesses before they arise and harmonize *qi* that is not at ease. When activated, the hundred passages flow smoothly; when held, the blood of the three palaces congeals. Truly, they are the great discipline of nurturing life and the wondrous art of dispelling illness.

*Baopuzi Inner Chapters*, Scroll Four
*From the Daoist Canon, Baopuzi Inner and Outer Chapters*

From the *Imperially Commissioned Complete Library of the Four Treasuries*, *Baopuzi Outer Chapters*, Scroll One
*Ge Hong of the Jin Dynasty*

**“Secluded Excellence”**

The Master Baopuzi said: There was one who cherished the Recluse of the Mound—he disdained the restless wandering of the worldly, lamented the bitter toil of grasping and spitting forth, and relinquished lofty aspirations in the land of rivers and seas. Thus he tilled the soil himself upon the saline-alkali flats, concealing the six stratagems within his breast like a sealed sack, harboring jade-like brilliance yet never revealing it. His pure voice went unheard, his radiant artistry unseen by the world. He turned his back on the morning splendor of vermilion gates, preserving tranquility within his thatched hut, cutting off all ties with the golden halls of the Jin and Zhang clans, and nurturing his vast *qi* among the company of secluded worthies. He deemed glory and eminence misfortune, and jade and silk no more than grass and earth. His spirit soared beyond the clouds, defying decrees to embrace the ways of high antiquity. Sheltered by the towering peaks of lofty mountains, he rested upon the fragrant mat of orchids and thoroughwort, rinsed his mouth with the limpid essence of rosy clouds, and ingested the refined essence of the eight minerals. His thoughts were as boundless as if dwelling at the edge of the rainbow, his intent as ethereal as if abiding in the realm of inverted shadows. The myriad things could not disturb his harmony; the four seas were insufficient to agitate his spirit.

Thereupon, the scion of the Qi clan, moved by this, sighed in lament: “That a steed of unmatched stature should dwell in an empty valley is a disgrace to Sun Yang. That a talent of surpassing brilliance should be left behind in times of peace is the fault of those who recognize true worth. How can we allow such an outstanding man to preserve his solitary integrity, thereby depriving the sagely court of his presence?”

Would the virtue of the great Yu and the wisdom of the Duke of Zhou ever find their use? Thereupon, he approached and expounded:

“Merely to hear of one who flaps his wings and tenses his frame yet cannot soar through the nine skies, who plods toward the farthest reaches of the empyrean yet merely orders the common norms—such a one is no hero of towering stature. Now, sir, you wield resolute judgment as if grasping a gleaming blade, and your writings, resplendent as brocade, surpass even the rarest of texts. Your ornate phrases rival the flocks of birds and beasts, your dragon-like patterns emerge in the darkest night, your treasures of jade and coral lie hidden in the abyss, you lie dormant in the height of summer, and conceal your brilliance in the midst of spring. Though you lower your curtain to delve deep in thought, exhaust your brush in florid prose, subtly elucidate the Supreme Ultimate, and expound the primordial origin—when your words bring joy, even wooden effigies smile; when they bring sorrow, even clay figures weep bitterly. Yet if you weigh lightly, you are like a swan’s feather sinking in the Weak Waters; if you bear heavily, you are like jade and stone swept away by raging waves. In separation, even liver and gall become as distant as Hu and Yue; in union, the myriad differences merge into one harmony. In incisive discourse, you are as stern as autumn frost; in gentle speech, as tender as ice melting into blossoms. When you topple the lofty, even the highest peaks crumble; when you elevate the lowly, even the deepest pools rise in grandeur. Sheltering the pure, you lean upon the outer light of darkness; purifying the turbid, you instantly clarify the Yellow River.

Yet you cannot bestow great kindness upon the common folk, nor inscribe illustrious merit in the annals of the imperial house. Your name will vanish like morning dew, your body will dissolve like the ephemeral mayfly. In an instant, you may be exalted to the treasure of sages, yet forget the fleeting passage of time, lamenting the approach of old age. Such a path is not one you should choose.

I have heard that the greatest are Heaven and Earth, next are ruler and minister, and the former sages, grieving for their times, sought to enact the Dao, restless even three months without a lord. Now, our sagely sovereign is of one mind with Yao and Shun, pitying the common people so that every household may be enfeoffed. Thus, some bear the tripod and ascend like dragons, others strike their horns and sing like phoenixes—no need to await the rush-wheeled carriage before acting, no need to wait for King Wen before rising. From the outset, the Five Phases wax and wane with the seasons. In advancement, there is the benefit of a clear path; in retreat, no entanglement like a wet fox’s tail. The wise preserve themselves, while those who propagate transformation succor the common folk. Let them follow the orchid’s fragrance and bend its stem, bathe in the limpid waves to dispel sorrow—like Shen Jing responding to the bright mirror, or the square and round conforming to compass and rule. Thus, merit reaches above and below, and grace extends to the eight directions. This is why the Tang dynasty stood so majestic, why Chonghua ruled with reverence, why the Lord of the West divided the realm, why King Fa revolutionized the age, why Huan and Wen unified the land, and why Gaozu of Han answered Heaven’s call. Never has there been a time when the gathering of worthies did not serve as the six pinions, or the reliance on heroes did not serve as the boat and oars.

If each were to cling to the loftiness of those who ‘wash their ears,’ or hold to the role of the plowman, then the transformative teachings of antiquity would not be established, the splendor of enlightened governance would not shine forth, the songs of wise rulers and good ministers would not be composed, and the vast net of Heaven would not be cast. Therefore, those who conceal their talents value adaptability; those who excel in seclusion prize the revelation of their uniqueness. In times of chaos, to hide one’s radiance and obscure one’s light in the clouds is to leave the brilliance of Heaven unmanifest; to sheathe the tiger’s fangs and grasp its horns is to leave its power to rend and tear unexpressed. If the great sword Tai’e lies hidden and unstruck, its decisive might remains unseen. If the swift Lü’er keeps its hooves still and does not gallop, its wind-chasing speed remains unapparent. To remain silent is to be no different from Zigong when mute; to close one’s eyes is to be no different from Li Zhu when blind.

Sir, you purify yourself yet overlook the disorder of the great moral order; you find contentment yet forget the duty to bring peace to those above. You remain bound by the mechanism of existence, yet leave no enduring name like metal or stone. Even the common man would flush with indignation—how could a man of true refinement remain indifferent?

When the carpenter’s line is applied, the wood straightens; when Zheng music advances, the elegant withers. When Youyu was raised, the four evils were executed; when Confucius entered, the young Shaoyu was vanquished. The thunderous drum startles even the owl; when the sun rises, the firefly’s glow fades into obscurity. If you do not rescue those afflicted by the ‘summoning of souls,’ how can you display the extraordinary skill of the physician of Yue? If you do not aid a world beset by calamities, how can you reveal your uncommon and far-reaching capacity?

To sit in lofty repose and watch others drown is not the way of one who aids the world; to hold the Dao yet mislead the state is not the task of a true leader. If you wait for the grasses of mid-Tang to sprout in the morning sun, or the phoenix’s song to resound at dawn, if you await the solitary-horned beast to stand in the suburbs or the interlinked trees to cluster in the wild, then the long banners will remain furled, the weapons of war will stay sheathed, and Shaobo will retire from his achievements—who then will lift you up?

Alas, how deeply you have sunk! I earnestly hope, sir, that you will not delay, but swiftly return.”

Thus, Master Huai-Bing gazed afar in serene detachment, his spirit roaming the celestial thoroughfares, his emotions and thoughts drifting into boundless distance—seeming as though nothing existed beside him. He lowered his gaze and replied:

Alas, have such words truly been spoken? The perfected person acts through non-action, dwelling in the void of the spirit, never enslaving their will to emolument or gain. Thus, harm and humiliation cannot touch them. They do not hesitate on perilous paths, so stumbling and downfall cannot become their affliction. Though coarse greens fail to suffice, their mind finds ease in a feast of a hundred delicacies; though they rank among the common and the lowly, their heart rejoices as if they ruled a kingdom. They sleep in the huts of Yi Liao, close the gates of Gan Mu, befriend Zhuangzi and Liezi, and dwell in humble lanes—steadfast as a mountain, unshaken. How could they harbor any concern for expansion or withdrawal?

Desire, when broadened, muddies harmony, so they relinquish worldly affairs without entanglement. Those at the pinnacle of rank bear profound anxieties, so they turn from power and profit without lingering doubt. Their nobility does not depend on titles, their wealth does not rely on riches. They companion with clouds and roc-birds in lofty flight, never entangling their wings in the stench of rotting rats. They find true abundance in frequent emptiness, never altering their joy in a simple gourd and bamboo bowl.

Moreover, the Way of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi stretches into the distant past, yet human life flashes by like a white colt glimpsed through a crack—swift as a shooting star’s fleeting appearance, fleeting as a flying arrow’s lightning passage. Why not roam freely in self-contentment? How could one torment the body for the sake of external things?

The kite does not entangle itself in nets; the unicorn does not fall into traps. Even birds and beasts know to avoid peril—how can those who wander in the dust fail to take heed? Consider Yao Li, who destroyed his family to render service; Ji Xin, who rose in flames to deceive Chu; Chen Jia, who slit his throat to prove his brother’s loyalty; Zhong You, who threw away his life to fulfill a pledge; Ying Men, who fell on his sword to lose his mind; Nie Zheng, who was moved by a zither’s lament to slaughter a lord; Jing Ke, who severed his shin to repay Yan and Chu; and Gong Han, who received a severed head in grief. All were the mad delusions of the profoundly foolish—how could the supremely wise ever choose such paths?

Those with generous stipends bear heavy responsibilities; those with exalted rank endure mental toil. Thus, Zhuangzi fished with his line and ignored the honor of ministerial office; Bo Yi plowed with his plowshare and disdained the loftiness of feudal lords. Yang Zhu found peace in the butcher’s stall; the petty seekers of advantage remain blind to what is truly desirable. They gather without choosing the right tree; they serve without assessing the times. Greedily advancing, they do not consider the disaster of overreaching; accepting office, they do not weigh their inability to bear the burden. When discussing honor and nobility, they invoke Yi Yin and the Duke of Zhou to save the drowning; when speaking of the perils of overreaching, they conceal the overturned tripod and refuse to remember. They watch for the river dragon to sleep so they may pluck its luminous pearl; they bask in the favor of the imperial measure, hoping for freedom from calamity. They indulge in the fleeting comfort of the water clock, blind to the inevitable danger. They covet the ephemeral glory of the morning mushroom, yet aspire to the longevity of the great tree. It is like treading thin ice in anticipation of summer’s heat, or climbing a rotten branch in expectation of a fierce wind. The deep-sea fish is lured by fragrant bait; the marsh pheasant swallows poisonous grain. They chew spoiled meat to fill their hunger, and guzzle poisoned wine to quench their thirst.

In ancient times, when Jizi saw ivory chopsticks, he wept; when Confucius heard the sound of an ominous bell, he sighed deeply. For they traced the beginnings to foresee the ends, and thus knew what was to come. Yet how can those who blindly step into the trap perceive what lies ahead? How can the allure of profit and desire grow ever more intense?

King Cheng of Zhou was virtuous, yet believed slander; the Duke of Zhou was sagely, yet fled to the southern lands of Chu. He entrusted his lament to the owl’s cry and barely escaped through the sealed golden casket. If even such enlightened rulers, who appear but once in an age, could face displeasure and slander at any moment, how much more so for others? Virtue does not rise like a violent wind to revive withered grain; affairs do not become weighty merely by bearing jade tablets. Alas, tears and lamentations come too late—how could it be otherwise?

When gradual infiltration persists, even the strongest glue and lacquer dissolve; when prolonged soaking continues, even bone and flesh grow estranged. The accumulation of dust and feathers sinks ships and snaps axles; the repetition of slanderous words turns rumors into tigers. Jiang Chong, though base, was not kin to the crown prince; the stepmother, though a mere successor, was not close to Bo Qi. Yet the false accusation of digging up roots destroyed the bond of father and son; the deception of a singing cricket shattered the love of natural affection. How much less can one be certain of others?

Alas, Wu Yun remained loyal yet his corpse drifted in the river; Bai Qi upheld righteousness yet slit his own throat. For when one sees through to the core, the heart grows cold—how could even a master craftsman not be confounded?

Moreover, I would seek to extend my meager talents to echo the crowd, relying on my solitary correctness to reprove the many. Yet if I do not see that though metal can overcome wood, an awl cannot fell the forest of Deng; though water can conquer fire, a ladleful cannot extinguish a mountain ablaze; a drop of glue cannot clarify the Yellow River’s turbidity; a sliver of ice cannot cool the scorching heat of Xiao Qiu—then how can I not know that those who preserve both body and name are exceedingly rare, while those who first rejoice and later wail are many?

To fear the perils of overreaching yet not extinguish the desire for glory; to dread disgrace yet not dispel the impulse to contend—is like fearing dampness yet swimming in the deep abyss, hating one’s shadow yet avoiding all shade, drilling a hole in the boat to stop a leak, or adding fuel to quell boiling. The seven-foot frame is the vessel of life—one cannot return it maimed. The heart within the breast is governed by oneself—one cannot let it drift into dissipation.

Plow the fields to eat; dig a well to drink. Wear coarse cloth to cover yourself; dwell in a humble hut for shelter. Sing and recite for joy; breathe deeply to prolong life. Roam freely among bamboo and silk; entrust your feelings to the brush of the Dao. Maintain constancy and await the end—this is enough.

Moreover, when the Dao is present, one is exalted; when virtue prevails, one is noble. To cast aside a luminous pearl to shoot a sparrow—only a fool would do such a thing. Why must one seek prominence through power or wait for stipends to be sated?

Those who are content in poverty consider the absence of wealth as riches; those who delight in humility regard not serving as honor. Thus, Ruan Ji floated on the sea to purify his spirit; Huzi was content to retire and plow. Pang Degong suffered punishment for his virtue; Xin and Wu were ensnared by charges of great merit. A single branch suffices to shelter the phoenix’s feathers—why need a vast forest? A shallow pool is enough to float the dragon’s scales—why require the deep sea? Coarse greens surpass the eight delicacies; a cold spring surpasses fine wine. Straw sandals outshine red shoes; a patched robe rivals imperial robes. Clasping one’s hands brings more ease than leaning on a staff; the rustling of branches brings more joy than strings and bamboo. A thatched cottage outshines vermilion pillars; uncarved rafters are more precious than ornate beams. Ascending Song Peak as one’s terrace; sheltering beneath a rocky overhang as one’s mansion. Accumulating writings as one’s granary; treasuring discourse on the Dao as gold and jade. Abandon the petty attachments of ordinary men; diminish the desires of the mediocre. Roam the nine marshes in joy; dispel worldly wisdom to transcend the vulgar. Like the inchworm, conceal your light; uphold simplicity and display clumsiness. Know when to stop and be content. Then, savor the fragrance of magic mushrooms; let your spirit soar with the floating clouds. Bask in the radiance of the nine suns; attach your wings to the lofty parasol tree. Look down to gather on the sacred isles—how could this compare to driving a chariot and crouching in the mud, or being draped in embroidered robes like an ox at sacrifice?

Lord Qi Shi declared: To enter but never emerge is to be mired in favor and forget retreat; to depart but never return is to abandon office without righteousness. Thus the enlightened, knowing the body is not their own possession, entrust themselves to circumstance. Whether rising in prominence or dwelling in obscurity, whether speaking or silent, they hold nothing as fixed. When silk bundles fill the courtyard and torchlight blazes in the hall, the noble man’s Dao prevails. When fortune smiles like the sun at midday, they act. But when the age is plunged into the misfortunes of yang’s ninth phase, when slander triumphs, they do not step beyond their gate, sealing their mouths like a tied sack—useless. Dragons rise and phoenixes retreat, adapting to the times. This is why the ancients sometimes fled peril and chaos, refusing to enter, or took flight at the first sign of danger, not lingering even a day: they feared the fire on Mount Wu might consume orchid and mugwort alike.

Now the sagely emperor governs the age, the world is level and the Dao at peace. Benevolence extends to all living things, his virtuous influence spreads far and wide, his awe-inspiring might reaches the four quarters. The nine realms are drenched in his grace; his earthly virtue bears all things with profound capacity. He solidifies the celestial vault with towering majesty. In divine transformation, clouds drift and rain descends; in mystical beneficence, primordial qi swells boundlessly. The four gates stand solemn and vast, welcoming all; the sovereign’s thought embraces the heroic and free, fostering good governance. This is a rare opportunity, a once-in-a-millennium turning point—and yet you, sir, cling to the lopsided path of reclusion, blind to the perils of excessive restraint.

You focus solely on cultivating inner strength like Shan Bao, never considering the threat of a tiger’s assault from without.

Should one hear of a man drowning while fording a stream and thus declare all boats doomed to fail, or witness the treachery of Shang Chen and conclude that no heir to the throne is trustworthy—such reasoning is flawed. Master Huai-bing replied: “The flourishing of sagely transformation truly accords with your lofty discourse, yet the matter of withdrawal or engagement is a matter of individual inclination. When Yao and Shun reigned above, the Jishan and Yingyin valleys still sheltered recluses who dwelt in nests; when the Xia sovereigns governed the age, the remote gorges harbored worthies who grasped their ploughs. Did they truly fear such dangers? No—each simply followed what brought them peace.

Thus, one may exalt his resolve and refuse to serve kings and marquises, upholding the hexagrams’ symbols as a common man’s duty. Yan Zhou preserved his integrity, and the sages approved. As for me, I wander carefree among hills and gardens, withdrawing my traces to the wilds. My choice stems from this truth: my talents are not those of a statesman, and I lack the capacity to govern the people. Now, when a host of scholars arises like clouds, when the eminent and wise gather like scales, when the halls are filled with civil and martial worthies, and all affairs are at peace—why should I seek to cast my flickering light among the sun and moon, or strike a earthen jar beside a mighty bell? To offer a light fan in the season of solid ice, or a fur coat in the height of summer, would only hasten ridicule for untimely uselessness.

Yet if I devote myself to studying the classics and composing writings, I may preserve my authenticity, establish my name, and benefit the later age. If I force myself into what I cannot bear, I shall only stumble and fall, inviting blame and regret. Thus, like the little fox, I dwell in my strengths to safeguard my weaknesses. Though I possess no merits from serving at court nor toils from campaigning at the frontier, I still refine the young, cultivate the Dao, and nurture governance—different paths leading to the same end. I am no rigidly aloof man.

Even the humblest may preserve his single integrity—how could I compare to Xu You? In this sagely age, tolerance is extended, and I am granted the same latitude as the recluses of the Tang. Is this not enough? Prince Qishi, suddenly abashed, composed himself and changed his expression, saying: “Sir, your words uphold teaching and aid instruction; your prose exposes deceit and resists perversion. You champion quietude to curb the restless competition of the age, and revive Confucian learning to mend the broken thread of discourse. Who but a man of emergence could order the constant norms? Who but a dragon could instruct the young? Under heaven, all are subjects—must only those who wear court caps and hold tablets be deemed right, while those who delight in hunger at rustic gates be deemed wrong?

Are the masses not lost in delusion? Those wandering the vastness of Yunmeng must rely on the Southern Pointer to know the Way; those adrift on the boundless sea must gaze upon the North Star to find their return. Now, having heard your noble instruction, I perceive my blindness. I shall don my robes and cap, take up my humble steed, and aspire to the raft of universal love, advancing without prejudice in my choices.”

**The Recluse, Part Two**

The *Baopuzi* states: “In times past, I wandered...”

On Cloud Terrace Mountain, I encountered a recluse and found an official in his presence. The official declared: “With radiant clarity above, the sovereign governs the Eight Extremes, uniting Hua and Yi in common allegiance, while distant lands offer tribute. Yet you, sir, roam the remote wilds of cypress and pine, forsaking the martial and virtuous, abandoning the multitudes like birds scattering. Times change, customs shift, and worldly affairs no longer bind—yet those who eat bark and dwell in mountains, casting off external burdens, were once deemed pure and lofty, while now they are but fugitives in flight.

“The noble man perceives danger before it takes shape and severs calamity at its inception. Is it not so that one avoids the inner peril of Zhang Yi, yet grows complacent in the outer harm of Shan Bao? Fearful of fullness, he resists contemplation; unmindful of the chaos near at hand, he shuns the shallow perils of ox tracks only to plunge into the unfathomable blades of a hundred knives. He flees the muddy shallows where feet may wet, yet casts himself into the furnace’s fire, all unawares.”

The recluse replied: “He who sharpens his resolve on fledgling mice knows not the purpose of the *zōuyú*; he who triumphs in gleaning courtyard grains cannot fathom the distant aim of the phoenix and simurgh. Like the scorched cicada mocking the cloud-soaring *peng*, or the morning mushroom marveling at the great *chun* tree; like the pit-frog doubting the sea or the snake deriding the dragon—such is your joy and fear in urging or dissuading.

“Ah, do you truly grasp the lofty rhythm of the profound and vast?”

“Fortunate am I to have been born in the age of Yao and Shun—what worry have I that I may not attain this man’s resolve?

The official replied: ‘Of old, the proud and aloof Hua Shi refused to contend for rank, dwelling beyond the sea’s edge, yet the Grand Duke executed him. Is not your seclusion, sir, perilous indeed?’

The recluse answered: ‘Lu Shang excelled in waging war but fell short in governing the state. He could not emulate the profound Yellow Emperor, whose virtue embraced heaven and earth, nor match the vastness of the seas and mountains in honoring worthies and esteeming virtue. Delighting in nurturing talent, he yet took pleasure in execution, neglecting benevolence and righteousness. Thus, the calamity of his slaughter took root from the moment of his enfeoffment. When the Duke of Zhou heard of this, he knew his state would not endure.

‘For attack and defense demand different dispositions; the Dao prizes adaptability. Yet Lu Shang lacked the art of boiling scales to draw forth distant steeds, nor did he possess the reins to guide far-reaching governance. He thrust forward the tactics of battle arrays, loathing those of lofty integrity—relying on armor to perfect his blades, and further, on swimming to shoot fleeing foes. Could he truly have hoped to aim at the mark with such measures?

‘Tilt the nest of common birds, and the numinous phoenix will not alight; drain the pool of fish and turtles, and the divine dragon will flee afar. Rip open the wombs of ordinary beasts, and the qilin will not stand in their fields. Harm even a single scholar, and the heroic will not tread your land. Lu Shang founded a dynasty and bequeathed its legacy to posterity, yet he unfurled the banner of harsh cruelty, unsealed the casket of savagery—just enough to drive the talented to serve other states, to chase away the worthy and leave them to enemy hands.

‘How far removed is this from those who bought the bones of a dead steed to attract swift hooves, or bowed in humble lanes to repel Qin’s armies! What say you, sir—how does Lu Shang compare to the Duke of Zhou?’

The scholar replied: ‘I cannot judge.’

The recluse said: ‘The Duke of Zhou, a great sage, humbled himself before the lowly: he would spit out his food and grasp his hair, fearing to lose men. From thatched huts, seventy scholars came; from among commoners, he took disciples, ten of whom he treated as teachers, twelve as friends—all drawn to court by his gravity.

‘Had Lu Shang occupied the Duke of Zhou’s place, these men would have become corpses strewn in the marketplace, carcasses rotting in ditches. Yao could have compelled Xu You and Chao Fu to serve; Shun could have coerced Shan Juan and the men of Stone Gate; Yu could have forced Bo Chengzi Gao; Tang could have pressed Bian Sui and Wu Guang; King Wen of Wei could have bent Gan Mu; and Duke Ping of Jin could have pressed Hai Tang. Yet they honored and set them free, esteemed and held them in weight—not because the six rulers were weak, but because the hundred virtues differ in their heights, and silence is hard to equal.

‘They admired the beauty of honoring worthies, shamed the ugliness of harming the good. To employ such men would not have increased their might; to cast them aside would not have left their offices empty. Following their resolve could spread refined customs and foster flourishing virtue, spurring the greedy to withdraw and shaming the reckless. Though their talents might not suffice for immediate use or match the finest at court, was this not better than cringing and fawning, flattering the powerful, clutching gifts and bearing bribes, racing through the night to vie for favor, scrambling for passage, trading in fame, discarding virtue and conduct for the root of literary learning, or rushing to join the clamor of partisan factions?

‘If even those six rulers would not press their words upon reclusive scholars, would they have resorted to the blade? The sages are truly worthy of emulation—Lu Shang was clearly in error.

‘Though Emperor Gao of Han had many flaws in his conduct and little acquaintance with the classics, his magnanimity was vast, his tolerance boundless. He did not press the Four Elders of Mount Shang, though he hungered and thirsted for them. Only when the crown prince humbled himself to invite them did he make them his wings. He revered virtue, restrained his emotions, mourned the yellow swan with a sorrowful song, and thwarted his concubines’ ambitions. Such was his esteem for worthies—no wonder he rose from commoner to rule the realm. His capacity surpassed others indeed.

‘As for Lu Shang’s execution of Hua Shi, it was out of fear that he would discourage the masses. Yet the true affliction of the age—does it lie in this?’”

Those who hasten forward in pursuit of office neglect their conduct and calling—yet the true affliction of the age is not the abundance of those content with poverty and humble station. Suppose worthies often fix their gaze upon such men: those whose hearts cling to power and profit, whose ambitions know no limit, can never sever these habitual desires to admire those who withdraw in quietude. Since the dawn of time, they have been many, yet those who forget wealth and forsake rank remain fewer than one in ten thousand.

Confucius himself studied under Laozi yet could not cultivate his nonaction; Zigong and Yuan Xian were fellow disciples yet could not emulate his purity and austerity. The Four Hermits of Mount Shang lived in the same age as Chao Fu and Xu You, yet Wang Mang shared his era with the two Gong brothers—none could follow their example. Even if the common folk were flogged and shamed, forced to pursue the likes of Hua Shi, they would still refuse. How, then, could Lu Shang fear that Hua Shi would corrupt the masses? Failing to grasp this, he applied military law to a time of peace, wrongfully harming a worthy—his cruelty was grievous indeed. Only his great achievements prevented his downfall.

Moreover, before Lu Shang met King Wen, he too endured poverty and lowliness. The common crowd despised him; an old woman drove him away. His hired labor went unsold; his butchery and angling brought no gain. Not a single soul admired him. In his reclusion, why did he alone fear that Hua Shi would discourage the masses? Had King Zhou of Yin fled and sought refuge, would Lu Shang, even at the point of death, have called it a crime? Cao Cao, too, enforced harsh laws and was swift to kill, yet in his heart he sought to employ Kongming. Kongming plainly declared he had no wish to serve, and Cao Cao dismissed him, saying, “It would be wrong to humiliate a man of lofty character in the court of a corrupt ruler.” Though Cao Cao lashed the Nine Provinces and laid the foundations of an empire, he did not act recklessly.

Yet as turmoil persisted and competition became custom, some thrust themselves forward with bribes like dragons leaping, while others ascended through factional ties like phoenixes soaring. Wind shapes habit, and custom obscures the great Dao. Later generations, ignorant of the teachings of the Ru, came to believe that wealth alone could establish one’s person. When a man of outstanding integrity, who never leaves his home yet quietly savors the Dao, appears, should he not be sought out and honored to revive the virtue of humility?

If Sun Wu and Wu Qi bore arms, their strength was that of a single man, yet their strategies surpassed those of ten thousand. Now, if a great Ru were sent as an emissary—not to handle pressing affairs but to teach in the mountains and forests—could he not mold the young and expound the refined rites of reverence? Why must one harness a great elephant to catch a mouse or saddle a phoenix to ride? Then the bells and tripods would resound with his name. As for those who wither in the marshes and thickets, who live and die in vain—what worth do they hold?

The recluse replied: “You may be said to guard a molehill and play upon a fox mound, never having ascended the heights of Langfeng to gaze upon the clouds, nor floated upon the limpid shallows to cross the Milky Way. The so-called men of aspiration need not seek office, nor must they strive for merit and fame. The highest attain the nameless; the next, the unnameable. They soar on wings to surpass the crowd, their tracks vanish like a wheel’s hub—doing what ordinary men cannot, severing what those of mediocre talent cannot sever. Whether few or many, they are not measured by common standards. Serene and pure, they are unstained by fame or rank. Their pure influence is enough to cleanse the filth of a hundred generations; their lofty standards are enough to rouse the turbid men of later times. Why must they don crimson sashes and purple robes, wear crowns and ride carriages, adorn themselves with the embroidered hides of sacrificial oxen, or swallow the fragrant bait of Zhan He? One morning they blaze like the scorching sun, by evening they crumble into cold ashes.

The sparrow does not trade its thorny thicket for the distant clouds; the king salmon does not abandon its deep pool for the vastness of the sea. Tigers and leopards enter grand halls and grieve; swans and cranes ascend the peaks of Song and grow sorrowful. Each creature finds peace in what it excels at—none rejoices in success or mourns in loss. The men of the world, however, are all like this: a single day without a lord leaves them restless and anxious. They are like the jade piled in Lantian or the timber filling Deng Forest—though skilled craftsmen and great artisans may use them as they please, why must one drown fish or sink birds?

Noble reclusion and lofty withdrawal were approved by the ancient sages. Some emerge, some withdraw—each follows his own inclination. What the scholar values is the establishment of virtue and the creation of teachings. To practice filial piety, friendship, benevolence, and righteousness, to maintain purity and loftiness—this is to establish virtue. To exhaustively study the ancient texts and produce writings of brilliance—this is to establish teachings.

Shanjuan had no merit in governing the people, yet he cannot be said to have diminished the customs of the age. Confucius had no achievements in warfare, yet he cannot be said to have fallen short of Han Xin or Bai Qi. To preserve both name and person intact is the highest worthiness; to dwell in reclusion and fulfill one’s aspirations surpasses even the ancients. Boyi and Shuqi, though inflexible in their principles, were praised by the ancients for not lowering their aspirations or humiliating themselves. Not lowering their aspirations shows they esteemed the loftiness of reclusion; not humiliating themselves shows they knew the defilement of bondage.

The purity of the sages was praised by Mencius, who also said that the heavenly nobility surpasses official seals. To cultivate one’s aspirations and forsake glory was esteemed by Xunzi. When the weight of the Dao and righteousness is recognized, kings and dukes may be held lightly. Yet what the world fears is power, what it values is profit. Those of great virtue who remain obscure are dismissed as mediocre; those of small capacity entrusted with great tasks are hailed as lofty scholars. Some risk danger, brave peril, cast aside their lives, and hurl their bodies beneath a thousand blades—all to seize glory and splendor in a single morning. Compared to those who scorn the four seas and cherish their own necks, how deluded they are!

One who serves the world says: ‘The reclusive scholar, content in the mountains and marshes, does not bear the world’s honors. Unfettered and unrestrained, he is of no use to his age. To covet rank and profit is truly a useless thing in the world.’ The recluse replied: ‘The unicorn does not bark, the phoenix does not herald the dawn, the celestial steed does not pull the plow, and the ancestral priest does not manage the kitchen. Moreover, the sun shines with great brightness, illuminating all without exception, while the nurturing warmth of the gentle wind is the work of the moon.’”

The radiance of lamps on a quiet night, the smelting of metal and stone for practical use—these are the work of fire. The world cannot endure for long without the sun, nor can it last a single day without fire, yet their magnitudes cannot be equated.

Beyond the rivers and seas, the vastness of heaven and earth unfolds: rising as clouds and rain, descending to form the hundred streams. Yet the daily use of these great waters does not match the irrigation of a well’s shallow blade, nor does the flooding of fields and gardens compare to the nourishment of a humble ditch. Which, then, is truly vast?

Jie and Zhou were emperors, while Confucius was but a minister’s minister. Yet when men are likened to Jie and Zhou, none fail to rage, while to be compared to Confucius brings universal delight. Thus, nobility and baseness do not lie in rank alone.

Mencius said: “Were Yu, Ji, and Yan Yuan to exchange places, they would all act the same.” Zai Yu also declared Confucius far greater than Yao and Shun. If commoners and kings are weighed equally, and scholars exalted above all, then the virtue of the Tang and Yu dynasties was virtue alone—what need had it for office?

Moreover, when the sun’s brilliance ascends through creation, turning heaven and earth within its embrace, the mind remains vast and unburdened by worldly affairs. When the pure and white is untouched by glory or disgrace, the desires of the vulgar cannot gladden the spirit, nor can the confusions of the crowd sway one’s resolve. Glory and splendor are but growths to be excised, and the ten thousand things no more than the wings of a cicada.

Would one of such disposition deign to bend his limbs, bow his bearing, and measure his conduct by what displeases him? Would he labor over what he has cast aside, force a smile to advance, or cringe to retreat—fearing the displeasure of the vulgar or the wear of his own body? Would he dull the Dragon Spring sword into an awl, mute the sacred drum into a child’s toy, wield the Yellow Thearch’s scepter as a sickle, or lower the imperial banner beneath the branches of the scholar’s tree?

King Gu of old cast aside his staff and fled, while Yu Yi entered a cave to escape. Ji Zha withdrew to till the fields, and Lao Lai irrigated gardens to keep his distance. Each followed his own inclinations—none would trade their way.

Thus, purity untainted is true wealth, and a body free from servitude is true nobility.

Should one cleave the tally and hold land, what is called emolument and gain is but a semblance—no true wealth or nobility. Moreover, the higher the office, the heavier the burden; the greater the merit, the more men envy it. Only in poverty and lowliness does none contend with me, allowing one to treasure it long and live without care.

To wash one’s furs and mend coarse cloth, to tend mallows and discard weaving, to leave beans uncovered and dine on rough grain—this may invite the scorn of those who fawn and dissemble. Yet to erect screens and seal off chambers, to feast on three returns of jade-laden dishes and the luxuries of marquises, to bask in the ease of Anchang—this brings the burden of usurping rank and wallowing in filth. Better to wander in the canon of the spirit, exhaling the old and inhaling the new, seeking only to fill the belly. At the tip of the plowshare or amidst the threads of the loom, one gazes at the river and is sated; the body gathers to a single branch and rests in abundance. The teeming ten thousand things crumble into dust and ash.

Gruel to fill the mouth, hempen robes and patched gowns—such plainness frees the will, neither sorrow nor joy. This is true honor and joy, likened to having nothing at all.

As for office: if one seeks a name, then inscribing a hair suffices to bear one’s surname—why rely on the good historian or the engraving of tripods? Mencius did not count arrows and stones as merit; Yang Yun did not seek to enrich the age by governing the people. To seek benevolence and attain it—is this not enough?

Yet the official says: “Those who flee the world are disloyal; how can they dwell in the ruler’s land and eat the ruler’s grain?” The recluse replies: “What do you mean? When Yan Hui died, Duke Ding of Lu was to offer condolences in person. He sent someone to consult Confucius, who said: ‘All within the seas are subjects.’ Duke Ding then ascended by the eastern steps and performed the rites of a lord. By this reasoning, all within the shores are the king’s subjects—this much is clear. Those at court exert their strength to serve; those in the mountains and forests follow virtue to curb greed. Though the paths of purity and turbidity differ, their return is the same: all are subjects. The king knows no bounds; the world is his home. Where the sun and moon shine, where rain and dew fall—all is his domain. How can one suspend oneself in empty space, feed on sips of rosy clouds, and refuse to dwell within it? Does the earth not feed? Does the grain not grow?”

The gold and jade of mountains, the pearls and cowries of waters—though not stored in the treasury nor used for daily court needs—are still the ruler’s wealth. Reclusive scholars, not ranked among meat-eating officials, are like the treasures of mountains and waters. Are they not also possessions of the state? Xu You did not flee beyond the four seas; the Four Hoary Ones did not escape to the farthest wilds. Thus it is said: “All within the realm, from the noble to the humble, are the emperor’s subjects.” Gan Mu did not bear arms to guard the frontier or build ramparts on the battlefield, yet he rendered the service of strengthening Wei. Now, those who dwell in reed huts within the realm, chanting the Way of the former kings, teach the people the virtue of yielding—if Confucian and Mohist teachings do not decline, this too is what Yao and Shun would approve.

In the past, Boyi and Shuqi refused to eat Zhou grain, and Bao Jiao starved to death beneath a bridge—how can their stubbornness serve as a model? Formerly, Emperor An sent dark silk and jade to summon Zhou Yanzu; Emperor Huan sent dark silk and jade to summon Wei Xiuming; Emperor Shun sent dark silk and jade to summon Yang Zhongxuan. Each accepted the appointment as Attendant Gentleman but did not take office. Emperor Wen of Wei summoned Guan You’an, who also refused. He then decreed that local authorities annually provide one sheep and two *hu* of wine. Emperor Huan sent dark silk and jade to summon Xu Shuzi, who accepted the post of Governor of Taiyuan and Minister of the East Sea but did not assume office. Emperor Shun sent dark silk and jade to summon Fan Jigao, who declined, prompting the decree that local authorities annually provide one sheep and two *hu* of wine, along with a ceremonial desk and staff, treating him with the rites due a teacher. In Emperor Xian’s time, Zheng Kangcheng was nominated by the province as Filial and Incorrupt, Outstanding Talent, and Excellent and Upright, and summoned fourteen times by the Three Ducal Ministers—all of which he declined. The imperial carriage summoned him as Gentleman Consultant, Erudite, Minister of the Capital, Attendant Gentleman, and Grand Minister of Agriculture—none of which he accepted. Emperor Zhao’s imperial carriage summoned Han Fu, who arrived and was granted fifty bolts of silk, along with sheep and wine. Fa Gaoqing was twice nominated as Filial and Incorrupt, five times summoned by his province, eight times by the Three Ducal Ministers, nine times as Excellent and Upright, and three times as Erudite—all of which he declined. Emperor Huan sent dark silk, jade, a covered carriage, and a light chariot to summon Han Boxiu, who did not come; he sent the same to summon Jiang Boya, who accepted the post of Palace Grandee but declined the governorship of Fang—yet all were treated with esteem and not subjected to coercive summons.

If these emperors’ commendation of reclusive scholars was not misguided, then the execution of Hua Shi by Lü Shang was excessively harsh and cruel—this much is clear. The official, thus dismayed, lost himself in reflection and sighed deeply, saying: “Now I realize the principle of transcending the common—it is not something the mediocre and petty can perceive.”

**Exhortation to Study (III)**

The Master of Embracing Simplicity said: Study is the means to clarify and settle the nature and principle of the mind, to winnow away the dust and grime, to carve and polish the unrefined ore, to sharpen the dull blade, to awaken the Dao and brighten the intellect, to adorn and dye the plain silk, to examine the past and foresee the future, to range widely and encourage achievement, to look up and observe, to look down and scrutinize—all of which lie within the purview of the kingly Way. Advancing, one may govern the state; retreating, one may preserve oneself. Thus, the sages and worthies were all untiring in their diligence, striving day and night without cease, studying until the water-clock ran dry and the sun stood at noon without laying aside their books, enduring hunger and cold, peril and hardship without abandoning their pursuit. How could this be for the sake of seeking favor in the present age? Truly, it is the natural joy of the endeavor itself.

The shallow skills of carving and engraving, the easy arts of archery and charioteering, still require habitual practice before one can master them—how much more so the vastness of human principles, the profundity of the Dao and virtue, the transformations of yin and yang, and the subtleties of spirits and gods, distant and obscure, dark and recondite, truly difficult to comprehend! Though one may say a color is white, without dyeing it will not be lustrous; though one may say a taste is sweet, without blending it will not be savory. Thus, the jade flower, if left unpolished, will not emit its radiance by night; the crimson blade, if left untempered, will not achieve the strength of the pure steel sword. Fire, if not drilled, will not ignite; if not fanned, will not blaze. Water, if not channeled, will not flow; if not accumulated, will not run deep. Though the raw material lies within me, its completion depends on external means.

Only by ascending the Towering Wind and grasping the Pillar of Dawn does one realize the gloom and narrowness of the well and valley. Only by perusing the Six Classics and savoring the Hundred Schools does one perceive the utter blindness of facing a wall. To seek knowledge without study is like desiring fish without a net—though the mind may toil, there will be no harvest. To pursue breadth and exhaust principle is like entrusting oneself to a following wind—without bodily labor, one may reach far.

Cosmetics, when applied, enhance the beauty of Xi Shi and conceal the ugliness of the wen; profound learning in the classics, when deep, allows the brilliant to soar freely and the dull to awaken. The catalpa and nanmu may soar to the clouds, yet without the craft of Lu Ban, they cannot become towers and pavilions. A man may be naturally bright and clear, yet without understanding the right and wrong of great principles, he cannot be called a gentleman.

To surpass a thousand *li* in a single morning, one must rely on the swiftness of Retreat Shadow’s steed. To cross the vast waves and reach distant shores, one must depend on the vessel and oars. To see beyond all bounds without descending from the hall, one must turn to the written records. To fathom the depths of the abyss without exhausting the spirit, one must find a luminous teacher. Thus, cinnabar and verdigris transform plain silk; instruction dispels ignorance. When one parts the dark clouds and raises the great light, the forms of the ten thousand things are revealed without concealment. When one unfurls the bamboo and silk and examines past and present, the secrets of heaven and earth are laid bare—how much more so the workings of spirits and gods, and even more, the affairs of men!

Can mud be made as hard as lacquer? Can gold, jade, or crooked wood be worked to meet the carpenter’s square? Can the hundred beasts be trained for battle formations? Can the six livestock be taught to advance and retreat? Can deep-swimming fish be moved by sound? Can inanimate stones be stirred by sincerity? How much more so for those who bear the Five Constants and are endowed with the most refined spirit?

The lowly and high-stepping steeds are shaped by training; birds of prey are tamed by practice. Though common horses and wild hawks share the same nature, one is adorned and prized while the other remains coarse and base. If one persists in navigating the shallows without ceasing, one will surely merge with the vast sea. If one adds but a single basket of earth without rest, one will surely reach the towering summit.

When great rivers surge, dragons and serpents swim in schools; as days pass and months progress, virtue is established and the Dao perfected—then one may rest in the dreams of the sages, and what need has one merely to break free from shackles? Alas, how grievous the plight of the benighted!

In former times, Zhong You wore a rooster’s crest and a sash adorned with chirping cicadas, brandished his sword, and danced with bared blade, boasting of the strength of Mount Tai’s bamboo, as if to rely on the natural vigor of the unyielding. Yet Confucius, with gentle guidance, infused him with moral instruction, and thus nurtured a disciple who ascended the hall and ranked among the four sages. Zhang Liang, though once a coarse man who gathered ruffians, was gradually steeped in the Dao’s teachings and transformed into a renowned scholar, one who could stand as an equal before kings and nobles. How could he have merely escaped mediocrity?

Thus, the wise grieve the fleeting nature of life in the world, lament the swift oblivion of those who vanish without renown, heed the solemn admonition of “morning’s truth,” and yearn to penetrate the unbroken thread of the Dao. They fear the bright warning of impending decline, awaken from the madness of heedless thoughts, and refuse to idle away their days in mere satiation. They do not squander even an inch of time, take the flowing river as a mirror to spur their resolve, and mourn the lightning speed of passing moments. They sever frivolous attachments, reduce worldly trivialities, cleanse their hearts of poverty’s anxieties, and cast off boundless desires. They cease the games of hunting, gambling, and idle play, correct the indolence of daytime naps and listless sitting, and knowing that empty musings yield no benefit, they rouse themselves onto the sagely path.

They learn to accumulate, inquire to discern, advance virtue and cultivate their work, review the old to grasp the new. Even the sage Duke of Zhou read a hundred chapters daily; Confucius, of heavenly endowment, wore through the thongs of his bamboo scrolls three times. Mo Di, the great worthy, filled carts with his writings; Dong Zhongshu, a man of his age, never glanced beyond his garden gate. Ni Kuan carried his books while weeding with a hoe; Lu Sheng cut reeds to transcribe texts. Huang Ba studied while in shackles; Ning Yue doubled his efforts through night and day. Thus, they delved into the profound mysteries of the Dao, plumbed the depths of subtle words, observed ten thousand ages as if in a single day, and knew the eight wilds as if their own courtyard. They examined the waxing and waning of the Seven Luminaries, traced the transformations of the Three and Five, discerned the signs of rise and decline to come, verified good and ill in past events, and foretold the fortunes of the Yellow and Mysterious as if reading their palms. They discerned incipient signs as if already fulfilled. Thus, their great virtue and towering achievements crowned their age, and their pure fragrance and illustrious renown spread without end.

Moreover, upon hearing the cry of the *shangyang* bird, one prepares for floods; consulting the *niaonu* oracle, one brings order to the east. Inquiring of the floating gourd, one speaks of colors and tastes; questioning the earthen dog, one knows the burial sheep. Through the *Lingbao* scriptures, one understands the forms of mountains and streams; from a broken chopstick, one expounds the *Zhuanche* classic. Gazing at the *libi* constellation, one distinguishes the signs of yin and yang; from the winter locust, one perceives the errors in intercalary months. What mystery is there? It is learning alone.

Even children’s rhymes aid the sage’s ears and eyes—how much more the profound and vast teachings of the ancient texts? Talent and nature vary in excellence; thought and reason differ in depth. Some possess innate knowledge and mature early; others must be led by the ear before they understand. Swiftness is like the feet of the *lü* steed; slow comprehension is like the wings of the quail. Though the former may gallop far and halt abruptly, they do not surpass a single step. Though the latter may advance but a foot at a time, if they persist without ceasing, they may cross mountains and marshes. Is not the distinction between bright and dim learning much the same?

In youth, the will is single and hard to forget; in age, the spirit scatters and is easily lost. Therefore, one must devote oneself to study early, so that practice becomes as natural as inborn nature.

As for those of extraordinary talent who, in their prime, encounter misfortune—though they lose the dawn at *Yanggu*, they may yet gather it at *Yuyuan*. One then knows that late sowing in good fields is better than a year of barren waste. The parable of the candle’s light is apt indeed.

In this troubled age, the teachings of the Ru have declined, and the paths of Wen and Wu are on the verge of collapse. Some drown in sensual pleasures; others race along the paths of rivalry. The poor and solitary who master the Six Arts are suppressed like resources in a drought; those who cling to the wind and attach themselves to phoenix wings, though of mediocre talent, may yet soar to the clouds. Those who abandon fundamentals for trivialities call it diligent cultivation; those who embrace the classics and seek within themselves are deemed obscure and impractical. Thus, all are dust-stained, rain-soaked, frost-crowned, and ice-trodden, clutching their yellowed scrolls and holding fast to purity, yet drawn to the easy paths of crooked ways, hoping for morning sowing and evening harvest.

If, instead, one lowers the curtain and rests on a high pillow, roams the spirit through the Nine Classics, delves into the subtleties of the arts, savors the profound flavor of the Dao, and dwells in refined seclusion—if one establishes an unshakable resolve, unfurls one’s green banner in the bitter cold, does not measure the world to choose one’s path, and does not drift with the crowd like floating duckweed—how few are such men!

Those who rush anxiously toward advancement and fret over setbacks—how can they abandon the broad and easy thoroughfare to cling to the most difficult and surely impassable road? This is why there are no men by the riverside, why the scholars of *Zijin* lament the customs of the age, why those who grieve for the Dao sigh in sorrow and sink into melancholy.

Yet cold and heat alternate, adversity ends in prosperity, and Wen and Wu rise in turn—this is the constant pattern of nature. May the bandit hordes be swept away, the restoration flourish in our time, the Seven Luminaries follow their ancient courses, and the celestial order be renewed! May the great furnace of the Nine Suns turn the great potter’s wheel, the imperial model open the mysterious pattern to guide all things, and the myriad beings be shaped and refined, their finest blossoms cultivated! May the accumulated dust be swept away, evil be transformed into good, weapons be sheathed, and bows cased! May the schools of the *Biyong* be revived, the sons of the state gathered, culture and virtue flourish, golden sounds and jade tones resound, and wind and clouds descend before the dawn! May the travelers from the hills and gardens, the phoenixes with folded wings, soar in the clear void; may the swift steeds with sturdy necks gallop a thousand *li*!

May those of hidden brilliance, stifled and unrecognized, those of vast learning and deep understanding—men like Shen Gong and Fu Sheng—be summoned with crimson carriages and rush mats, their pent-up energies released, their plain virtues displayed! May they assist the sagely age in reaching the lofty heights of Tang and Yu, and speed along the broad path of grand peace! May the mysterious stream moisten the nine realms, the celestial wind spread beyond all bounds! May the Five Punishments be set aside and songs of praise arise, harmonious qi flourish and fine harvests grow—is this not joyous indeed?

Once, the Second Emperor of Qin held no esteem for the Ru arts, abandoning the Way of the former sages to study the laws of punishment and imprisonment. The people saw no virtue—only the sound of execution. Thus bewildered, they knew not how to turn back; lost, they knew not how to save themselves. They plunged from the heights of the clouds, only to be crushed to powder in the unfathomable depths. From the exalted to the base, can there be no mirror?

**Chapter Four: Exalting Teaching**
The *Baopuzi* states: Those who fix their gaze upon autumn’s finest hairs do not perceive the radiance of heaven’s patterns. Those who immerse their hearts in trivial affairs do not grasp the profundity and vastness of the Ru Dao. Dwellers in the stench of salted fish forget the fragrance of orchids; the deeply lost cannot find their way back. Those who receive the guidance of Mo Di possess no crooked or hollowed wood; those who are steeped in the teachings of the Dao produce no perverse or deviant people. No method of cultivation surpasses learning.

The breadth of learning lies in tirelessness; tirelessness lies in steadfast resolve. If resolve is not steadfast, the poor and lowly will be consumed by the struggle for survival, while the rich and noble will drown in ease and pleasure. Thus, those of far-reaching vision and profound knowledge appear but rarely across the ages, while the ignorant—those who see no farther than a wall—throng shoulder to shoulder.

If common scholars by day till the soil to feed themselves and by night kindle firewood to pursue their studies; if those in office, in the intervals of feasting and revelry, occasionally turn their gaze to exhortation and admonition—then the world would know no utter fools, and men like Ziyou and Zixia would not be lacking.

Yet there are those pressed by hunger and cold, whose coarse fare is insufficient, whose skin is chapped by wind and frost, whose mouths lack even husks and chaff. They have no means to seek a teacher, their families face imminent crisis. If they set aside the plow, the farm lies fallow; if they take up the scroll, their livelihood suffers. Though their learning is incomplete, they may be excused.
