These are like a steed of a thousand *li* trapped beneath a salt cart, or a blade of finest ore never tempered in the forge of Ou Ye. But as for the scions of kings and nobles, who idle in luxury and pleasure, who dance amid silks and brocades, ignorant of the hardships of sowing and reaping—whose eyes grow weary of the profound and whose ears tire of the refined, whose noses are sated with the fragrances of Zheng and Wei—how can they be compared?

Amidst the cloying sweetness of orchid and musk, their palates grow jaded beyond fine meats and rich grains. In winter, they tread in the warmth of sable and fox; in summer, they flutter in the lightness of gauze and silk. They ride forth in carriages grander than Qing Feng’s, and retire to banquet in halls resplendent as Bi Fang’s. Vermilion and kingfisher blue adorn their pillars and beams, while their chests and coffers overflow without end. They array allure and charm to delight the heart, drown themselves in fine wines to sink into drunkenness—becoming champions of gluttony and drink, masters of gambling and games. They neither comprehend literature nor recognize scholars, whom they regard as mere weeds. Their speech and writing lack classical grounding, their arguments tangled in confusion. When pressed in debate, they speak only of warfare; when faced with doubt, they wither before their time. Even if they could distinguish beans from wheat, what difference would it make? They are blind indeed!

The *Baopuzi* states: It is said that the emperor’s eldest heir must enter the Grand Academy, study under a master, and learn the Dao, taking his place among the sons of the state. Only after knowing how to be a minister can one become a ruler; only after knowing how to be a son can one become a father. Thus, learning is established before office, for to govern without learning is like wielding a knife to carve—inevitably causing harm. This is what Zheng Qiao lamented when he spoke of indulging emotions and desires, calling it inhuman.

Yet the noble scions are born within the depths of the palace, raised by the hands of women. They have never known the labor of worry or fear. Some are scarcely free of swaddling clothes before being clad in robes of blue and purple; others have barely outgrown their garments before assuming high and illustrious positions. They wield the power of life and death, control promotions and demotions, decide honor and disgrace through their grants and withdrawals, and sway fortune and misfortune with a word. Love and hate shift without pause, while praise and blame assail their ears. Suspicions arise from vague resemblances, where truth and falsehood blur; they seize opportunities to accuse without cause, or fabricate slander from empty trust. The permutations of flattery and deceit are endless, their intricacies beyond the reach of cunning manipulation or the scrutiny of brush and ink.

Without the arts of learning, how can they discern the true from the false in right and wrong? Moreover, the events of past and present, the principles of self-realization—without these as guides, how can they avoid the disasters of overturned nests and wrecked chariots? The sages of old, though high in station, never forgot the dangers; loving their children, they sought to teach them the proper way. They refined and polished them, guarding against corruption, selecting enlightened teachers to shape their character, and choosing worthy friends to influence them. They urged them to broad reading, showed them success and failure, bade them examine the past to understand the future, observe others to know themselves. They drove them onto the straight path, restrained them within bounds, and instilled awe in their hearts.

Should one cling to the edge of a thousand-fathom cliff, trembling as though riding a galloping steed across thin ice, one may yet avoid far-reaching regrets and preserve one’s integrity and good fortune.

Of old, the Dou clan reaped the blessings of the Dao’s teachings, while Huo Yu suffered the calamity of following his own whims. Zhongshan and Dongping found security in their love of antiquity, whereas Yan and Ci, facing walls, courted peril. The past is not forgotten—it serves as a fine mirror for the present.

Tang and Wu flourished under the influence of Yi and Lü, while Xin and Gui, swayed by flatterers, fell into ruin. In choosing friends and teachers, one must be especially discerning, selecting those of humble origins and virtuous conduct—men who stand firm in hardship and command respect through their singular resolve. Let their learning be like that of Zhongshu or Huan Rong, their integrity as unyielding as Gong Sui or Wang Ji. Let them discourse day and night on the supreme Way of loyalty and filial piety, clarifying the patterns of survival and ruin, purging defilements, guarding against waywardness, and correcting crookedness. One must suppress personal inclinations, adhere to the law, and enter the teachings of virtue.

In the late Han and the final years of Wu, matters stood otherwise. Officials were chosen for their carriages and caps, appointed on the basis of partisan praise. Though they bore the name of teachers and friends, they offered no substance—no one to correct oversights. Far from being harmless, they brought harm instead. What they expounded was not the Dao and its virtue; what they recommended was not loyalty and benefit.

Only did they indulge in novel melodies and alluring beauties, appraising with delicate skill the purity or muddiness of songs, the length or brevity of flutes, the strength or weakness of hounds and horses, the merits of hunting grounds, the preferences of divergent paths, the refinement or crudeness of carvings, the dexterity or clumsiness of *tanqi* and *mocu* games, the victories or defeats in fishing and fowling, the grace or homeliness of courtesans and concubines, the rusticity or elegance of garments, the excellence or flaws of carriages, the precision or coarseness of bows and swords, gathering the strange and uniting the rare—until their excesses knew no bounds, growing daily and monthly more extravagant.

When they spoke of palaces, they likened them distantly to Jasper Terraces and Jade Chambers, or imitated nearby the grandeur of Epang and Lingguang, deeming a thousand gates and ten thousand halls too cramped, the Kunming and Taiye lakes too shallow. They mocked thatched roofs as unworthy, earthen steps as crude. The people’s strength was exhausted by labor, their stores squandered on frivolities. They raised earthen mountains to rival Song and Hua, dug canals to mimic the Nine Rivers. They ascended lofty towers and opened lattice windows that pierced the clouds. Lewd music clamored to bewitch the ear, fluttering sleeves dazzled to confuse the eye. The debaucheries of Pu Shang and Bei Li followed one upon another—now wailing, now shouting—turning day into night. They lingered over cups of wine, drowned in the throes of revelry. Some built emerald canopies in lush groves, others shot fierce birds in the wilds or raced swift steeds over treacherous paths. They exposed their retinues to the blazing sun, setting out with torches and returning by starlight. State affairs were neglected, rewards and punishments abandoned.

Some launched ornate boats upon the shimmering waves, cast dense nets in the green rivers, dangled fragrant bait in rippling pools, or unleashed singing cormorants in clear streams to snare fish with sinking lines. They loosed high-flying arrows to bring down light-winged geese, or wove *fufu* nets in the forests, encircled mountains and marshes with layered battues, arrayed crimson pennants among the wind-tossed grasses, and galloped fleet steeds across the plains. They unleashed hounds to tear at cunning beasts, flew swift hawks to seize soaring birds, felled raging rhinos with powerful crossbows, and slew bears and tigers with long spears. Such pursuits, year after year, brought no weariness, continuing without end.

To this were added the frequent seasonal visits—welcoming and seeing off guests, celebrating and congratulating, exchanging gifts with close associates, and receiving distinguished visitors bearing tokens of esteem. The affairs of the world pressed urgently, bending and exhausting all. Thus did refined propriety drift ever farther away, and indulgent dissipation take deeper root, until the distance from the learning of the Ru grew vast indeed.

How rare in such an age were those who could perceive the principles of rise and decline, rise above the depths of dissipation, forsake the perilous paths of corrupt virtue, and tread the great Way of enduring order!

Alas! Thus it is that those who preserve the state and secure their families are exceedingly rare, while those who collapse in ruin, weeping blood, are without number.

Now, with sagely brightness reigning above, the ancient ways are examined to succor the world, dikes are strengthened to prevent breaches, and praise and blame are made clear to manifest encouragement and deterrence. One must hope that the imperial clan, the noble houses, and the wealthy and powerful will surely vie to esteem the Ru arts, restrain extravagance in the literary arts, and set aside the ideas of Lao and Zhuang. What is not urgent is the refinement of the Six Classics’ true Way.

**The Way of the Ruler, Fifth Chapter**
The *Baopuzi* states: The pure and refined ascends and floats upward; the turbid and yellow sinks and flows downward. Thus are the distinctions of rank and awe established. The sages of old drew from the Two Modes to establish the Way of ruler and minister, and by setting offices and dividing duties, the flourishing of harmonious transformation was achieved. He who would rule others must first cultivate himself to lead the Four Seas, cast aside partiality to equalize the kingly Way, and dispel private attachments to exemplify perfect impartiality. He must model the cosmos to encompass the ten thousand differences. Once truth and falsehood are made clear beyond all things, he must further rely on his own discernment. Once hearing is sharpened by what comes before him, he must further rely on his own perception.

He must emulate the water-level to advance the good, and the carpenter’s square to dismiss the wicked. He must display virtue and block transgression, employ the near and cherish the worthy, allowing compasses to exhaust their circles and squares to fulfill their angles. He must wield the adze to test its cutting edge, entrusting tasks without measure and employing talents without misallocation. He must examine names and demand realities, frequently inspect and diligently relieve. He must establish canonical teachings to set the people’s ultimate standard, carefully weigh praise and blame to manifest encouragement and deterrence, restrain disorder to prevent usurpation and excess, and scrutinize正直 and crookedness to avert regret and resentment.

In bestowing, let there be no favoritism in judgment; in deprivation, let there be no concealment by the Hundred Schools. Let the Six Arts be the craftsman’s tools, loyalty and trust the guiding principles, governance the exercise of kindness and harmony, and regulation the application of ritual and punishment. He must elevate the obscure and redress the suppressed, stir the clear currents to purify the turbid, ensuring that things follow no devious paths and affairs adhere to no improper divisions.

Those who stand in court or govern the people must not encroach upon offices or overstep bounds. Those who advance to war must not shrink from danger or evade commands. He must please the near to embrace the distant, cultivate culture to attract the estranged, enrich the people’s wealth and grain, and broaden the paths of advancing virtue. He must block the proliferation of deceit and cunning. When affairs demand it, let punishments be clear and laws strict; when trials are adjudicated, let compassion and reverence prevail. When transformation is pure and governance orderly, let flaws be concealed and faults hidden.

The Five Teachings lie in magnanimity. Externally, he must gather the many officers in civil and martial arts; internally, he must build the inner bastions of kinship, making close and distant relatives support one another. The tail becomes the body’s trunk, and though branches may flourish, there is no fear of harming the root. Though streams may swell, there is no risk of turning from the source. Like a stone basin or a mountain’s peak, he must firmly check covetous designs. Seeing the collapse of the Three Miao, one knows the springs of rivers cannot be dammed. Witnessing the loss of hidden shadows, one realizes that perilous cliffs cannot be relied upon.

The Yangtze and Han remain, yet strong foes humiliate the sword’s edge. Armies unite as one, yet Ziyang’s clan is exterminated. The Four Peaks and Three Hills do not belong to a single family. Metal walls and boiling moats are not as secure as human harmony. Guarding lies beyond the seas, not in mountains and rivers. Thus, the worthy ruler, ever fearful of insufficiency, reforms his faults in dread of excess. When plans are deemed sound, he still contemplates danger without respite. Though victorious in war and lands expanded, he remains vigilant against complacency and retires at dusk.

He models the vast heavens to extend his radiance, emulates the earth’s thickness to broaden his bearing, and wields the sun’s brilliance to illuminate the distant. He relies on spring’s warmth to nurture and autumn’s frost to discipline. He repays counsel to compare similarities and differences, balances scales to weigh the many words, and empties himself to exhaust the sentiments below. He attributes merit to encourage posterity.

Governed by method, his beginnings and ends may be exhausted. Ordered by measure, disparities may be gauged. Towering like the windswept heights above the sky, none below can estimate his weight. Profound as the abyss with its myriad blades, none nearby can fathom his depth. Thus, the ruler’s source and flow are inexhaustible, and the talents of the hundred officials are fully displayed. My bounds are without limit, and their capacities may be measured.

When he issues commands, they resound like thunder’s clap, unaltered by perverse arguments. When he establishes laws and creates systems, they shine like the Seven Luminaries adorning the sky, unbent by love or hate. His grand strategies and far-reaching plans gather like dense clouds high above. Abiding in steadfastness to complete affairs, he stands firm as Mount Song’s roots in the earth. He discerns the hidden and manifest before their signs appear, examines praise and blame from the mouths of cunning speakers, and does not allow simplicity to scatter into artifice or integrity to dissolve into duplicity.

Though capable of independent judgment, he must broadly accept the humble’s advice. Though devoted to refinement, he must purify his ears to the murmurs. When the people suffer cold and hunger, he pities them and blames himself. When the people are at fault, he deems it his own responsibility. When auspicious omens arrive, he credits the gods’ aid. When Heaven’s wrath descends, he reflects on the prayers of the mulberry groves. He does not hesitate to reform what should be changed, nor is he ashamed to retrace his steps when lost.

He glares like a tiger to warn the secretive, and stands firm as a unicorn to welcome the upright. If no elders sing in the fields, he is ashamed to hear harmonious music. If the people lack grain, he is humbled before sumptuous feasts. Facing the vastness of the heavens, he fears the toil of those who come willingly. Savoring tender delicacies, he worries over the untimely harvest. Hearing the music of pipes and strings, he grieves over excessive pleasure. Gazing upon splendid adornments, he frets over harsh levies.

He follows Yao’s coarse robes and models Wen of Wei’s plain silks. He recalls the Xia’s humble palaces, recognizes the unbuilt Terrace of Dew, and learns from Zhanghua’s calamity and Epang’s swift ruin. In proclamations and oaths, he remembers the loss of trust in a single moment. In amusement, he perceives Daji’s bewitchment. In campaigns, he measures strength and timing, ensuring no hundred *li* resound with the people’s lament. In executions, he follows human sentiment and reason, ensuring no unjust souls cling to the wine-skin.

He takes warning from Du Bo’s spectral vengeance and the cries of the wronged. He heeds the great delusion of Duke Xian of Jin in deposing his heir, and the annihilation of Liu Biao’s line in appointing a concubine’s son. In hunting, he rejoices in losing game to gain worthy men, and delights in casting nets to welcome the distant. In favoritism, he fears the slander of Zhao Feiyan and the monopolization of Flying Swallow. In sole reliance, he perceives the perils of the deer and horse. In honoring the upright, he recalls Gaozu’s reception of advice and Jing’s execution of Cuo.

In indulgence, he guards against Yi Di’s influence. In yielding to flattery, he examines Luan Ying’s removal and the banishment of Zhengzi. He learns from the white dragon to curb recklessness, and observes the emaciated steed to temper insatiability. He prevents the transformation of humans into swine within the six palaces, and halts the pursuit of "sweating blood" horses from distant lands. He removes vicious dogs to avoid the disaster of wine-cups, and purchases horse bones to attract swift steeds. He spares the angry frog to encourage bravery, and avoids the mantis to inspire martial valor.

He listens to Gonglu’s forthright words and embraces Baoshen’s integrity. He plucks the useless hairs from back and belly, and grasps the six pinions for soaring flight. He silences the flattering tongues to heed the admonitions of the *Siyuan*, and prunes the withered and decayed to promote Liang Bo’s beauty. He exiles the seductive Ji to dispel bewitching allure, and retires Zixia to prevent the temptation of the "leftover peach." He conceals the fish in the abyss and wields the handle of the sharp blade. He does not shrink from the trouble of moving firewood to prevent the cost of scorching.

He fosters the cultivation of integrity and shame, and clarifies the standards of examinations. His anger does not exceed the law to inflict cruelty, nor does his joy transgress statutes to bestow lavish gifts. He severs affection for those he loves when they transgress, granting no pardon. He adopts goodness from those he dislikes when they labor, granting no exemption. He humbles himself to seek loyal counsel and welcomes unwelcome words.

His decrees are like thunder’s roar, unshaken by sophistry. His laws shine like the stars, unbent by partiality. His strategies gather like clouds, his steadfastness stands firm as mountains. He discerns hidden truths, examines praise and blame, and prevents simplicity from scattering into artifice. Though decisive, he seeks the humble’s advice; though refined, he purifies his ears. He pities the people’s suffering, blames himself for their faults, credits the gods for blessings, and reflects on his errors in calamity. He reforms without hesitation, retraces his steps without shame, warns the secretive, and welcomes the upright.

He is ashamed by the people’s want, humbled by their toil, and grieves over their excesses. He follows the ancients’ frugality, learns from past calamities, and heeds warnings from history. He executes justly, rewards fairly, and listens to all voices. He governs with method and measure, towering above all, profound beyond fathoming. His rule is inexhaustible, his officials’ talents fully displayed. His commands resound like thunder, his laws shine like stars, his strategies gather like clouds, and his steadfastness stands firm as mountains. He discerns hidden truths, examines praise and blame, and ensures no simplicity is lost to artifice. He governs with wisdom, listens to counsel, and rules with impartiality.

Yet he does not shun broad appeals for counsel, nor reject slanderous words. Though cast down, he does not spurn them; though flaws be minor, he records great merit. He forgets recent wrongs and remembers distant deeds, so that Cao Gui and Meng Ming find renewed purpose, Wei Shang and Zhang Chang achieve vengeance for past shame. The treacherous minister who shot the hook earns merit in restoring order; the captive strategist Li Zuoche offers counsel to halt the spear. Thus, owls and kites transform into mandarin ducks and phoenixes, deceit and falsehood turn to loyalty and steadfastness. Fragrant blossoms flourish in barren salt flats, radiant pearls emerge from mud and mire. Dull blades are sharpened, and all nine virtues harmonize in perfect accord.

Facing west, he hesitates to defer to the talents of teachers and friends, honoring the elderly to foster filial piety and fraternal duty. Thus, those coiled in the depths look up to ascend, and those dwelling in mountains descend to gather. Brilliant and flourishing, they assist within; fierce and mighty, they defend without. Governance is secured above, and the people submit below. Virtue shines near, and its blessings extend afar. The wise exert their strength in their posts, while the common folk yield their disputes in marshes and wetlands.

Thereupon, he purges oppressive laws and revives the pure breeze of grand harmony. With carriages of rushes and gifts of jade and silk, he draws forth the hidden talents of hills and gardens. Eminent ministers assemble to deliberate the gains and losses of the Dao. He reduces the ranks of shepherds and those who do not brew fine wine, expands the gates of humility, and blocks the paths of vain competition. He commends the conduct of righteousness and integrity, upholds the standards of the Dao and simplicity, so that those who cleave to virtue rejoice as tender shoots in sweet rain, and those who return are like flowing streams converging in the great river. The people rest as securely as green leaves clinging to sturdy branches, and those with left-folding robes gaze up as stars revolve around the North Star.

Thus, the seven celestial bodies do not stray from their course, aligned with the supreme pole. Cold and warmth do not err in their seasons but follow their proper order. The four spirit-beasts appear, auspicious fungi glow, sweet dew drips from the heavens, and bountiful grain sways in full ears. Red drought-demons flee to the divine moat, and plague spirits are bound in distant lands. Rivers do not surge in strange tumult, nor does the Southern Dipper stir the violence of withered grain. Things do not wither in unnatural decay, nor do people utter sighs of lament. Prisons stand empty, the five punishments lie dormant, and the calendar’s renewal reaches where caps and sashes do not, where felt and furs, mountain-dwellers and river-exiles all embrace joy, transform their countenance, and respond to harmony. Distant lands offer rare birds to the vermilion court, jade rings arrive from the westernmost reaches, and round-headed tribes swiftly reform as if mist harmonizing with a strong wind. The remote borderlands obey his commands like an echo answering sound, like a perfect note responding to pure tone.

Such is the height of flourishing rule, the path trodden by the Three August Ones and Five Emperors. Thus, he secures the ancestral temple without end and extends his lineage through a hundred generations.

For when roots run deep, branches flourish; when those below rejoice, those above find peace. If a horse is untrained, even Zaofu cannot urge it beyond a thousand *li*; if the people are not attached, even Yao and Shun cannot achieve the beauty of heaven’s harmony. When a horse reaches its limit, unruly behavior arises and collapse follows—this is the sole cause for concern. When the people suffer, rebellion multiplies and disaster inevitably strikes. Should one not tremble with vigilance, awaiting the dawn? Should one not quake with apprehension, ever mindful of peril?

If a ruler does not clarify his thoughts on order and chaos, nor deeply reflect on the signs of ruin, though his eyes may discern the finest autumn down, his ears distinguish the purest and harshest of the eight sounds, though his literary brilliance cascades like jade from his brush-tip, his martial prowess wields the gleaming blade as if it were an extension of his palm, though his heart retains the recitation of ten thousand texts and his tongue dispenses eloquence like surging waves—all this avails nothing when the earth crumbles beyond salvation, when dissolution is already upon him.

Why is this so? Because he neglects the great to obsess over the trivial, immerses himself in the affairs of subordinates while remaining blind to the foundational origins. If only he could act with frugality beyond excess, tread as if over deep abysses and thin ice, remain ever wary of reckless haste even in times of safety, and never abandon the fear of ruin even in moments of stability. If he grasps the main cords to order the fine threads, wields the Dao’s principles to govern the multitude, employs the combined strength of Han and Bai to break enemy lines, and exhausts the talents of Xiao and Cao to administer the state; if the armored heart is resolute in its courage and the strategist’s mind collaborates to advance its far-reaching plans—then the ruler, though at ease within his jade chambers, wandering the cloud-topped pavilions, where wine spoils in sweet brews and musicians weary from their dances, may still govern with folded arms, entrusting matters to the worthy, and rest upon his pillow, holding them accountable. Why must he dwell in humble thatched huts, partake of meager fare, endure the toil of guarding the gates, or bear the hardships of a life diminished before he can bring prosperity to the common people and extend the Dao’s harmony beyond the seas?

Deluded rulers, however, act otherwise. In their governance...

Some are benevolent yet indecisive, where vermilion and purple blur in chaos, the upright go unrewarded, and the wicked escape punishment. Others are harsh and cruel, wielding authority without mercy, their punishments excessive and unforgiving, never matching crime to consequence. Their foundations crumble, their roots exposed, yet they perch precariously like nests upon a canopy, comparing themselves to the sun and moon, likening their stature to Mount Tai. They believe that those of towering virtue are easily matched, while the cautious and reverent fall short of their mark.

Thus, the innocent suffer unjust punishments, and the people see no virtue—only the specter of execution. Officials deem the compliant as worthy, promoting those who curry favor while sidelining the capable. The court fills with the mother’s kin, who monopolize power, while decisions rest in the hands of flatterers. Those of distant lineage or merit are overlooked, their talents disregarded, while the trusted are petty sycophants. The forthright are suppressed, their voices drowned in the chorus of conformity, while the favored rise on empty praise, their achievements unexamined. Those entrusted with governance and defense—some corrupt their posts, others sow discord in the court, some falter in weakness, and others, timid and craven, squander military advantage. Yet the ruler, blind to their failings, refuses to demote or dismiss them, instead clinging to hope for their redemption. Small vessels tasked with great burdens inevitably court disaster. Meanwhile, the truly capable—those of broad vision and unsupported merit—languish in obscurity, some clad in coarse robes at court, others sunk in poverty, their Way confined, their strength untapped. Such is the root of decline and ruin.

Moreover, they ignore the classics and their warnings, neglecting study to indulge in revelry. They build towering pavilions while neglecting the Dao, expand parks and gardens while narrowing benevolence, deepen moats and ponds while shallowing trust and faith. They delight in hounds and horses yet despise forthright counsel, prize jewels and jade while scorning wisdom and strategy, adorn themselves in fine silks while skimping on relief for the poor, delay aid while hastening to amass wealth, obsess over the hunt while neglecting the harvest, exalt the wealthy while devaluing lives. They elevate entertainers and dismiss scholars, lavish favor on courtiers while slighting warriors, drown in sensual pleasures while ignoring state affairs, prioritize drunken revelry over judgment, impose harsh labor while withholding rewards, and squander resources on frivolous crafts, hoarding delicacies while the people starve. In such a state, ruin cannot be blamed on Heaven, nor weakness attributed to others.

Fortune and misfortune lie within oneself—were Tang and Wu exceptions? Of old, King Wen of Zhou buried the unburied bones, and the world praised his benevolence; King Zhou of Yin carved out Bi Gan’s heart, and the seas reviled his cruelty. The gaze of all rests upon the ruler—praise or blame, success or failure—hinges not on quantity but on weight. When praise is abundant, even distant tribes embrace virtue, yet it cannot be feigned. When blame accumulates, the heart of the realm fractures, and no words can mend it. Thus, small virtues, though of little immediate benefit, must not be neglected, and minor vices, though seemingly harmless, must not be indulged.

Those who indulge their desires without sharing joy with the people find no solace in their sorrows. Those who erode their foundations and heighten their perch fail to see the collapse beneath them, and none remain to prop them up. Then, reins slip from their grasp, divine artifacts are seized and never returned, strength is exhausted, wealth depleted, and the people can bear no more. Resentment festers below, Heaven’s wrath gathers above. Fields become bandits, and within the tent, Qi and Qi plot; Ji Chang seizes Yin from the west, while Chen and Wu rise with swords drawn. Liu and Xiang brandish spears, their fury like a gale. Scaling ladders breach the hundred-zhang walls, blades clash beneath the palace gates, and flaming arrows rain upon the inner palace. The outer defenses crumble, and only then do they lament, seeking distant sages, sweeping the path to welcome recluses, yearning for Yi and Lü from the mountains, summoning Sun and Wu from the wilds. They beg for wise counsel yet heed none, seek grand strategies yet ask no questions—like pouring water from the sea to douse a burning mansion, or crafting a boat after the flood has swept away the home.

The lofty title of "majestic" cannot be claimed through arrogance and stinginess, nor can the seal of Mount Tai’s enfeoffment be secured through wanton desire. Even the sagest of rulers, spurring their chariots to the limit, feared falling short of the past—yet mediocre sovereigns, ambling at a leisurely pace, believe themselves superior. Some, in safety, contemplate peril; others, in peril, indulge in ease. Some, having achieved order, remain vigilant and unyielding; others, teetering on the edge like a string of eggs, remain oblivious, unaware. Without the downfall of tyrants like Xin and Gui, how could the value of reverence and wisdom be so keenly felt? Let this be ever in mind—oh, ever in mind!

**Ministerial Integrity (Chapter Six)**

The Master Embracing Simplicity said: In the ancient eras of Tang and Yu, though they examined antiquity and revered wisdom, they still awaited the assisting brilliance of their ministers to achieve towering success. Thus, the imperial enterprise flourished, all affairs coalesced, the Four Gates stood solemn, the Hundred Offices proceeded in harmony, and the barbarians posed no threat to the Central Kingdom. The Phoenix nested in the Aligned Chambers—

This may be likened to the head and the limbs: though there exists a distinction of honor and humility, in truth, they rely upon one another as one body. The ruler must measure ability before bestowing office, lest the overturned tripod’s ruin ensue. The minister must weigh his talents before accepting, that he may avoid the calamity of exile.

Those who, like shadow and echo, bow and obey every command are but hollow vessels of idleness. Those who defy orders, confront authority, and steadfastly reject self-interest are the pillars that secure the throne. Those who anticipate desires and flatter are the sycophants. Those who correct errors and rectify deviations are the state’s unyielding backbone.

If one must prostrate before the executioner’s block to offer forthright remonstrance, or cling to the cauldron’s edge to speak truth—when loyalty is met with suspicion and slander prevails—then exile may be endured. If one’s death would serve no purpose but to compound the ruler’s faults, then departure is permissible.

In all actions, if they are not grounded in the canonical teachings, they must not be undertaken.

When at rest, they do not follow established norms without cause. Petitions and solicitations find no place; personal barriers are never breached for private ends. Punishments are administered with clarity, never indiscriminately. What grieves them is that rewards are not bestowed upon those who curry favor, that they do not usurp authority to amass power, that they do not tolerate corruption while feigning purity. In office, they strive to speak fully to correct errors; in retirement, they ponder how to recommend the worthy without obstruction. Rising early and retiring late, they are anxious that the people’s affairs are not at peace. Their personal conduct is frugal, their aspirations restrained, as though guarding against treading on thin ice. They accept counsel and present scholars, yet never boast of it; they do not dwell on gains that are not righteous. In their hearts, when standing in court, they hold fast to the integrity of an arrow’s shaft; in private, they take the completion of good deeds as their standard. When facing peril or encountering difficulty, they abandon their families without hesitation. When wielding the scales of justice, they weigh with impartiality, making no distinction between near and far. They emulate the directives of Xiao He and Cao Shen, admire the extraordinary strategies of Zhang Liang and Chen Ping, pursue the loyal devotion of Zhou Bo, model themselves on the uprightness of the two Bao, uphold the frugality and integrity of Yuan Ang, and maintain the steadfast resolve of Tian Fen. In the end, they willingly accept the fate of Yao Li, who burned his body, while guarding against the disaster of Han Xin and Ying Bu, who lost their loyalty.

They do not shirk labor when sent forth, nor do they boast of merit upon return. They ascribe achievements to others, take blame upon themselves, and yield precedence to those below. Their sincerity is reverent and trembling, ever as though the awe of Heaven’s majesty were before their faces. Day and night, they remain vigilant, as though a seething cauldron stood at their side. When burdened with great trust, they take Yi Yin and the Duke of Zhou as their models; when exerting themselves in the four directions, they follow the examples of Ji Dan and Shao Hu. In sending off the departed and attending to the living, they exhaust their loyalty without wavering. When seizing traitors who violate the law, they are as fierce as hawks and falcons preying on sparrows. In defending the frontiers, they emulate the lofty footsteps of Wei Jiang and Li Mu. In governing the people, they aspire to the virtuous transformations of Wen Weng and Ren Chen.

Those who are utterly loyal do not use their state for personal gain—how much less would they employ their wisdom to mislead their ruler? Those who are steadfast in righteousness do not fear the destruction of their ancestral temples—how much less would they shrink from demotion or disgrace? Thus, they are able to preserve their meritorious honor, ennoble their kin, and entrust their legacy to posterity. As the odes of the people resound and the ritual vessels of Kunwu endure, the capable inscribe their achievements. What men were Hou Ji and Gao Yao, if not such as these?

The *Baopuzi* states: If a minister’s merit is not substantial, he feels shame at the emptiness of his generous salary. If his achievements are not flourishing, he is humiliated by the undeserved height of his rank. Walking in trust and contemplating compliance, he aids both Heaven and man. Fearing excess and dwelling in humility, he ensures lasting fortune. Every step adheres to the rules; every raised hand follows the marking line. Though honors may be delayed, disgrace and regret will surely be far off.

If, however, one harms the ruler to curry favor with subordinates, abandons the public good to pursue private ends, flatters and obeys to add water to water—even when the ruler’s actions are flawed, he smiles and praises them, repeatedly offering trinkets to lead the ruler into wrongdoing—using clever words to undermine governance, his countenance seeking to please, he obscures the ruler’s clarity below and blocks the path of worthy men’s advancement. Externally, he forms alliances with foreign powers; internally, he cultivates factions that betray the public. Though his talent may suffice to gloss over wrongs, his words to embellish faults, and his authority to rival that of Zhao Gao in dominating the court or Dong Zhuo in his unchecked power—there has never been one who did not end with his body steeped in the executioner’s blade and his family consumed by fire.

Yet the foolish and blind abandon the correct to follow the crooked, forsake the true to consort with the false. Though ruin looms, they do not alter their course. The calamities that befall them do not descend from Heaven.

The *Baopuzi* states: A minister is like the limbs to the body—when treading on ice or grasping thorns, he cannot refuse. Thus, the ancients compared him to the earth: dig into it, and springs of water emerge; plant in it, and a hundred grains flourish. The living stand upon it; the dead enter it. Though his merits are many, he does not expect reward; though his toils are great, he does not dare complain. Clearly understanding this principle is the key to preserving oneself.

The *Baopuzi* states: A minister’s duties are manageable when his responsibilities are limited, but when his charge is vast, much will be neglected. Without the strength of Meng Ben and Xia Yu, one cannot shoulder the weight of multiple men; without the exceptional ability of ten thousand, one cannot oversee unique and complex situations. This is why Han Xin was punished for overstepping his authority and Ziyuan feared the disaster of being unequal to his task.

If, however, one’s talents are unparalleled, both civil and martial virtues perfectly balanced, possessing within the lofty calculations of a trusted advisor and without the far-reaching strategies of a master diplomat—though affairs may be numerous, all will be accomplished; though paths may diverge, both will be traversed. To abandon such a one is to disrupt the proper order; to entrust him is to find no equal. In such cases, one may take on multiple roles. For those not of this caliber, they should reflect carefully: if a weak cart bears a heavy load, how rarely does it reach its destination!

Ordinary men, when rewarded with honor, do not consider the later perils. Once they are drowned in disaster, it extends to their ruler and kin—is this not lamentable? All decline the axe and adze, yet when in high office, none yield their positions, even when unfit for the duties. Alas! This is why Chen Ping and Li Yiji issued warnings about insufficient strength, and Zhang Liang loftily withdrew to practice restraint.

**Good Governance, Seventh Chapter**

The *Baopuzi* states: Birds that flock together without choosing their trees will surely be those whose nests are abandoned. Those who enter service without assessing the times will surely be men who face danger and disgrace. When the times are favorable, they soar like dragons riding the clouds; when the times are lost, they drift like great fish stranded on high ground. Thus, the wise conceal their talents and await their moment, biding their time to act.

If lofty crags are about to collapse, thin threads cannot hold them; if the Dragon Gate boils over, a handful of earth cannot dam it. Therefore, they do not recklessly seek profit or gamble on luck.

The *Baopuzi* states: The Duke of Zhou’s regency, Yi Yin’s demotion of Tai Jia, Huo Guang’s deposition of the Prince of Changyi, and Sun Lin’s removal of the young emperor—these are called setting aside the Dao to employ expediency for the sake of stabilizing the state. Yet the Duke of Zhou was exiled, with slanderous rumors filling the roads; Yi Yin ultimately faced execution, and a great fog lasted three days; Huo Guang nearly brought ruin upon himself, and his family was soon destroyed; Sun Lin’s head and feet were separated before the mulberry trees he planted had time to change. Their cries of laughter had not yet faded when their wails of sorrow began.

"If, when in peril, one does not support, of what use is that minister?" Seven upright ministers could not save a ruler without Dao, allowing villains like Wang Mang to arise, using old precedents to embellish their wrongs while harboring the hearts of wolves and jackals. The foundation of disorder was laid by Yi Yin and Huo Guang. Future gentlemen should take deep heed of this.

The matter of deposing and installing rulers is a minor compliance with the great reversal—it cannot be prolonged. The deceit of Zhao Wang was already met with censure; how much less can one depose a ruler and expect it to be acceptable? Such men all calculated their actions and, once successful, gradually faced calamity. Yet those whose schemes began in secrecy, who were betrayed by greedy men, whose clans were exterminated and ancestral temples destroyed, while others were enfeoffed—such cases are not few.

If there are treacherous ministers aiding and abetting tyranny, like Jie’s Gan Xin, Zhou’s Chong Hou and E Lai, or Li’s faction—would it not be easy to replace them with the loyal and good? Remove the many evils at the ruler’s side, banish the corrupt to the four frontiers, hold the borders with arms, uphold the straight Dao, guard the law, maintain strict discipline, wield the axe with righteousness, stand with solemn dignity, reward with certainty, punish without exception, allow no transgressions, appoint the worthy, employ the capable, associate only with the loyal, never act without authorization, proceed only after petitioning, correct the ruler’s errors with reason when he strays, and tremble with reverence, never forgetting respect. Thus, the state will be eternally secure above, and one’s own person will be free from harm below. When the task is accomplished, do not remain; resign and retire. Select the loyal and capable to take one’s place—is this not more than sufficient?

Why must one seize the sovereign’s seals and endanger the lord one professes to serve? The ruler is as Heaven, as a father—if the ruler may be deposed, then Heaven too may be altered, and a father too may be replaced. Those whose merit overshadows the age go unrewarded; those whose might overawes their lord court peril. Such men, though peerless in victory and conquest, in meritorious toil, are like the bow cast aside once the birds are slain, the hound boiled once the hares are caught. How much more so when one deposes one’s lord and yet expects the successor to cherish him? Is this not akin to a son who abandons the mother who bore him, casts her into the mountain valleys, takes another to raise him, and then boasts, “I am as filial as Boyu or Zeng Shen—only my parent was unfit for service, so I discarded her”? Though others may feast him with triply sacrificial meats, attend him at dusk and dawn, how can they truly hold him dear? Even if he is trusted, men like Huo Guang, though showered with ranks, titles, and boundless rewards, were exalted only by stratagem—was this their true devotion?

To cast off a wife and slander her former husband, to buy a slave and revile his former master—even common men find such acts unjust. Why? Because they deeply wound the bonds of natural affection. Thus, Yue Yang was shunned for his ruthlessness, and Qin Xi was favored for his excessive indulgence. Yet世人 deem Tang and Wu righteous and Yi Yin and Huo Guang worthy—this only encourages treason. Moreover, deposed rulers are not always wholly at fault: some, while aiding young lords, abuse their power and accumulate great sins, plotting future harm. When their influence still holds, others replace them to avert immediate disaster, securing their own advantage under the guise of stabilizing the realm. Once authority is seized and life-and-death decisions are made, the deposed lord’s divine mandate is lost, and all sins of the lower ranks are laid at his door. Though some may see this, who dares speak openly? Without a Dong Mou or Zhu Xu to devise a plan, without a Nan Shi or Dong Hu to record the crime, who in later days will set the record straight? Only the discerning will realize that the wickedness of Jie and Zhou was not so great, nor the virtue of Tang and Wu so splendid.

The canons all exalt the ruler and humble the minister, strengthen the trunk and weaken the branches. The *Spring and Autumn*’s principle holds that Heaven cannot be opposed. The sages’ scriptures teach that serving father and lord is as one, and the people’s lives rest on these three bonds, equally upheld. To permit the deposition of rulers is to open the gate to unrighteousness, to let the low overstep the high—how can this serve as instruction? Vulgar scholars, mired in sophistry, debate whether Tang and Wu were like those who ate horse liver to condemn such acts, failing to grasp that adaptability in change is more precious than rigid adherence to goodness, that one may act against the norm without rebelling against principle.

Yet earlier writers did not refute such deeds with the great Dao, allowing those of such intent to sharpen their blades and ascend crumbling peaks—this is no way to prolong life, secure the age, or avert danger. Though their fame may briefly flourish and their rewards swiftly gather, it is no different from the sacrificial ox adorned in embroidery, the deep-sea fish coveting buckwheat, the parched man drinking from the clouds, or the starving man gorging on rancid meat. Those who wield the brush all praise such acts as fine tales, turning unpardonable crimes into wisdom in change, leaving men to sigh in endless lament.

If some admonish me that this harms the sages and will surely bring censure, I reply: Shun and Yu were tested within and without before receiving the mandate at Wen Zu’s shrine. Even if one wished to harm the sages, how could they be harmed? Once, Yan Yannian impeached Huo Guang for unrighteousness, and the court fell silent, unable to refute him. How much less should I fear, when my words serve as a warning to the age and name no one in particular? As the saying goes...

**Chapter Eight: The Difficulty of the Times**

The Master of Embracing Simplicity said: To fulfill one’s duty without seeking reward may be possible. But to demand that one’s words be heeded and one’s person be secure—this must await a time when the times are not adverse. For those who scheme and obstruct, even the bandit Zhi could not surpass them. They exalt the powerful and suppress the lowly, detest the upright and revile the just, dreading the censure of the impartial and fearing the exposure of their crookedness. Thus, they strive to eliminate those who surpass them to avert their own punishment. Enlightened rulers do not appear in every generation, and mediocre sovereigns confuse black and white. Unable to accept and employ loyal counsel, they may even leak the most earnest admonitions. Thereupon, flatterers like Hong Gong and Shi Xian adorn their cunning words to fabricate semblances of virtue, feigning public spirit to advance their private schemes. Now, those who offer arts of longevity are met with the penalty of immediate execution, and those who propose plans to secure the throne swiftly incur the calamity of endangering their own lives. Thus it is said: It is not the speaking that is difficult—it is the timing of speech that is hard.

Even when the worthy advise the sagely, they may not be immediately accepted. Thus, Yi Yin petitioned Tang seventy times. When the wise counsel the foolish, their words will surely not be received. Thus, King Wen’s remonstrance of Zhou was ultimately rejected. That one’s words are not believed may still be tolerable. But consider the executions of Li Si and Han Fei, the mutilation of Sun Bin by Pang Juan, the slander of Qu Ping by Shangguan, and the framing of Chao Cuo by Yuan Ang—such cases are too numerous to record. Truly, serving as a minister is no easy path, and not by one route alone!

Those who depart and never return leave their achievements to posterity, while those who conceal their talents to await the right moment find that such opportunities arise but once in a hundred lifetimes. Ministers of towering merit appear but once in ages, while sycophants and flatterers fill the pages of history—how grievous is the difficulty of encountering the right time, so extreme as this!

From this, I know that men like Lü Shang of the Wei River banks and the recluses of the cliffs and caves—those who bore the instruments of royal counsel and held the talents to save their age—ended their days among the ranks of common laborers, wielding fishing rods and leaning on staffs, never to meet a King Wen or a Gaozong. Such men cannot be faulted.

**On the Governance of Offices, Ninth Chapter**

The *Baopuzi* states: The swift gallop of the famed steeds Lu’er and Ji was due to the driving of Zaofu. The orderly succession of Yu and Ji to the hundred offices was owing to the sovereigns of Tang and Yu. Thus, without toil, they reached a thousand *li*; through yielding and deference, their praises resounded.

Yet when lowly slaves ride fine steeds like *Suqiang* or when King Xin of Yin rules over the Three Worthies, should they seek to charge ahead, the reins will chafe and the yoke press tight; should they strive to offer exhaustive counsel and exhaust their loyalty, disaster will strike like a loosed crossbow. Thus, the chariot overturns on treacherous roads, and the state collapses beyond recovery.

For this reason, noble steeds are ruined by clumsy drivers, and wise scholars stumble in benighted times. Confucius could not halt the Duke of Lu from setting out, nor could Yan Ying prevent the rebellion of Cui Zhu—such men possessed the talent, but their lords were unworthy.

The lord is like a vessel; the minister, like its contents.

A vessel small cannot contain a thing large; thus, a child may cast aside a thousand pieces of gold to chase a butterfly, and the people of Yue may spurn the eight delicacies to relish frogs and toads. What they prize is not truly precious, and what ails them is their failure to recognize what is essential. For if a thing is not put to use, though rare, it is not valued; if none partake of it, those who would harm it will surely come.

Of old, Duke Ling of Wei heard sagely words yet startled repeatedly; Duke Xiao of Qin listened to lofty discourse yet fell into fearful slumber. To seek the achievements of grand governance and the merits of able ministers while retreating in their pursuit is like racing backward to chase the swift—setting out for Chu yet heading toward Yan.

**On the Pursuit of Rectitude (10)**
*Baopuzi* states: The Southern Sea draws the tributaries to form its unfathomable depths; the Celestial Gardens pile wood and stone to reach the utmost heights of heaven. A towering edifice pierces the clouds by the accumulation of many beams; a wheel’s curve and a yoke’s straightness leave no part wanting. Thus, when the sagely ministers of the *Yuankai* assisted [King Wen], their transforming virtue reached heaven; when those of pacifying might were complete, the treacherous schemers of the hunt lay dormant. This is how Shun and Yu came to possess the realm without contention, and why Duke Ling of Wei, though arrogant and self-indulgent, did not perish.

When many strengths combine, even ten thousand *jun* are not too heavy to lift; when the wisdom of the multitude is employed, even myriad tasks are not too burdensome to bring to order. Thus, the centipede dies yet does not topple, and the state with many scholars falters yet does not fall. Yet a dam’s stitching cannot mend seams, nor can an awl’s drill sever; oxen and horses cannot guard the gate, nor can chickens and dogs pull the carriage. Employ each in its strength, and no task will fail; avoid their shortcomings, and the world will waste no talent.

**On Honoring the Worthy (11)**
*Baopuzi* states: One who abandons a light boat to cross boundless waters does not see certain passage; one who seeks grand governance without worthy assistance has never heard of success. The great roc’s ascent through the void relies on the strength of its six pinions; the deep-dwelling dragon’s flight to heaven depends on the clouds’ companionship. Thus, summoning the worthy and employing resources are the sovereign’s essential duties; establishing merit and accomplishing deeds are the thoughts of the eminent and talented.

To delight in order and stability yet neglect the wise is no different from desiring a distant journey while casting aside the *qiji* steed. To lift the obscure from their rustic seclusion, to raise the overlooked from their hidden depths, to assign them tasks commensurate with their talents, and to reward them with emoluments befitting their merits—this is how a lord treats the worthy. To toil day and night in public service, to exhaust heart and strength in the hundred offices, to advance the good and expel the wicked, and to leave no knowledge unapplied—this is how a minister repays his patron.

The world may have reclusive worthies, but no lord stands alone; scholars may delight in retreat without worry, but a ruler cannot bring order without ministers. Thus, Fu Yue and Lü Shang did not eagerly seek recognition, for their virtue was complete and they held kings and dukes in light esteem. Yet Kings Tang and Wen of Zhou dreamed of them—obtaining the worthy is how great merit is built, for it requires able assistance.

The peril of life arises within the depths of the palace, and one grows up in...

The hands of palace women know nothing of the hardships of sowing and reaping, nor do they understand the principles of anxiety and fear. Entrusted with the household and the succession of the ancestral line, they are blind to the rise and fall of the state. Their urgent concern lies in extravagance and waste, their chief pursuit in hunting and feasting, drowning in revelry and music. Hearing lewd sounds, they startle to listen; seeing alluring sights, they shift their gaze. Their ears and eyes are enslaved to these two things alone, their judgment of people clouded, their spirits untrained in the classics. They know only play and amusement to please their hearts and minds, unaware that extraordinary men can secure the altars of state. Rhinoceros horns, elephant tusks, pearls, and jade—though worthless—arrive from ten thousand *li* away, while vessels that could steady a tottering realm, though capable of action, sink into oblivion within the four borders. The twin scourges of illness have already taken hold, yet they seek good physicians; the disaster of a sagging roofbeam has already gathered, yet they ponder schemes—how is this different from digging a well when fire breaks out or surveying fields when hunger strikes?

Even the mediocre and dull cannot be left untended and expected to exhaust their strength—how much less can one hope for sudden usefulness from those without prior cultivation?

**XVI. Employing the Capable**

Some say: "A tail larger than the body cannot be wagged; a minister more worthy than his lord cannot be employed." Thus, forcing down what the mouth cannot contain will surely choke, and entrusting power to one whose talent is unmatched will invite contempt.

The *Baopuzi* says: How perverse is this claim! In ancient times, Jingzi assumed the reins of state as a mere boy, and the kingdom was saved—thanks to the twenty-five elders who gathered around him. Zijian of Jian rose from commoner status to govern a great commandery, his success due to the many men greater than himself who brought their aid. Duke Huan of Qi killed his brother to seize the throne, his conduct like that of birds and beasts—yet when he unbound his hair, poured libations, and entrusted all affairs to the Master of the Left, he became the first of the hegemons. After Guan Zhong’s death, however, disasters arose in quick succession. When Lu employed Jizi, for over twenty years there were no flawed policies at home and no encroachments abroad; the people, though perishing, suffered no famine or plague—was this not because his talent fell short of his achievements? Yet when he took charge himself, the calamities were as described.

Emperor Gao of Han decided defenses within the palace curtains and determined victory a thousand *li* away—yet he was not the equal of Zhang Liang and Chen Ping. He trained troops in great numbers and grew ever stronger, yet in battle he was no match for Han Xin and Ying Bu. Only by employing them all together did he complete the imperial enterprise. Thus, swift steps and weary marches are not as good as riding a carriage; chasing and pursuing are not as good as borrowing the skills of others. Strong crossbows, though hard to draw, can shatter the sturdy and reach the distant; great ships, though hard to board, can carry heavy loads and cross deep waters. Fierce generals, though hard to control, can break enemy offensives and expand the borders; lofty worthies, though hard to approach, can put the constant norms in order.

In the past, Duke Ai of Lu was a mediocre ruler, yet Confucius, the supreme sage, did not dare but devote himself wholly to his service. Duke Jing of Qi was of limited ability, yet Yan Ying, a man of great worth, did not dare but offer his utmost sincerity. How could a minister presume to compare his wisdom and strength, his strategic vision and capacity, with those of his lord? Must one wait for a Yao or Shun before serving? What task is not the lord’s? What command is not the people’s? To feel shame that one’s lord does not measure up to Tang and Yu—this is the mindset of the enlightened.

**XVII. Honoring the Worthy**

The *Baopuzi* says: When You Yu was among the Rong, Duke Mu of Qin was filled with worry; when Dechen was killed, Duke Wen of Jin rejoiced. When Yue Yi departed, Yan fell into ruin; when Fan Li and Wen Zhong entered, Yue became a hegemon. These were men who could break states and destroy families—how could their presence alone be so weighty and their absence so light?

The tomb of Liu Xiahui still blunted the edge of the Xiongnu’s might—how much more would his presence in the court? The reclusion of Ganku still repelled the attacks of a neighboring state—how much more if he were placed at the right hand of the throne? The mere likeness of Zhi Du made fierce enemies tremble; the corpse of Zhuge Liang still made great states hesitate to act. With such men to repel invaders, the land would surely not be encroached upon; with such men to lead the army, the lord would surely not be humiliated.

Thus, the enlightened lord distributes silken gifts in humble alleys, raises the forgotten feathers in withered groves, and sends carriages flying across river bridges. He opens the four gates without tiring, spares no gold or jade, travels a thousand *li* without reluctance, humbles himself without shame, and lowers his words without disgrace—all to make the pursuit of worthies his foremost duty and the acquisition of men of talent his greatest treasure. Those who recommend them receive the highest rewards; those who obstruct them are deemed usurpers of office.

Thus, the Duke of Zhou presented his gift in a thatched cottage; the Lord of Shao bowed to Chang Hong in the marketplace. When Master Zou crossed the border, the Lord of Yan swept the path before him; when Zhuangzi had not yet eaten, the Lord of Zhao stood in attendance. Duke Wen of Jin received Hai Tang, yet sat with lowered mats and dared not straighten his posture; the Marquis of Qi visited Jiqiu, though weary from frequent visits, and did not shirk the labor. King Ding received a whipping from Bao Shen; Duke Jian removed his armor before Gong Lu. Though they lowered their lofty status and suppressed their pride to honor the lowly, in the end they only sharpened their vision and extended their sight, lent their ears to broaden their hearing. Like dragons soaring and tigers crouching, it was only fitting.

**XVIII. On Punishment**

Bao Puzi says: None do not esteem benevolence, yet none can govern purely by benevolence. None do not despise punishment, yet none can order the people without it. All say that a sagely ruler governs the age, and customs turn as grass bends before the wind; the Dao spreads and transformation ripens, so that peace prevails—yet when punishment is used, I must discuss it thus:

Virtue and instruction are the sacrificial robes of embroidered splendor; punishment and penalties are the armor and helmet that ward off blades. If one were to govern the cunning and violent with virtue and instruction, it would be like facing sharpened halberds in ceremonial vestments. To apply punishment and penalties in a well-ordered age would be like ascending the ancestral temple clad in armor. Thus, benevolence is the instrument for nurturing all things, while punishment is the tool for correcting wrongs. I wish to benefit them, yet they seek to harm; if benevolence fails to reform them, only punishment can halt their transgressions. Punishment serves as the assistant to benevolence—this much is clear.

Consider those who cultivate embryonic breathing, regulate their breath, exhale and inhale, absorb the radiance of the sun, turn their gaze inward, and practice the bear’s climb and the bird’s stretch—these are the arts of long life. Yet they are arduous and slow; few who attempt them succeed, and one in ten thousand may attain mastery. When illness is grave, pain severe, the body weakened, and life in peril, one cannot but attack it with iron and stone, treat it with potent medicines. If one were to abandon the remedies of Bian Que and instead aspire to the Way of Master Red Pine and Wang Qiao, the dead would be legion.

Benevolence in governance is not without its beauty, yet the common people, crafty and deceitful, pursue profit and forget righteousness. If one does not align them with awe, correct them with punishment, and yearn from afar for the customs of Fuxi and Shennong, chaos will become irreparable. Its calamity will be profound, and using killing to halt killing—is this a matter of delight? The creation of the Eight Trigrams exhausts principle and fulfills nature, making clear the use of punishment and imprisonment, as seen in *Shi Ke* bound with ropes and fetters, and in *Xi Kan*, where peril is habituated. Thus, the use of punishment is ancient indeed.

Even in the time of the Yellow Emperor, whose sagely virtue was lofty, he personally led expeditions, fought a hundred battles, left corpses strewn at Zhuolu, and blood flowing at Banquan—yet he could not ensure that his age knew no rebellion. Even when he sheathed his weapons, how could he make all the people good, never commit crimes, and govern themselves? In the flourishing age of Yao and Shun, which mirrored Heaven, punishment was still used: banishment, execution, exile, and exile afar—only then did the world submit. Emperor Wen of Han, whose silence was profound, rivaled the heights of Cheng and Kang—yet he still executed four hundred men, and many died under the lash.

Just as the master carpenter does not abandon the plumb line, so that no wood remains crooked, the enlightened ruler does not abolish execution and punishment, and thus no governance decays into ruin. The Way of Heaven and Earth cannot be purely benevolent: the Azure Yang unfolds the harmony of nurturing and ripening, while the White Autumn enforces the severity of withering and killing. When the melting wind fans, the withered and emaciated revive; when white dew congeals, the luxuriant blossoms wither and fall. Thus, the myriad things flourish, and the year’s work is accomplished. If warmth knew no cold, creeping things would not hibernate, and roots and plants would bloom in winter. If leniency knew no severity, villainy would arise, and sharp weapons would be ever at hand.

Therefore, the enlightened ruler employs clear rewards to uphold the upright and certain punishment to eliminate the perverse. The instruments of encouragement and deterrence are none more essential. To encourage the people, one establishes instruction, balancing leniency and severity so that the timid are not bullied and the resolute do not harm kindness. The crimes subject to the Five Punishments number three thousand—this is to ensure that defenses are not bent. When the Minister of Justice carries out punishment, the ruler does not raise his hand—this is to ensure that the law is not abolished. If defenses are bent, treachery sprouts; if the law is abolished, calamity and chaos multiply.

A ruined state is not without decrees; the trouble lies in their profusion and lack of enforcement. A defeated army is not without prohibitions; the trouble lies in their establishment and lack of observance. Thus, myriad evils spread unchecked, and inferiors grow insolent toward their superiors. Rewards are valued for matching merit, not for their weight; punishments are valued for fitting the crime, not for their cruelty.

If the whip and rod are abandoned in the household, servants grow lazy; if military campaigns cease in the state, subordinates grow remiss. Affection must await respect, or it will surely fail. Thus, rites are established to exalt virtue, and punishment is created to enforce awe. For virtue requires the support of awe to endure. If Ban Chu were to discard the compass and square, no object would be true to its form; if the enlightened ruler were to release the reins of law, deceit and cunning would run rampant.

Though Yao and Shun’s benevolence was like Heaven, they did not pardon the Four Crimes. Though the Duke of Zhou was kind to his brothers, he did not spare the Two Uncles. Confucius executed Zheng Mao; Emperor Wu of Han killed his nephew—both shed tears, yet the law left them no choice. Thus, executing one to shake ten thousand, sacrificing the few to accomplish the many: like combing tangled hair, the benefit is widespread; like excising an abscess, the preservation is great. Therefore, cauterization and acupuncture, though painful, cannot be halted, for they cure illness. Punishment and law, though they prevent ugliness, cannot be abolished, for they save the state.

An army of six legions is not necessarily all brave; charging into battle and plunging into fire are things men dread. Yet if one urges them with a serene countenance, few will risk their lives; if one drives them with decapitation, none will fail to fight fiercely. Thus, those who serve with laughter do not match the speed of those who advance at a shout; those who are lured with kindness do not equal the order of those driven by execution. Therefore, though one may be at ease in the deep valley, the law must be strict; though Wei Zi was swift to discard ashes, his punishments were severe. For to use what they fear to restrain what they trifle with is the art of preserving the people whole.

The art of discerning illness lies in preventing disease before it arises; the essence of governing chaos lies in averting calamity before it comes. If one were to use light punishments to forbid grave crimes, or employ lenient laws to guard great profit, setting them forth in ever-greater detail—yet offenders would only multiply. This is like digging a pit to block the road: not the method of a humane ruler.

The good governor must first rectify himself to lead others, govern the near to order the distant, and not bend the law to follow his inclinations. He must punish the guilty without pardon—like Shi Que, who sacrificed love to destroy his kin, or Duke Wen of Jin, who suppressed his feelings to execute Jie Zitui. Thus, benevolence is the adornment of governance, while punishment is the bridle and reins of ruling the age. Adornment is not the most urgent thing for the body, yet bridle and reins cannot be absent for even a moment.

If solemn reverence slackens even slightly, indolence and insolence arise; if majestic severity relaxes for a moment, the multitude of evils takes heart. To fail to anger when anger is due is to make a tiger of a treacherous minister; to fail to kill when killing is due is to unleash a great bandit. Water, long eroding the river and mountains, begins with a handbreadth; a towering tree of a thousand spans starts with a hair’s breadth. A fire kindled by drilling wood can be extinguished with a ladle of water; an unhatched swan’s egg can be crushed in the palm. Yet when it rides the whirlwind and stirs the vast wilds, or beats its six wings to soar into the morning clouds, even the wise and brave cannot control it.

Thus, the enlightened ruler governs difficulty while it is easy, eliminates evil while it is still small. He does not boast of his goodness to foster disorder, nor wield the axe with hesitation. Punishment, then, is the sacred instrument of the state, to be wielded by the ruler alone—it cannot be entrusted to others, just as a long sword cannot be grasped by the blade, or a giant fish cannot be drawn from the deep.

This is the origin of rise and decline, the source of safety and peril. Tian Chang’s usurpation of Qi, the Six Ministers’ partition of Jin, Zhao Gao’s assassination of the Qin emperor, Wang Mang’s seizure of the Han—these all began with the gradual accumulation of frost before the ice hardens.

Some sigh forever by the seashore, others clutch their hearts at Wangyi Palace—calamities that spread to ancestral temples serve as warnings to those yet to come. This arises from admiring empty fame in bygone ages while forgetting the real disasters at hand.

Some argue: "The rise of penal laws belongs to degenerate times. The Way of establishing people lies only in benevolence and righteousness. If I remain serene, the people will naturally rectify themselves; if I am without desire, the people will naturally return to simplicity." The caution against overcooking the fish is not to make it too elaborate. Govern with leniency and love, and you will win the multitude; employ joy in command, and your subordinates will draw near. Thus, Mencius regarded embodying benevolence as peace, while Yang Xiong called Shen Buhai and Han Fei butchers. Excessive reins and tight bridles are not the driving of Master Fu; harsh punishments and severe penalties are not the Way of the Three August Ones and Five Emperors. In the time of Youyu, the hand did not need to wave, the mouth did not need to speak—merely facing south in reverence, and governance flourished in harmony. Fu Sheng merely led by example, strumming his zither and chanting poetry; without descending from his hall, the foolish and wise alike were brought to order.

If one can enrich the impoverished, lighten taxes, relieve the destitute, promote the overlooked, employ the worthy, encourage agriculture, reduce expenditures, attract the estranged with ritual, and embrace the distant with virtue; if one can mold them with the *Chengjun* and govern them with the *Xiangxu*; if the transformation from above inspires goodness like grass bending before a sudden wind, and those who wash their hearts and reform their faces do so like clear waves rinsing away light dust—then the court will be filled with groups yielding virtue, the countryside will be free of those treading paths of impropriety. The prisons will empty, the weapons of Chu will forever be stilled. Why must rewards and punishments be the means to govern a state?

The *Book of Changes* states: "Clarify punishments to rectify laws." The *Shu* says: "Pity those in judgment; in court, bestow ranks; in the marketplace, execute punishments." This has been so since antiquity. Is it merely that in degenerate times, when benevolence abounds, laws are not established, and when authority is weak, subordinates encroach upon their superiors? If laws are not established, all affairs will be thrown into chaos; if subordinates encroach upon their superiors, rebellious signs will sprout. By the Three Dynasties, the purest essence had already diluted, and the great simplicity further scattered under Qin and Han. The Dao declined in former times, and customs grew shallow in the present. To now seek to restrain and rectify deceit without words, to transform cunning and treachery by relinquishing the reins—this is like riding a galloping horse on a perilous path while discarding the rudder and oars, drifting on a hollow boat to brave the waves; like chasing thieves with bows and courtesies, or slaying Chao Cuo to repel the Seven States; like brandishing weapons to pacify the Red Eyebrows—such measures have never been seen to succeed.

The Three Sovereigns walked, the Five Emperors trotted, and since the age of hegemons and kings, they have galloped without rest. When governance falters, officials deceive the people with craft, bandits and thieves operate openly. Shaving heads and branding faces are insufficient to punish the shameless; exterminating clans cannot deter covetousness. Multiplying eyes to broaden vision, piling ears to sharpen hearing, raising torches to untangle stagnant affairs, and exhausting the heart to stem the source of treachery—yet still, the marketplace and court echo with sighs, and the frontiers suffer wrongs unheard. Those who wield power to oppress and favor may act beneath the gaze of all; those who harm their families and endanger the state may scheme within their own walls. To govern a decadent age with the Way of Great Heaven, or to quell boiling chaos with a song of uniformity, is to fail to recognize the need to adapt to changing times, to understand the necessity of adjusting gains and losses.

This is like carving a mark on a boat to seek a lost sword, or shooting an arrow at the sky to hit a target five paces away; like casting aside rhinoceros-hide armor to wade into unfathomable depths, or donning a light robe to endure sweltering heat; like trying to untie a knot by stomping on it, or scratching one’s cheek to relieve an itchy back—nothing could be more foolish. One should first issue commands and only then execute punishments, uncover the truth without delight, so that Bo Shi harbors no resentment for losing his fief, and Yu and Rui know shame without litigation.

If the violent conceal their nature and wield power without fear, if one tolerates disgrace and lets the weeds grow unchecked, relying on grand words while forgetting the urgency of festering sores—how is this different from one parched with thirst pointing to the distant sea while the water rises to engulf them, or hastily building a boat in the forest as the floodwaters surge? How can one escape the fate of Kuafu or avoid the disaster of drowning?

The world disparages Shen Buhai and Han Fei’s practical methods while praising Laozi and Zhuangzi’s extravagant theories. Yet in governance, none can do without punishments. Those who kill are pardoned for their deaths; those who injure others are absolved of their crimes—this is like offering mud and broken tiles to starving people in the morning. The words of Daoists may be lofty, but applying them is as impractical as using a *Ganjiang* sword to sew thread, or a giant elephant to catch a mouse; as futile as golden elixirs crossing stormy waves, or jade horses racing a thousand *li*.

If one were to follow their words, one would burn the stocks and destroy the prisons, abolish officials, erase penal codes, melt down weapons, level city walls, disperse treasuries, destroy tally seals, dismantle barriers, and smash measuring tools. One would blind Li Zhu’s eyes and deafen Shi Kuang’s ears, drifting untethered, returning to nature, untrained and uncaring, forgetting all distinctions between rivers and lakes, court and wilderness. If the world were empty of people, one might never interact until death—such ideas are easy to debate but impossible to practice.

Vulgar Confucians only know that Zhou rose through benevolence and Qin fell through severity, yet they fail to realize that Zhou’s success was not purely due to benevolence, nor Qin’s downfall solely due to severity. In ancient times, Zhou employed corporal punishments—amputating feet, slicing noses—and at the covenant of Mengjin, those who arrived late were executed. They swore oaths of collective punishment, yet their actions were not entirely benevolent. By its later years, Zhou neglected laws and played with empty texts; rulers grew tyrannical, edicts failed to reach beyond the cosmos, rituals and music no longer emanated from the throne, and subordinates vied for power like serpents devouring their own tails. They cut off the source and blocked the flow, casting aside caps and tearing robes, some drowning in the Han River, others exiled to Zhi. The defeat of losing authority stemmed from insufficient severity.

Qin’s early rise saw officials of talent like Shang Yang and You Yu implementing laws within, while generals like Bai Qi and Wang Jian conquered without. They annexed the weak, attacked the benighted, established dominance, and secured hegemony, swallowing neighbors and crushing rivals, expanding territory and repelling barbarians, transforming like dragons and glaring like tigers—all through clear rewards and certain punishments, laying the foundation for imperial rule. Yet by its later years, Qin grew arrogant in success, indulging in extravagance and excess, compounded by tyranny. They built the Great Wall for ten thousand *li*, constructed over a thousand palaces, amassed bells, drums, and female musicians without moving them. The labor at Mount Li, the taxes of half the land, the conscription of the poor—these, along with the brutality of burying scholars alive, the campaigns against the Xianyun in the north and the Baiyue in the south, mobilized millions of troops for decades. The world mourned separations, every household sighed in resentment; bones piled into mountains, empty sacrifices spread across the fields. Xu Fu departed, and the people wailed in grief; Zhao Gao entered, and the court filled with wolves. The realm sought rebellion, nine out of ten homes stood empty. Was Qin’s downfall truly due to severity? This was Qin gaining through severity, not losing through it.

The twofold purpose of punishments lies in crafting skillful governance, yet those who employ them to their own detriment wound the state instead. A realm governed by the Dao may be aided by punishments, for they suppress deceit and falsehood, guard against depravity, and correct wayward intent—like a net whose ropes are cut, its mesh disordered. To offend Heaven by misapplying punishments is to court swift peril. Water and fire, too, sustain life yet also destroy it, depending on whether one can wield them. If growths and tumors are left untreated and the arts of Bian Que are not practiced, how can one hope to attain the longevity of Lao Peng?
