When treacherous factions abound yet no strict laws are enforced to curb their transgressions, the blessings of enduring peace remain unseen.

One must instead select men like Zhang Tang, entrusting them with the administration of law, and appoint officials like Zhao Yu and Chen Chong, charging them with meticulous scrutiny. Let the enlightened ruler remain vigilant above, while loyal and upright ministers devote themselves below. Upon spying wrongdoing, they strike like hawks and falcons seizing sparrows; at the first sign of disorder, they uproot it like weeding a field, cutting away the foul and overgrown. If rewards are never misapplied and executions never miss their mark, then the path to Great Peace need not be trodden anew—transgressions will become rare. Yet to abolish punishments and achieve order? I dare not affirm such a claim.

Some may argue: "Are punishments not indeed a means to aid instruction, promote goodness, and restrain wickedness?" As for the corporal punishments of antiquity—might they be restored?

The *Baopuzi* replies: Why should they not? In former times, the Zhou employed corporal punishments for seven hundred years, while the Han abolished them, yet their dynasty did not endure as long. They replaced such punishments with flogging and caning, resulting in many deaths—outwardly a lighter penalty, but in truth, a means of killing. When a crime was too severe for a non-capital sentence, the only recourse was penal servitude, flogging, or caning. If a general amnesty were declared, the offender would suffer no lasting harm, merely shaving the hair of one reborn or striking wounds already healing—hardly sufficient to deter crimes deserving of death.

By abolishing corporal punishments, no intermediate penalty remains between death and lesser sentences. Thus, those not condemned to death must be punished with penal servitude, flogging, or caning, making it impossible to calibrate severity. Moreover, crimes are infrequent, and when they occur, execution breeds deep resentment, while flogging breeds little. Many would rather risk such penalties. Without corporal punishments, crimes just short of capital offenses often go unpunished.

If, however, corporal punishments were substituted for death in cases other than treason, rebellion against ruler or kin, military offenses in wartime, or murder by one’s own hand, they would suffice as deterrence. The punished could still perform labor, their lives spared, though their bodies would bear lifelong marks. The sight of such mutilation would chill the hearts of all, instilling fear in those yet to offend and serving as a warning to future generations—far more effective than execution. Though execution is severe, the condemned are buried after three days, their fate unknown to most, unseen by many.

Corporal punishments, however, serve as enduring admonitions. During the Wei dynasty, this matter was debated repeatedly. Eminent scholars, well-versed in governance, universally agreed that corporal punishments should be restored, though dissenting opinions were raised, none held merit. Emperor Wu of Wei concurred but hesitated, for the frontiers remained unsettled, and distant peoples, unaccustomed to unified rule, might misinterpret the amputation of limbs or the severing of ears and noses as cruel tyranny. Thus, he temporarily suspended the practice, awaiting the pacification of all quarters.

The learned Yang Xiong also advocated for the restoration of corporal punishments, yet their abolition had persisted for so long that those who sat in counsel did not deem it urgent.

**Master Baopu, Outer Chapters, Scroll One**
*From the Siku Quanshu, "Baopuzi Inner and Outer Chapters," Category: Masters, Daoist Works*

**Scroll Two**
*By Ge Hong of the Jin Dynasty*

**On the Examination and Recommendation of Officials (Shenju, Fifteenth)**

Master Baopu said: The towering heights of Mount Hua and Mount Heng reach the heavens because their foundations are deep. The resplendent achievements of Yao and Shun were made possible by their worthy ministers. Even with the hands of Sun Yang, without the hooves of a *qiji*, one cannot traverse a thousand *li*. Even with talents steeped in antiquity, without capable aides to execute tasks, one cannot bring order to the myriad affairs.

Though a ruler’s wisdom may rival the sun and moon, and his divine insight perceive the unseen, the ten thousand threads of governance cannot be managed alone, nor can trivial matters be personally overseen. He must rely on eyes to broaden his vision and ears to extend his hearing. Truly, he requires ministers to assist and support him. Thus, the sage-kings of old all rooted their hearts in seeking worthies, making the recommendation of talent their foremost duty. They bestowed jade and silk upon recluses in their hills and forests, dispatched carriages to remote valleys, labored in the search for men, and found ease in employing their abilities. From the lofty halls to the lowliest posts, all—whether discussing the Dao or governing the state—were entrusted with office. Through reverent self-restraint, they achieved effortless rule; through the suspension of punishments, their transformative influence spread without bounds, and the myriad regions knew peace.

Officials are established and duties assigned as one constructs a house: if a single component is unfit, collapse is inevitable. Yet those who have not yet offered tribute or been recommended are measured by the Four Categories, the Three Affairs, and the Nine Ranks. From these, only the most outstanding and exceptional are selected. But by the late Han, during the reigns of Emperors Huan and Ling, power had slipped from the imperial house into the hands of treacherous ministers. The net of governance was full of holes, the dikes had burst, the wind of virtue had withered, and instruction had faltered. Integrity was suppressed, flattery exalted; those who trod the Way were demoted, while the wealthy and powerful advanced. Competition became the custom, shamelessness the norm. Some offered bribes to sell themselves, others peddled letters of recommendation from influential men. Some relied on the prominence of fathers or elder brothers to secure appointments, while others, through years of bowing and scraping, were eventually recruited.

When the scales of selection are unbalanced, weights and measures become confused. When measuring vessels are crooked, quantities are muddled. Without clear standards, right and wrong blur; when the rules are skewed, the impure proliferate. To govern with such men is to invite cruelty and rapacity. They extort and amass wealth to recoup the cost of buying office, and once installed in court, their misrule surpasses tangled silk. They promote the mediocre and incompetent as their clique, and how can one expect the grass to bend before the wind when such men are in charge? Is this not like demanding luminous pearls from worthless shards or pure tones from an untuned instrument?

How can we fail to purify the turbid, let the light sink, sift the sand, and refine the good from the bad? How can we not tighten the methods of rigorous examination, heighten the barriers against greed, and fear the calamities that arise from such corruption? In ancient times, when feudal lords recommended men of talent, those who chose well were deemed meritorious and rewarded with higher rank and expanded territory. Those who chose poorly were deemed at fault and demoted or stripped of land. Yet even then, they could not escape the *Book of Songs*’ censure of "great carriages" and "eating without labor," nor the praise for those who "cut timber by the river" and "set traps for hares." How much worse, then, is the crime of recommending the unworthy or the peril of receiving those unfit for office? Once the scales are misaligned, how can the balance of many and few be restored?

The solitary and lofty, those who conceal their talents awaiting their worth, languish in obscurity and hardship. The petty and frivolous, who curry favor through social connections, seize opportunities and rise swiftly. When owls and vultures soar, mandarin ducks retreat into seclusion; when jackals and wolves dominate the roads, qilin flee. Promote the good and teach the people, and the unvirtuous will distance themselves. When the treacherous and false are honored, the heroic and upright withdraw. Those of noble character disdain association with the base and are ashamed to join the ranks of the gluttonous. If appointments are uniformly misguided, the worthy will seal their talents in silence. When the worthy seal their talents, villains will draw one another in. When villains draw one another in, the path of petty men lengthens. When the path of petty men lengthens, *taowu* and other monsters stand shoulder to shoulder. Thus, songs of praise fall silent, and cries of resentment rise in a clamor.

Men of towering talent, confident in their abilities, bend or stand, speak or remain silent, as fate dictates. They entrust success and failure, poverty and prosperity to nature. How can they avoid falling behind the factions of the powerful and submitting to the strong? Men of extraordinary virtue move only when propriety demands; they stand firm as mountains and still as deep pools. Few recognize them, while the clamorous and ambitious slander and obstruct them. Thus, even a ruler who longs for worthies may never learn where true talent lies. Those who cherish the Dao wish to serve but find no path. Though they possess the talents of Hou Ji and Xie, and the vast capacities to surpass their age, they remain mired in obscurity until death, never gaining rank or office. Meanwhile, those with factions and power gather like scales on a fish. With too few men for too many posts, how can one avoid scooping up and employing even the mediocre?

During the reigns of Emperors Ling and Xian, eunuchs held power, and a cabal of villains wielded authority, endangering the loyal and good. In the imperial court, appointments were mismanaged; in the provinces and commanderies, recommendations were treated lightly. When appointments are mismanaged above, governors and prefects are unfit for their posts. When recommendations are treated lightly below, the worthy among the *xiucai* and *xiaolian* are not selected. Thus, the people of the time said: *"Recommend a* xiucai *who knows no books; examine* xiaoxing *and find the father living apart. The pure and white are muddied like sludge; the finest generals retreat like chickens."* They also said: *"In ancient times, men studied the classics diligently to advance; today, they seek office to avoid work."* Such was the depth of their indignation.

At that time, offices were put up for sale like goods in a marketplace. Those vying for positions bought them like merchants. Those with bribes advanced without qualification; those with empty fists were recruited on sight. The more one paid, the higher the office; the less one paid, the lower the rank. Thus, the Eastern Gardens amassed wealth from selling offices, and Cui Lie was mocked for the stench of copper. The ruler set the example, and the ministers followed suit, exceeding him in excess. Flatterers and favorites monopolized discussions; relatives and hometown acquaintances controlled evaluations. The Ministry of Personnel and the Bureau of Merit became brokers, each demanding their price. How could the poor and upright hope for advancement?

This was the state of affairs, yet the distinction between right and wrong remained as stark as ice and charcoal. Those who hated integrity despised those not of their faction; those who inverted standards feared others’ judgment. Those who advanced through improper means dreaded unfavorable talk. Thus, they conspired to fabricate slander, heaping false accusations upon the virtuous, creating blemishes where none existed, and none dared to rescue or promote them. As a result, Zeng Shen and Min Sun were slandered like Prince Shang; Confucius and Mozi were defamed like Robber Zhi. Those who cherished integrity and upheld principle perished in the mire, while the cunning and deceitful soared like rainbows. Could one not tremble at the calamities that stem from such a system?

Yet the shallow-minded common folk, unable to distinguish between right and wrong, call those who uphold the Dao "sunken in obscurity" and deem those who tread the proper path "adept at shifting with the times." How can those who drift with the wind and flow with the current ever return to the labor of virtue or the toil of learning? Thus, all abandon the wearisome task of self-discipline and rush toward the swift rewards of bribery. This is indeed the very reason the Han dynasty collapsed—a lesson future generations must heed deeply.

Some may say: "Your critique of the Han’s late-era examination system truly diagnoses its ills. Now, those who wish to avoid past mistakes and steer clear of ruinous paths have adjusted their methods to prevent laxity and corruption. Yet just as the rustic songs of Pu Shang and Ba people have supplanted the proper music of contentment, and as minor ailments of the skin go untreated until they fester, how can one not exert effort to remedy this? There are scholars of fine appearance and noble bearing, their dignified presence overflowing, yet they harbor emptiness within, their substance insufficient. If one judges by appearance alone, worthy talent may not be found; if one tests them gradually, there is no time for haste. What then is to be done?"

The *Baopuzi* replies: "To know others is wisdom—even sages find it difficult. If all governors could discern true talent before its use and ensure the constancy of their nature and conduct, it would indeed be no easy task. Yet if they collectively set aside private interests, exhaust their wisdom, remain unmoved by profit or desire, and refuse to bend to favoritism, then those they wish to recommend must be scrutinized with careful thought, investigated thoroughly, their reputations examined alongside their conduct, discrepancies weighed to expose false adornments. Let their clans praise their filial piety and brotherly love, let their villages attest to their trustworthiness and integrity. If they have held minor office, let their loyalty, purity, and administrative skill be proven—even a single brocade strip reveals craftsmanship, and a mouse’s gnawing tests courage.

Furthermore, all candidates for recommendation and examination should, as before, be tested on the classics and required to compose policy responses. If their answers are fraudulent, they must be permanently barred from office, punished with prohibition from service. If their responses are inadequate, governors and prefects who endorse them should be dismissed; those with many failures should be demoted, while those with few should be restricted in promotion. If one accepts bribes and recommends the unworthy, upon discovery and verification, they shall be stripped of rank, permanently barred from office, and no amnesty shall pardon them. Those they recommend shall share their punishment.

If this system is enforced for a year or two, many will cease seeking recommendation, and it will become clear how long the empire’s examination system has been imprecise. Beyond this, those who cultivate virtue and diligently study will surely increase.

Moreover, for all officials in office, those guilty of public offenses shall be dealt with according to the law. Those whose crimes of greed and corruption do not warrant death shall, even after completing their sentences or receiving amnesty, be permanently barred from office—lesser offenders for twenty years. Thus, even the corrupt will be transformed into Boyi and Shuqi. But if officials who amass mountains of illicit wealth upon discovery plead for mercy, escape punishment, and within days return to office, even Zeng Shen and Shi Yu will become Robber Zhi.

If this is so, then even if all recommended scholars are qualified, they will not tolerate the misconduct of their superiors."

Some may ask: "One who speaks well may not act well. Even if a candidate excels in classical examinations and policy responses, how can one be certain of their administrative ability?"

The *Baopuzi* answers: "In ancient times, archery was used to select men—how much more so the mastery of classical arts? If we abandon testing, what other method is superior? This is the way."

For lush grasses do not flourish in barren soil, nor do giant fish arise in shallow waters; maxims are not spoken from the mouths of mediocre men, nor do lofty writings fall from the brushes of the dull-witted. Thus, perusing the *Hongfan*, one recognizes that Jizi possessed the instrument for ordering the world; examining the *Nine Strategies*, one sees that Master Fan harbored the art of governing the state; studying the writings of Guan Zhong, one understands his capacity to restore order from chaos; and reading the texts of Shen Buhai, one perceives his mastery of the Dao of hegemony and kingship.

Now, if candidates for filial piety and incorruptibility must be tested on the classics without error, and if cultivated talents must respond to policy questions without missing the intent, then they cannot be obscured or concealed. Even fine generals and high-ranking officers, selected for their courage and martial prowess, are further tested in archery—how much more so for scholars of the literary arts? Even if one cannot guarantee that all selected will be worthy and capable, it is surely better than not testing them at all. Moreover, if this single measure is implemented, all those who ought to be recommended for office throughout the realm will dare not neglect their studies. This one provision alone would greatly benefit the cultivation of customs and education.

If the realm fears the consequences of reckless recommendations, if all men abandon their pursuit of fortune by chance and return to the foundation of learning, then the Dao of the Ru will flourish, private bribes will gradually cease, extraordinary talents can be employed, and offices will no longer lie vacant.

Some may say: "Sir, you seek to hasten the laws of recommendation, but the punishment of lifelong禁锢 is too harsh and severe, and many will fear it. Tight reins and frequent checks are what even Bo Le would not employ; strict laws and severe punishments are what virtuous governance shuns."

The *Baopuzi* replies: "Medicines that nourish the bones and replenish the flesh are excellent for nurturing the body and prolonging life, yet they cannot save one from drowning or heatstroke in an emergency. Governance that emphasizes tolerance and forbearance may befit a simple and honest people, but it is insufficient to rescue a state in decline and chaos. When tigers and wolves press near, if one does not brandish a spear or unsheathe a sword but instead strums the zither and recites poetry, I have yet to see how one’s life can be preserved. When flames engulf a house, if one does not rush to pour water but instead bows and circles in ceremony, I have yet to see how the fire will extinguish itself. To discuss this with those who wish to sell offices is like debating the capture of thieves with Robber Zhi."

The *Baopuzi* says: "Now, under the unified rule of all under heaven, where the nine regions share a common wind, the king’s institutions and decrees ought to be uniform. Even small measures of weight and volume must not vary from place to place—how much less can the standards for men of talent be left unchecked and inconsistent? Though Jiangbiao is distant, lying near the sea’s edge, it has been steeped in the Dao and transformed by culture for over a thousand years. Though it was once briefly severed for less than a hundred years, the pursuit of Ru learning was never abandoned. Only because its territory is smaller than the Central States is the number of its scholars not equal in size—yet in virtue, conduct, talent, and learning, its finest, such as Ziyu and Ren An, are no less than those of the Central States.

In the early years of the Wu king, those recommended as candidates were exempted from testing due to their adherence to the established standards. Now, as the era of Great Peace has neared forty years, they are still not tested. This is why the Ru learning of the Southeast has declined compared to the past. Such a policy treats them no differently than those in barbarian dress—it does not distinguish them. Moreover, even the noble-minded treat others with ritual propriety—how much more so should those who are as kind and joyful as parents? Laws that invite calamity and decrees that harm transformation—these are what we speak of.

If candidates for recommendation are no longer tested, they will surely adorn themselves and vie for empty reputations. Who will then willingly open a book and study? This is what is meant by ‘enriching them only to ruin them.’ There are those with a natural love for antiquity, who delight in the arts and literature, who do not serve for salary, who savor the Dao and forget poverty—men like Gao Qing and Zhou Shenglie, whose learning is profound yet who do not seek office, who follow the Way and spurn glory and profit. Such men are one in ten thousand. As for those like Ning Yue, Ni Kuan, and Huang Ba, their diligent self-discipline in the classics was not innate but arose from suffering, hardship, and distress, through which they sought to elevate themselves by means of classical arts. Had it not been for the age of Emperor Wu of Han, even men like Zhu Maichen and Yan Zhu would not have read books.

Now, if the path to wealth and honor is readily available, and those who happen to have some inclination toward learning yet lack a natural love for it, how will they willingly endure hardship, perform menial tasks as students, travel far to seek teachers, and inquire after the Dao? In times of war, martial prowess is prized and literary arts decline; common men regard Ru scholars as servants and slaves, and view the classics and treatises as worthless weeds and dust. Why is this? Because their reputation has been tarnished. This is the reason."

To refrain from using analogies is like selling ceremonial caps to the people of Yue or offering centipede-snake talismans in the Central Plains. Now, if the system of examinations and evaluations were uniformly implemented far and wide, many would surely carry their satchels a thousand *li* to seek teachers and friends, redirecting the expenses of gifts and bribes to purchase books and records—this would not take a full day to unfold.

The *Baopuzi* states: "Scholars of talent and learning who are fit for the *xiucai* and *xiaolian* examinations are already rare enough. If, in the present age, officials were appointed as they were in the reigns of Emperors Huan and Ling—where those in charge of the imperial secretariat did not prioritize wealth and favor—then even those who could recite all the classics from memory without error would still be wronged and slandered, prevented from advancing."

I have long lamented that those in authority have not taken sufficient measures to prevent such abuses. My suggestion is this: for those to be tested in the imperial examinations next year, this year should see the appointment of learned officials and talented scholars to devise comprehensive preventive measures. All drafts should be collected and sealed within the palace halls. At the time of the examination, the questions should be swiftly distributed, and all personal connections and influences thereby severed. Those responsible for overseeing the process should be gathered in one place, with high-ranking officials from the central government personally supervising and strictly prohibiting any collusion. All communication and entry or exit should be forbidden until the examination is complete. Violators should be punished without pardon. If such measures are implemented and entrusted to reliable hands, the loopholes will be sealed.

A wise ruler relies on the impossibility of deceiving himself, not on others’ unwillingness to deceive him. Why should he feel shame in enforcing such strict regulations?

If the examination system for the classics is established, then the empire cannot but establish official academies, and people will naturally devote themselves to study. During the Han dynasty, the Four Categories of Examination included a clear understanding of laws and ordinances as a path to officialdom. Yet today, those in office—regardless of rank—are largely ignorant of the law. Some legal texts are obscure and difficult to comprehend, while petty clerks, often obstinate, are left to adjudicate cases. Thus, matters of life and death are entrusted to the frivolous, and the lives of the common people are placed in the hands of the uninformed. When officials themselves do not know the law, they are deceived by their subordinates without realizing it. Those who draft judgments are often muddled, unable to discern whether a case conforms to the law or not. They do not inquire nor refer it to the proper authority, but instead decide matters by whim, stumbling and erring without regard for the law. If upright and capable officials were all required to pass an examination on the laws and ordinances—just as they are tested on the classics—and those who scored highest were appointed according to their talent and rank, then the empire would surely see fewer officials who manipulate the law and fewer unjust verdicts.

**On Interpersonal Relations (Section Sixteen)**
The *Baopuzi* states: I hold that friendships should not be tainted by superficiality or insincerity. Yang Yunyou criticized those who, though equal in status, reputation, lineage, or age, diverge in conduct and aspirations, and with such men I have never associated. Some flaunt their seniority or stride ahead with lofty disdain; others neglect old friendships that have declined or dismiss promising newcomers as unrefined. Some judge others by rank rather than virtue; others, though talented, wise, pure in conduct, and noble in character, are overlooked because they have not encountered a true patron. Meanwhile, those who happen to rise in rank or gain office—though dull and mediocre—are inevitably sought after. Such men may command fleeting influence, making insects soar and sparrows reach the heavens, wielding power like a blade or a measure to dispense fortune and misfortune. When they favor someone, they exalt them with dazzling splendor; when they withdraw, they leave them withered like plants in spring. I am too constrained and ashamed to share the world with them, whether in adversity or prosperity.

Yet frivolous men, lacking any sense of integrity, never reproach themselves. With unwavering resolve, they rush through the night to guard the gates of the powerful, fawning and flattering with obsequious words and pleasing expressions. They carry wine and gifts, seeking favor through persistent toil. Even after long effort, they may still be spurned, only to resort to intermediaries to secure an introduction. When accepted, their joy surpasses that of a prisoner granted amnesty; when rejected, their dejection exceeds that of one stricken by illness or bereavement. But success and failure are matters of fate, and the Dao values uprightness. Prosperity and adversity are left to nature—why lament the paths one takes? Are such petty men not contemptible?

Human dispositions differ, yet how far apart are they truly? I often sigh and feel shame for them. Once, Zhuangzi saw the multitude of followers in Zhuangzi’s retinue and discarded his remaining fish. I, too, lament that worldly men do not eagerly seek worthy connections. Gazing at those who gallop through the dust, I see they do not strive to cultivate virtue or seek improvement within themselves. Instead, they chase after influential friends to form cliques, believing no principle more essential and no urgency greater in the world. Those who stand firm and independent are dismissed as stingy and inept, while those who grovel with servile deference are praised for understanding the ways of the world. Such customs have become ingrained, and none escape their sway—how lamentable!

Some men, though morally deficient and high in rank, are swollen with arrogance and avarice. At the mention of wealth, they startle and tremble; when they encounter extraordinary men, they doze off. Threadbare scholars in coarse robes, carrying satchels—though their learning shines and their wisdom runs deep—are treated as dust and weeds, never welcomed. Yet men like Cheng Zheng and Wangsun Jia, who ride in fine carriages, wear luxurious robes, and carry gold and jade, though ignorant of even basic propriety, are met with haste, their food spat out and hair grasped in eagerness. I regret not holding office, for without the axe and its handle, I cannot purge the state of such filth, banishing them to the four frontiers or casting them into the northern wastes. Though they may blaze with power, wielding authority like a blade or a measure, their influence enough to move mountains and uproot seas, their breath enough to lift one from the mire to the clouds—if they come to my gate, I have no time for them. Yet many lower themselves, forcing smiles and crawling forward to seek their favor, hoping for some benefit. Alas, how vast is the gulf between the intentions of the living and the unworthy!

I live in a sagely age yet remain mired in obscurity and lowliness. Looking back, I see none who once shared my path, and now, gazing ahead, I cannot catch up. Is there no reason for this? Yet if my nature cannot endure it, I must follow my own inclinations—this is a principle I cannot abandon.

Some may object: Times change, and the world shifts; ancient and modern pursuits differ. One must act according to the present, establish a name, and achieve success. Those who carry gold and jade through peril are like those who entrust themselves to a light boat. The phoenix that gathers in the empyrean relies on the power of flight. The fragrance of orchids is the work of the clear wind. The reclusive scholar who rises from obscurity is aided by a true patron. Now, sir, you insist on befriending only those of pure conduct and refined character, favoring only the most discerning. Alone and aloof, you lose the company of your peers, making enemies of the world and drawing the scorn of the vulgar. How does this align with the lofty principle of harmonizing with the world while preserving one’s integrity?

If you compare wisdom before befriending, even a commoner in a thatched hut would not deign to associate with the noble Duke of Zhou. If you measure talent before associating, even Confucius would have no disciples entering his inner chamber.

The *Baopuzi* replies: I have heard that those who choose friends carefully do not lose worthy companions, while those who form indiscriminate bonds often regret it. Thus, the sages of old selected friends before forming ties, not the other way around. Your argument is a worldly calculation; my resolve is the resolve of a reclusive scholar.

You speak of jade floating and birds soaring—all have their causes, and indeed, this may be a distinct principle, a doctrine of its own. Yet I would rather be an uncarried treasure, an unsoaring roc, an unscented orchid. Moreover, is not the scholar diminished by possessing the luster of night-shining pearls, the vastness of the sky-spanning wings, the fragrance of everlasting blossoms, or the immortality of the orchid?

Furthermore, when fame exceeds reality and rank surpasses talent, those who hold such positions seldom escape calamity or disgrace—how then can associating with them be considered honor or fortune? By this reasoning, even if one befriends them and gains success, it is not truly worthy; if one shelters under them and errs, it is like relying on a rotten tree to brace a collapsing beam. If they cannot even prevent their own downfall, how can they save me from a shattered skull?

I have heard that the true man, self-sufficient and beyond external things, regards the common crowd as if pressed into unavoidable contact—though they number in the thousands, they are but lice infesting his robes or wens clustering on his body. To lose them, though they number in the tens of thousands, is like flying dust leaving Mount Song or withered branches falling from Deng Forest. How can their presence be counted a gain, or their absence felt as a loss?

As for friendship, it must be founded on those who are upright, candid, and learned—who correct one’s errors, reject falsehoods, speak without being asked, and make no excuses even in death; whose bond remains unbroken through winter’s cold and summer’s heat. Yet such people are not easily found. Some diverge in speech and path, others shift in love and hate; some unite in prosperity and part in decline, while others forget loyalty at the sight of profit. In today’s world, such friendships are like birds and fish forming bonds or ice and charcoal sharing a vessel—how can their union endure?

Even between father and son, bound by nature, preferences should align, yet Zizheng and Zijun differed in judgment, and Boqi of Nanshan disputed with his father, their faces turned away and hearts divided. Such discord has existed since ancient times. To lump them all together—is this not difficult indeed?

The common crowd forms bonds without regard for shared purpose, chasing fame and following power. They flock when warm and scatter when cold, refuse to correct faults, and ignore those lost in delusion. When profit arises, they seize it alone without sharing; when harm comes, they flee to save themselves without aid. When opportunity favors, they take first without yielding; when danger looms, they betray others to secure themselves. With friends like these, it would be better to have none. The world is not entirely devoid of suitable companions, but those who bring benefit are few, while those who create burdens are many.

Discerning others is a skill even the greatest sages find difficult. Yet those of limited talent, lacking in judgment, seek to broaden their circle, hoping all will stand firm through peril and hardship, bearing each other’s burdens through adversity. I have never seen such abundance. Even searching for flawless jade atop a mound or phoenixes in a wren’s nest would not be as hard.

I do not dare claim that beyond the southern slopes of Lantian or within the cinnabar caves of Danxue, no such people exist—only that they are rare indeed. When natures differ, gold sinks while feathers float; when aspirations clash, fire rises while water descends. Even the craft of Creation or the skill of the Great Potter cannot force alignment—how then can it be compelled?

As for myself, I am by nature dull and slow, further burdened by a heaven-given laziness. Games of skill, gambling, and all frivolous amusements I despise; racing, hunting, and idle wandering I do not pursue. I have no taste for jesting or banter—all these are the pleasures of the age, yet I lack them entirely. Thus, my close companions are few.

Moreover, I cling to honesty and freely speak my loyal counsel. Few willingly accept bitter medicine, and I further expect them to heed my advice, to curb their recklessness, to temper their excesses—all things the common crowd finds displeasing. To cast aside one’s true nature for hollow conformity, to force oneself into the mold of others’ affections—this I cannot bear. Yet if I persist unchanged, can I not be called blind to the times and inept in great matters?

If friendships do not endure, if bonds form only to break, both parties earn a reputation for inconstancy and lose the beauty of a lasting union. When closeness fades, affection turns to resentment; when distance grows, suspicion takes root. This is the natural course—how can one not choose carefully?

To raise a cup, clap a back, clasp hands—these fleeting moments may multiply in number, yet I fear they cannot endure in wholeness.

Some may ask: "Then should one have no companions at all?"
I reply: How could this be so? Those who fear water need not abandon boats and oars; those who dread injury need not forsake axes and adzes. The Way of companionship has been esteemed since ancient times. If Heaven and Earth do not join, there is no great harmony; if high and low do not unite, their aspirations diverge. Without great harmony, the two qi remain divided; when aspirations diverge, the world has no true states.

Yet while beginnings are easy, endings prove difficult. The danger lies in binding oneself to the wrong person, squandering the great for petty strife. The *Book of Changes* praises "gold and orchid," and poetry sings of "a hundred friends." Though one may have brothers, they are not like friends born of shared purpose. The Three Beneficial Bonds were cherished even by the greatest sages. Disciples honor their masters, harsh words do not arise, and Guan Zhong escaped execution to establish the Five Hegemons—all through the power of friendship. Ziyuan abandoned his post as village chief to ride in the vermilion carriage, likewise by the strength of his companions.

A single flute cannot produce the harmony of *Shao* and *Xia*; a lone hue cannot complete the splendor of dragon-embroidered robes. One flavor cannot blend the savor of Yi’s cauldron; a solitary tree cannot become the lush forest of Deng. The lofty gardens of Penglai reach the heavens through the accumulation of countless stones; the vast Southern Sea swells with the confluence of a thousand streams. When the bright mirror is raised, even a tilted cap is revealed; when Xihe’s light shines, even crooked shadows are exposed. Only when the archer’s bow is well-strung do the ailments of misdirected arrows vanish.

When true friends unite, the Way of assisting virtue is perfected. The enlightened understand this well. In their aspirations, they carefully choose those who surpass them; in their humbler associations, they discern those of like mind. When together, they discourse on the Dao and advance in power; when apart, they harmonize their hearts like paired wings. In times of prosperity, they collaborate in worldly affairs; in times of peace, they refine their understanding of principle. In peril, they succor one another. They would feel shame if their praise of one another were confined to two distant lands, lest Wangsun and Gongyu alone be celebrated for their cap-brushing friendship. Only then is the Way of old companionship truly esteemed.

Yet discerning true from false is no easy task. The rise of power and profit breeds shifts in allegiance; accumulated slander erodes even neck-severing pacts, and gradual corrosion dissolves the firmest bonds of glue and lacquer. Thus, some lament the loss of former affection, while others bear grudges over trivial slights, forgetting past ties. The friendship of Yuanbo and Juqing shines only in antiquity, while the betrayals of Zhang Er and Chen Yu recur even today—a lamentable pattern. The phoenix does not perch on the same branch as the owl; the qilin does not herd with wolves. Clear springs do not mingle with muddy torrents; the wise and enlightened do not dwell with the deceitful and benighted. Why? Because gradual contamination corrupts, and the straight path, when violently coerced, invites harm.

Someone asked, "May I inquire how to preserve the Way of perfect friendship?"

The Master of Plainness replied: "When a gentleman severs ties, he does so without malice—how could he then speak ill of a former friend? He would sacrifice his life for a friend—how could he compete over rank or status? In good friendship, there is intimacy without disrespect, harmony without conformity. If a friend errs, he admonishes him with solemnity; if he is told of his own faults, he corrects them without hesitation. He does not withhold counsel to avoid offending, nor reject advice to spare his own ears. He does not use clever rhetoric to excuse wrongs, nor flowery words to gloss over faults. He does not feign agreement while harboring discord, nor conceal true feelings behind a false smile. He does not flatter to his face and despise behind his back. He does not resent another’s superiority but guards against his own shortcomings, drawing out their strengths while concealing their faults. Externally, he is free from scheming slander; internally, he sheds the burden of rivalry. Only then is the bond of *Lu Ming* preserved and the sting of *Fa Mu* silenced.

Yet some treat friendship lightly, forming bonds without weight, deepening ties without difficulty, only to thin them just as easily. What begins like shadow and echo ends as distant as Shen and Chen. The closest camaraderie turns to bitter enmity; clasped hands become sworn foes. Such misfortune leaves no path for redress.

In the decline of the Han, the reins of governance slackened, and public duty waned. The customs of contention flourished, where defying the times was deemed purity, and saving the world was seen as shame. The great bonds of hierarchy and propriety collapsed. Those in office neglected their duties, chasing superficial acclaim over substance. State affairs were abandoned for hollow praise; corruption accumulated, yet their merits were lauded. None failed to spur their carriages, racing through frost and heat to the gates of the powerful, adorning their names with empty splendor in the mouths of the influential, securing undeserved ranks from the merchants of office. When desires clashed, they turned on one another in mutual slaughter.

Thus, Gongshu Chang, distressed by such decay, lacked the strength to correct it but could not bear to witness it. In outrage, he penned his treatise, barred his door, and severed all ties. This was indeed a passionate response to the times—yet in overcorrecting, he strayed from the eternal teachings. One should simply distance oneself from corrupt factions and guard against the roots of flattery. Why must one strip naked to shame the extravagant or starve to mock the gluttonous?

If repeated admonitions go unheeded and great disorder ensues, then the abandonment of ritual and propriety may be justified.

---

**On Recognizing Limitations (Chapter Seventeen)**
The Master of Plainness said: The finest steed may gallop across the plains, outstripping the sun, yet cannot tread upon ice to cross deep waters. The fierce tiger may roar like thunder and rend its prey, yet cannot soar through clouds to ascend the heavens. The great roc cannot flap its wings within a cage, nor can the swift sparrowhawk strike like lightning at the banquet table. All things have their limits—so too do people.

Thus, one who harmonizes yin and yang may not excel in all virtues; one who masters the great classics may falter in minor details. Hui Shi, paragon of prime ministers, could not command a boat to brave the waves. Emperor Gaozu, peerless in martial prowess, could not manage his household or regulate his conduct. Han Xin, master strategist, could not tend to farming or trade to escape hunger and cold. Zhou Bo, pillar of the state, could not answer questions on finances or legal disputes.

If we discard the long for the short, then the talents of Yi Zhi and Fu Yue would go unused. If we demand perfection in both grand and trivial matters, then the achievements of those who govern the world and aid its people would never be recognized.

Heaven cannot level its northwest; earth cannot elevate its southeast. The sun and moon cannot illuminate dark crevices; the wind cannot stir waves in a well. A toothpick cannot match the pine or oak in length; an ear pick cannot rival the rafters in size. A pellet of mud can fell a bird worth a thousand gold, while a needle of a few fen outstrips a long sword in sewing. Why must one harness a great elephant to catch a rat or enlist the roc to herald the dawn?

Thus, Jiang Ziya sold fish without success before finding his master in King Wen and Wu. Jiang Sheng was scorned in a hundred villages before standing unmatched among the Three Huai.

---

**On Selecting Talent (Chapter Eighteen)**
The Master of Plainness said: Ornate prose is not for the blind to appreciate; extraordinary talent is not for the shallow to recognize. If one’s gaze cannot reach others, then dragon-embroidered robes and plain hemp are valued the same. If one’s judgment cannot discern, then the brilliant and the mediocre are judged alike.

What the eye does not see, the spirit does not embrace. Where no comparison is made, harm inevitably follows. Moreover, love and hate, approval and disdain, have never been uniform across time. Customs shift, and the same object may command a high price in one era and be worthless in another. The jade pendant of the Xia sovereigns, once prized beyond cities, is now cheaper than bronze or iron. Thus, reclusion to pursue one’s aspirations was once the mark of a noble scholar, but today, mountain-dwelling scholars are deemed unworthy. The pillars of sagacity in ancient times have become the criminals of benighted ages; the steadfast rocks of the past are the feeble remnants of later days.

The noble man who walks the Dao is not merely a towering wall of a thousand fathoms—his depth is vaster than the Lüliang rapids. Yet the shortsighted and narrow-minded cannot appreciate him, just as the shallow cannot fathom him. Thus, they deem him strange and belittle him, resent him for not seeking their favor, and despise him for not valuing their approval. If he is not esteemed or employed, what more is there to say?

Thus, there are those who cast dust upon white jades, who breed sores upon skin of jade—slandering flaws in unison, they assail the independent, as if Zeng Shen were a bandit or a robber, and as if Chao Fu and Xu You had been thieves and burglars. If not for the luminous mirror of the Dark Heavens to reveal the subtle, who could sift through mud to draw forth sunken jade, or dredge the river to pluck pearls from the depths?

When fine jades lie unsold in the marketplace, how much worse their fate when veiled in uncut stone? When extraordinary scholars are barred at the gate, how much more so when they remain hidden in the marshes? Sun Bin longed to deploy his secret strategies, yet Sima Qian had his feet cut off; Han Fei wished to establish his governance, yet Li Si had him killed; Jia Yi was impassioned with statecraft, yet was pushed aside by warriors; Liu Xiang, loyal and upright, possessed the tools to save a nation in peril, yet was ensnared by Hong and Xian. Thus, Bian He wept blood while clutching his uncut jade, and Qin Xi shattered his skull in despair.

Jade and stone are easily distinguished, just as the worthy and the foolish; the love for treasures runs deep, as does the fondness for scholars. Yet even when precious gems—so readily discerned—are paired with objects of profound affection, they still incur punishment and mutilation through the ages, enduring false accusations. How much worse, then, for the worthy, who are difficult to recognize and not a priority in the minds of men? When nothing is there to guard against, schemers still draw legs on snakes; treacherous ministers fear the harm of loyalty and integrity. Those who bend their bodies resent the upright bearing of Mohist scholars; those who strip naked at night despise the coming of a bright candle. Thus, lofty reputations and virtuous deeds are suppressed and uncelebrated, while baseless slander takes shape before its shadow, emerging without cause. Without a Chu man’s wailing recommendation, a one-in-ten-thousand encounter is all the more impossible—such is only fitting.

For those who take jade to be stone will also take stone to be jade; those who take the worthy to be fools will also take fools to be worthy.

Taking stone for jade does no harm, but taking fools for the worthy is the diagnostic sign of ruin. For when ruin is diagnosed, though one may yet live, one is bound to perish—just as when the pulse is dead, though one may yet breathe, one is bound to die. Can one not be cautious?

Alas! How tragic that none reflect on this. Of old, Confucius, the supreme sage, was entangled by the men of Qi in the east and blocked by Zixi in the south. Wen Zhong, a man of great worth, was at first not even acknowledged by the customs of Jing, and in the end, though he wandered in the courts of kings, he could not establish his merit in his prime. Is this not difficult?

For jade like Jielü and Heili cannot be sold without a Tao Qi; a weight of a thousand jun cannot be lifted without a Meng Ben or Wu Huo; the strains of "White Snow" cannot be played without a Ling Su; and talent surpassing the common run cannot be employed without an enlightened lord. Yet luminous pearls, treasures of the night, do not dim their essence for lack of seekers nor sell themselves cheaply to base merchants. Great bells and Zhou tripods do not, for being cast aside, diminish their dignity nor accept elevation by dwarfs. The zithers of Yiyang and Yunhe do not, for being unused, cease their song to vie with lewd melodies. Virtues that crown the multitude do not, for being suppressed, tread the byways or sever their integrity to follow the vulgar.

Thus, the He-bi jade becomes stagnant goods; supple wood is discarded as useless; the ore of the Red Blade never passes through the furnace of Ou Ye; and the ranks of the Yuan Kai never gain the opening of the Four Gates.

**Chapter Nineteen: Mandate**

The Master of Embracing Simplicity said: Among my friends there is one called the Recluse of Cold Lodge. Serene and joyful, pure and still, he forgets both form and spirit. Outwardly, he does not adorn himself with the guise of startling the foolish; inwardly, he does not harbor the mind of deliberate action. His spirit roams the ancient texts, and his joy makes him forget sorrow. His brush races the last rays of Fuxi and Nüwa; his gaze lingers on the afterglow of Wangshu. The Dao is never too distant for him to guard against; his words, though subtle, are never unrefined. Yet his carriage does not halt at the gates of the powerful and influential; his letters do not pass through the courts of the noble and mighty. Thus, his name does not leave his thatched door; his person does not depart his furrowed fields.

Thereupon, the Winged Brightness Grandee questioned and challenged him, saying: "I have heard that when the coiled dragon rises, dark clouds gather; when the transforming influence of the Dao spreads, hidden talents emerge. Thus, the broad streets resound with the songs of unicorns; the sacrificial vessels bear the traces of soaring winds. When such influence touches them, they reap profits beyond measure; when it spurs them, they ascend beyond the heavens. They shine with dazzling light in the Valley of the Sun; they spread great brightness through the Nine Heavens. Their merit casts a shade that endures forever; their fame resounds without limit. Therefore, though the path of a thousand ren is near, those who do not walk it cannot reach it, even with the speed of the wind. Though the space beneath the eaves is low, those who do not rise cannot attain it, even with the wings of the great peng."

Moreover, how can one who rests content beyond the vast wilderness, who folds their wings upon the branches of secluded parasols, ever hope to emulate the trustworthy, chase the light, or shake off their lightness beneath the azure vault?

Alas! The span of years swiftly fades, never to return; the world drifts like a fleeting shadow, never to repeat. The wise tread upon frost and know the inevitable coming of solid ice; dwelling in beginnings, they perceive the destined end of all living things. The six dragons hasten their course through the great chaos; the peak of glory vanishes in an instant, announcing dusk. Thus did the ancients reflect upon the current, sighing in decline, or glance at the passing gap and rise in sorrow.

The Master possesses a transcendent capacity born of the age, harboring sublime excellence beyond the common, his keen brush vast and deep as surging waves, his sixfold ingenuity pent up in profound reserve. Yet he cannot ascend the swaying heights, nor soar aloft to shine with pure radiance through the Nine Mysteries. His vessels remain unarrayed at the end of the ritual stands; his person is not spared the toil of bearing firewood. Still, he offers harmonious tones in a deafened world, peddles ceremonial caps in a land of unbound hair—vainly striving to awaken the literary realm, sharpening his intent to fathom the divine, cherishing jade-like purity within his embrace, and pouring forth gem-like words from brush and ink. His body suffers the hardship of repeated want; his spirit labors between steadfastness and loftiness. It is as though a foot-long jade disk were buried beneath layers of earth, or brocade were sealed within a locked coffer—forever without the wealth of exchange, who would recognize the treasure thus concealed?

When the dragon and steed are fettered, how can they be distinguished from the lame donkey? When the red blade sheathes its edge, how does it differ from a leaden knife? The sturgeon and salmon do not dwell in ox tracks; the great peng does not linger in the brushwood. I wish the Master would forsake the dragon-serpent’s den, ascend the path of advantage and visibility, cast off the seclusion of his courtyard, and awaken from the two mice. Let him depart the barren valley to scale the heights, don the crimson robes to replace the plain, and vie with the dawn’s swift winds rather than tarry through the night. Let him gather his noble vessels in the exalted realm, turn toward the blessings of tripods and cauldrons, and in the evening become a man of renown—what need has he to admire the fern-picking hermit?

Residing in the cold, the Master replied: I have heard that the subtle mechanism of the spirit is distant and obscure, the mingling of fortune and misfortune entwined in the interplay of concealment and revelation, the rise and fall entangled in the ebb and flow. The fleeting cannot escape the shadow that pursues the body; the joyful in success cannot avoid the ruin of principle. The craftsman who chases the branch’s end does not match one who emerges from the primal forge; the one who mends the already-made does not equal one who returns to the source beyond the uncarved block.

Thus, the wise inchworm willingly bends to preserve its extension; the one who discerns closure and opening does not grieve or rejoice over adversity or prosperity. Moreover, the great potter shapes things, the great image flows through forms—yet haste and stillness diverge in aspiration, soaring and sinking clash in disposition. Gold and jade are heavy; feathers and down are light. The narrow-minded cling to the dregs; the profoundly enlightened roam free in the pure and mysterious. The stagnant marsh accepts the trickling stream yet chokes on the vast sea; the whale swallows a hundred rivers yet remains unfilled. The goby and shrimp leap joyfully in the mire; the red dragon soars mightily through the lofty heights. Those who chew the fragrant bait rush to their death in pursuit of desire; those who savor emptiness and blandness embrace the harmony of heaven and hasten toward life. The one who recognizes the divine mechanism gazes upon the formless without confusion; the one blind to fortune and misfortune is startled by the strong crossbow’s bolt. Each clings to their preferences—how could they willingly change their course?

I have heard that the five jades cannot carve themselves from the Song Mountains; the soaring serpent cannot streak like lightning without mist. The dragon’s abyss cannot sever rhinoceros hide without grasping its blade; the great bell cannot resound without being struck. The golden fungus requires the autumn wind to gleam; the oriole awaits the misty haze to refine its song. The fine steed does not recklessly gallop into peril; the noble man does not risk his name through deceitful encounters. When fate is adverse, he sinks into obscurity; when the time is right, he soars high in the celestial court. The scholar who advertises himself is not truly lofty; the woman who offers herself is not truly chaste. Why must one emulate the lofty standard of washing one’s ears or imitate the honor of bearing the sacrificial stand?

When one is in dire straits, Yu the dancer may whirl in obscurity while the great Taifu Lü Shang is driven away by a foolish old woman; Fan Li suffers humiliation amid the reeds of a sinking mat. Men of refined bearing conceal their brilliance in farming and herding. Yet when their time comes, the Huaiyin Marquis casts his fishing rod and declares himself sovereign; Wen Zhong removes his straw sandals to don the azure sash; Fu Yue lays down his trowel to discourse on the Dao; Guan Zhong sheds his shackles to become chief minister. The noble man stores his talents in readiness, accumulates virtue for action—when the time is not right, he does not appear; when the lord is not worthy, he does not serve. Rise and fall depend on circumstance, while withdrawal or advancement is not bound to any fixed course. In stillness, he becomes the paragon of recluses; in action, the model of great ministers. Some devote their thoughts to establishing words; others inscribe merit in the vessels of state. Though their paths differ, their ultimate aim is one.

A scholar may cultivate conduct worthy of honor, yet cannot compel the world to honor it; he may possess talents fit for use, yet cannot compel the age to employ them. Clad in coarse cloth, eating wild herbs, angling for fish and setting snares for rabbits, his heart is joyful and his mind content, as if he would spend his life thus. Donning court robes, riding in carriages, draped in crimson and girded with purple, he remains as if these were his natural garb, like a commoner in plain clothes—such is the bearing of the perfected man. If one possesses the treasures of the banquet hall yet lacks the purity of a life confined to four bare walls, the fault is one’s own. That few recognize one’s worth, that rank and position do not come—if jade is called stone, if the phoenix is mistaken for a quail, the blame is not mine.

To fret over recognition or chafe at obscurity is the way of ordinary men. To nurture qi with boundless composure and desires with serene indifference—this is the state of one free from vexation. When the time is right, the Dao is practiced and one’s vessel is great; yet the heavens and earth do not rejoice. The man who understands fate does not grieve.

As for those who peddle goods worth ten thousand in hopes of a hundredfold return, losing their very flesh and bones in the bargain—I have no leisure for such pursuits.

**On Names and Reality (Chapter Nineteen)**

A disciple asked: “I have heard that in the late Han, during the reigns of Emperor Ling and Emperor Xian, evaluations of men became chaotic and reckless. The talented and reclusive languished in obscurity while the gluttonous and rapacious rose to power. Names no longer matched reality; prices no longer reflected value. Those who prospered were deemed worthy, while those who struggled were called fools. What was the reason for this?”

The Master Embracing Simplicity replied: “The thunderclap may roar, yet some do not hear it; the seven luminaries traverse the sky, yet some do not see them. Is it only the senses that suffer deafness and blindness? The heart and spirit may be equally obscured. Thus, one may hear profound words yet fail to recognize them—not for lack of ears. One may see extraordinary men yet fail to know them—not for lack of eyes. It is because one’s hearing does not reach the subtle, one’s vision does not attain the marvelous.”

Those of great wisdom and far-reaching capacity stand firm as mountains, while those of petty intellect and narrow ambition drift like tumbleweeds or float as duckweed. Only by standing as mountains can one remain unshaken; only by floating as duckweed can one flow without stagnation.

In the realm of goods, those sealed and stored in wait for buyers are often the rarest treasures, hardest to sell, while those loudly touted with drums are quickly exhausted, though flawed. In the realm of talent, those whose roots run deep in the sacred peaks may tower a thousand *ren* and spread their canopies over ten thousand *mu*, yet go unnoticed; those planted along the thoroughfares, though crooked and meager, though swift to rot, are still put to use. Thus, the ancestral halls hold withered willows carved into ritual vessels, while the remote valleys teem with catalpas left uncut. Those who usurp the name of blossoms soar like ants into the clouds; those who lose their true worth sink like jade dragons into the Nine Springs. Thereupon, the sparrow hawks beat the wind in lofty flight, while the divine phoenix folds its wings in seclusion. Lead and tin fill the treasuries of *Tai’e* blades, while dogs and sheep, frivolous in gait, assume the guise of tigers and wolves.

The flatterer, wielding precious bribes as mighty wings, reaches heights unattainable; riding factions as boats and oars, crosses distances unnavigable. Rising early, standing at attention, anticipating desires, and obeying commands—his glib tongue and artful words seem eloquent; his hearsay and gossip seem learned; his treacherous heart wears a gentle face, seeming kind; his filthy deeds appear pure, seeming upright; his delight in others’ faults seems loyal; his disregard for taboos seems forthright. Thus, he gains much access.

Moreover, those who curry favor hope for my desires, and I desire what they desire; those who do not seek me, I cannot love—such is the natural principle. The worthy are ever few, the foolish ever many; the many form cliques to conceal flaws, while the few stand alone and unsupported. Flatterers draw one another up, blocking the right path; the wise and sagacious remain in lowly positions, unseen. The way of the thatched hut is violated, and those who ride oxcarts rise in swarms. The lofty dragon falls, weeping blood in streams. Zixi banished the great sage Confucius; Zang Cang slandered the epochal Mencius—neither of these two escaped such misfortune. How much less, then, those who come after? This calamity has arisen alongside the opening of the universe, and its bitterness is not confined to a single age.

Looking back through the ages, this affliction has been common. The slow and lame crane their necks in ornate carriages, while swift steeds are left to pasture in the wilds. Some already occupy the seats of state, exhausting the nation; others are exiled to Shen and Hai; some have their sinews torn out in the ancestral temple; others meet their end at Wangyi. All because what they guarded was not true, and loyal ability went unused.

Thus, the enlightened ruler diligently seeks the worthy and eagerly promotes the extraordinary, clearing obstructions and strictly preventing blockages. When talent is truly sufficient for the task, he does not scruple over butchers or fishermen; when words are verified as sound, he draws them from the ranks of soldiers or exiles. Some are raised from the cattle’s mouth to join the ranks of officials; some are lifted from fetters to bear the weight of the state. Only thus can great deeds be accomplished, frontiers expanded, distant lands subdued, might established, and achievements endure, passing down through the ages.

The upright and honest are hated by crooked wood; the pure and impartial are despised by the treacherous. If the ruler cannot wield the dark mirror to search out the hidden but must rely on those in power to make recommendations, then, observing past ages, one finds that those who monopolize authority invariably promote only their own adherents.

Those who seek personal gain slander what they fear and promote what they favor—what they fear are the utterly impartial, while what they favor are those who share their private interests. When the utterly impartial are employed, cliques of treachery are shattered; when a multitude of private interests prevail, the ruler’s authority is usurped. The shattering of treacherous cliques is the path to prosperity and peace; the usurpation of the ruler’s authority is the gradual onset of peril and ruin. In slandering what they fear, they fear that their words are not cutting enough—even mutilation by amputation or disfigurement would not satisfy them, and thus they must eliminate such men to feel at ease. In promoting what they favor, they find even the most lavish praise insufficient—even meteoric advancement would not content them, and thus they must endanger others to secure their own position. Hence, those who embrace injustice and die, or those without fault who are demoted, have long had their origins here.

Thus, those who embody the Dao and align with truth stand towering and apart, their talents far-reaching and their aspirations lofty, their conduct as pure as frost and their thoughts as boundless as heaven and earth. Their bearing is as measured as the scales of Yu and Kai, their demeanor as deep and still as a hidden abyss, as steadfast as a mountain. They would rather preserve their integrity in obscurity than bow in servility for fleeting gain; they delight in hunger and humble dwellings to uphold their noble principles. They conceal their talents and preserve their true nature, awaiting the natural end of their years, emerging only when the time is right, acting only when propriety demands. Clad in coarse cloth, chewing on wild greens, they remain untroubled. Though their hair may turn yellow with age and their fate remain unfulfilled, they feel no regret. How could they stoop to the height of Mount Tai to fit a square peg, or gather the wings of the heavens to serve as a morning alarm? How could they submit to the ranks of petty officials, stifled beneath the mediocre, abandon the forests of the phoenix to dwell in the brambles, or vie for perches with crows and owls, bending a foot to straighten a fathom?

Moreover, the demeanor of the great worthies is marked by supreme simplicity, their tastes so plain as to seem desolate, yet they are self-sufficient, serene, and unassuming. Few recognize their wisdom, and though the times do not employ them, they feel no distress. Though they may go days without even the humblest grain of porridge, they would not exchange it for the richest feast of injustice. Though their garments may be threadbare, lacking even a full year’s warmth, they would not delight in the white fox fur of a lawless ruler. They alone may loosen their hair, recline at ease, and guard what they possess, refusing to bow or lower their brows to seek what they do not need. They do not associate with those whose virtue is meager but whose rank is high, nor do they befriend those whose names belie their reality. They do not pursue fleeting glory or chase after power, nor do they consort with the cunning or the mighty. They do not engage in trivial debates or vulgar persuasion, nor do they follow those who bow in servility. Outwardly foolish yet inwardly enlightened, their faces may be unwashed yet their conduct pure—profound as the heights of Mount Song, their depths unmeasurable by any scale, vast as the boundless expanse.

Vast as the ocean, its depths defy measurement by the dou or the hu—towering in height beyond the reach of a thousand *ren*, its wealth surpassing that of the hundred officials. Behold those petty thieves: they are but weeds in the wild. Lofty are the principles of those who stand aloof from the world, soaring beyond the realm of autumn clouds, their spirits transcending the mundane. Dwelling at the summit of the Nine Heavens, though impoverished and lowly, they cannot be coerced by threats, nor swayed by profit, no matter how dire their suffering. Their undertakings may be heard of, yet never fully grasped; their grasp may be seen, yet never debated.

Thus, those who despise them raise a chorus of scorn and cast sidelong glances, while those who cherish them are few and feeble, offering no aid—like a handful of soil vainly trying to dam a bursting river, or a ladle of water seeking to quench a prairie fire. Thereupon, the drums fall silent, muffling the roar of thunder; the *tao*, the *pi*, the *gao*—their clamorous din—are left to clamor unchecked. Fragrant orchids are uprooted, foul abalone is worn as adornment, the sacred *chang* is poured out and discarded without shame. Watered-down wine is poured in libation upon the sacred mound, while blood-sweating steeds are driven away, their ears drooping. The lame and the halt are made to gallop before the imperial chariot—this is why the ancients embraced sand and bore stones to plunge into the currents, choosing the burial of fish rather than endure the same world as these. So be it.

Alas! Yet to cast fine millet into the mire is not because the night-shining pearl is false—it is that none recognize it. To cast the vermilion quiver aside unbent is not because the *Fanruo* bow lacks strength—it is that none appreciate it. Thus, jade and chalcedony await *Jing He* to reveal their price of linked cities; the *Wuhao* bow requires *Feng Men* to display its power to pierce armor. The *Feitu* steed depends on *Ziyu* to soar on the wind; the talented man relies solely on a true patron to give his strength. If fine jade does not emerge from the deep mountain, if the good bow is not carved from a hundred layers, if the *Jilu* steed is not harnessed to the vermilion carriage, if the man of destiny does not tread the path of rank and power—who then would know their ability to unfurl radiant splendor, to strike down cloud-scraping birds from a thousand fathoms, to gallop swift as the wind, or to bring order to the hundred offices?

Yet if they meet no such fate, they may not be mixed and kneaded with tiles and stones, nor yoked in lowly harness like rotten wood, nor ranked among the common and the petty. Alas! To draw a thornwood arrow and expect a master archer from the *Quguang* marsh, to rely on a weary nag and seek to follow the tracks of *Zhou Mu*, to wield an axe and hope to match the dual craft of *Ban* and *Mo*, to neglect true talent and yet desire the proper ordering of constant norms—is this not difficult? Though name and reality may leak away in this generation, their virtuous renown may yet be sought by those to come.

Rejoice in Heaven and know fate—what is there to fear, what to grieve? Abide in time and follow the natural order—what is there to resent, what to blame?

**Chapter Twenty: Clear Discernment**

The *Baopuzi* said: It is commonly held that those of unmatched martial prowess are fit to be supreme generals, and those well-versed in order and chaos are suited for the throne of the Three and the Nine. Yet Zhang Fei and Guan Yu, each a match for ten thousand men, both lost their heads and brought humiliation to their lord, their severed crowns offered up—were they not lacking in something? Kong Rong, though refined in conduct and surpassing in letters, failed utterly in governance, his downfall lying precisely where he should have excelled. Deng Yu and Ma Yuan were mere scholars from the fields, yet adept in the art of war; Xiao He and Cao Shen never delved into the classics, yet excelled as chief ministers. From this, we see that truly knowing men is no easy task.

If one insists on testing them before employing them, one fears the tripod may topple and the broth spill. If one relies on words and appearances, one risks mistaking the specious for the true, for falsehood and authenticity are often intertwined. Yet the world deems this an easy matter—merely glancing at a man, they claim to grasp him entirely. I remain deeply skeptical and dare not assent.

To distinguish the worthy from the unworthy, to perceive the spirit through form—this depends on the man himself. It cannot be forced. Unless one’s clarity rivals the sun and moon, or one’s hearing transcends sound itself, one must proceed with gradual discernment, advancing step by step. One cannot entrust great responsibility all at once, nor lightly lend the sharpest blade, for once given, reclaiming it is exceedingly difficult, and the damage incurred may be great.

Should one’s dimness in one matter guarantee ignorance in all others? Those who resemble me are not necessarily useful, nor are those who differ from me necessarily negligible.

Some may object: Heaven hangs its signs, and earth bears its forms. Gazing at mountains and measuring rivers, one may infer their heights and depths; when winds rise and clouds take flight, one may divine fortune and misfortune. The wise man, seeing a mountain not yet exhausted, perceives the fine jade within; beholding a shore not yet withered, senses the luminous pearl submerged in the deep. When a comet appears, he knows the *zhan* fish is about to die; when the sun and moon are eclipsed, he recognizes the *qilin* locked in combat. The weight of Mount Hua and Mount Tai can be known without scales, just as the unfathomable depths of rivers and seas are fixed without measurement.

The wings of the swan and the swift hooves of the *lu* steed, though they have not yet flown or run, are surely light and swift. The sword of Hao Cao and the dagger of the Xu clan, though they have not yet struck, will undoubtedly sever with precision. The calf of the brindled ox has the capacity to swallow a bull; the fledgling osprey bears the fierce mien of its kind. Where plants flourish, the soil is rich; where fish grow large, the waters are vast. A tiger’s tail does not attach to a fox’s body, nor does ivory emerge from an elephant’s mouth.

Shu Yu’s insatiable greed was evident from his birth; the signs of Shi Wo’s betrayal of our clan were manifest from the moment he opened his womb. When Wu Chen was but a child, Shen Tong foresaw his theft of a wife; Zhang Fu recognized Chen Ping’s future greatness at first glance. Fan Li vanished into the Five Lakes because of Goujian’s wasp-like eyes and bird-like beak; the people of Zhao abandoned their ambitions for conquest because of Bai Qi’s sharp brow and piercing gaze.

King Wen met Lü Shang beneath the mulberry’s shade, and before the shadow had shifted, knew him worthy of command. Emperor Xuan beheld Zhuge Liang, and before the sundial’s shadow had changed, entrusted him with his heart and mind. Guo Tai, though of middling talent, excelled in knowing men: in Yingchuan, he befriended Li Yuanli; in Chenliu, he allied with Fu Weiming; in Waihuang, he drew close to Han Zizhu; in Puheng, he revered Chou Jizhi; in his school, he gathered Pang Degong; observing the plowman, he discerned Mao Jiqi. He saw that Meng Min, though burdened with a load, would drop it without a second thought, and warned Yuan Ai of his impending ruin—his words proved entirely accurate.

Must one not be able to discern the keen from the dull by their outward signs, judge urgency and composure by their demeanor, gauge brightness and obscurity by their voice and bearing, observe their generosity or stinginess in fitting measure, assess truth and falsehood in their words and deeds, examine their conduct in both public and private, and compare their beginnings and ends with the evidence of their trustworthiness? Is this not an easy task?

The *Baopuzi* replied: I do not claim that men are entirely unknowable, nor that discerning them is without principle. I merely say that this art exists, and that great clarity is required—yet men of the world each presume themselves capable. Thus, I offer these words as a warning to those who would entrust responsibility.

For a man’s imposing appearance does not guarantee his worth, nor does a frail frame ensure his folly. The roaring man is not necessarily brave, nor is the gentle man necessarily timid. Some may share the same outward form yet differ in intent; others may have distinct natures yet converge in purpose. This is not like the fixed seasons of heaven and earth or the unchanging contours of mountains and rivers.

Things may be distant yet easily known, or near yet difficult to fathom—just as the eye can survey the vast heavens but cannot perceive the nape of one’s own neck, or as the ear can hear thunder yet cannot detect the sound of ants and lice.

Tang Lü and Fan Xu were skilled in physiognomy, yet could only discern longevity, poverty, wealth, rank, and status—they could not fathom a man’s temperament, whether broad or narrow, nor the rise and fall of his aspirations and conduct. If even the emperor finds this difficult, how much more so the common man?

You cite examples of judging by form and challenge me with talk of spirit—yet you have not grasped the root and have strayed from the point. The arrows of the archer who never misses may all pierce the autumn down, yet if he cannot consistently hit the target, he is not truly skilled. Shen, the mother of Shu Xiang, may have had one success, but she could not repeat it. Yao, who studied the ancients, misplaced his trust in Ji; the sagely Duke of Zhou erred in his appointment of Confucius. One may discern the rise and fall of distant omens yet fail to recognize a worthy man standing before him; Yan Shigu could judge purity and impurity across a thousand years yet was blind to the extraordinary talent within arm’s reach. If knowing men is so exceedingly difficult, then even Guo Tai’s judgments must have surpassed those of the sages.

Yet his successes were obvious and easily recognized, while his failures went unrecorded. What is truly valuable is to perceive outstanding talent among the nameless, to spot the swift steed on the slopes of Wu, to pluck the pearl-bearing oyster from the ninefold abyss, to point out the luminous jewel amid the piled stones. Like Bo Jie, who recognized the instrument of perfect pitch in the ashes of a fire, or Zhang Heng, who selected the bamboo of exquisite resonance before it was used—amidst the gathering of six armies or the bustle of the marketplace, a single glance should suffice, without confusion, to probe the hidden depths of a man’s heart, to determine his course from beginning to end. This is the untransmitted marvel of true discernment.

If, as you suggest, one must wait to observe the entirety of a man’s conduct, to witness the beauty or ugliness of his deeds, then it is like weighing silk threads after they have been measured on the scale, or declaring the length of cloth after it has been cut—anyone can do this. Why would the wise need to trouble themselves?

---

**Chapter Twenty-Two: Conduct and Character**

The *Baopuzi* said: To emulate the all-encompassing embrace of the Mysterious and the Yellow, to illuminate the radiance of the sun and moon, to display the patterns of the Dao and manifest brilliance—this is to surpass the vulgar and the empty. To embody the subtle workings of the spirit while appearing foolish, to never repeat a mistake while seeming obsequious—such is the worthy man.

To dwell in the tranquility of non-action, to tread the path of integrity and uphold impartiality—such is the man of the Dao.

The *Baopuzi* said: One who exhausts all offerings in life and death, preserving even his hair and skin to glorify his name—this is the filial man. One who extends compassion to all living beings, constantly forgiving others in his dealings—this is the benevolent man. One who aligns his life with the nation’s cause, enduring peril and hardship with unwavering integrity—this is the loyal man. One who perceives principles in what is obscure and foresees fortune and misfortune before they arise—this is the discerning man.
