One who measures order and chaos to expand or contract, carefully weighing departure and approach to preserve himself—this is the wise man. One who flows with fortune and adversity as one, following his nature without obstruction—this is the enlightened man. One who refuses to bend a foot to straighten a yard, or endure humiliation to cling to life—this is the refined man. One who acts with measured dignity, ever composed and free from regret—this is the steadfast man. One who embodies the purity of frost and snow, unstained by power or profit—this is the incorruptible man. One who remains steadfast from beginning to end, unwavering through cold and heat, though perilous—this is the righteous man. One who upholds a single word through long years, unchanging even in decline—this is the trustworthy man. One who wields sharp insight in speech, his words resplendent, clear, and just—this is the literary man. One who rouses heroic resolve, wielding arms to quell disorder—this is the martial man. One who delves into ancient texts, mastering past teachings to exhaust principle—this is the learned man. One who sharpens his mind in refined virtue, begrudging even a moment’s delay in cultivating power—this is the self-cultivating man. One who possesses vast knowledge yet treats wealth and gain as if they were lost—this is the incorruptible man. One who remains unchanged by gain or loss, unshaken by desire—this is the steadfast man. One who rushes to aid in hardship, taking others’ toil as his own duty—this is the devoted man. One who maintains purity and integrity to the end, refusing to yield even to avoid harm—this is the principled man. One whose brilliant wit dispels stagnation, his concise words clarifying all—this is the eloquent man. One who humbly defers to others in lowly positions, yet grows more reverent when honored—this is the modest man. One who fosters harmony among kin, ever upright in pursuing principle—this is the dutiful man. One who decisively cuts through entanglements, wielding the inked line without partiality—this is the capable man. One who discerns truth amid confusion, resolving hesitation with perfect judgment—this is the judicious man. One who tracks the waxing and waning of the seven luminaries, deducing the patterns of rise and fall—this is the diviner. One who charges into blades without fear, wrestling tigers in the wilds—this is the brave man. One who commands with solemn presence, enforcing laws without wavering—this is the stern man. One who devises ingenious tools to aid utility, mastering both sound and calculation—this is the skilled artisan. One who defies the strong without hesitation, undaunted by peril—this is the resolute man. One who toils tirelessly day and night, enduring hardship without complaint—this is the diligent man. One who remains unshaken by slander, unmoved by fear—this is the unyielding man. One who hears praise without joy, faces hardship without faltering—this is the composed man. One who acts decisively when right, undeterred by doubt—this is the decisive man. One who follows the inked line in advance and retreat, never seeking fortune through recklessness—this is the prudent man. One who upholds rites and propriety in competition, blameless even toward distant kin—this is the virtuous man. One who treads the plain Way without desire, unmoved by the times—this is the unadorned man. Those who possess none of these virtues yet fail to follow the path of the good—these are the inferior.

His disciples asked: "We have heard the marks of the good man’s conduct. May we now learn of the deeds that warn the common folk?"

The *Baopuzi* said: One who neglects to care for his parents, harming the Dao to indulge himself—this is the rebellious man. One who harbors deceit to seize fleeting glory, benefiting himself at the cost of his life—this is the treacherous man. One who abandons the right path of benevolence and justice, endangering others to secure his own safety—this is the treacherous man. One who delights in strife without restraint, slandering the upright to harm the honest—this is the wicked man. One who deviates from the inked line to wound others, delighting in killing and cruelty—this is the cruel man. One who adorns false doctrines to spread slander, entangling the loyal and pure—this is the slanderous man. One whose words are cunning but deeds betray them, walking in filth yet feigning purity—this is the flattering man. One who ignores right and wrong, indulging anger to vent rage—this is the violent man. One who feigns small virtues to gain trust, secretly harboring malice toward all—this is the deceitful man. One who follows the wind to curry favor, gauging the ruler’s intent to support wrongdoing—this is the sycophant. One whose words shift without thought, making empty promises—this is the hollow man. One who sees profit and forgets righteousness, casting aside shame for petty gain—this is the greedy man. One who gazes upon beauty and loses his heart, adorning himself in finery to indulge in depravity—this is the lustful man. One who hesitates in success, errs in action, and regrets often—this is the dim-witted man. One who rejects teachings and acts alone, ashamed to seek counsel from those wiser—this is the arrogant man. One who knows good but does not act, accomplishing little despite much effort—this is the inept man. One who neglects virtue and cultivates power, currying favor to ingratiate himself—this is the corrupt man. One who takes shortcuts for quick success, wielding bribes to seize advantage—this is the crooked man. One who is haughty and cruel, delighting in humiliating his betters—this is the brutal man. One who endures wrongs yet falsely confesses, trembling at the slightest hardship—this is the cowardly man. One who nitpicks in crowds, speaking recklessly to the wrong people—this is the shallow man. One who fails to grasp what is fitting, aspiring yet falling short—this is the foolish man. One who knows right from wrong yet refuses to change, hearing good advice yet worsening—this is the deluded man. One who lacks compassion, cutting ties with kin and friends—this is the callous man. One who resents what he cannot attain, rejoicing in others’ misfortune—this is the envious man. One who hoards wealth and scorns righteousness, ignoring the suffering of others—this is the miserly man. One who courts danger for gain, undeterred by disaster—this is the foolish man. One who is narrow-minded and clannish, obsessed with profit—this is the petty man. One who indulges in hunting and gambling without end—this is the dissipated man. One who forgets the distinctions of rank, delighting in extravagant display—this is the extravagant man. One who wallows in sensuality, neglecting proper observances—this is the dissolute man. One who lacks ambition for self-cultivation, neglecting his household—this is the lazy man. One who lacks resolve, acting carelessly without thought—this is the frivolous man. One who hears of the Way and acts as if drunk, stirred by talk of wealth—this is the deluded man. One who judges poorly with many errors, blind to right and wrong—this is the clumsy man. One who hates the worthy and scorns their words, deaf to lofty counsel—this is the perverse man.

Those who see crimson and purple yet cannot distinguish them, even when admonished to their ears yet fail to awaken—these are the benighted. Those who violate the Way and virtue, hesitating and faltering, recklessly transgressing rites and laws without regard—these are the disorderly. Those whose every action invites ridicule, whose every word defies reason—these are the inept. Those who serve the powerful as if slaves and bondservants, yet turn their backs on the weak and forget past kindnesses—these are the treacherous. Those who abandon old friends of humble means, slighting scholars and acting with arrogance—these are the haughty. Those who forsake aging kin to pursue endless desires, neglecting office and study for distant wanderings—these are the reckless. Those lacking steadfast loyalty and trustworthiness, forsaking gratitude to chase profit—these are the traitors. Those who feign agreement to your face but slander you behind your back—these are the deceitful. Those who cultivate aggression and stubbornness, rejecting loyal counsel and refusing advice—these are the obstinate.

The *Baopuzi* states: Human talents are not easily discerned, for truth and falsehood may resemble one another. Among scholars, there are those whose appearance is refined, their bearing elegant and poised—at first glance, they dazzle the eye; upon closer meeting, they please the heart. Their demeanor is like that of dragons and tigers, circling with perfect form. Yet their minds are clouded, their spirits dull; their talents are nonexistent, and all they possess clings only to their skin. They cannot utter a single insight, nor string together half a sentence. In office, they cannot govern the people; in war, they cannot command troops. In administration, affairs collapse; in missions, they bring shame. In action or stillness, they are never fitting; in advancement or retreat, they are never apt. This is the first difficulty in discernment.

There are scholars whose appearance seems unadorned and weary, their bearing coarse and unrefined, their voice and manner subdued. In movement, they are stiff and awkward, yet they harbor hidden brilliance and treasure profound learning. Their conduct is lofty, their abilities surpassing those of the finest ministers; their writings flourish like spring forests. In office, all affairs prosper; in war, they achieve complete victory. This is the second difficulty in discernment.

There are scholars whose strategies are profound, their schemes divine, their wisdom encompassing success and failure, their thoughts penetrating the obscure and mysterious. Their talents are all-encompassing, their abilities suited to the highest affairs. Yet their mouths cannot convey what their hearts hold, nor can their brushes capture their intent. In casual encounters, they appear no different from the ordinary. This is the third difficulty in discernment.

There are scholars who are quick-witted and sharp, their words artful and dazzling, their analogies flowing like a deep spring, their manner forceful. Yet what they speak, they cannot practice. Skilled in recognizing the past, they falter in managing the present. When governing, governance falters; when shepherding the people, the people resent them. This is the fourth difficulty in discernment.

There are scholars whose outward demeanor is reverent, their words and bearing respectful, yet their spirits are slack, their hearts scattered and unrestrained. When entrusted with tasks, they feel no concern; when placed in charge, they bring no order. This is the fifth difficulty in discernment.

There are scholars who can draw a bow to its fullest, shoot arrows through stone, ride backward, stand on tiptoe, and master all five weapons. Yet their bodies are light, their thoughts shallow; their hands are strong, but their hearts are timid. In mock trials, they have no equal, but in real use, they prove ineffective. At the sight of dust from an approaching enemy, they flee in panic; at the sound of the foe, they lose their nerve. This is the sixth difficulty in discernment.

There are scholars whose manner is unyielding and slow, their words few, their appearance plain. Their conduct is flawed, lacking small courage, constrained and cautious. They endure insults without retaliation, like birds with clipped wings and lowered talons. Yet their courage is steadfast, their hearts resolute. They fear no powerful foe, and where righteousness lies, they face death as if returning home. Even if dismembered, they will not alter their resolve. This is the seventh difficulty in discernment.

There are scholars who are filial and fraternal, gentle and kind, their demeanor calm and refined. They walk in trust and think in compliance, never transgressing propriety. In hardship, they remain pure, their integrity as clear as ice and frost. Yet they are slow and impractical, unable to grasp essentials or act on opportunity. Their undertakings yield no success; in their own conduct, they are reckless. When entrusted with tasks, they fail to rise to the occasion. This is the eighth difficulty in discernment.

There are scholars whose conduct is lofty and unconstrained, their character stern and towering. They whistle and strut, disdaining their peers and scorning convention. They are careless in small matters, their faults exceeding reason, acting as if no one else exists. They form cliques to attack and slander, aligning with those who share their failures. Scholars and friends do not follow them, and critics dismiss them. Yet in court, they stand with unyielding integrity, knowing no restraint in their loyalty to their superiors, and governing their subordinates with clarity. This is the ninth difficulty in discernment.

There are scholars who are generous and broad-minded, humble in receiving others, concealing faults and hiding flaws. They are warm, respectful, incorruptible, diligent, and modest, aiding the endangered and preserving trust. They entrust their lives without doubt, and orphans under their care are secure. Yet though pure and good, they are blind to power; though benevolent, they lack decisiveness. They cannot reward the good or bear to punish the wicked. Their loyalty and integrity are abundant, but their practical abilities are lacking. Like one holding an axe, they hesitate, abandoning laws and indulging wrongs, mixing right and wrong, ultimately leading to failure and defeat. This is the tenth difficulty in discernment.

Things may appear similar yet be fundamentally different, seeming one way yet being another. To judge without confusion, one must perceive both form and spirit—how much more so for those who are not sages? Thus, in employing talent, selecting scholars, fostering closeness, and forming friendships, one cannot but be discerning, cannot but be thorough in testing.

Moreover, the confusion of human nature and conduct may begin upright but end in depravity—like Wang Mang, who at first seemed finer than Yi and Huo, yet in the end was more vicious than Zhao Gao. Such transformations are not something even men of middling talent can fully anticipate.

If scholars were as easily distinguished as the wren from the swan, the fox and hare from the dragon and qilin, then the Four Fiends would not have gained office in Yao’s court, Guan and Cai would not have nearly endangered the Zhou dynasty, Confucius would not have erred with Zhan Tai, Yanling would not have regretted discarding his gold, Yi Yin would not have labored seventy times, and Xiang Yu would not have regretted dismissing Fan Zeng.

The trouble lies in the confusion caused by things like *wufu* jade resembling true jade, the *jiaomiao* moth mimicking the phoenix, frozen ice resembling crystal, and smoke resembling misty vapors. Thus, even the wise may err.

How much more difficult is it for the emperor? And how much more so for those near at hand!

The truly great and luminous mirror, darkly discerning the subtle and spiritually weighing all things, perceives with clarity what lies hidden—gazing upon mountains, it recognizes uncut jade; facing rivers, it knows true pearls. Thus, even amid the most difficult distinctions, it makes no errors of acceptance or rejection. By this means, judgments are rightly divided, praise and blame justly apportioned. Were this so, Wu Ding would not have ruled alone, nor would Fu Yue and Lü Shang have remained long cast aside; Wang Mang and Zai Pi would not have completed their evil, and Gong and Shi Xian would have found no place to conceal their deceit.

Such is the rough outline of selecting men, the general principle of careful choice—yet the subtleties of seeking the right person cannot be fully captured even by the finest brush.

**Case 21: Quelling Litigation**
Master Gu’s nephew, Liu Junshi, argued:

The human order begins with husband and wife, their union modeled upon the Two Modes. Thus, the great rite of marriage was held in highest esteem by the ancients. To join two families in goodwill and continue the ancestral line, the host bowed in welcome at the gate, received the charge in the temple, presented dark and light silks as gifts, and personally handed the reins to the groom. If the groom was in three years’ mourning, the command was given; the bride’s family assented and dared not alter it. After the great mourning had passed, they sought the groom’s permission; only when he gave his word was the marriage finalized. Such was the reverence for deference.

How could there be litigation before the wedding feast? Yet in later ages, frivolity and disrespect have harmed customs and corrupted morals. Betrothals are promised but not fulfilled, leading to suits that bring shame and clog the courts. Now, let all who dispute marriage before sharing the wedding meat be deemed to have broken their bond, and let the betrothal gifts—wine, silk, and cloth—be returned. Those who have separated once shall repay double the betrothal gifts; those who have parted thrice shall repay triple. Thus, those who separate will have no cause for litigation, and the greedy will find no profit in delay. This is the essential method of royal governance, an enduring law that cannot be changed.

*Baopuzi* replied:

Master Liu grieves the decline of virtue and deference and laments how litigation harms the people’s transformation. Though he hastens to end lawsuits within families, the value of harmony is not thereby served. He fears the world will sink into mutual ruin, so he devises bold proposals to salvage what has been lost, offering fine plans to rescue a wayward age. By this means, he seeks to replace shameless customs and transform a clamorous society. Yet distinguishing truth from falsehood is inherently difficult.

Truly, this is an enduring law for governing the state, a most beneficial discourse. Yet Hong, being dull and unversed in the highest principles, in my haste to respond, harbor some doubts.

Marriage bonds are formed by mutual consent without coercion—one party selects with care, the other finds satisfaction and thus grants consent. Light promises later regretted incur blame upon the woman’s family, for breaking faith and abandoning trust, seizing or yielding at whim. Though strict prohibitions and severe regulations are imposed, they cannot fully prevent such transgressions. Now, by allowing the unrestrained to indulge their desires and doubling the betrothal gifts, the poor are deterred, while those of ample wealth may fulfill their wishes. Yet if the later suitor proves wealthy and offers to augment the betrothal gifts, the woman’s family will gladly accept. Thus, the first family must remain silent, unable to voice objection. Examining the matter by its true nature, can they not but sigh in resentment?

Those who defy authority regard death as a return home, and from this, bloodshed will arise. If one merely spares them the minor disgrace of litigation while fostering deep, unbearable grudges, it is like cherishing the trivial cost of rent while forgetting the ruinous toll of decay. When purchasing goods in the marketplace, if one raises the price only to seize them, few will endure such provocation without anger. How much more so when the wife one awaits is taken away? Should this law be enforced, those who marry—though they perform the *naji* rites and the bride’s welcoming—will still harbor the fear of seizure. Why? Because Master Liu’s argument fixes the marriage bond at the *tonglao* ceremony. Thus, even if the woman’s family has accepted betrothal gifts for years, they may still wield arbitrary power, confident they can seize her back multiple times. If the groom is dissatisfied, he may repudiate the match, and enmity will swiftly lead to disaster—nothing is more grievous than this.

In former times, lawmakers carefully considered the entire process, blocking the path at its outset to prevent the sprouting of trouble. Their foresight was keen and unerring. The system of *baoban* (returning the betrothal gifts as a formal rejection) was likely designed with this in mind. Suppose a woman possesses peerless beauty, a face to topple cities, and a figure beyond compare. If she catches the eye of a powerful minister or nobleman, who, captivated by her allure, forgets propriety and decorum, spares no expense—even offering ten times the betrothal gifts—would he hesitate? The House of Hua did not shrink from killing Kong Fu to take his wife, nor did the men of Chu refrain from seizing the bride intended for their lord when they beheld her beauty. By this reasoning, would they scruple to exhaust their household wealth to aid the woman’s family in repaying the first suitor’s betrothal gifts? Petty men, fickle and quick to take offense, readily nurse grudges. They delight in abandoning the declining and pursuing the ascendant, trampling the cold to chase the warm. If this law is enacted, it will surely lead to the poor and lowly being deprived while the rich and powerful prevail. I wonder, what measures does my lord propose to guard against such abuses?

Some suggest: Let the woman’s family receive the betrothal gifts without regard to amount, but require immediate *baoban* (formal acknowledgment of receipt). Thereafter, have witnesses—no fewer than ten—record their names on a separate document to account for those who may travel afar or die. Further, require the woman’s father, elder brother, or paternal uncle to respond to the groom’s family in their own handwriting. If any breach of promise occurs and the evidence is clear, the woman’s parents and brothers shall all be punished. With penalties so severe, might this not bring an end to litigation?

**Admonition on Wine, Twenty**

The Master Embracing Simplicity states: What the eye delights in must not be indulged; what the ear takes pleasure in must not be followed; what the nose favors must not be yielded to; what the mouth craves must not be pursued; what the heart desires must not be gratified. Thus, those ensnared by the eye are surely drawn to wanton beauty and gaudy adornments; those ensnared by the ear are surely captivated by seductive melodies and lewd sounds; those ensnared by the nose are surely allured by the fragrance of *chai* and *hui*; those ensnared by the mouth are surely tempted by rare delicacies and fine viands; those ensnared by the heart are surely lured by power, profit, and renown. When all five senses are thus bewitched, the calamities that follow and the harm they bring to the self—are these not indeed to be believed?

Therefore, the wise restrain their nature with stern discipline, curbing the spirit from recklessly chasing external things. They govern it with tranquility, augment it with foresight, and suppress its passions as firmly as one fortifies a dike against bursting. In guiding their nature, they are more vigilant than one who reins in a galloping steed with rotten bridles. Thus, they preserve longevity within and avoid entanglements without.

Hunger and cold are hard to endure, yet those of pure integrity refuse grain and silk obtained through unrighteous means. Poverty and lowliness are difficult to bear, yet those of lofty aspiration shun the perilous honors and wealth of turbulent times. For when calculation prevails, the heart remains whole in its capacity for forbearance; when the Dao triumphs, all that harms one’s nature is cast aside.

The near-flavor of wine and sweet liquor is a poison that breeds illness—though it offers not a hair’s worth of benefit, its harm mounts to mountains. The nobleman corrupts his virtue through it; the petty man hastens his guilt. Few who indulge and become infatuated escape calamity. Men of the world know this truth, yet none can wholly abstain, nor even temper their indulgence. They surrender to the immediate desires of heart and mouth, lightly summoning the very source of disaster, like one parched who gorges on cold drink: though it slakes thirst for the moment, it endangers the body.

From minor disorder to total ruin, no misfortune is unconnected to wine. Yet the common crowd remains steeped in revelry. At first, their feasts are solemn and dignified: words are few, demeanor composed, songs like the *Yin Zhu* gentle, melodies like those of *Zai Hao* joyous. They raise cups wishing ten thousand years of life, reciting the virtue of temperate restraint. Before the sun has shifted its shadow, limbs grow light, ears tingle. Vessels of glass and conch shells are filled to the brim; penalties for excess are enforced with haste. Drunkenness knows no end, restraint cast aside like a wheel chock tossed into a well. Mouths foam, noses run, heads drip; order dissolves into chaos. They dance wildly, abandon their seats, clamor and shout as if boiling over like a cauldron. Some argue, insisting on victory; others cackle alone; some prattle without reply; others vomit across the mats; still others collapse. The finest entertainers lose their grace, while the virtuous and steadfast glance about warily. The timid mimic the boldness of Qing Ji, the slow and steady whirl like tumbleweeds, the solemn and composed leap like deer and dart like fish. Those once reticent in cold or heat now clap in unison, their voices harmonized. The meek and unassuming become wildly arrogant, their eyes aloft. The decorum of modesty and shame is shattered, and the maladies of recklessness erupt. Base natures are exposed, and the bearing of arrogance emerges. Spirit muddled, essence befouled, judgments overturned.

Some race chariots or gallop horses toward ravines without fear, treating the perilous Nine Bends like an anthill. Others climb heights and stumble, tumbling into the abyss yet unaware, mistaking the depths of Lüliang for a cow’s hoofprint. Some vent their rage on objects, or pour wine for their wives and children. They heap cruelty upon servants, wield sharp blades against possessions, kindle fires in their halls, cast treasures into the depths, and redirect their wrath toward the innocent. They inflict violence upon friends and subordinates. There are those who have slain their lords or been executed by them, who have provoked superiors and suffered humiliation, who have spoken eloquently yet defied reason, bowed and prostrated themselves in vain. Ministers and children breach propriety before their rulers and parents; the lowly and insolent mock their elders. They call candid speech slander and take loyal admonition as personal affronts. Thus, blades are drawn without thought of peril, cudgels swung without regard for what lies ahead. They provoke blood feuds, invite capital punishment.

To slight one’s elders is to forfeit respect in the village; to insult another’s father or brother is to invite a blade from their sons and brothers. To speak of what others hold taboo is to rouse the wrath of the strong. To exact harsh reckonings is to find no forgiveness even when sober. A moment’s folly breeds lifelong regret, and a hundred ailments fester in the depths. Even a team of galloping steeds cannot outrun past mistakes. Though one may wish to reform, there is no path back. This is what the wise guard against most deeply, yet the foolish cannot escape.

The litany of its disasters and defeats is beyond enumeration, yet no gathering abandons it. Cups are raised to the ears, regardless of capacity; dregs are measured by the drop, delay deemed slight. They pour from baskets for those they respect, exhausting themselves in vain effort. To refuse is to invite resentment; to urge is to provoke insult. Thus, flushed faces and ugly words arise.

Wind piercing the viscera leaves one disoriented; in severe cases, it brings self-injury and self-reproach. All who suffer such illness are filled with dread, swallowing bitterness and enduring pain in hopes of swift recovery. Yet the sickness of drunkenness is no different—why flee the wind in seclusion but fail to curb one’s passion for wine? If one truly feared the wind and loathed illness, the faults of dissipation would cease, and the errors of indulgence would end.

The harm of wind is treatable, just as the havoc of wine lies in a single breath. Yet in the throes of drunkenness, the mind is thrown into chaos, clinging to existence yet teetering on oblivion. They see Mount Tai as a pellet, the vast sea as a basin. They howl at the sky as if it were falling, cry out to the earth as if it were collapsing. They lie in wait for tigers and wolves, leap into wells and fires without a thought of peril. How, in such a state, can one uphold the rites of reverence, guard against lapses in joy or anger?

Of old, Di Yi warned Yu of the dangers of wine, yet Yu built mounds of lees and pools of liquor. The tyrants Jie and Zhou perished through it. Feng Hou was punished for raising his cup, Jing Sheng ruined by his three ceremonial goblets. Liu Song rotted his bowels with summer’s cooling drink, Guo Zhen lost his mind to daily drunkenness. Xinling’s excess shortened his life, Xiangzi’s debauchery disordered his rule, Zhao Wu lost the people’s favor, Zifan was executed, Han Hui forfeited his mandate, Guan Fu’s clan was annihilated, Chen Zun met his end, Ji Bu was banished, Zijian was dismissed, Xu Miao was silenced—all by this same vice.

The world delights in it, loves it in great numbers, yet those who warn against it or fear it are few. Outnumbered, how can good counsel prevail? I can only urge the noble to temper their indulgence.

In years past, during famine when grain was dear, men killed one another in drunkenness. Governors and prefects imposed bans on wine, issuing strict orders and repeated decrees. Officials searched and seized, flogging offenders. Many were shamed, half were beaten to death. The prohibitions grew ever stricter, yet violations multiplied. Some brewed in underground pits, carried wine in oil bladders, or concealed it in their robes. The people’s devotion to drink was profound indeed.

As a man of humble station, I append these empty words to the end of this book—what can I do? Moreover, those who govern, though they enact laws, cannot themselves renounce this vice. They relax their own restraints while demanding it of others. Without leading by example, how can the people believe? With such teaching, how can it be effective? With such prohibition, how can it be enforced?

Those who sell wine, if they abandon their trade, face hardship. Thus, they adorn their shops, bribe officials, and rely on the powerful. Local authorities dare not intervene. The powerless alone abstain, while the influential monopolize the market, erecting stalls and cornering profits. They double prices, sell openly, and fear no law. When profit outweighs punishment, who can resist?

Some may object: "The downfalls of Jie and Zhou, the ruin of Xinling and Han Hui—were these solely the fault of wine? Were they not also undone by sensual excess?"

The claim that Bao Si brought ruin to Zhou and thus rulers should abolish their six palaces, or that the peril of Epang led to Qin’s downfall and thus kings should dwell in thatched huts—such reasoning would have Yao and Shun drinking from a thousand goblets and a hundred cups, and Confucius, who knew no limit to wine, deemed capable. The Duke of Zhou never cleared the meat and wine from his table, yet he established rites and composed music. Emperor Gao of Han reveled in grand drunkenness, yet he slew the serpent and rallied his troops. Yu Gong drank a full *hu* and judged cases with greater clarity. Guan Lu downed three *dou* and spoke with dazzling eloquence. Yang Yun kept wine ever at his lips, and the Supreme Mystery was thereby attained. Ziyu drank until he knew nothing, yet his hegemonic achievements were raised high. A single jar of unstrained wine, poured out, gladdened the three armies. The cup that dispels poison, passed around, moved even the horse-thieves to remorse. Joy was completed, rites were upheld, merit was recognized, and spirits descended to commune with men—without wine, how could these be achieved? Within, it hastens the gathering of uncles; without, it welcomes honored guests. Like the Huai and Mian rivers, its value is prized in the *Spring and Autumn Annals*. From this, how can one admonish against it?

The *Baopuzi* replies: There are indeed tales of wine-flags lingering from the past. It is like the celestial bodies hanging in the sky, their brilliance unmatched—sun and moon, the origins of water and fire, all reside therein. Yet when regulated and deployed, they nurture life and establish merit; when misused, they burn and drown, bringing death. Can one rely on the celestial bodies in the sky and claim water and fire do not kill? The foremost necessity for life is none other than food, yet overindulgence in eating causes stagnation and masses. How much more so with the poison of wine and sweet ale?

Had Jie of Xia, Zhou of Yin, Xinling, and Han Hui not been swept away by the decadent sounds of亡国, nor drowned in the chaos of倾城’s seduction—all fueled by wine’s fumes, their natures inflamed, their power emboldened by drunkenness—they would not have reached such extremes of indulgence, forgetting the arts of self-cultivation. You trace the branches but miss the root; you call it not the disaster of wine, yet from where does disaster arise? It is like knowing muddy rain soils one’s robes but not recognizing the clouds that produced it, or fearing only the dust that stings the eyes while ignoring the gale that drives it. Tales of a thousand goblets and a hundred cups are baseless words, absurd claims—no wise person would believe them.

The sages’ distinction lies not in their talent or wisdom extending to their physical forms—none were seven *chi* or three *zhang* tall, nor a myriad times larger than others. How could a single day’s hunger bring them to such extremes? Confucius feared the alteration of his nature and dared not descend into disorder. The Duke of Zhou, though he bowed a hundred times a day and left meat and wine untouched, remained ever vigilant, trembling with caution. If even such sages were thus, how can ordinary men avoid regret?

Emperor Gao of Han, responding to heaven and inheriting the mandate, would have slain the serpent even without drunkenness. Yu Gong was astute and clear in his judgments, weighing matters large and small with fairness, never erring in right or wrong. Thus, punishments were not excessive, the world bore no grievances, and the people were content. His heavy drinking did not impede affairs; if one speaks of great drunkenness, it merely rendered him senseless—what loss was there to his talent for resolving doubts? Never have I heard of Gao Yao, Fu Hou, or Zichan deciding cases while drunk.

Guan Lu was young and rarely engaged in weighty discourse, so he borrowed wine’s influence to bolster his courage. Had he exceeded his limit, he would surely have become confused. Yet when he divined the subtle in hexagrams, sought the transformations of gods and demons, observed qi and complexion to determine rise and fall, listened to birdsong to foresee the future, watched wind and clouds to avert misfortune, or read the steles of Huan to discern fortune and calamity—did he require wine to do so? Yang Yun was a man of broad learning, his thoughts lofty, his intellect vast, his endowment from heaven—how could he rely on external things to aid his writings? His frequent drinking was but a passing preference, or perhaps a means to dispel illness through the power of medicine.

Ziyu’s ambitions were long set; even without drunkenness, he would have departed all the same. The joy of the three armies at the jar of unstrained wine was a metaphor: if rewards and punishments are just, authority and favor aptly applied, strategies long and short deployed with timeliness and flexibility, then soldiers will act with resolve, and men will gladly risk their lives. Without such, even if wine flowed like a river, how could it decide victory or defeat? Duke Mu of Qin’s drunken pardon of the horse-thieves was a momentary lapse, an abandonment of law that fostered evil—what is there to praise? How does it compare to caution?

*On the Absurdity of Illness*, Chapter Twenty

Bao Puzi said: In the world’s constant flux, ritual and teaching have gradually declined, reverence and deference no longer exalted, while arrogance and rudeness have become the custom. Companions at drinking feasts now squat or sprawl; in the heat of summer, they bare their heads and strip their bodies, their sole concern the games of *chupu*, *tanqi*, and *weiqi*. Their discourse never strays beyond the realm of sensual pleasures, their speech never leaves the company of brocade and fine silk. Their wanderings never depart from the gates of the powerful and the wine-loving, nor do they ever hear words of pure discourse or discussion of the Dao. They take coarse jests and mockery as their first priority, esteeming those who do likewise as lofty and refined, while dismissing those who do not as dull-witted and rustic.

Thus, the common rabble and the vulgar crowd admire them as moths drawn to a bright flame, and imitate them as light feathers borne on the whirlwind. Their taunts and jests may target ancestors or descend to wives and daughters. Those who initiate such talk strive to make it as cutting as possible, while those who respond fear their retorts may not be weighty enough. The instigator gives no thought to the consequences of an answer, and the respondent feels shame if his words fail to fully avenge the slight. Like reaping the millet of Zhou or cutting the wheat of Wen, their feuds arise from grievances that cannot be laid to rest.

Those with sharp tongues rely on strength and ally with power; the glib and quick-witted borrow the sting of the *rou* wasp to pierce with the *fen* barb. To fail to respond is deemed clumsy and inferior; to yield first is considered defeat. In such exchanges of enmity, how can one remain silent? Even those of talent and discernment are thus treated by others.

Those skilled in such exchanges rely on circumstances, gauge opportunities, and model their responses on established forms, drawing from antiquity to illustrate the present. Their words are subtle yet reasoned, refined yet amusing, striking the mark without wounding. They avoid what others taboo and do not trespass on what others cherish. As for those who lack such skill—when they speak for others, they distort the straight and force the crooked, leaving their listeners stunned. Whether fair or foul, their words aim only to sever ties, and far from being harmless, they bring only ruin.

There are those, too, who are quick to anger or of a nature that brooks no offense. Unable to endure, they fling their robes aside, seize thorns, and come to blows. Vile words are hurled at those they once revered; joy turns to enmity, friendships shatter, reputations crumble, and feuds take root. Some hurl cups and goblets; others expose hidden secrets. Of old, Chen Ling was struck by an arrow, and the Guan clan was wiped out—not by fate from heaven, but by the power of words. The pivot of disaster, the arbiter of honor and shame: the warning of the three seals was no deception.

Even thunder cannot retrieve a past mistake; the finest craftsman cannot polish a tarnished word. Though one may not always weigh their words thrice before speaking, a light jest can still quell brewing strife. Honor your speech, respect your bearing—let your words be free of fault, your manner neither haughty nor lax, so that your conduct may be emulated, admired, feared, and loved. This is the art of avoiding disgrace and the essential path to preserving friendship.

Moreover, those who slight others do not love their own kin; those who provoke fights do not value their own bodies.

Those who fall into unfiliality—can one not scrutinize this? Yet the deluded lack self-awareness, and the willful shun admonitions that grate upon the ear. They loathe virtue and lack the needle of forthrightness to pierce their illusions; they are bewildered by confusion and lack the compass of self-reflection. Flatterers and petty men laugh in approval of what is good, while those who feign compliance clap their hands in praise of merit. Thus the deluded remain unaware of their errors, believing themselves possessed of Yanzi’s caution and surpassing eloquence—unaware that this is but the banner that summons calamity, the tally that invites harm, the post-horse that overturns the body’s carriage. Is this merely a matter of diminishing one’s present reputation or tarnishing one’s posthumous renown? Even with lifelong prudence, one cannot undo a single morning’s transgression; even with the wealth of Tao Zhu, one cannot redeem a single misword. A slip of the tongue strays a thousand li; a wounding word cuts deeper than a sword. Small faults accumulate into glaring ones, shallow lapses deepen into ruin. A goose feather can sink a dragon boat, and many light burdens can break a sturdy axle. A gust of wind can burn a towering hall, and a termite can topple a tree of a hundred spans. Why did the ancients tread so cautiously, while modern men are so reckless and arrogant? Thus men of lofty virtue turn away at the sight of dust, while the frivolous and shallow flock like echoes to a sound, shadows to a form. Those who plot affairs lack the aid of the wise; those in peril find no one to admonish them. Good historians hold their brushes, finding no deeds worth recording; eloquent speakers fall silent, their words unworthy of transmission. No fine reputation endures, while shameful rumors spread without end, leaving behind only ruin for future generations—neither a model to emulate nor a record to preserve. This is the disgrace of the aspiring scholar. To persist in error without correction is to abandon the smooth path and plunge into thorns, to forsake sweet springs and swallow wolfsbane. Is this not the very case of deeming small good as unworthy of pursuit and small evil as harmless to indulge, until evil accumulates beyond concealment and guilt grows beyond absolution?

I urge the people of this world to reform their deceitful ways, to cast off their arrogance and stinginess, to rid themselves of boastful and haughty men, and to cease all mockery and improper speech. Then the gates of Zhao Sheng will not turn away guests, and the cudgel of Huang Zu will find no use.

The *Baopuzi* states: Some, lacking pure virtue to command respect, rely on brute force to demand fear. When among others, they stand rigidly, refusing to sit, and vie to occupy the highest seat. They scowl and raise their voices, driving others away to assert their dominance. When displeased, they seethe with resentment and refuse to yield. In narrow paths, they refuse to share the way, striding forward with flailing arms, shoving others into peril. If not immediately avoided, they escalate their aggression. Alas! How far this is from the ancients, who, though humble, were never surpassed—yielding the path, deferring to others, modest and unassuming, standing as if their robes could not bear their weight, walking as if their bodies could not contain themselves. How shamelessly inferior are these men! Those of noble conduct and pure integrity, though they may humble themselves, are still esteemed by others. If one’s conduct is not lofty, even if one acts with arrogance, others will not respect them. If one outwardly adopts human form but inwardly loses humaneness, they will be despised, not revered.

In former times, when Zhuangzi had not yet dined, the King of Zhao stood at his side; when Zou Yan entered Yan’s borders, the lord of Yan swept the path before him. In Kangcheng’s village, the Xiongnu prostrated themselves in homage; in Linzi’s court, none failed to show reverence—not through force, but through virtue. One who amasses wealth through banditry, though rich, is not worthy of praise; one who coerces others through violence, though feared, is not worthy of honor. Yet the common people engage in such acts without shame. Those who hear their words recoil as if a vulture’s cry has sounded; those who see their faces shrink as if a demon has appeared. Wherever they go, it is as if a monstrous omen has descended; wherever they travel, it is as if a pack of tigers has gathered. Fools emulate them, boasting of their boldness; petty men cite them as the first step toward chaos. Disorder has no fixed cause—this is its origin.

Yet those who dare to act thus are not necessarily inherently wicked. Most come from families of rank and influence, or doors of power and patronage. They initially perform good deeds to steal hollow reputations, and once their names are vaguely established, their true natures emerge. Some use wealth and beauty to curry favor with the powerful; others exploit the times to attain frivolous honors. Some marry into noble families, while others manipulate praise and slander to align with authority. Their ambitions swell, their demeanor grows unchecked, and their faults multiply. They form cliques and expand their influence, their aspirations soaring, their conduct beyond reproach. No restraint can curb them, no criticism can strike them. They become flies with the heads of eagles, rats within temple walls—wherever they are not yet present, they wield power and fortune to control others. Those superior to them dare not speak, and if they do, they feign ignorance. Those beneath them dare not voice dissent, and if they do, they cannot suppress it. Just as pests harming grain perish with the first frost, so too do flatterers and villains meet their downfall in stern times. The only recourse for those who would preserve their integrity is to refuse to serve them and to decline to imitate them. But without an axe handle, what can one do?

The *Baopuzi* states: The *Book of Songs* praises the *suijiu* bird for its distinction; the rites dictate that men and women should not behave improperly. Without a matchmaker, they do not meet; they do not sit together, exchange words, or share objects; they do not hand things directly to one another. Sisters who marry out do not share seats with their brothers upon returning. Words from outside do not enter the inner chambers, and words from within do not leave. Women do not go beyond the door to send off or welcome guests; when traveling, they cover their faces. On the road, men take the left, women the right—such intimacy is already excessive. Moreover, men do not stay indoors during the day unless ill, and they do not die in a woman’s hands—let alone anyone else’s. In former times, the woman of Lu did not seclude herself deeply enough, leading to the scandal of Yuluo; Kong’s wife did not guard her chamber closely enough, sparking the disaster of Hua Du. Shi Ji’s lack of caution brought shame upon her descendants; Wangsun’s loose speech led to her confinement. Yet today, common women abandon their proper duties and neglect their weaving and spinning. They idle about, forsaking household chores to cultivate social visits, traveling to see relatives by starlight, torches in hand. Their retinues fill the roads—maids, servants, officials, and soldiers mingling like a marketplace. Along the way, they jest and mock, their behavior detestable. Some stay overnight at others’ homes; some venture out at night and return. They amuse themselves at Buddhist temples, watch fishing and hunting, climb heights, gaze upon waters, travel beyond their borders for celebrations and condolences. They ride in open carriages, curtains drawn, traversing cities and towns, drinking and toasting along the road, singing and playing music, each vying to outdo the other. Such improper habits become custom, leading to all manner of impropriety. The source of licentiousness is not urgently addressed. To govern one’s household, one must first set an example for one’s wife. I hope that gentlemen will slightly curb such behavior, ensuring that women have no external affairs—this is how to guard against subtle faults.

The Master Embracing Simplicity said: Frivolous men tread the paths of the lofty and profound, forming bonds through wealth and patronage, and once their rank and position are roughly secured, they turn their backs on ritual and rebel against teaching. Claiming to follow their nature and talent, they refuse to conform to norms, forcing themselves into unbridled abandon. They deem arrogance and lack of restraint as magnanimity, and those who cherish caution and integrity as petty and narrow. Thus, when the year-end drums sound, the idle and dissolute—white-haired dogs and drunken ears—gather in cliques, banding together to roam without discernment. Extraordinary scholars and great Confucians may live just beyond the fence, yet they pay them no heed. Wherever their whims take them, though distant, they must go, hand in hand, sleeve linked to sleeve, to wander and assemble. They enter others’ halls and chambers, gaze upon their women, pointing out flaws and praising beauty, judging ugliness and comeliness. What is the purpose of such behavior?

Some, finding the master of the house unapproachable, gather at the front gate, putting on airs and finery. If their demands are not met, they no longer merely peer and eavesdrop but break down doors, scale walls, and bore through gaps, as if raiders or bandits have arrived. If concubines or maidservants hide but are discovered, they search out secluded spots and drag them forth—how monstrous!

The gentleman, even in his own home, does not expose his family’s unpreparedness. Upon entering a gate, he raises his voice; ascending the hall, he lowers his gaze. Yet these men barge into others’ homes—what principle justifies this? Nevertheless, rootless and spineless men, eager to follow the crowd, regard those who engage in such behavior as intimate friends and those who refuse as discourteous. They truly believe this is how the world must be. Thus, they clamor and crowd into rooms, gazing upon wives, squeezing onto narrow seats, exchanging cups at close quarters, singing lewd and seductive tunes to stir the passions of the fair, howling and clamoring, jesting and mocking, exhausting every vulgarity and obscenity. They laugh at the great virtues of men, trampling propriety and decorum. Jie’s downfall, Zhou’s ruin, the Zhou’s collapse, and Chen’s destruction all stemmed from a lack of ritual—how much more so for common folk?

Sincerity that does not arise from the heart renders repeated oaths futile; when intent is fulfilled and spirit attains its aim, even form and vessel may be forgotten. The gentleman’s bonds are forged through the Dao and virtue, through shared aspirations and kindred spirits, thus becoming pure and enduring. The petty man’s connections are bound by power and profit, sealed by familiarity and disdain, thus turning sweet but ending in ruin. Why must they gather in chambers, display intimacy before wives and concubines, and only then divide affection and closeness?

The ancients, discerning the crooked paths of licentiousness and blocking the inroads of perversity, were truly wise. Those who cultivate such wisdom are gentlemen; those who turn their backs on it are petty men. Yet when prohibitions are enforced, even the inner palace has men who tunnel through walls; when nets are full of holes, the mulberry groves harbor eloping women. If left unchecked and indulged, it is like unleashing a raging fire upon the Yunmeng Marsh or opening the floodgates of a vast reservoir. Can a towering blaze be extinguished with brooms and dustpans, or a deluge stemmed with a handful of earth?

Yet custom and habit persist, and all say: “This is simply what the young lords and nobles of the capital and great cities do.”

I often refute them, saying: The Central States are the very source of ritual—could ritual truly be thus? Such practices arise from decline and disorder, not the customs of a well-ordered age. Even Laozi, paragon of purity and emptiness, dared not indulge his desires, lest they disturb the mind’s clarity. If even a man of Liuxia Hui’s spotless conduct and lofty virtue, after repeated encounters, could not prevent passion from stirring within or desire from showing on his face—how much more so for others? Those of indifferent temperament are rare as one in ten thousand, and those who suppress their feelings are even harder to find. Can such conduct be allowed to grow?

Though impoverished scholars may recognize the harm in these customs, lacking both reputation and influence, how can they correct them? I have often lamented this, earning only the scorn of my peers, who call me rustic and unrefined, unable to adapt to the times. Yet I hold fast to my convictions—why should I abandon what I know to be right for what others deem acceptable? Such sighs are not few, but alas, what can I do? Those already steeped in depravity and sinking into ruin—how could they heed unwelcome truths or turn back from their distant, errant paths?

The *Baopuzi* states: Among the common folk, there is a custom of humiliating women—mocking them with vulgar words, demanding insolent replies in crowded gatherings before their own kin. The shame and degradation are unspeakable. Some are pinched and beaten, others bound and hung upside down; drunken guests, unrestrained, inflict injuries so severe that blood flows and limbs are broken. How lamentable!

The ancients grieved at partings without candlelight, mourned for parents without music. Ritual texts deem marriage shameful to celebrate, not to congratulate. Yet people today cannot even uphold these old traditions. Those who are respected in their villages, whose words carry weight among scholars—should they not sternly correct such behavior? Why follow the current and perpetuate these vile customs?

Yet these practices have persisted so long among the people that none recognize their wrongness. Gentle persuasion cannot curb them; only harsh punishments could stop them. Thus, the teachings of Zhou are scorned, and Confucius is slandered. Reckless abandon is hailed as transcending the world. Some seize power through upheaval, others rely on powerful patrons to soar and gather. Then they grow arrogant, boastful, their airs towering, their strides haughty, their gazes distant. They are self-satisfied, looking down on those who lag behind or lose their way—even the truly exceptional are dismissed like weeds.

Some recline on pillows to receive guests; others feign illness to avoid them. They expect scholars to crowd their gates like forests, their carriages to choke the lanes, and call this the mark of nobility—claiming it cannot be otherwise. Yet by the measure of power and rank, the Duke of Zhou was diligent in welcoming guests with utmost courtesy. By the standard of reputation, Confucius was patient and encouraging in teaching. Both took humility and hard work as their duty, never prizing arrogance. But by the late Han, all had changed.

Disheveled and unkempt, some received guests without belts, others sat barefoot with legs splayed. At gatherings of friends or outings for pleasure, none spoke earnestly of self-improvement or the cultivation of virtue. No one discussed the subtleties of the Dao or the essence of its teachings. When they met, they no longer inquired after each other’s well-being or reminisced about separations. Guests would enter shouting for servants; hosts would watch for visitors while calling for their dogs. Those who did not act thus were deemed unworthy of friendship. When feasting together, they squatted like foxes or drank like cattle, competing for food, tearing at meat, grabbing and snatching without restraint. All sense of shame was lost. Those who joined in were praised; those who refused were scorned as inferior. All day, no word of moral principle was spoken; all night, no beneficial admonition was given.

They misrepresented Laozi and Zhuangzi, prizing reckless abandon over restraint, dismissing minor courtesies as beneath the "perfected person." Unrestrained arrogance was called embodying the Dao. Alas! How pitiful—how sorrowful!

Thus, they mocked their kin to entertain guests, exhausted debauchery to forge bonds, and leaned on each other to display affection. Those who stretched their legs were praised as graceful and refined; those of dignified bearing were dismissed as rustic and dull. Those who spoke with authority were called overbearing and rigid; those who answered clearly were accused of rudeness and abruptness. All these frivolous, glib men—though skilled in flattery and quick to form cliques, mutually praising and promoting each other for swift success—were in truth shallow and hollow, like the gourd-vessel metaphor of the Shu people. Their chests held not a single line of classic texts; their knowledge extended no further than wine and meat. They were the "arrogant and fierce" who obscure virtue, the "reckless and greedy" who covet wealth and gluttony—the "worthless sons" described by Zuo Qiuming.

If asked about the subtle words of the ancient texts, the workings of gods and spirits, the transformations of the myriad things, the wonders of distant lands, the great rites of court and ancestral temples, the ceremonies of suburban sacrifices and grand offerings, the origins of the Three Beginnings and Four Commencements, the principles of yin-yang and the calendar, the regulations of military and state affairs, or the differences and continuities of ancient and modern institutions—they would be stunned, speechless, shifting uncomfortably, at a loss for words. Though inwardly they might sense their ignorance, like facing a wall, they would never admit their shortcomings. Instead, they would forcefully boast: "Trivial old stories—those are for pedantic scholars who chant over dead texts and grovel in dusty alleys. Such things are beneath us!"

They know the harm of ignorance and the value of great scholars; they recognize the errors of their habits and the folly of their ways. Yet they remain deluded, clinging to their old, dissolute ways, for these do not hinder their advancement. If a noble person were to rebuke them with stern words, even those who defy authority and persist in evil would surely be startled, their hearts melting like ice, their faces changing. They would turn and flee—but alas, such a day will never come.

*Baopuzi, Outer Chapters, Scroll 2*
*(Subcategory: Daoist Works, Baopuzi Inner and Outer Chapters)*

*The Imperial Four Treasuries: Baopuzi Outer Chapters, Scroll 3*
*By Ge Hong of the Jin Dynasty*
*Chapter 26: "Mocking Delusion"*

Baopuzi states: When the turbid and the clear were first sundered, the myriad creatures came to life. Among the feathered kind, some could converse; among the furred tribes, some spoke with wisdom. Gibbons knew the way home, and cranes foresaw the future. Dark birds divined yin and yang, while ants discerned distant springs and streams. Even the tortoise and milfoil could not surpass them, nor could Gan De and Shi Shen outdo them. Yet only humans, lacking propriety, fail to rank among the noble by nature.

In the earliest times, the people knew no hierarchy. The supreme sages lamented the crudeness of such chaos and pitied the baseness of dwelling in nests and caves. Thus they built beams and roofs to separate from the flocks of birds and beasts, and established rites and ranks to distinguish the gradations of dignity. They taught the people to turn and bow, instructed them in yielding and deference. When standing, they bent like a full vessel; when saluting, they clasped hands like drumsticks striking a drum. They regulated the steps of approach and retreat, the postures of gazing and standing, the manners of facing and responding. The *Three Hundred Rites* served as the dike against excess and deficiency—this was the people’s great concern.

Thus, solemnity crowns the *Qu Li*, and a dignified countenance heads the *Five Constants*. When stepping out, one maintains the reverence due to guests; when at leisure, one heeds the caution of solitude. Yan Hui composed his bearing even at nightly ablutions, while Zhong You tied his cap-strings in the face of death. To guard against even momentary lapses into carelessness—this is how to secure peace above and govern the people below. Without this, nothing can suffice.

A person’s possession of propriety is like a fish’s possession of water. Though a fish may briefly survive without water, its withering is inevitable. A person who abandons propriety, though still breathing, has already stepped onto the path of ruin. Lu adhered to the Zhou rites, and no enemy troops invaded; Wei followed Ganku’s example, and raiding armies turned their banners. The state of Chu, though armored in a million men, was mocked for its withered might; Qin, though it seized the passes of Xiao and Han, found no willow groves to break its fall. Was this not the consequence of casting aside the Three Foundations and severing the roots of reverence?

How much worse in times of ease and indulgence, when chaos endures and the winds of instruction decay? The rites of restraint are discarded, and manners of simplicity are lost. Those who draw near to others now encounter a decline in governance; the common people suffer the collapse of the Dao’s guiding threads. It is like fish leaving the water or nets, or beasts escaping to the open land—trapped without refuge.

Since the onset of turmoil, the forms of things have shifted repeatedly. Hats and shoes, robes and sashes, carriages and implements—all have changed with the sun and moon, never settling into constancy. Now long, now short; now broad, now narrow; now high, now low; now coarse, now fine—their adornments shift without pattern. Those who delight in novelty mimic these changes morning and evening, so that what the capital’s elite favor—great brows and distant temples—is imitated even in distant lands, where half the forehead is shaved.

I, in truth, am but an ordinary man, clumsy at following custom. My attire and possessions cannot keep pace with these endless changes. Where no harm comes from remaining unchanged, I have never altered them. Though I am mocked and pointed at, I pay no heed. Not that I stubbornly defy the crowd—truly, I see no need to change.

The superiority of the Central States in most matters over the lands south of the Yangtze is undeniable, yet not all their ways are without flaw. The noble man, in practicing ritual, does not seek to alter local customs—this is why, when traveling to foreign lands, he does not abandon the laws of his native place. How much less should one, in the very land of one’s parents, discard established practices to forcibly adopt new ones?

In calligraphy, Wu boasts masters such as Huang Xiang, Liu Zuan, and Cen Boran, while Zhu Jiping stands as a peerless hand of his age—just as the Central States have Zhong Yuanchang, Hu Kongming, Zhang Zhi, and Suo Jing, each excelling in their own style, all possessing ancient forms and fully meeting the demands of their craft. Yet I say: to abandon a long-practiced method and laboriously study the calligraphy of the Central States is still unnecessary—how much more so when it comes to altering one’s speech to imitate another tongue? Failing to achieve the ease of the original and instead earning ridicule for one’s clumsiness—this is like the man who, unable to master the steps of Handan, crawls away in shame. Such folly is but a minor matter.

Worse still are those who, in mourning, mimic the wailing of the Central States, their cries suddenly devoid of genuine sorrow. In ancient times, Zhong Yi and Zhuang Xi never forgot their native accents, and the sages approved. Confucius said of one who has lost a parent: "It is as if an infant has lost its mother—how could such wailing be mere ordinary sound?" Better that grief be abundant even if ritual falls short, for weeping is but the venting of sorrow—what does refinement or clumsiness matter? Yet now some polish their tones, which is far from true anguish.

I have also heard that among the elite in deep mourning, some, when ill, consume mineral powders with frequent meals, suspending medication to drink wine as if it were their very lifeblood. When illness grows critical and they cannot endure the cold, they allow curtains, bedding, and cushions to be arranged as they please. Thus, even men of talent and strength among the lowly no longer remain in the mourning hall but retire to separate chambers with high beds, thick quilts, fine food, and strong drink. Some even drink with close companions, draining their cups and tossing them aside until they sink into drunkenness, claiming this is the way of the capital. Is this not lamentable?

In my own village, the virtuous elders of former times, when facing grave hardship or illness in old age, adhered to ritual: though sackcloth remained upon their bodies, they did not allow grief to destroy them. They ate gruel, their mouths untouched by sweetness. Even the unworthy of later times could not help but look up to them. Yet now, men bring such misfortune upon themselves—how vast is the distance between then and now!

It is commonly said that those in the Central States who observe mourning are excessively extravagant, but this is not so. I have heard that during the mourning periods of Emperors Xuan, Jing, Wen, and Wu of Jin, all emaciated themselves beyond the prescribed limits and did not follow the Wang clan’s twenty-five-month ritual, instead observing a seven-month mourning period. At the time, all in deep mourning throughout the realm took these four emperors as their model. How is it that people today have not heard of this and falsely slander the refined? Is this not confounding?

*Critique of Arrogance, Twenty-Seven*
The Master of Embracing Simplicity said: "Born into..."

Those born into esteemed families or raised in positions of blazing influence often do not seek arrogance—yet arrogance comes to them of its own accord. Only those of extraordinary capacity can avoid the excess of self-satisfaction. For when one toils in humility and self-restraint, many gather to support him; when one is haughty, slow, and proud, many swiftly depart. Herein lies the pivot of survival and ruin—how can one fail to weigh it carefully? Is this not a grave delusion?

There are also those who rise from lowly origins, their caution hardening into display: they hunch their shoulders, shrink their steps, bow deeply, stand sideways, lower their brows, bend their knees, and cling to the powerful. Through connections and maneuvering, they leap ranks out of turn, sprouting wings and shedding their former selves like cicadas molting from the earth. Soon they strut, eyes no longer scanning the ground, their vessels full, their hearts content, regarding others as mere mustard seeds. Some cluster in dense crowds, their pipes and flutes clamorous, guests filling their gates until they no longer receive them. Even among those they once associated with, they may favor a few, though such favor is rarely fully deserved. It is merely that their pursuit was more diligent, their visits more frequent, their gifts and flasks never absent. This is what Mencius called "loving without respecting—treating them like swine and cattle."

Yet many claim this is the way of self-respect. The way of self-respect lies in humbling oneself before the lowly to cultivate one’s character—not this. Such behavior is but the corruption of a declining age, a chronic malady of a rotting custom. How tragic that so many partake in it!

As for those of towering stature and vast capacity, whose bearing is free and whose spirit soars far beyond, who walk alone in solitary grace, content in the ebb and flow of the world—whose spirit ascends beyond the Nine Heavens, whose Dao is complete within, who leave the external world behind, whose hats are tattered, whose shoes are worn, whose belts are frayed—how could they deign to compete with vulgar men for petty advantages, to flatter and fawn for favor, to mimic the fawning sycophants of the imperial gardens, or to buzz like spring cicadas and summer flies? If judged by appearance, they are found wanting in refinement; if measured by worldly standards, their coarse simplicity obscures their profound spirit. People see only their rough exterior and cannot perceive the depth of their essence, attending to the skin but not the heart. Though they hold hidden brilliance and embrace the extraordinary, surpassing their age in every deed, their words could awaken the world to truth, their speech could resolve the confusions of past and present, their wisdom is boundless, and they speak only of the Dao—yet a thatched hut cannot contain their thunderous resonance, nor can a dwarf block the radiance of their sharp blade. Thus, fools mock them as common and dull. Only one like the man of Hanbin could recognize a pearl in a muddy clam; only one who weeps blood could discern a night-glowing jewel in the depths of a dark ravine. The firefly swarms in the mosquito’s brow, the giant roc soars beyond the sky; the minnow dwells in the hoofprint’s water, scorning the leviathan that crosses the sea. Their paths and wisdom do not intersect—how can they align? There is no cause for surprise. Those who resent them cannot remain silent, for they wish that gentlemen in office would not judge others by their arrogance, but instead encourage humility to uphold the celestial order.

The Master of Embracing Simplicity said: "When people today hear of Dai Shuluan or Ruan Sizong defying convention and indulging their eccentricities, they call it magnanimity, failing to measure their own capacities. These men were no peers of the truly proud, yet their imitators now cause chaos: some bare their necks and go hatless, some strip and squat like barbarians, some wash their feet in crowds, some relieve themselves before others, some keep guests waiting while they eat alone, or serve wine only to their favorites. Such behavior belongs to those beyond civilization—it is no delight for those within it.

Even Dai and Ruan, with their talent and learning, suffered for their unchecked eccentricities; their gains never outweighed their losses. Had these two lived with restraint, conducting themselves with reverence and caution, engaging others with earnestness and managing affairs with care, where could they not have gone? How much more so for those far beneath them who emulate their ways—they will hasten their own ruin before the sun sets. How could they not be met with disdain, let alone be esteemed for their purity?

Long ago, when Xishi clutched her chest in pain and lay by the roadside, her beauty and fragrance drew all who saw her to admire her and pity her suffering, lingering in concern. Then a neighboring woman, envying her, feigned illness and collapsed by the path. But her ugliness and foul stench repelled all who passed; they scowled, covered their noses, and hurried away. Today, those without Dai and Ruan’s natural disposition who imitate their arrogance are like that ugly woman, blindly measuring herself against a standard beyond her.

Even emperors treated the Three Elders and Five More Elders with the respect due to teachers, for they led by example in reverence. One who lacks propriety invites deep reproach. He who is slow with others will not honor his own kin. If one wishes to be respected, one must first respect oneself. To neglect good deeds is to become evil; to avoid great matters is to remain small. King Zhou’s tyranny earned him the title of ‘lonely man,’ while Confucius, a mere courtier, was called the ‘uncrowned king.’ Thus, the noble man is not defined by wealth or rank.

Now, to engage in conduct that violates propriety yet resent hearing reproaches of swift death is like carrying a pig and hating others for calling it foul, or wallowing in mud and resenting others for calling it filth. Long ago, Xin You saw men with unbound hair performing sacrifices and knew the Rong would soon flourish. I observe that in the time of Huai and Min, arrogance and rudeness were already common, and later the Qiang and Hu invaded and plundered the capital. Only then did people realize these were the omens of earlier misfortune.

Now, as the realm turns toward peace and renewal, how can we not correct past errors and cultivate the beauty of order? One who enters a pack of tigers and wolves only later appreciates the strength of the swift runner; one who lives in a society where propriety has decayed only then understands the steadfastness of the refined. As the Dao declines and the world retreats, worthy scholars and Confucians should grieve together, strike their hearts in shared lament, and correct these ills. If their strength is insufficient, what then? They should at least maintain their integrity like the bamboo and pine, unchanged through the coldest winter. How can they instead rush to join the mediocre, seeking favor with such men? They stray ever farther from the Way—how grievous this is!

Yet some stand firm in their principles, unwavering, not swaying like tumbleweeds to follow the crowd, not altering their elegance to mimic vulgarity. No one can hate them, for all recognize their goodness. But those who expect others to be unlike themselves will despise and reject them. Alas! Is this the way of a declining age? The noble man can remain lofty, upright, and unyielding, unswayed by俗, stirring clear waves to cleanse the murky flow, wielding sharp arrows to pierce the crooked. He may not be accepted in his time, nor gain wealth or rank—but if the heavenly nobility remains within him, he stands alone. Even if he does not succeed, what sorrow or regret could he have?"

Yet how exhausting it is—to fell the root and patch with tiles, to feed on dregs and dig in mud, to sever one’s feet to fit the shoes, to destroy the square to fit the round! The steadfast man cannot prevent others from hating him, yet the Dao cannot be bent. He cannot prevent others from humiliating him, yet his conduct remains his own. He cannot prevent others from casting him out, yet his integrity cannot be altered. Thus, once his lot is fixed and his resolve set, neither encouragement nor discouragement can sway him; joyful acceptance of Heaven’s decree and knowledge of fate keep sorrow and fear at bay. Though hardship and distress may press upon him, he grows only more steadfast; though adversity and misfortune may assail him, he does not regret. One who can truly devote himself thus—how could he ever drift like grass or duckweed, seeking to force a square peg into a round hole? Would he ever do what ritual discards?

The *Baopuzi* states: I have heard that in the late Han, those without conduct ranked and graded one another. The haughty and arrogant, those who refused to submit to the discipline of the Dao, became the leaders and heroes, the "four-cornered and eight-reaching" men. All turned their backs on ritual and teaching, following instead wanton perversity, slandering the upright, and attacking the blameless. Their cliques mouthed foul words and practiced base deeds—all their doings were too shameful to speak of. The ancients spoke of "understanding and penetrating" as grasping the Dao and attaining to humaneness and rightness. How could they have meant understanding and penetrating licentiousness and debauchery? Such men are like Robber Zhi, who claimed to possess the Way of the Five Constants. This vulgar corruption of human judgment is more grievous than the coming of bandits and thieves—yet its damage cannot last long, nor is it merely a matter of ruining one’s robes.

As for the scions of noble houses and those in office, who scorn the laws and appear bareheaded, stripped to the waist, and squatting before guests—having disgraced their heavenly offices, they corrupt the common folk and the young. Some of these men may possess pure and upright natures, or they may be frivolous and vain in reputation, yet they personally engage in such conduct. The common people then take this as the finest way to establish oneself in the world. Those who uphold ritual restraints find it bitter and difficult, and such men are often poor and lowly. Those who indulge in arrogance and abandon find it pleasant and easy, and such men are often eminent and successful. Thus, the vulgar all abandon the former and flock to the latter.

In the world, some who in youth lacked pure and unsullied conduct later gain wealth and rank through buying office, or perhaps their knowledge suffices to adorn themselves, and their cliques suffice to promote one another. The unprincipled then point to them as proof: "Those who indulge their passions suffer no hindrance to their brilliance, while those who bind themselves to the Way cannot escape poverty and lowliness." Yet they do not know that those who rise to glory do so by luck, and those who suddenly fall do so by misfortune—neither outcome stems from their conduct.

As for these "four-cornered and eight-reaching" men, they cherish those who attach themselves to them. For such followers, putting on shoes is too much—tying their belts is too slow. They enter hand in hand, sleeves linked, ascending the hall and entering the inner chambers. When they go out, their carriages press close; when they guard a pass, they charge straight through. Those who bestow favors receive much; those who make requests are always heard. Their desires are invariably fulfilled; their words are always indulged. When they are in trouble, they are rescued. When they recommend someone, even a lame donkey is priced like a dragon steed. When they slander someone, even a filial son is spoken of as if he were Shang Chen. Thus, the rush of petty men toward them is like breaching a dam a myriad blades high to release pent-up waters, or setting a raging fire to the withered grasses of Yunmeng Marsh.

Desire and ambition, solemn and harmonious, flourish in orderly succession—yet this is like scorching ice to make it burn or piling dry ashes to kindle a blaze.

**Chapter Twenty-Eight: The Hundred Li**

The Master of Embracing Simplicity said: The Three Ducal Ministers and the Nine Chamberlains sit and discourse on the Dao, while provincial governors and prefects grasp the net’s headrope and lift its mesh. The higher their office, the graver their cares and the more onerous their duties. Of these, none is weightier than the hundred-li magistracy. For it is here that the clamor of exactions and demands converges. If the governor or prefect is worthy but his magistrates and district chiefs are unequal to their tasks, the affairs of state will not be set in order, and the ten thousand mechanisms of government will fall into disarray. The damage wrought will not be confined to a single jurisdiction.

Magistrates and district chiefs ought to be men of the highest caliber—yet they are often inferior to officials of the central ministries. The reason for this is no mystery: it lies in the failure to act with perfect impartiality and the persistence of private favoritism. Some are the sons or younger brothers of the powerful, appointed on the strength of their family’s reputation; others are mediocrities advanced through the intercession of influential patrons. Some are chosen because of long-standing personal ties, others because of kinship or other connections. Though their unfitness is known, they are still appointed. Occasionally, such men may prove competent, but they are far from universally so. The majority are unworthy.
