Few are those who strive to prove their worth, diligently adhering to frugality and integrity, laboring day and night in the public service to earn the people’s praise, fearing that their achievements may fall short, ashamed that their patrons’ judgment may have erred. The common run of men, narrow in capacity and limited in aspiration, are driven by greed, seeking only to enrich themselves. They indulge their desires without restraint, commanding with a mere glance or gesture. Local officials, knowing they can rely on their superiors’ support, dare not impeach or censure them. Instead, they fear making enemies and thus suffer their rapacity and cruelty in silence.

How, then, can the common people avoid suffering and rebellion? When rebellion spreads, they have no choice but to band together as outlaws. A towering hall may be shaken by a breeze no thicker than a hair; a thousand-foot dike may collapse because of a single ant-hole. How can such dangers not be guarded against with the utmost vigilance? How can they not be eradicated at their source?

Yet those who wield the scales of office often weigh candidates by their own whims, while those who hold the axe of authority bend the measuring line to favor their own. Since they do not select men for office on the basis of merit, and since those seeking office do not consider their own fitness, governance decays and order unravels. Some officials are muddled, arrogant, and extravagant, oppressing the people; others are harsh, cruel, and tyrannical, provoking resentment and rebellion. Some are dull, withdrawn, and indolent, leaving affairs in chaos; others are careless and negligent, allowing institutions to crumble. Some delight in launching grandiose projects, exhausting the people’s strength; others harbor fugitives and bullies, terrorizing the populace. Some, ignorant of the law, are deceived and obstructed; others, addicted to music, wine, and sensual pleasures, neglect their duties. Some waste their days at weiqi and gambling, abandoning their responsibilities; others hunt and feast, forgetting the people’s affairs. Some, failing to review petitions and lawsuits, throw the judicial system into disarray.

When the people can endure no more, they rise as bandits and rebels. Once the outbreak is reported and the culprits are bound in the thornwood stocks, the ruler’s wisdom is impugned, the penal codes multiply, and the people’s suffering deepens. Appointing the wrong man is like harnessing a wooden horse with ornate trappings—how can it gallop to keep pace with the wind? Or like yoking a broken-down nag to a dragon’s chariot—how can it shine with radiance in the celestial thoroughfares?

If those who hold the reins of state and dispense the sovereign’s commands would but scrutinize the glances of a Liang Yue, ensuring that no lame or inferior steed is mistakenly placed among the fine steeds, and if those who lack self-awareness would fear stumbling mid-journey and thus refuse to harness a broken-down nag to the phoenix’s yoke, what cause would there be to worry that the myriad affairs of state would not flourish? What concern that the four frontiers would not be pacified? The Three Sovereigns would not be so far beyond reach, nor the Five Emperors so difficult to emulate.

**Chapter Twenty-Nine: Drawing Close to the Obscure**

The Master of Embracing Simplicity said: When a man of outstanding talent encounters a ruler of great discernment, their bond is as firm as metal and stone before the mulberry and catalpa have even begun to wither. When a man of superior ability meets one of profound insight, their mutual understanding is assured without need of trial. Soaring like a swan borne on a mighty wind, gliding like a dragon ascending through startled clouds—how could such a man be neglected because of lowly station?

If obscurity and neglect could be dismissed with indifference, then Jiang would not have served the Zhou. If remoteness and humble birth could be justly spurned, then Mao Sheng would not have been honored in Zhao. If long-cultivated conduct alone determined advancement, then Ning Qi would not have risen to prominence in Qi. If high repute and established name alone secured office, then Chen Ping and Han Xin would not have been employed by the Han.

The wise man looks to the great and overlooks the small; he is neither envious nor grasping. How, then, could he be so petty as to weigh firewood before counting the grains of rice to be cooked? Or discard a jade because of a blemish, or pluck out hairs to search for a mole?

**Chapter Thirty: Weighing the Ages**

Some say: The authors of ancient books were men of great talent and profound thought, and so their writings are abstruse and difficult to understand. Modern writers, by contrast, are shallow in thought and limited in ability, and so their works are plain and easy to grasp. Compared to the obscurity of the ancients, their clarity is like a ditch beside the Yangtze and Yellow Rivers, or an anthill beside Mount Song and Mount Tai.

Thus, waters that do not issue from Mount Kun cannot swell into mighty currents to flow eastward; books that do not come from men of genius cannot achieve the far-reaching elegance of true literary rhythm.

The Master of Embracing Simplicity replied: One who is versed only in trivial matters cannot be questioned about the boundless expanse of the nine heavens; one who is confined by narrow conventions cannot be expected to grasp the unique insights of the solitary sage. The men of old were neither spirits nor immortals. Though their physical forms may have been refined in the crucible of time, their spirits live on in the present, and their emotions are revealed in their words. The meaning of their writings can still be discerned.

Moreover, the obscurity of ancient books does not necessarily mean that the ancients deliberately sought to make them difficult to understand. Some of the difficulty arises from changes in language over time, or from differences in regional speech. Others stem from the ravages of war and chaos, the long burial of texts, the decay of bamboo and silk, and the loss of many works. Some texts are fragmentary or incomplete; others have lost entire passages or sentences. Thus, they seem abstruse and profound, though they may not be so in reality.

Furthermore, the *Book of Documents* is a compendium of government affairs, yet it cannot compare in refinement, richness, and splendor to the elegant edicts, military dispatches, and memorials of recent times. The *Book of Songs* is a collection of ornate verse, yet it does not match the vast and resplendent grandeur of the *Shanglin Fu*, *Winged Hunters*, *Two Capitals Rhapsody*, and *Three Capitals Rhapsody*. If this is so, how can the philosophical works of the ancients surpass those of modern authors?

Yet the clinging adherents of antiquity, blind and deaf to all else, insist that the ancients’ works are divine and modern writings shallow. They value what is distant and despise what is near—a prejudice of long standing. Thus, a new sword gains value through fraudulent engravings, and a worn-out prescription is treasured because of a forged title. So too, though ancient books are plain and unadorned, vulgar scholars declare them to have fallen from heaven.

Though modern writings gleam like gold and jade, the common crowd treats them as mere tiles and pebbles. Yet ancient books, though numerous, are not necessarily all excellent—they should be regarded as mountains and abysses for scholars, where those who wield the brush may gather timber, hunt, and fish within them. However, just as the forests of Dong’ou and the groves of Changzhou, though abundant with catalpa and elm, cannot be called the grand splendor of towering halls or the resplendent beauty of ornate chambers; and just as the marshes of Yunmeng and the marshes of Mengzhu, though teeming with fish and game, cannot be deemed the lavish feast of simmering broths or the exquisite flavors of fine delicacies—so too do the ancient *Book of Documents* and *Book of Songs* contain principles of meaning, yet they are difficult to surpass in combined excellence.

Consider scholars: all possess virtue and conduct, yet one may excel in literary arts—this cannot be reduced to a single standard. Or take women: all are of peerless beauty, yet one alone masters a hundred skills—this cannot be conflated as identical. If we compare them: in praising palaces, how do the eulogies of Xisi’s *Luqin* compare to Wang Sheng’s *Rhapsody on the Lingguang*? In depicting hunts, how do the poems *Shutian* and *Luling* compare to Sima Xiangru’s *Rhapsody on the Shanglin*? In extolling sacrifices, how do the hymns *Qingmiao* and *Yunhan* compare to Guo Pu’s florid *Southern Suburban Sacrifice*? In recounting campaigns, how do the compositions *Chuche* and *Liuyue* compare to Chen Lin’s mighty *Martial Army*? From these examples, the truth becomes clear.

In recent times, Xiahou Zhan and Pan Anren both composed *Elegies on the Departed*, such as *White Blossoms*, *Southern Reeds*, and *Luxuriant Millet*. All eminent scholars and refined talents who appreciate literature agree that the *Three Hundred Poems* of old cannot match the works of these two masters. Moreover, the ancients conducted all affairs with unadorned simplicity, while today, nothing escapes embellishment—such is the natural course of changing times and shifting eras.

Fine brocade, though lustrous, is no less durable than a straw raincoat; a painted carriage, though elegant, is no less sturdy than a crude cart. Writing is akin to speech: if, in conversation, one speaks as if separated by the vast distance of Hu and Yue, never to be understood, how can such words instruct or admonish others? If clarity and ease are the measure of eloquence, why should books prize obscurity? Just as boats and carriages replaced wading and walking, and brush and ink supplanted knotted cords, later works that surpass earlier ones in excellence are too numerous to enumerate. The world delights in such progress—why then should literature alone be deemed inferior to the ancients?

**Chapter 31: On Reducing Superfluity**

The Master of Embracing Simplicity said: To pacify superiors and govern the people, nothing surpasses ritual. It weaves the principles of human relations into a comprehensive whole, indeed a meticulous system. Yet why must the rites of capping, marriage, feasting, and archery be so excessively intricate? Though human relationships prize order above all, it suffices to establish distinctions of rank and express reverence—why insist on the endless complexities of ascending and descending, bowing and yielding, kneeling and prostrating?

In former times, when the realm was at peace and the four quarters untroubled, men of antiquity might occasionally practice such rites. Yet officials would spend months lecturing and testing, enforcing discipline with whips and rods, compelling day and night study at the cost of sleep and meals. After long training, they would be examined for a single day: scroll in hand, they performed the rites, following the text in every gesture. Errors brought demotion or punishment, yet still they faltered, failing to grasp the essence. To make such rites the constant practice of the people would be nearly impossible—just as Mozi said, "Even after generations of study, one cannot master them; in a single year, one cannot perform them correctly."

The ancients sought counsel from woodcutters and gleaners, heeded the songs of children and the words of madmen, yet still exercised discernment. Mozi’s critiques cannot be entirely dismissed, but his emphasis on penal laws and shortcuts, while addressing practical matters, fails to fully expound the kingly Way. Yet his objections to lavish burials and excessive mourning rites are not without merit.

Since the Jian’an era, the Wei dynasty’s regulations for funerals and mourning have emphasized frugality—here, Mozi’s principles find application. I believe that now, with the chaos of war settled and the realm at peace, the ruler should commission scholars of profound learning and broad knowledge, men capable of adapting and refining without being shackled by foolish conservatism, to revise the *Three Ritual Classics*. They should excise what is unnecessary, trace the origins and evolution of rites, consolidate their essentials, and compile them into a coherent system. Where redundancies, obscure phrases, or divergent expressions convey the same meaning, retain only what is practical and discard what cannot be regularly observed without loss. In the end, the rites should be concise and clear, free from obscurity or stagnation.

For ceremonial vessels—plates, stands, goblets, and chalices—as well as attire, carriages, banners, colors, palace architecture, distinctions of rank, court audiences, guest-host protocols, funeral and burial rites, suburban sacrifices, ancestral temple ceremonies, and rituals for the altars of soil and grain and the mountains and rivers—all may be simplified to ensure frugality. For frugality eases compliance, reduces expenditure, and minimizes errors in practice. Reduced costs spare the people from harsh levies, while simplicity prevents officials from making mistakes.

The gestures of bowing, yielding, ascending, and descending should suffice to convey meaning without descending into petty details. Each category should be distinct yet easy to reference. The present *Five Rituals* are a tangled mess of overlapping, contradictory, and redundant elements, their branches and leaves scattered, their repetitions and cross-references multiplying. Even diligent scholars struggle to navigate them, their doubts and objections growing endlessly, their differences unresolved. With each passing year, the confusion deepens, and none but the most discerning avoid bewilderment, hesitation at the crossroads, or exhaustion in the thicket of doubts. They toil and strain, comparing and collating, yet often spend years without resolution, their efforts leaving no room for doubt, their groans and sighs echoing as they dissect and analyze. Their hair turns white in pursuit of such refinements, their days wasted, other pursuits abandoned. The young grow weary and disheartened—truly, the harm is endless.

The commentaries and exegeses sprawl beyond the original texts. If we were to break them apart, merge them with common practices, organize them by category, and prune the excess, preserving only the essential framework and clarifying the precepts, the result would shine as brightly as the sun and moon, as vivid as the five fixed colors. Scholars would find their labor reduced a thousandfold, and the disputes of pedantic Confucians would cease. Future generations, looking back, would praise our age as we now praise the Zhou.

This is no different from abandoning stone-boiling or blood sacrifices—there is nothing to fear in such change, no regret in discarding the old, no reluctance like that of pushing a cart or leaving a nest. Yet those who cling to convention, upon hearing these words, will surely stare in shock, calling me a madman.

The Three Kings did not follow the same music, nor did the Five Emperors inherit the same rites, yet all succeeded in transforming customs, pacifying superiors, and governing the people. Whether they reformed or preserved, added or subtracted, all achieved the same good ends. Why must one ride a boat to climb a mountain, a horse to cross a river, wear armor in the ancestral temple, or don heavy furs in the height of summer?

If ancient practices must never change, then coffins should not replace firewood, and burial garments should not replace nakedness.

---

**Chapter 32: On Valuing the Broad**

The Master of Embracing Simplicity said: The orthodox classics are the deep seas of the Dao and virtue; the subsidiary texts are the rivers that deepen them. Look up, and they are the bright stars that aid the three luminaries; look down, and they are the thickets that embellish the towering peaks. Though their paths differ, all lead to moral advancement; though difficult to embark upon, all converge in cultivating transformation. Thus, the learned trace the roots to encompass the branches, grasp the guiding principles to attain the ends.

The ancients lamented the scarcity of talent, saying that worthy successors appear but once in a hundred generations. They did not discard the night-shining gem because it was not from Kunlun, nor did they reject instructive words because they did not come from sages. Thus, even the clumsy poems of village alleys or the crude oaths of military camps—though their language was coarse and their analogies vulgar, though they filled no more than ten slips—were still recorded and arranged. The minor sayings of the Hundred Schools align with the classics, just as different vessels, though varied, all serve to extinguish fires, and different medical techniques, though distinct, all cure illness.

Since the Han and Wei dynasties, the profusion of writings has grown ever greater. Though their meanings plumb the depths of the mysterious abyss and their words surge like waves, their influence can summon auspicious omens from the heavens, evoke blessings from the earth, call ring-necked pheasants from beyond the wilds, and secure the realm within the four seas. Near at hand, they quell the roots of disaster; far off, they bequeath blessings for ages to come. Yet the age lacks sages to assess and order them, so they cannot gallop like fine steeds across a thousand *li* or be appended to the *Three Canons* of antiquity.

The narrow-minded, shackled by their shallow learning, remain trapped in the trivia of philology, dismissing the extraordinary as worthless. Some call them "minor arts" unworthy of study; others claim that breadth confuses the mind. Yet they fail to see that a mountain is made of grains of dust, that a hundred or a thousand can sum to millions or billions. Many colors combine to form the splendor of imperial robes; many sounds blend into the harmony of the *Shao* and *Hu* music.

Some prize the shallow, trivial verses of poetry and rhapsodies while scorning the profound, rich, and expansive subsidiary texts. They dismiss incisive and essential words as dull and clumsy, and praise flashy, trivial eloquence as refined and skillful. Truth and falsehood are inverted, jade and stone mixed together—like equating grand music with rustic tunes, or dragon-patterned brocade with coarse hemp. Alas, how lamentable and regrettable that all drift along in this delusion!

Some argue: "Though writings may be abundant, they merely polish words and dazzle with ornament—what bearing do they have on gain or loss? Far better is the silent instruction of virtue and conduct. Thus, Yan and Min stand foremost, while Fang and Xia rank next in the fourfold classification. Learning is the root, conduct the branch. Then is not literary composition a mere secondary pursuit? Yet you, sir, do not exalt its source but prize only its flow—is this right?"

The Master Embracing Simplicity replies: "Virtue and conduct are matters of action, their excellence or inferiority easy to discern; literary composition is subtle in form, its essence difficult to grasp. What is easy to see is coarse; what is difficult to know is refined. Precisely because it is coarse, its assessment has fixed standards; precisely because it is refined, its appraisal resists uniformity. Is it not fitting, then, to set aside the easily seen coarse and discuss the hard-to-know refined?"

Another objects: "Virtue and conduct are the root; literature is the branch. Thus, in the sequence of the four categories, writing does not occupy the highest place. Then are not all writings but the dregs of secondary matters, like straw dogs after sacrifice, their high or low rank plain to see? Might I hear of the essential nature of literature?"

The Master Embracing Simplicity replies: "One may discard the fish-trap once the fish is caught, but until the Dao is realized, one cannot do without the trap. One may abandon writing once the Way is practiced, but until the Way is enacted, one cannot do without writing. Consider the vast or constrained scope of brushstrokes and tonal patterns, the density or sparsity of phrasing and allusions, the length or brevity of sources and ultimate attainment, the depth or shallowness of implicit borrowing and explicit citation—how immeasurably distant they are! Even the space beyond the heavens or the tip of a hair cannot convey their remoteness. How they vie with one another! Even the radiance of the Three Luminaries cannot compare to their magnitude or minuteness. The sharpness of Dragon Abyss swords or the dullness of lead ingots cannot match their keenness or bluntness. The lightness of swan feathers or the weight of piled gold cannot equal their levity or gravity. Clear and turbid, uneven and aligned, each bears its own master; bright and dim, they differ in kind; strong and weak, each follows its own qi. Yet vulgar scholars, seeing only those who can dip brush and compose, lump them all together as one. This is why Bo Ya forever longed for Zhong Ziqi, and why the man of Ying desisted from wielding his axe. For those who carve and chisel may stand shoulder to shoulder, yet Ban and Di alone bear the title of unmatched craftsmen; those who pluck the zither may be legion, yet only Ziqi grasps the difficulty of true attunement. A thousand steeds may fill the stables, yet only Qiji commands a price beyond measure; ten thousand beauties may be counted, yet only Mao Qiang and Xi Shi possess transcendent allure. Truly, there are those who far surpass the multitude.

Moreover, to compare literature with virtue and conduct is like comparing ten feet to one zhang—yet to call it a secondary matter is unheard of. The celestial bodies that hang in the heavens, the praise accorded to Yao and Shun, the resplendent majesty of the noble man, the flourishing brilliance of the dawn, the posthumous titles bestowed upon sages—all from a single word—were these not all matters of wen? The Eight Trigrams gave rise to the adornments of the simurgh; the Six Jia emerged from the burden of the sacred tortoise. Where wen resides, even the lowly become noble—how can the hide of dogs and sheep compare?

Furthermore, the root need not always be weighty, nor the branch invariably slight. Silk brocade relies on plain cloth; pearls and jade reside in oyster and stone. Clouds and rain arise from a handspan; rivers and seas begin with a foot. Thus, though literature ranks after virtue and conduct, it cannot be called a secondary matter."

Another asks: "The deeds of the present age often fall short of antiquity, and its writings and compositions are likewise inferior. Is this the natural principle of a declining qi and fortune?"

The Master Embracing Simplicity replies: "Though the hundred schools' words may arise in succession, they all stem from the thoughts of great scholars and the hands of accomplished talents. Compared to the ancients, they need not all be inferior. Some possess vast, profound, and boundless depths, harmonizing with the ineffable. Their inner sources are unfathomable; their outward flow, inexhaustible. They trace their lineage to the long and expand upon the subtle, their wondrous transformations unbound by the square or circle of fixed rules, their lateral connections unobstructed by the constriction of a single path. Thus, those who favor only sour or salty cannot know their flavor; those whose thoughts are limited cannot grasp their spirit.

The soaring dragon ascends unhurriedly, gazing back as it scales the clouds; the swift steed strides slowly, breathing as it covers a thousand li. Yet the ant marvels at its stairless ascent, and the sluggish nag resents its gradual surpassing. If one gallops through the midst of poetry and discourse, circling within the bounds of commentaries and records, judging the extraordinary with common sentiment, measuring the boundless with a narrow gauge, seeking the utmost refinement with the crudest tools, or probing the profoundest depths with the shallowest wits—even from childhood to hoary age, one will not attain it.

Those who delight in swiftness will praise it as fine, while those who fail to comprehend will condemn it as foul—such is the natural principle. Thus, what they do not understand, they dismiss as empty and absurd, and though they may revere it out of respect, they do not truly believe it, thereby harming others without intent.

Moreover,世俗 generally venerates antiquity and disdains the present. Though there be steeds that chase the wind, they say they do not match the chariots of Zaofu; though there be treasures worth cities, they say they do not equal the tears of the man of Chu; though there be swords that cleave metal, they say they do not rival the forging of Ou Yezi; though there be medicines that revive the dead, they say they do not compare to the compounds of Bian Que and Hua Tuo; though there be men who surpass the multitude, they say they do not match those recorded in bamboo and silk; though there be books that benefit the age, they say they do not equal the writings left by former generations. Thus, Confucius was not esteemed in his own time, and the *Great Mystery* was scorned by his contemporaries.

Vulgar scholars often say: 'Today’s mountains do not match the height of ancient mountains; today’s seas do not match the breadth of ancient seas; today’s sun does not match the brilliance of the ancient sun; today’s moon does not match the clarity of the ancient moon.' How could they ever admit that today’s talents do not fall short of ancient bones? To value what is heard and belittle what is seen is a malady not of this age alone.

In former times, those who smashed lutes and burned texts surely had their reasons. Such is the thirty-third fault of the Han."

Bao Puzi said: Surveying the records of former ages, I find no period where the moral defenses of the Dao and the corruptions of custom were more severe than at the end of the Han. Those who held the reins of power—eunuchs and their ilk—grasped the divine regalia and wielded the state’s authority. They deposed the upright and promoted the perverse, harmed the virtuous and indulged their whims. They whispered in secret, turned their backs on those they hated, feigned deafness to truth, and followed the deluded. Kindred evils banded together, drawing in wicked factions, amassing wealth without limit, yet unwilling to disperse even the smallest coin to relieve the destitute and impoverished.

Promotion in office was unattainable without great wealth; justice in litigation was denied without lavish bribes. Though their power and influence were vast, they could not utter even a single word to advance the most eminent talents of the age. Their appointments never extended beyond the relatives of wives and concubines; their favors never reached beyond the circle of their closest attendants. They heeded not the censure of Zang Wen, nor followed the righteous example of Jie Hu’s impartiality. They distributed emoluments to mimic Wang Lin and pursued affairs like the wandering Fang Hui. Thus, men like Liezi filled the streets, yet none received the succor of Zheng Yang; lofty spirits gathered in numbers, yet none encountered the recommendation of Bao Sheng. Independent minds were suppressed, while those who flattered and attached themselves were advanced. This is why Fan Ju covered his mouth in silence, and Feng Tang sighed without end.

At that time, the prevailing custom was to consume without labor, to conceal one’s virtue, to obscure the worthy, to envy merit and resent it, to slander the pure and white, to shun forthright loyalty and entrap it, to detest those who stood alone and cast them out. The pliant and obsequious were cherished and protected; the upright and unyielding were reviled and abandoned. They nurtured wolves and leopards while slaughtering unicorns and lynxes; they cultivated brambles and thorns while cutting down pepper and cassia.

Thus, those who were proud and unrestrained, rolling like pellets or drifting like duckweed, were called magnanimous and great of spirit. Those who were petty, harsh, and treacherous, harboring venom like scorpions, were called just and upright. Those who were glib, clever, and quick-witted, with sharp tongues and petty eloquence, were called refined and discerning. Those who were abrupt, provocative, arrogant, and reckless were called towering and extraordinary. Those who indulged in wine and women, dissolute and shameless, were called untrammeled and unfeigned. Those who sought without restraint, took without scruple, and seized what was not theirs were called lofty and unconstrained. Those with disheveled hair and coarse garments, who roamed and gathered without propriety, were called broadly learned and universally loving. Those who defied the classics, perverted the sages, and parroted heterodoxy were called disciples of Zhuangzi and Laozi. Those who mocked the refined, ridiculed the elegant, bullied their superiors, and treated them with contempt were called free-spirited and refined. Those who destroyed their integrity to conform, who echoed others like an echo, were called unparalleled talents. Those who relied on the powerful, hoarded wealth, and monopolized goods were called masters of adaptability. Those who shirked paperwork and sought empty reputations were called men of great enterprise and lofty ambition. Those who depended on powerful relatives and held positions beyond their ability were called heroic peers. Those who bribed influential families to purchase titles and ranks were called generous and noble. Those who formed cliques for mutual praise, whose deeds contradicted their words, were called cultured friends. Those who practiced heterodox arts and sorcery, invoking ghosts and spirits, were called spiritually attuned sages. Those who practiced divination, deceitfully predicting fortune and misfortune, were called seers of past and future.

Those who excelled in horsemanship and spearplay, possessing the bravery of a single warrior, were called generals of strategic brilliance. Those who abandoned the Dao and virtue, merely echoing the vulgar, were called heroic talents and great scholars. As for those of steadfast integrity and lofty conduct, whose spirits were pure and dispositions vast, who did not ingratiate themselves with smiles nor bend their words to deceive, who bore frost and trod snow without compromising their principles, who upheld the Dao and adhered to rectitude, standing aloof and unyielding—though the wind raged, their branches did not bend; though their bodies were distressed, their resolve did not waver—advancing, they spoke with incisive words and forthright counsel, attacking faults and admonishing shortcomings; retreating, they were sincere and selfless, acting without reservation—these were called dull and benighted.

Yet those who rose early and retired late, who diminished their meals to serve the public, who toiled and exerted themselves in governance, were called petty functionaries and vulgar officials. Thus, the wise and perspicacious concealed their brilliance, feigning obscurity; the noble and outstanding remained silent, pretending foolishness. The lowly and humble soared to choose their trees; the constrained and bound conformed, serving as "court recluses." The wise refused to reveal their secrets; the brave did not display their resolve. Loyal and forthright men found retreat difficult; the wicked and treacherous prevailed. The tide of perversity surged unchecked; false paths spread unobstructed. Thus did the age reach such a pass...

Where superiors were overawed and inferiors neglected, thieves and bandits proliferated. Eunuchs usurped the sovereign’s authority, and the lives of the Three and Nine were held in the hands of mediocre underlings. Loyal and eminent scholars were branded as partisans, imprisoned, executed, or eradicated. The realm groaned in anguish—innocent and blameless men shut their doors only to meet disaster. A wisp of smoke rose from behind the palace screen, and a whirlwind’s conflagration swept through the cosmos. A hairline fissure opened in the skin, and waves surged to the four extremities. The golden fortress was overrun by common brigands; the boiling moat was crossed by a single reed. The keen and sharp melted away at the sight of dust; conscripted soldiers turned their weapons and fled. Flying blades assailed the inner chambers; barbarian robes were looted from the imperial precincts. Millet and sorghum sprouted in the ancestral halls; brambles and weeds flourished upon the jade steps. Cloud-capped towers became dens for foxes and hares; the ceremonial gates of state were trodden into paths for tigers and leopards. The eastern academy was reduced to ashes; the common people were scorched and drowned in the fires of the abyss. The wicked harmed their houses and ruined their states, yet their crimes were inscribed on bamboo and silk. No eulogy came from the upright historian; no virtuous renown was carved in metal or stone. What was the cause? The loss of men.

**Chapter Thirty-Four: The Loss of Wu**
Master Baopu said: The final years of Wu, though separated by eras, suffered the same maladies as before. Having failed in that time, they could not correct themselves in this; though the lessons of chaos and collapse were not distant, they trod the ruts of overturned chariots. Witnessing the scramble for the crossroads, they forgot the disasters that befell them; laughing at the complacency of lice in quietude, they did not perceive that circumstances had changed and calamities were equal. They saw the boats competing to cross sink, yet did not realize that though the paths differed, the drowning was the same.

I was born in the Jin era and did not witness these events, but my teacher, Master Zheng, recounted them in full from personal experience. He often instructed me, saying: The late years of Wu suffered from maladies even more severe. The worthy were not employed; the dregs filled the ranks. The framework of governance collapsed into disorder; laws that could swallow boats leaked everywhere. In tribute and selection, those who offered heavy bribes took precedence; in official appointments, those with powerful factions were favored. Without wealth or influence, one might toil for years without hope. Those of pure virtue and lofty conduct harbored their brilliance in obscurity; those with talent and strength trod the path to the clouds, ascending through office. The ruler was benighted above; ministers deceived below. Without faction, one could not compete; without advancement, one turned against the public good. The customs of betraying the common weal grew ever more severe; the Way of integrity and uprightness was utterly ruined. Thus, the sparrow, startled by the wind, soared to the clouds; the rotting boat, trusting in swift waves, raced like lightning. The phoenix furled its six pinions among thorns; the prow of the yacht stagnated in stagnant waters, its oars unmoved.

The aides who held the reins and the officials who shepherded the people were either relatives of the empress dowager or flatterers. In office, they offered no loyal counsel to correct errors or supply omissions; in retirement, they lacked the competence to hear lawsuits. In empty talk, their mouths spewed frost and snow; in conduct, they clung to filth and dregs. They wove webs of slander and praise, wielding power and fortune in place of farming. Their carriages and robes gleamed so brightly they could be used as mirrors; their mansions were so grand that flocks of crows perched upon them. Their shouts were swifter than thunder; their blessings and curses faster than spirits. Their influence and profit toppled commanderies; their stores and accumulations enriched the public treasury. When they went out, they were attended by the guards of Di and Huang; when they entered, they strolled among the carved beams of jade. Their retinues formed armies; their gates became markets. Oxen and sheep covered the plains and lowlands; ponds and fields spread for a thousand *li*. Yet outwardly, they feigned the frugality of Yu Qingping and Zhongshu, while inwardly, they emulated the vigilance of Tao Kan and Wen Xin. In truth, they were as corrupt as Anchang, Dong, and Deng. Though they might receive guests, they did not extend the courtesy due even to distant relatives. Starving scholars received not a single measure of grain, yet their halls overflowed with gold and jade, their chambers teemed with singing girls and concubines. A thousand merchant ships rotted with grain; ten thousand granaries brimmed with stores. Their gardens and parks rivaled the Shanglin; their mansions usurped the Taiye. Meat and grain were left to rot for dogs and horses; treasures overflowed their vaults.

In their dealings with scholars, they showed no kinship, however distant; in their own indulgence, they exhausted every luxury. Some never opened the books of laws and ordinances yet occupied the seat of the Grand Judge; others did not know where their desks were placed yet held pivotal posts. They were ignorant of the names of the Five Classics yet enjoyed the emoluments of Confucian offices; they could not distinguish the cold and heat of a foot-long ruler yet sat in the halls of composition. Their brushes were not reckless, yet they coveted the glory of debate; they lowered their brows and drooped their wings, yet filled the ranks of impeachment. They could not discern the fine or coarse among men yet were entrusted with the governance of appraisal. Ignorant of the military might of the Three Powers, they suddenly found themselves beneath the canopy of authority. Repeatedly, they suffered the shame of routs, yet as generals, they did not lose the glory of the vanguard. Unable to distinguish the difference between beans and wheat, they were sold the near office of imperial counselor. Men of fish-like substance and dragon-like pattern—seemingly fitting yet fundamentally flawed—rejoiced when placed in water but grieved at the sight of a heron. Though threatened with axes and halberds, enticed with the treasures of a fallen city, they could not spur their leaden blades against rhinoceroses or gallop their lame nags to chase the wind. Not that they did not fear severe punishment, nor that they did not desire fine rewards—it was simply that their talents were unequal to the task. What could be done? To entrust them with the illumination of the hundred offices and the harmonious management of great affairs was like placing ten thousand *jun* upon a crimson boat, or seeking a thousand *zhong* in a single *sheng*. To tether straw dogs and demand the prowess of hawks and magpies, or to leash chickens and ducks and expect the soaring might of eagles—such futility was all too clear.

The lords of Wu did not reflect upon this. They did not add vigilance to their evenings, nor did they employ the flattering and mediocre with heavy responsibilities. The signs of peril and imminent collapse were as evident as the sun and moon, yet they deemed themselves as secure as towering mountains, aspiring to the heights of Yao and Shun. Their eyes were wearied by the dazzle of brocade, yet they did not use this to verify the right and wrong of remonstrance. Silks and grains were squandered on the unworthy, yet they did not use them to relieve the hunger and cold of their soldiers. Their hearts were delighted by flattery, yet they did not use this to contemplate the principles of survival and ruin. They treated the source of rise and fall with levity and neglected the weight of the ancestral temples.

The Master Zheng further spoke of his teacher, Master Zuo, who lived in reclusion on Mount Tianzhu, neither seeking official emolument nor befriending the feudal lords. Yet his heart yearned for great peace and grieved for his homeland. Thus, he sighed deeply beneath his thatched roof and said to his disciples:

"The vermilion talisman lies dormant, the yellow essence stirs anew; the pivotal axis is restored at the Supreme Culmen, the purple canopy turns back to the Quail’s Head. To succeed Heaven and govern all things, to illuminate the eastern lands of Xia—let the benevolent breeze spread beyond the borders, let the profound grace fill the cosmos. Tongues of distant tribes press close at court, their tribute of coarse reeds filling the halls. Vast and towering, it reaches above and below, a realm of universal peace.

Yet this order is easily sustained, yet the Five Constants lie silent, the *Nanfeng* hymn unchanted. Above, the Way of ‘guarding oneself’ is unattained; below, the song of ‘O how prosperous!’ goes unheard. Flying dragons soar but do not alight; deep-dwelling dragons coil but do not leap. The *zouyu* hides in obscurity; the vermilion blossoms sprout but do not bloom. Yin and yang clash in disharmony; cold and heat transgress their seasons. The Seven Governors warn of peril; mountains and valleys shift their places. Thunder rumbles ominously in the month of the dragon’s seclusion; hoarfrost descends with killing force in the season of summer’s glory. The earth’s torch does not shine; sweet springs do not well forth. Ramparts rise on the frontiers; fine grain does not ripen. What else could be the cause?

Truly, it stems from the Four Perils unremoved, the primal and harmonious not promoted. The employed are unworthy; the worthy are unemployed.

Yet the lofty, in their vast forbearance, hold jade within and guard purity of purpose, desireless amid worldly things. Concealing their talents in profound obscurity, they find contentment in obscurity, forsaking the world. Not acting against propriety, not appearing out of season, they endure hardship without distress, poverty without regret. Rejoicing in Heaven’s decree, they merge honor and disgrace, advancing without delight, retreating without sorrow. Such men would rather die in obscurity by their earthen windows—how could they deign to display their priceless treasures to vie with the sale of mere swallow stones?

The Ways of Confucius and Mozi once went unheeded; Meng Ke and Yang Xiong likewise faced adversity. That virtue goes unrewarded in its time is nothing new. The world lacks the keen-eyed Li Zhu to distinguish black from white; the era wants the swift Guan Qing, its fine steeds broken and bent."

Amidst golden coffers lie heaps of worthless gravel, while jades and precious stones are cast aside in ditches and canals. Master Carpenter Shi passes them by in distant oblivion; catalpa and elm go unrecognized. Alas! My time is ill-fated—neither early nor late—soon to witness the land of Wu transformed into Jin’s city walls, its southern folk turned into northern subjects. These words still ring in the ear, and Ge Hong of *Baopuzi* says: “The words of these two gentlemen may serve as a warning for posterity.” Thus I record them here, that future generations may know the fall of Wu was not sent down from Heaven.

If one were to conceal a state’s ills and refuse even the slightest censure, then Dong Hu’s straight brush would be of no value, and Jia Yi would be mocked for his *Faults of Qin*. **Guarding Obscurity, Thirty-Five**

*Baopuzi* says: Among my friends there is a gentleman who dwells in reclusion, admiring the unclaimed land of Qinshui. He chose a secluded plot to build his home, sharpening his skill in the literary arts while neglecting the plow. Year after year, his harvests fail, and hunger shows in his face. Someone once challenged him, saying: “Understanding ritual begins with a full granary; generosity flows from abundance. Prosperity arises from surplus, while frugality stems from want. The *Poet* praises the ten thousand measures of grain, and the *Eight Governances* place agriculture first. Tilling the soil is the foundation of great virtue; paired plowing brings unchanging joy—the dwelling of extraordinary men. Advance, and you may soar with the phoenix; retreat, and you may join the ranks of Tao and Bo. In office, you would be a Boyi; at home, a Qianjin. Thus the ancients divided fertile fields to share their bounty, toiled with their limbs to sow and reap, scattering beans and millet in lush abundance, nurturing vegetables in orderly rows. They filled a thousand granaries with wheat and black millet, amassed countless bushels in their storehouses. They rode forth in chariots for the hunt, returned in court robes to feast on jade-like viands. Yet this gentleman’s dwelling fares otherwise: in scorching drought, the grain flies away as dust; in heavy rains, the land is drowned. Hills and fields bear no sprouting shoots; waters yield no ripened stalks. Weeds and chaff fill the granary, firewood rots in the kitchen. Still, he clings to his resolve, awaiting the hare at the snare, untroubled by thoughts of advance or retreat.

I fear the fate of Boyi will soon befall him, that the hardship of Danshan will befall him in an instant. Others may grow cold at the thought—why does he remain so unconcerned? He who sees the opportunity yet does not act cannot be called wise. To cling to one’s land without change is the way of the common crowd. How could one who delights in the stench of rotting fish forget the orchid’s fragrance? How could one lost in confusion so easily alter his nature? Why has this gentleman remained so long unaware?” The rustic is perplexed.

He does not recognize what is meant: the court robes and crowns are not garments to shield against frost and arrows; the classics and canons are not tools to relieve hunger and cold. Why not turn his gaze to the fertile plains of the four suburbs, take up the good work of the field and plow, set aside the impractical Six Arts, and gather a myriad granaries to trade for what is lacking?

The Reclusive Master replied: “One deaf cannot be urged to distinguish refined music from vulgar tunes; one blind cannot be held to tell cinnabar from lacquer. The frog in the well cannot be spoken to of the vast ocean; the common crowd cannot be instructed in the classics. If you, sir, know only the petty achievements of the old farmer yet fail to grasp the colossal folly of one who faces a wall—what difference is there between gathering grains of sand while discarding a priceless pearl, or turning toward a flickering candle while turning one’s back on the bright sun?

Tastes and preferences cannot be measured by a single standard; paths and pursuits cannot be exchanged between self and other. He who aspires to ascend the windswept heights of Langfeng or scale the peaks of Song and Hua will not linger on a molehill. He who intends to cross the Southern Sea and sail the vast ocean—how could he idle in a stagnant pool? Thus, one who lends a clear ear to the *Nine Shao* finds the clamor of the village unable to please his ear; one who feasts on the grandest meats and spreads a banquet of a hundred dishes finds the bitter herbs and smartweed unable to sweeten his palate.

The roc soars to the crimson clouds in lofty flight; the wren flaunts its wings in the humble thicket. Though their paths through marsh and land differ, each attains the utmost in flight. The moon’s phases pass swiftly—how fleeting the life of the morning mushroom, which knows not the mayfly! In an instant, the wild horses of summer exhaust their breath; the minnows and bream dart about with exposed scales, while the sacred dragon remains unused in the depths. Unfathomable are the ways of action, divergent their intentions—how can one grasp them?

Though my coarse fare may not fill my belly, I am content with a life beyond the need for grand feasts. Thus, Liezi did not covet the salary of Zheng Yang despite his want; Zeng Shen did not trade his poverty for the riches of Jin or Chu. To preserve subtle teachings on the verge of extinction was the distant legacy of Zhou and Kong. To be zealous in the pursuit of profit is to be a criminal in the eyes of Master Meng.

One who seeks the distant does not tarry at crossroads; one who acts does not attempt to master both plowing and learning. To toil in body while at ease in spirit—what harm is there in living simply? Yet if one dwells in fertile lands and grows idle, idleness leads to neglect of learning, and neglect breeds extravagance. The pure find fortune gathered to them; the extravagant find calamity rushing toward them. When fortune gathers, even the humble may grow extravagant, and even the declining may rise. When calamity rushes in, even the strong may weaken, and even the living may perish. This comes unbidden and arrives unsummoned.

Thus, the noble man, wishing to correct what is amiss, must first rectify its root. If he dams the flow, he must first block the source. When the work of Dao and virtue is established, the gates of extravagance and waste are shut. Jiang Wang attained such virtue that his fields no longer needed sowing; Chong Hua, the great sage, fished without casting nets. Then did the jade tablet proclaim the blessings of Yingqiu, and the great merit stood twenty *zhang* high—why then mock idleness or scrutinize talent as if appraising a minister?

Those two divided their wealth, taking the lesser share as sufficient; I now yield the world’s abundance, dwelling in this barren land of hardship and stone, forsaking the rich fields of Anchang for the desireless outskirts of the northern wall. If even the myriad things may be deemed small, what could ever be lacking? The North Star is revered among the stars for its unchanging position; the Five Peaks are honored among the mountains for their immovability. The cricket, though it moves often, is not prized; birds and fish, when sated in the depths, meet with disaster.

I shall now clear the overgrowth of the Nine Classics and sow the fine seeds of the Six Virtues. Though my fields lie far from the highest grade of soil, will not their harvest be abundant? Why must one toil at plowing and sowing between heaven and earth?

Of old, Xu You discarded his wealth to halt theft; the Yu family shattered their jade to curb greed. Shu Guang scattered his gold to spare his descendants from disaster; Sun Ao chose poverty to dispel the anxieties of desire. Niu Que, laden with treasures, invited bandits; Tao Kan, with his hoarded wealth, summoned misfortune. The contrast between gain and loss is clear—can one afford not to take heed?”

At this, the questioner fell silent for a long while, his mouth agape yet unable to speak, his head bowed yet unable to lift it. He sighed deeply and said: “At first, I sought to establish words of immortality, not to be drowned in the pursuit of property. I chased the achievements of the lowered curtain, not to let my gaze wander to the garden. You, sir, with your fondness for the tender chick and your schemes for pheasant and crab—do you not find this base? How vulgar! That you did not know this sooner!”

**Chapter Thirty-Six: Embracing Poverty**

The *Baopuzi* said:

In the late Han, the flame of the dynasty dimmed, dragons warred and tigers contended, the Nine Lands split asunder, and the Three Families seized the tripods. There was a man called Master Le Tian who fled turmoil, drifting from place to place, wandering through Min and Yi. At first, he dwelt in intimacy with Wen Xiu, yet remained unknown to Kongming. His words were lofty, his conduct square, standing alone and apart from the crowd. The people of his time held him in awe, and none dared approach him. Though the power of the two lords of the age could not be defied, they allowed this scholar to languish in obscurity. His teeth aged like the mulberry and elm, yet he did not alter his coarse cloth. The ruler of the time longed for worthy men, but those in power neither recommended nor promoted him. He hid in the humble lanes of Wu, concealed in a thatched hut by the pivot of the gate. He forsook the affairs of governing the world and neglected the business of sustaining life. His meals of wild greens were often empty; he did not plan for the evening by morning.

Thereupon, a young man of conventional society visited and questioned him, saying: “I have heard that one with the talent of Yi or Lü does not long remain mired in poverty and lowliness, nor does one possessing the methods of Yi Dun endure hunger and cold for long. The wise value knowing when to change; the intelligent test their worth by not clinging to the past. When Fan Li emerged, he aided Wu and saved Yue, becoming a counselor for the age; when he withdrew, he engaged in trade and amassed wealth of ten thousand gold. Poverty is among the six misfortunes; wealth is among the five blessings. The *Book of Songs* praises the fortunate; the *Book of Changes* values gathering people. Cast fragrant bait, and sturgeon and salmon come; offer a rich reward, and the resolute are roused. Sima Xiangru shed his apron and held the vermilion tassel; Chen Ping left his humble door and enjoyed the fief of Maotu. Lü Buwei was thus fed by a domain of ten thousand households; the Marquis of Jiang was thus delivered from prison’s confinement. Thus, the lowly minister’s frugality brought regret and disgrace, while the drifting old woman’s generosity earned a thousand gold in return.

You, sir, possess no extraordinary strategies like Xiao Bo, yet you focus your keen thoughts solely on the Six Classics. You ignore the real disaster of starvation, yearning instead for the empty fame of immortality. You are ashamed to seek office through devious means, disdain to peddle your talents for glory, hope for the stratagems of King Wen, and await the clearing of the Yellow River. You willingly endure the emaciation of Liezi, aspire to preserve your spirit while neglecting your conduct—how is this different from painting a fine steed to replace the toil of walking, or pointing to the sea to quench a parched mouth? Casting a fishing net upon a towering peak or dangling a hook from the tip of a tall tree, though you may deem it right, is this not still impractical and absurd?

Now, within the seas, the realm is divided, heroes vie in strength, and the cunning of the age are exiled like Xia. The swift and the slow race along the same path; jackals and wolves bare their fangs in mutual strife. Those in power cast nets to ensnare the upright; scholars of purity are trampled underfoot on the path. Pure scholars abandon the Dao of emperors to practice the arts of the Five Hegemons; great minds forsake the Four Disciplines to heed the monthly critiques. Those with full baskets advance from the wilds; those lacking resources retreat from the court. Those who grasp alchemy push through the golden gates to ascend the jade halls; those who recite esoteric texts make lifelong enemies and sink into the mire. Those with lavish gifts turn tiles and stones into jade; those with meager offerings discard dragons and fine steeds like weeds. Those with many allies soar with the whirlwind; those with few companions swim with the lame turtle. A pellet of mud cannot dam the boiling of Pengli, yet how can a lone worthy reverse the current and avoid the error of fleeing the world?

Now, sir, you have no store of grain at home, no bundle of dried meat to offer abroad. You merely harbor hidden brilliance like the phoenix, conceal patterns like the tiger and leopard, speak like the surging waves, and array your words like brocade. Yet you freeze and starve within crumbling walls—what plan do you have to avoid pity? Why not launch a light boat to seek patronage, ride swift sails to distant lands? Trade rare goods in the north and south, gather gold and jade from Jiuyi, follow the distant footsteps of Jiao Lie, and secure fine office in this clear age. Instead, you exhaust yourself in writing—is there any end to this molting like the cicada? You merely wear yourself out for fame, which even the Yellow Emperor and Laozi would scorn.”

Master Le Tian replied: “When the Six Arts are thoroughly studied and the Eight Compilations fully mastered, this is wealth. When brushes are wielded to compose, and virtuous words are inexhaustible, this is nobility. Seek benevolence, and benevolence arrives—what more is there to desire?”

As for dwelling deep in the abyss to nurture the numinous, dismissing the myriad things to find contentment, casting worldly dust from perilous paths, and clarifying the spirit in profound silence—never gazing through windows for distant vistas, discerning right from wrong without confusion—even if you set before me tripods and thrones, I would not alter my resolve. How could I then fret over poverty and vie with merchants for profit, or grieve over hardship and compete with the vulgar for success?

If you know that trading can amass treasures and tilling can stave off hunger, you must also recognize that the deer-chaser heeds not the hounds, and the distant traveler arrives not late. Moreover, Lü Shang, though but a knife-wielding butcher, later revealed his brilliance. A weight of ten thousand *jun* may be heavy, yet a whirlwind cannot shift it; the *Xiaoshao* melody may not reach its ninth movement, yet the sacred birds do not bow in ceremony. Thus, those who await the ascending winds to soar into the azure skies do not crouch in the tips of mugwort and wormwood; those who gallop with reins of orchid to scale the six directions do not contend for paths with the crowd.

The herd of lame donkeys fears the perils of filial devotion, so Zichun trembled at the thought of his father’s fall. The supremely wise do not prize hard-won talents, so Yao and Shun discarded gold and cast aside jade. The enlightened avert disaster before it arises; the wise, hearing of profit, weigh its harm. Yet you urge me to launch a boat, diligently seeking wealth within gilded halls—encouraging me to sling a Sui pearl to shoot a sparrow, or snatch meat from a tiger’s jaws. You would have me risk my body in the unpredictable, brave grave dangers for distant gains, forgetting the clear warnings of flesh and hair, chasing ruin in peril, hoping for fortune in calamity.

Then there are those who overturn chariots on flaming wheels, topple trees from cliffs, split rocks with flying stones, or stand where mountain peaks loom over surging waves—light boats drift like dust, strength fails the will, and all they can do is weep without achievement, only then awakening to the foresight of the wise.

Of old, Yan Hui was praised for his purity amid hardship; Chen Ping escaped peril by having no gold; Guanghan perished for his love of profit; Niu Que was reduced to ashes for carrying treasure. A common man dies in vain for clinging to jade; Feng Hu summoned disaster with fine furs. Now you would have me wield the craft of teaching thieves, urge me toward the path of inviting bandits, and proffer poisoned wine in toasts—this is no way to nurture life.

Rather, the scholar treats the *Three Rites* as gold and jade, the *Five Canons* as zither and zheng, discourse and study as bells and drums, and the Hundred Schools as reed pipes and flutes. Let those who savor the Dao be sated by its words, and those who intoxicate themselves with virtue be awakened by its principles. Rise above the common stream, tread loftily, surpass the ages, and let your name resound. Gallop alone on the long path—what need have you for the filth of wealth?

For those who hoard much lose abundance; those who favor modesty are envied for their fullness. Concealing a night-shining pearl invites swift destruction; following the tracks of an overturned cart ensures a fall. Overloading a boat sinks it; seeking victory destroys life. These are the concerns of lesser men, not the pursuits of those who embody the highest virtue.

Thereupon, the questioner stood in bewildered silence, lost in thought, and begged to be enrolled as the least of the disciples, forever treasuring the true formula for long life.

*Renming*, Chapter Thirty-Seven

Bao Puzi said: The disciples together debated the precedence of *ren* and *ming*, each presenting their views, and so they consulted me. I told them:

The Three Luminaries suspend their images—this is *qian*. The earth bears all without end—this is *kun*. *Qian* possesses *ren* and also *ming*; *kun* possesses *ren* but lacks *ming*. Is not the distinction between high and low thus vast?

Only the sage aligns his virtue with Heaven. Thus, Tang Yao crowned his canons with "reverence and clarity," while Confucius placed "illuminating *yi*" at the head of his chapters. "Illuminating, illuminating on high"—this is the title of the sovereign. "The perspicacious preserve themselves"—this is the lost trace of the *Greater Odes*.

Even the flying midge and creeping worm possess *ren*, for their intent to cherish and nurture is evident, and their sorrow at chirping distress is manifest. Yet they rush into pits and wells without suspicion, enter snares without awareness—possessing *ren* but lacking *ming*, they hasten together toward calamity.

Had it not been for the blazing brilliance of the hidden sun, jade tablets and capped pillars would have been exchanged for woven mats; had it not been for the framing of halls and roofs, nests and burrows would not have been abandoned. The selection of grains to eliminate poison, the fashioning of garments to replace nakedness, the harnessing of boats and oars to cross impassable waters, the yoking of oxen and horses to rest from bearing burdens, the ordering of ranks and dignities to quell disorder, the forging of tools and weapons to guard against unforeseen threats, the creation of writing to govern the hundred officials, the establishment of rites and laws to refine customs—all these are the works of great *ming*, not the distinctions that partial men can discern.

If the heart does not depart from *ren* yet *ming* does not govern the state, how can the disasters of peril and ruin be averted? This much is clear.

To gauge prosperity and decline before their signs appear, to probe the subtle workings of affairs before their forms take shape, to point out hidden dangers beyond the surface of principle, to resist insidious influences at their root—this is the achievement of *ming*.

To extend compassion to all creatures, though wronged yet not retaliating, to alter the sacrificial victim upon seeing its trembling, to avoid trampling the tender reeds—this is the function of *ren*.

Otherwise, *ming* is but talent, and *ren* is but conduct.

The act of sacrificing oneself to perfect others may be striven for and attained, yet the clarity that fathoms the profound and probes the obscure cannot be falsely assumed. The distinction between refinement and coarseness stands starkly evident.

If one embodies the *ren* that cannot bear suffering yet lacks the *ming* that discerns right from wrong, the heart becomes confounded by truth and falsehood, the spirit disordered by crimson and violet, unable to distinguish grades or recognize good and evil. Failing even to secure one’s own safety, how can one establish a foundation to aid others?

Of old, the Duke of Zhou was not without fraternal love, yet he wept to restrain his kin; Shi Que was not without natural tenderness, yet he severed private ties to serve the public. For they clearly perceived the nature of affairs, did not drown in proximate sentiments, and thus became pure ministers, cutting off affection for the sake of benefit, curbing *ren* with *ming* to temper its use. *Ren* may be temporarily set aside, but *ming* cannot be absent.

The mandate of Heaven was revolutionized by its *ming*.
Xu Yan cultivated *ren* to unify the court, yet his domain cast aside the peril of moats and walls, and within, there were no shields or spears in readiness—ruin and destruction befell his state and house, a calamity born of *ming*’s absence.

The disciples said: Confucius sighed that *ming* and *ren* are burdens heavy and the Way distant. He also said: "If a man lacks *ren*, what can ritual mean to him?" And again: "If I claim sageliness and *ren*, how dare I?" Mencius said: "*Ren* is the dwelling; *yi* is the path." A man without a heart of compassion is not *ren*. The Three Dynasties gained the world through *ren* and lost it through its absence—these are the maxims of sages and worthies, the manifest proofs of bamboo and silk.

Yet you, sir, exalt *ming* without citing canonical evidence. I, a benighted student, remain perplexed.

The Master of Embracing Simplicity replied: The ancients said, "To love *ren* but not study is to be led into folly." You come close to this, my young friend. Once, the six states devoured one another, wolves and tigers vied in strength, cunning and deceit prevailed below, while virtue and morality were abandoned, and killing and aggression displaced yielding and deference. Mencius arose, seeking to suppress the rapacious and brutal, to exalt and elevate *ren* and *yi*—how could he not earnestly and repeatedly extol *ren* alone? Yet never did he utter a single word that *ren* surpasses *ming*.

It is like a time of plague, when physicians and shamans are prized, and all speak with one voice of medicines and stones—could one then claim that the art of acupuncture and moxibustion surpasses the Way of long life and enduring vision?

Moreover, I hold that the matters of *ren* and *ming* are spread across the four directions, aiming only to cut to the essence and illustrate the broad outlines, to grasp the spirit and point to one corner. Yet you still say, "In daily use yet unknown," and claim that *ming* lacks evidence—how so?

Qian declares, "Great brightness begins and ends, completing the six positions in their seasons"—this establishes Heaven to show that nothing is not encompassed. Kun states, "Utmost yielding, the myriad things rely on it for birth"—this is the virtue of Earth, which with *ren* bears and follows alone. Is not the principle of precedence and succession thus luminously clear?

The *Odes* says:
*"Bright, bright is the Highest Heaven,*
*Illumining the lands below.*
*Bright, bright is the Son of Heaven,*
*His illustrious fame unceasing."*

The *Changes* states: *"The king faces south toward brightness and receives its blessing."* The *Appended Judgments* say: *"Obscurely assisting the numinous brightness, numinously brightening it."* Here, brightness and the numinous form a single body—how could pure *ren* alone presume to reach it?

Confucius said: *"Keen of hearing, bright of vision, divine in martiality,"*—he did not say *"keen of ren."* Again he said: *"Of old, the bright kings governed all under Heaven,"*—he did not say *"ren kings."* The *Spring and Autumn Annals* states: *"Bright virtue is most fragrant,"*—it does not say *"ren virtue."* The *Documents* says: *"The sovereign’s head is bright indeed!"*—it does not say *"ren indeed!"*

Laozi sighs: of the highest scholar, he says *"bright-eyed, seeing in all four directions,"*—while of the declining and meager, he says *"losing the Dao, then virtue; losing virtue, then ren."* The *Changes* states: *"The king faces south toward brightness,"*—it does not say *"toward ren."*

*"If I desire ren, ren comes."* And again: *"To practice ren lies with oneself."* Thus, *ren* is something everyone can achieve. But how can one command brightness and keen perception? Hence Mencius says: *"Whenever people see a child about to fall into a well, none fail to rush forward to save it."* From this we see that none lack the heart of *ren*—only in its depth or shallowness does the distinction lie, while the division of brightness and perception arises in due time.

Of old, Cui Zhu did not kill Yan Ying. Though Zhu was greatly un-*ren*, he yet possessed a small *ren*. Thus even treacherous ministers and murderous sons may still have *ren*.

A disciple further says: *"The *Changes* speaks of establishing the human Dao as *ren* and *yi*—thus nothing in humanity surpasses *ren*."*

Baopuzi replies: *"The reason I speak thus is that *ren* lies in action—action can be established, and brightness, joined with the numinous, must be a talent bestowed by Heaven. It is not what can be taught to all."*

**Extensive Analogies, Thirty-Eight**

Baopuzi says: *"A weight of ten thousand jun must begin with a zhu; towering heights that pierce the clouds must begin with a hair’s breadth. Thus trickling streams gather to form the Southern Sea, and span upon span accumulates to reach the boundless expanse. The commonplace piles up to attain the gardens of the immortals, and the utmost heights of Heaven."*

Baopuzi says: *"Those who gallop swiftly may leave their shadows behind yet not grow weary; those who ride the wind and cleave the waves may cross peril yet not capsize. Thus the great ministers divided their duties, and the achievements of emulating Heaven were accomplished; Yi and Lü were entrusted, and the merit of revolution was achieved."*

Baopuzi says: *"Jade boats and coral oars are of no use in crossing rivers; golden bows and jade arrows cannot propel a shot. Thus those who are incorruptible yet lack governance are not instruments for quelling disorder; those who are refined and elegant yet lack administrative strategy are not talents for assisting a ruler."*

Baopuzi says: *"The towering heights of Langfeng and the immortal gardens do not borrow elevation from a mound; the luminous *xuan* and *jielü* jades do not seek display from common stones. Thus the heroic and peerless exude the fragrance of hidden orchids, startling all with their lofty bearing; the resolute and independent evoke the cries of myriad birds, their voices resounding."*

Baopuzi says: *"Ice and charcoal do not boast of their cold and heat; fine jades do not prove their worth by their luster. Thus the noble person, reverent in self-cultivation, does not fret over being unknown; the perfected person, dwelling in stillness, leaves behind praise and blame."*

Baopuzi says: *"A whirlwind may topple mountains yet cannot exert force on an autumn hair; fire may melt metal and stone yet cannot cast its glow upon a tiny stream. Thus Han Xin excelled in warfare yet was clumsy in the strategies of governance; the Marquis of Xiang kept the altars secure yet lacked ready eloquence in response."*

Baopuzi says: *"Those who pursue fame do not find risking their lives difficult; those who value themselves do not let near desires burden their hearts. Thus Ji Xin gladly turned to ashes without regret; Yang Zhu equated a single hair with a city of linked jade."*

Baopuzi says: *"The minnow does not comprehend the distant journey of the divine dragon; the teal and duck know not the mismatch of the swan and crane. Thus those who plow in pairs mock Chen Sheng for casting aside his plow; the shallow-minded ridicule Kongming for clasping his knees."*

Baopuzi says: *"The edge of a pure blade is tested against rhinoceros and buffalo; the excellence of proclaimed kindness is proven through clear trial. Thus when alike in office, the great ministers and petty officials are no different; when employed together, the swiftest steed and the slowest nag are indistinguishable."*

Baopuzi says: *"A vessel not of *hu* or *gui* must advance the sharp and retreat the dull. Though one’s measure be modeled on Yi and Lü, late in starting, one may yet arrive early. Thus the wren, weary of flight, does not surpass the tip of a thistle; the young phoenix, rising slowly, gazes about and cries to the azure vastness."*

Baopuzi says: *"Nine times severed, four times joined—the *wenzao* and *zao* plants thus display their numinosity. Branches broken, leaves crushed—the *chai* and *hui* herbs thus increase their fragrance. Thus Yi Wu, though shackled, built the achievements of rectifying the realm; the Marquis of Ying, though humiliated, left his meritorious deeds in Qin."*

Baopuzi says: *"When what is contended over is trivial, interests align and enmity forms; when good and ill take different paths, affairs diverge and knots arise. Thus the ugly Momu and the hunchbacked woman despised seeing Xi Shi’s lovely face; the young Shang Chen and Xiaobai resented hearing of Yan Zhou’s retired plowing."*

Baopuzi says: *"When acuity and dullness diverge in their tracks, those who lag behind feel shame and regret; when strength and weakness differ in their categories, those who lift tripods are envied. Thus Han Xin was promoted to prominence, and the mediocre grew sullen and resentful, hastening his downfall; Wu’an’s achievements were lofty, and Fan Sui adorned his words to ruin his affairs."*

Baopuzi says: *"A fatal illness will not reject bitter medicine; rotting wood will not accept carving and ornament. Thus Bi Gan, though not self-serving, had his heart cut out for his utter loyalty; Tian Feng, though farsighted, was executed for his forthright words."*

Baopuzi says: *"The solitary tong tree of Yiyang cannot, without a pillar, produce its mournful tones; the solitary bamboo of Great Xia cannot, without being blown, emit its clear notes. Thus when office is lowly, even Hou Ji and Xie cannot bring peace to the common weal; when authority is meager, even Yi and Zhou cannot attain the heights of governance."*

Baopuzi says: *"Those who climb heights are cautioned against the perils of the summit; those who cross deep waters meet disaster when their boats are overloaded. Thus in the west, Qin had Li Si, who rued his rise from Shangcai; in the east, Yue had Wen Zhong, who regretted his fullness of opportunity."*

Baopuzi says: *"Hard and soft possess unchanging natures; integrity and flexibility have their inherent qualities. Thus a hundred smeltings do not diminish the true essence of southern gold; peril and hardship do not make the resolute man lose his rectitude."*

Baopuzi says: *"Without following the Dao, wealth and nobility are not worth dwelling in; forsaking *ren* and abandoning *yi*, even long life to a hundred years is not worth begrudging. Thus Bian Sui cast himself into the river with a stone; Zhong You willingly went to the blade."*

Baopuzi says: *"High and low cannot be leveled as one; fine grain and coarse fodder cannot encourage or discourage alike. Thus Hui Shi fretted over the glory of a thousand chariots; Zhuang Zhou worried over the abundance of ways to catch fish."*

Baopuzi says: *"Withdrawal and engagement differ as ice and charcoal; impatience and calm diverge as flight and sinking. Thus Mo Di found joy in thick calluses; the northern elder found contentment in leaving the world behind."*

Baopuzi says: *"One who suits the heart finds shallow acquaintance yet deep affection; one who offends the spirit finds long association yet sudden estrangement. Thus, those of like voice tip their carriage canopies as if old friends; those of differing ways may share a lifetime yet feel no love."*

Baopuzi says: *"The grand ship with kingfisher prow is a fine vessel for crossing rivers, yet steered by a northern barbarian, it founders in the waves. The swift steed of *puchao* and *hanxue* is a fleet-footed courser, yet driven by one not a Zaofu, it stumbles on perilous paths. The sharp blades of Qingping and Haocao are peerless in keenness, yet wielded by one not of Yu or Yue, they bring self-injury. The keen troops and crack soldiers are divine instruments for quelling chaos, yet employed by one not wise and sagely, they hasten the disaster of self-immolation."*

Baopuzi says: *"Heaven’s order has its unchanging constancy; to violate reverence without ritual is to court swift death as with a sharp blade. Thus, the birds and beasts of the sacred isles, though they may speak, are not admitted among the noble sacrificial beasts; the ants and beetles, though they bear life, are not deemed capable of *ren* and *yi*."*

Baopuzi says: *"Slander cannot be dispelled by clever words, nor can deep resentment be dissolved by empty deeds. To quell strife not by the Dao or resolve it not by principle is like embracing ice to dispel cold, hefting a heavy furnace to repel heat, chasing light to escape shadow, or boring a boat to quench thirst."*

Baopuzi says: *"A wise ruler appoints men to office without exceeding their capacity; a loyal minister occupies his position without overstepping his measure. To grant office beyond one’s talent or burden with tasks beyond one’s strength is like filling an ice bowl with boiling water, wrapping a blazing fire in reed sheaths, fastening a ten-thousand-*jun* weight to a rotten rope, or loading a double burden onto a fragile skiff."*

Baopuzi says: *"The fur of leopard and fox is not for the woodcutter; the nine movements and six transformations of music are not for the deaf. To raise one’s voice in distant harmony is not for the common fool; to speak boldly and act resolutely is not for the man of meager virtue. To compose the *Baopuzi*—"*

Baopuzi says: *"When the people’s wealth is exhausted yet demands do not cease, when their strength is spent yet labor does not pause, to hope that resentment will not arise and long for lasting peace is like severing roots to graft branches, cutting flesh from the back to patch the thigh, gouging out eyes to broaden sight, or slicing off ears to sharpen hearing."*

Baopuzi says: *"Laws have no fixed form yet one admires their timely adaptability; achievements do not surpass the past yet one delights in frequent change to suit the俗. This is like trimming a tall horse to fit a low carriage, shaving the ankles to fit tight shoes, breaking a long sword to fit a short scabbard, or cutting a foot-wide jade disk to fit a narrow box."*

Baopuzi says: *"The great fish with long scales does not emerge from narrow valleys; the lofty trees where phoenixes perch do not flourish on low mounds. The profound maxims of the Nine Categories are not uttered by the mouths of the common and base; the exalted teachings of the Golden Tablets do not arise from the bosoms of ordinary folk. Seeing a branch a hundred arm-spans wide, one knows its root is not slender; beholding the vast and deep patterns of a man, one perceives his depth and breadth."*

Baopuzi says: *"The mulberry groves and dense forests have no cypress or pine to chill the air; the fertile fields near the city walls have no famine relief to dispel hunger. Yet from these come silken cocoons and fine fabrics, filling a thousand granaries and ten thousand storehouses. Thus, those who see far value the root; those who see near pursue the branch."*

Baopuzi says: *"One who grasps the coarse is bound to form; one who knows the refined attains the spirit. One who traces beginnings and sees endings has a thread of inference; one who grasps things before their signs appear has no need to borrow from external causes. Thus, to see heaven and earth by day is not deemed keen; to discern the finest hair at night is called peerless."*

Baopuzi says: *"The fragrant blossoms of spring cannot long leave their branches; the great fish that swallow boats do not abandon the deep waters to preserve their lives. Thus, one whose name is fair yet whose substance does not match will lack enduring reputation; one whose rank is high yet whose capacity does not suffice will not escape the downfall of inviting bandits."*

Baopuzi says: *"To endure the bitter medicine and piercing stone is to remove life-threatening illness; to don the heavy chill of armor is to ward off the rain of arrows. To embrace the constraints of pure conduct is to preserve the integrity of one’s work; to accept the most heart-chafing admonitions is to avoid the confusion of changing course."*

Baopuzi says: *"When phoenixes and simurghs vie for grain in the courtyard, they are loved no more than chickens and ducks; when dragon scales are mixed among the fodder of common beasts, they are defiled among the six sacrificial animals.
