Thus, the elder of Shang perched on the heights to spread his lofty and distant conduct; Bian Sui plunged into the depths to preserve his reputation of leaving the world behind."*

Baopuzi says: *"A deep well not dredged grows muddy and foul; fine grain not weeded becomes choked with tares. To study without reflection is to be beset by doubts; to lecture without precision is to prolong confusion and waste effort."*

Baopuzi says: *"To hoard ten thousand gold in chests and coffers, though one be frugal and never use it, is no different from poverty. To harbor refined thoughts in the heart yet never commit them to brush and silk is no different from the common and base."*

Baopuzi says: *"Nan Wei and Qing Qin, paragons of beauty, must still await splendid adornment to enhance their loveliness; Yan Hui and Zi Xia, though gifted with genius and brilliance, truly required the classics to broaden their wisdom."*

Baopuzi says: *"Red curtains and woven nets, layered and doubled, obscure even the fairest form; vermilion lacquer and intricate decoration, dazzling and bright, conceal even the gnarled wood. Thus, when the Six Arts are mastered, the base are transformed into gentlemen; when praise is widespread, the rustic are drawn near to the noble."*

Baopuzi says: *"In dense forests and tangled thickets, the feathered tribes gather like clouds; in deep and boundless waters, the scaled multitudes vie to arrive. Where virtue and work are vast, those who lodge their hearts are many; where flaws are noted and use is distant, those who draw near are few."*

Baopuzi says: *"To seek the fleetest of flying and swiftest of running creatures yet be unable to gallop freely on the Lü Liang rapids, to command creatures that tread waves and swim the depths yet be unable to scale heights and climb perilous peaks—thus, Li Zhu could split an autumn hair at a hundred paces yet could not distinguish the eight tones’ refinement; Zi Ye could harmonize the rarest of numinous sounds yet could not point out black and white at a foot’s distance."*

Baopuzi says: *"When the four faculties of hearing are widely opened, even Xihe receives the light of myriad fathoms; when the self is emptied, even the trickling streams gather. Thus, the way of broad inquiry is revered, and rare knowledge fills the court; the brilliance of the beacon tower shines, and extraordinary men knock at the gate. The tree for slander is set up, and faults are surely known; the drum for forthright admonition is hung, and straight words are surely offered."*

Baopuzi says: *"No one fails to praise the governance of Yao, yet not all of it was necessarily good; no one fails to condemn the rule of Jie, yet not all of it was necessarily bad. Thus, a single withered branch does not diminish the lushness of the forest; winter wheat does not undo the autumn’s severity. Xi Shi had her flaws, yet they could not lessen her beauty, for her beauty was abundant; Mo Mu had her virtues, yet they could not redeem her ugliness, for her ugliness was profound."*

Baopuzi says: *"Body and reputation are difficult to advance together; merit and spirit are seldom preserved in full. Those whose virtue is fragmented suffer hardship yet find stability; those who use it to navigate the world enjoy ease yet face many dangers. Thus, birds of prey are trapped by their own fierce strikes; birds of eloquence are caged by their own cleverness. Jade is cut for its patterns; the three metals are smelted for their splendor. Orchids and iris are burned for their fragrance; catalpa and nanmu are hewn for their resonance. For this reason, the azure dragon, seeing Boyi, ascends to the dark clouds; the divine bird, encountering Mengxi, returns to the cinnabar cave. Ziyong forever sighs at the greatness of the heavenly bond; Zhuangzi mourns the sacrifice of the patterned ox."*

Baopuzi says: *"A thousand elk turn their antlers, and even fierce tigers must sheathe their fangs; a thousand birds gather in flocks, and birds of prey must clench their talons. Thus, when slander spreads through the four lands, even the Duke of Zhou cannot suppress it; when critics fill the roads, even Zichan has no way to silence them."*

Baopuzi says: *"The beauty of Wei Shi cannot be surpassed by powder and rouge; the qi of the solstices cannot be augmented by breath. Therefore, those who harbor the measure of hidden excellence do not flaunt their bearing to display distinction; those who embody the vessel of transcendent rarity do not trouble themselves with petty praise to seek commonality."*

Baopuzi says: *"The qilin halts at the phoenix’s perch—what it fears is scarcity; the fox howls and the owl shrieks—what the world loathes is their abundance. Thus, when men of talent fill the court, those who seek worthies never tire; when slanderers wield power, the loyal and steadfast gnash their teeth."*

Baopuzi says: *"Great strength is not limited to Meng Ben and Wu Huo; graceful beauty is not confined to Zheng Dan and Mao Qiang. Swiftness is not the sole domain of Hua Liu and Su Shuang; decisive judgment is not the monopoly of Zhan Lu and Gan Jiang. Therefore, those who establish the enterprise of the Uncrowned King need not be the Qiu of Eastern Lu; those who practice the benevolence of healing withered bones need not be the Chang of the Western Neighborhood."*

Baopuzi says: *"The divine bird’s cry resounds in the morning sun—though it brings no benefit to creatures, none below fail to listen with rapt attention. The owl gathers at night on the eaves—though it causes not a hair’s harm, none fail to cover their ears and cast blame. Thus, good words travel far without fail to please; evil speech arrives near without fail to offend—like the sun and moon, whose steadfast brightness admits no decline, or the crooked arrow, which is despised the moment it appears."*

Baopuzi says: *"Shadows do not deviate from their forms; names do not stray from their realities. Thus, water without a source cannot stem the long flow that spreads eastward; untimely blossoms cannot endure the radiance of frost and ice."*

Baopuzi says: *"Beasts with saw-like fangs, though crouching low, are still feared; insects that wield axes, though hiding their form, still command awe. The noble man, though impoverished, cannot be slighted; the petty man, though exalted, is not to be esteemed."*

Baopuzi says: *"The qilin roams beyond the great wilderness, thus avoiding the disaster of traps and snares. The divine bird soars above the peaks of the Dark Garden, thus escaping the peril of nets and snares. Why must one dwell in crooked caves to harbor eternal fear? Why must one clutch reeds in the beak to wear a mournful guise? Therefore, those destined for the butcher’s block must love the fodder of the pen; those fated for the boiling pot must find comfort in standing in the courtyard."*

Baopuzi says: *"The wise value discerning the echoes of governance from a thousand years past to uncover the traces of rise and fall. The discerning treasure spotting phoenixes among the frostbitten to extract those who can save the age. Yet to heed the clamor of the many and seek counsel from the mediocre is not the clear hearing of Yan Zhou; to bend the talents of the distant worthy and consult the common man is not the extraordinary insight of the uniquely perceptive. Thus, to discuss the use of towering vessels with those who do not prize things is like having the blind point out the five colors; to plot the promotion of the fiercely upright with those who envy their betters is like entrusting the fox to mend the fur coat."*

Baopuzi says: *"The roc and the *luan* bird suffer peril on the steepest cliffs, unwilling to endure the drudgery of captivity. The *jiguang* hungers and thirsts in the frozen wilds, unwilling to accept the fullness of sacrifice. The men of Guzhu did not trade their fasting for the riches of the Deer Terrace; Zifang did not barter his poverty for the abundance of the Copper Mountain."*

Baopuzi says: *"Those whose aspirations align do not regard mountains and seas as distant; those whose paths diverge do not see a foot’s span as near. Thus, some cross vast distances to gather together, while others remain close yet never meet."*

Baopuzi says: *"The splendor of embroidered robes is not the work of a single dye; the towering majesty of Mount Song is not the accumulation of a single basket. Thus, when the Nine Ministers were employed, the achievements of King Kang flourished; when the Four Lords were entrusted, the meritorious deeds of the royal aides shone forth."*

Baopuzi says: *"The azure dragon soars to the heavens without wings; the flying snake darts like lightning without feet. The turtle hears well without ears; the earthworm projects its voice without a mouth. Thus, Gaoyao, though mute, matched the eloquent in merit; the blind man of Jin, though sightless, equaled Li Zhu in clarity."*

Baopuzi says: *"Those in high office do not necessarily match their positions in talent; those with fair reputations do not necessarily live up to their names. Thus, saw teeth cannot chew; the tongue of a winnowing basket cannot distinguish flavors. The ear of a pot cannot discern sound; the nose of a sword cannot detect qi. The eye of a cauldron cannot capture the moonlight; the legs of a bed cannot chase the flight of a galloping horse."*

Baopuzi says: *"The common traveler cannot draw the powerful bow of Yang You and hit the mark; Yanzi cannot grasp the reins and know of Dongye’s defeat. Thus, there are those who cannot lay the foundation yet recognize black and white at a glance; who cannot strum the zither yet understand the refined and the vulgar when they hear them."*

Baopuzi says: *"The water that irrigates ten thousand acres must flow from the highest peaks; the flood that crests the heavens must arise from the tiniest trickle. Thus, world-shaking merit must come from a vessel of unparalleled excellence; the strategy to steady a tottering state must spring from a mind that transcends the common. Therefore, the nest of the tiny midge has no feathers to ride the wind; the ditches and streams hold no luminous jades of the night."*

Baopuzi says: *"The whirlwind fans the prairie fire, causing it to burn brighter, yet when fireflies and candles encounter it, they are extinguished. Sweet rain and nourishing dew cause flourishing life to thrive, yet when withered trees receive them, they hasten to rot. The vermilion wheel and ornate hub are the great treasures of the talented, yet when borne by the unworthy, they invite disaster. The cauldron of ten thousand measures and the bell of a thousand *zhong* are the proper rewards for meritorious service, yet when bestowed upon the mediocre, they lead to ruin."*

Baopuzi says: *"Rhinoceros hide is made into armor for the exclusive use of the commander; kingfisher plumes are gathered into ornaments for the empress’s headdress. Though they leave the deep valley for the towering tree, in the reckoning of these two creatures, it is better to hide in the thickets and preserve life in the brambles. Thus, the divine turtle would rather drag its tail through the mud than be treasured in a silk-lined box; the marsh pheasant delights in pecking ten steps away to avoid the disaster of chickens and ducks."*

Baopuzi says: Partial talent is insufficient to govern the whole; narrow excellence cannot remedy the many shortcomings. Thus, the rooster knows the approach of dawn yet cannot fathom the reckonings of yin and yang; the crane recognizes midnight yet cannot grasp the principles of gnomon shadows. The mountain turtledove foresees fair weather and rain yet cannot comprehend astronomy; the snake and ant know the dwelling places of hidden waters yet cannot fathom geography.

Baopuzi says: When prohibitions are unclear and harsh punishments are imposed to quell disorder, when ancestral temple divinations are imprecise and troops are exhausted to invade neighbors—this is like cutting grain to count locusts, felling trees to kill borers, consuming poison to strike fleas and lice, or removing the roof to chase sparrows and rats.

Baopuzi says: Sharp blades emerge from dull stones; bright fires blaze from dark wood. Precious pearls arise from lowly clams; fine jade is hewn from ugly ore. Thus, one cannot measure the weight of Shun by his parents, nor gauge the merit of Wei and Huo by their ancestors.

Baopuzi says: When aspirations are fulfilled, the countenance is joyful; when intentions are thwarted, the face is downcast. When the root withers, the branches dry; when the source is shallow, the stream runs dry. What lies within must manifest without; what arises near must be evident afar.

Baopuzi says: Lovely forms and charming appearances differ in shape and hue, yet the delight they bring may be equal. Strings and bamboo, metal and stone—though their tones vary—equally please the ear. Casting arrows or sinking hooks, raising nets or lowering traps—though their methods differ—yield the same success. Establishing merit, composing words, or choosing one’s path—though their routes diverge—share a single noble aim.

Baopuzi says: Abundant profit harbors the source of harm; exquisite beauty invites disaster. Thus, southern birds are slaughtered for their patterned feathers; cave leopards die for their striped pelts. Sturgeon and carp accumulate, and the deep pools run dry; deer and elk gather, and the lush forests burn. Gold and jade are prized, and bandits arrive; fame and rank rise, and worries and vexations follow.

Baopuzi says: When autumn winds blow and the skies grow crisp, fine fans are set aside. When climbing perilous heights, light boats are abandoned. When weapons and war clouds arise, scholars retreat; when mourning and chaos are settled, warriors are dismissed.

Baopuzi says: What is valued at ten thousand gold need not be seen to distinguish its worth; what has branches as thick as an embrace need not be measured to know its size. Thus, gazing at the vast embankment of a flood, one knows it will not rise from a puddle; observing the boundless expanse of a literary masterpiece, one knows it did not come from a pedant.

Baopuzi says: Red blossoms and green grasses do not cling to crooked, withered stems; purple mushrooms and fragrant weeds do not grow in barren, salty soil. Thus, those who receive the jade tablet to announce success emerge from the gates of the four condemned; those who inherit the mandate from the cultured ancestors arise from the households of the stubborn and perverse.

Baopuzi says: Good words in a dwelling resonate without limit; crooked or straight, if untrue, find no response near or far. Thus, in the wilds of Song, the planet Mars retreated; in the Yin court, close kin fled. White jade rings came from beyond the borders of Shaoguang; deer and horses transformed within the palace walls.

Baopuzi says: Jing Ke and Zhu Hai did not display their courage at the gates of the timid; Meng Ben and Feng Fu did not brandish their weapons among rustic warriors. Eminent scholars and great minds do not refine petty arguments among the shallow; enlightened and noble men do not alter their brilliance amid the vulgar.

Baopuzi says: Bowing and yielding are not the demeanor for repelling invaders; armor and helmets are not the adornments of the ancestral hall. Sashes and pendants cannot be used to wield blades in battle; measured steps and regulated strides cannot save from fire or drowning.

Baopuzi says: Heaven and Earth nurture the myriad things, yet they do not recognize its grace—for its benefits are boundless. The great person transforms through spirit, yet the masses do not perceive its influence—for it governs what has not yet arisen. Thus, what is easily known is small; what is readily predicted is few.

Baopuzi says: E Huang and Ren Si were not praised foremost for sericulture; Tang, Wu, and Han Gaozu were not lauded for trivial conduct. Those who gaze clearly at the three luminaries have no leisure to bend over well pools; those who purify their hearing with the *Shao* and *Hu* have no time to lend their ears to the mulberry groves.

Baopuzi says: Appearances cannot always reveal the inner truth; reputation cannot always measure ability. Confucius resembled a stray dog; the Duke of Zhou was like rough-hewn timber. Gao Yao’s face was like a demon’s; Yi Yin’s form was like a withered skeleton. Yet Longyang and Song Chao were like clay figurines crowned with earth; the luminous pearl borrowed from Ru, Dong, and Deng was like brocade wrapping dust.

Baopuzi says: Yao and Shun could not transform the utterly foolish, so their teachings failed among their own descendants. Jie and Zhou could not corrupt the supremely wise, so their evil did not taint the Three Sages.

Baopuzi says: The greatest has limits to its power; the smallest has what cannot be taken. Thus, frost and killing cold cannot wither the lushness of beans and wheat; sweltering heat and oppressive humidity cannot melt the snows of the mountains. Whirlwinds churn the sea yet cannot make hidden springs surge; spring rains bathe the hills yet cannot make withered plants bloom.

Baopuzi says: Weeping blood-jewels must be polished to gleam; the *Chenlumenglao* sword requires a whetstone to sharpen. Fine steeds like *Liuhua* and *Hua* await the royal groom to gallop far; the *Ji* constellation relies on the carpenter’s square to perfect its virtue.

Baopuzi says: The perching phoenix and the soaring *yue* bird, though hungry and thirsty, do not wish to be caged in the kitchens of the palace; the divine *Cheng Huang* and *Tian Lu*, though famished, do not delight in fodder from the imperial stables. Thus, the old man who plucked cicadas forgot all things in the fragrant grove; the fisherman who dangled his line ignored the jade tablet of southern Chu.

Baopuzi says: Square and round differ in form; movement and stillness diverge in purpose. Thus, the celestial sphere is honored for its motion, while the Earth is revered for its steadfastness. The seven luminaries and four seasons complete their revolutions to achieve success; the Five Peaks and Six Pillars stand firm to serve as anchors. Thus, Song and Mo of Chu preserved their states through swift action; Gan Mu and Hu Yuan repelled enemies through inaction.

Baopuzi says: Those who find joy in hills and gardens may suffer in body yet find peace in spirit; those who exhaust themselves for others may rest in form yet labor in heart. Thus, those who carry jars to water gardens rejoice more than the grand ministers; those who vomit coarse grains delight more than those who feast from bronze tripods. Those who abandon their posts to escape the dark may appear dried and withered; those who wait through the night for dawn may weary and shorten their lives.

Baopuzi says: Benevolence and cruelty are as distant as heaven and abyss; good and evil are as distinct as being and non-being. The *Zouyu* treads lightly enough to walk on air; wolves and jackals pounce in packs to harm life. Yu Qing relinquished his minister’s seal to aid the poor; Hua Gong yielded his three offices to recommend the worthy. Li Si, envious of those better than himself, killed Han Fei; Pang Juan, fearing those not his equal, punished Sun Bin.

Baopuzi says: When one’s strengths are properly employed, no talent is discarded; when one’s weaknesses are scrutinized, nothing is suitable. Thus, light silk and misty gauze are exquisite in adornment yet cannot withstand arrows; the *Chenlu* and *Juque* swords are superb in cutting yet cannot be used to pick at one’s feet.

Baopuzi says: Minor flaws cannot mar a great vessel; brief ailments cannot burden enduring talent. The sun and moon bear the food of crows and kites, yet their blemishes do not hinder the splendor of their heavenly radiance. The Yellow River carries the turbidity of mud and silt, yet its towering waves do not impair its flow surpassing the mountains. A tree blocking the path cannot justify abandoning Guan Zhong; seizing fields cannot diminish Guan Yiwu. A stolen wife cannot discredit Xiao He; accepting gold cannot condemn Xiangru. Bribes cannot expel Chen Ping.

Baopuzi says: Tigers and leopards cannot strike and bite amidst waves and breakers; winged serpents cannot ascend on days without mist. When pheasants and hares are the prey, phoenixes cannot match hawks and falcons; when plows and ploughshares are drawn, scaled dragons retreat to their lairs. Thus, warriors and brave men are useless in times of peace; towering scholars and reclusive talents are not prized in eras of striving might.

Baopuzi says: When both are hobbled and yoked by the neck, the swift *qilu* is no different from a lame donkey. When deprived of forests and confined to cages, gibbons and apes are no better than badgers and raccoon dogs. When sheathed and unused, the *Longquan* sword is no different from a leaden blade. When great talent is given petty tasks, the extraordinary are no better than the mediocre. To demand the tracks of a thousand *li* from a tethered steed or to seek the merits of a master craftsman from a petty artisan—this is called lack of confusion, yet I do not believe it.

Baopuzi says: Those who savor bitter herbs and eat mugwort must lack discerning palates; those who discard jade and gather pebbles must lack the wisdom to appraise treasures. To scorn the *Nine Shao* and delight in rustic tunes, I know they cannot commune with spirits or emulate the flight of the *xiang* bird. To rely on the mediocre while possessing extraordinary talent, I know they cannot order the proper norms or attain great peace.

Baopuzi says: Those who grasp the ultimate principle of openness and obstruction are not distressed by adversity; those who understand that fate is inherent in nature do not rejoice excessively in the fulfillment of the Dao. Thus, when entangled in abysses and mires, they cast aside resentment and vexation; when ascending to the radiant throne, they show no elation. After three appointments, such is the man.

Baopuzi says: Prosperity and decline depend on fate; poverty and success are insufficient to judge a scholar. Gain and loss lie in fitting the moment; honor and disgrace cannot measure talent. Timing cannot be forced; encounters cannot be known in advance. Thus, the Grand Duke was a husband abandoned by an old woman; Han Xin was a starving beggar; Xiao He was a petty clerk; Qing Bu was a criminal in exile. When they moved like dragons among lizards and coiled like phoenixes among sparrows, the deaf and blind called their words strange.

Baopuzi says: The four spirit-beasts hide and roam as omens of great peace; recluses delight in seclusion as treasures of the state. Why must one crow at dawn or bear the bit and bridle of duty? There is the use of others’ abilities, and there is the use of my own. Those who follow their nature do not let fame disturb their harmony; those who cultivate life do not let things burden themselves.

Baopuzi says: Those who measure talent before appointing do not seek achievements beyond the capacity of the vessel; those who assess ability before accepting do not bear responsibilities beyond their strength. Thus, those who light fireflies do not trouble the vast sea; those who lift tripods do not employ Wu Huo. To haul firewood and transport salt is not a task for the hooves of fine steeds; petty duties and trivial posts are not enough to bend the extraordinary.

Baopuzi says: The flow of ditches and streams cannot carry the great ships of *Dai Bai*; vessels of a *sheng* or *ge* cannot contain the grain of a thousand *zhong*. Flickering lights cannot reveal the faintest glimmer; mediocre talent cannot distinguish the instruments of the exceptional. For the endowment of intelligence by creation has its roots.

**Baopuzi, Outer Chapters, Scroll Four**
*Guang Pi (Broad Analogies), Chapter Thirty-Nine*

Baopuzi says: To establish virtue, fulfill one’s words, and preserve unsullied conduct—this is wealth. Why must one seek the grandeur of jade and silk? To hold one’s aspirations lofty, neither yielding nor debasing oneself—this is nobility. Why must one covet the purple and crimson of high office? When the common folk cannot fathom one’s measure and mediocre men cannot weigh one’s scale, this is true elevation. Why must one tread upon clouds or walk the rainbow? When none who inquire can plumb the depths of one’s abyss or those who seek detect any lack, this is true profundity. Why must one dwell in cavernous rivers or the boundless sea? If these four qualities are complete, though one’s chamber holds but a suspended chime of poverty, can one not still envy those who smelt mountains and boil seas? Though one dwells among birds and beasts, can one not still avoid bowing before crimson wheels and ornate chariots?

Baopuzi says: The hidden spirit awaits auspicious clouds to ascend; the soaring swan relies on the stiff wind to traverse the void. The pale-scaled fish leaps only when the maiden’s hair is combed; the white pheasant arrives only when the Duke of Zhou appears. Jiang Ziya met King Wen and cast his line at Panxi; Han Xin and Zhang Liang encountered Gaozu and unleashed their talents to quell chaos.

Baopuzi says: To clarify the spirit in the profound unity is to forget the form and vessel; to distance lofty integrity from external things is to leave wealth and rank behind. Thus, the crippled *Zhilipeng* delighted in transcending creation, and the reclusive *Jisou* of the northern tribes found contentment dwelling in the peaks of Mount Song.

Baopuzi says: Coarse principles cannot fully penetrate perfection; no single ability can encompass all tasks. Thus, celestial bodies shine yet may be obscured by mountains and rivers; gold and jade are hard yet may be softened; firm ice may be melted. The Duke of Zhou could not match Bo Yi in scaling perilous heights; Confucius could not rival the swimmer of Lüliang in braving the torrent of a hundred blades.

Baopuzi says: Thunder cannot refine its sound to harmonize with bells and stones; the sun and moon cannot withhold their radiance to favor those who crave partial light. Great rivers cannot hasten their banks to suit those who wish to cross swiftly; the Five Peaks cannot level their summits to please climbers. Thus, a broad carriage cannot force its wheels to squeeze through a narrow path; a lofty scholar cannot suppress his integrity to conform to vulgar constraints.

Baopuzi says: Yin and yang extend their molding to benefit all things; the Three Luminaries universally illuminate. Mounts Song and Hua conceal infirmities to be called vast; the Northern Sea contains impurities to be deemed great. Eminent scholars advance with decorum; those who master the Dao embrace all with boundless love.

Baopuzi says: The armor of the divine turtle need not be used in battle; the horn of the qilin and claws of the phoenix need not be employed in combat. Thus, the wise do not sheathe their swords in times of peace; watchmen do not cease their patrols even when danger is remote.

Baopuzi says: Southern gold does not devalue itself for dwelling in obscurity; jade and precious stones do not cease their purity for lying in the depths. Those who uphold loyalty and the Dao do not alter their course due to adversity; those who guard integrity do not compromise for lack of reward.

Baopuzi says: Those who ascend the *Xuanpu* realize the lowliness of hills and mounds; those who float the boundless sea recognize the shallowness of ponds and marshes. Only after perusing the Nine Classics does one awaken to the depth of one’s former blindness; only upon hearing the supreme Dao does one know the delusions of vulgar custom.

Baopuzi says: The source of the primordial chaos yields no limpid streams; the root of a hair or down bears no branches of girth. A spark the size of a *cun* emits no distant flame; within a crack or crevice, no radiant throng gathers. A crooked hook casts no straight shadow; upon uneven ground, no level plane appears.

Baopuzi says: Without beholding the brilliance of jade and quartz, one does not perceive the baseness of tiles and pebbles. Without witnessing the patterns of tigers and leopards, one does not know the plainness of dogs and sheep. Only after hearing the nine movements of *White Snow* does one grasp the extreme vulgarity of the *Barbarian Songs*. Only upon recognizing the vastness of refined elegance can one lament the stubborn ignorance of the unlearned.

Baopuzi says: A jade bowl with no use is not as good as a whole vessel of clay; a torn inch of embroidered brocade is not as fine as sturdy, plain cloth. Thus, the impropriety of Xia Ji is not as pure as the solitude of a forsaken woman; the manifold crimes of the wealthy and noble are not as virtuous as the Way-walking of the poor and lowly.

Baopuzi says: Fierce beasts do not unleash their claws beyond measure; hawks and falcons do not spread their wings to strike recklessly. If one within the court does not assess virtue before advancing, and without does not weigh one’s strength, it is like casting a feather into a raging furnace or snowflakes into a boiling cauldron—like morning mushrooms testing the *Ganjiang* sword or calves and lambs challenging tigers and leopards.

Baopuzi says: When the Three Luminaries are obscured in the heavens, the clear light darkens on earth. When roots and fibers are uprooted here, branches and boughs wither there. When the Dao is lost nearby, calamity reaches afar; when governance falters above, the people suffer below.

Baopuzi says: Those who focus on the distant may err in the near; those who govern externally may invite trouble within. One who covers the head does not necessarily keep the feet dry; one who shields the belly does not necessarily protect the back. Thus, the First Emperor of Qin built walls to block the Hu, yet disaster arose within his curtains; Emperor Wu of Han unfurled banners across ten thousand *li*, yet rebellion sprang from his own palace walls.

Baopuzi says: Human talent has no fixed treasure; the use of instruments has no constant method. Those who advance or withdraw do so by adapting to the times; those who drive or restrain do so by aligning with the moment. Thus, when dark ice forms, the Five Brightnesses are set aside; when scorching summer blazes, furs and stoves retreat. When high-flying birds gather, fine bows are drawn; when cunning hares multiply, swift hounds run. When weapons rise, warriors rouse; when the *Shao* and *Xia* resound, literati emerge.

Baopuzi says: Those who stir the current to join the morning river cannot abandon the Five Cities to leap the Jishi Mountains; those who unfurl emerald leaves and sprout crimson blossoms cannot forsake their mighty roots to discard their lush branches. To lose the source and sever the root is seldom not to wither; to rebel against the sages and violate the classics is seldom to achieve success.

Baopuzi says: The Four Seas distinguish their sources; the Five Rivers diverge in their courses, yet all flow downward to the sea. Different paths lead to the same end; colors vary yet all are beautiful; sounds differ yet all evoke sorrow; fragrances are not uniform yet all are sweet; flavors are unequal yet all are delightful.

Baopuzi says: Things are valued for their utility, while ornamentation is secondary. To transform customs with virtue is primary; words are secondary. Thus, plain cloth can ward off cold—there is no need for sable and fox; unadorned simplicity can craft things—there is no need for eloquent embellishment.

Baopuzi says: When the gentle breeze stills, tumbleweeds drift; when the great net is spread, a myriad eyes align. Thus, there has never been a ruler who cherished humility yet whose subjects were arrogant, nor a lord who scorned treasures yet whose people were impoverished.

Baopuzi says: Some matters succeed through precaution; some things achieve the distant through near governance. Thus, to tend the pond at one’s steps may draw deep-swimming fish from the rivers and seas; to enrich the grove of the eastern sun may summon divine birds from the cinnabar caves. To carve images upon plates and basins may bring jade dragons down from the *Xuan* skies; to cast ashes upon a foot of water may alter the moon’s chariot in the great ultimate. Thus, Duke Wen of Jin turned his wheels at Yong and brave warriors flocked to him; Goujian of Yue bent his back before the angry frog and his soldiers embraced death lightly. The Nine-Nine was revealed, and the hidden talents of *koujiao* arrived; the bones of the dead were buried, and the benevolence of the Three Divisions spread.

Opuzi says: Fertile soil lies in the roots, and withered leaves bear blossoms; when the common folk take the ruler’s person as their model, transformation occurs without words. Thus, the Tang achieved great peace through deer-fur robes, and Duke Huan of Qi curbed extravagance by discarding purple. The construction of Zhanghua Terrace led to the excess of grand mansions, while the abandonment of Lutai brought about the spread of profound silence.

Opuzi says: The keen-eared discern the rise and fall in the echo of vanished sounds; the perspicacious perceive the workings of fate before signs take shape. Thus, the men of Yue saw in Duke Huan of Qi the symptoms of decline in an unnoticed illness, and Jizi recognized in the ivory chopsticks of Yin the coming disaster of Lutai.

Opuzi says: Heaven and Earth cannot dispense with spring and autumn to complete the year; a wise ruler cannot forsake punishment and virtue to achieve order. Thus, executing the noble establishes authority, and rewarding the lowly encourages goodness. If penalties reach the high, then the sprouts of treachery are crushed—this is not something the weak can accomplish. If kindness extends to the lowly, then distant people are drawn near—this is not something the miserly can achieve.

Opuzi says: He who sails the vast sea must carefully observe the winds and qi to secure safe passage; he who governs must tremble at the thought of gain and loss to enjoy enduring blessings. What the benighted ruler dismisses is precisely what the enlightened ruler values; what a ruined state discards is precisely what a well-ordered age practices.

Opuzi says: A hair’s breadth of error in the trigger deranges the entire aim; a misstep in granting or withholding here confounds right and wrong there. When good and evil are confounded, the constant norms are subverted; when merit and fault are unweighed, all undertakings collapse. Thus, the wise ruler’s rewards resemble spring rain, free from the excess of ceaseless downpours, while his punishments are like autumn frost, devoid of untimely severity.

Opuzi says: For one who wields the scales of justice, what is weighty cannot be falsified; for one who relies on law and measure, what is beloved cannot be made private. Thus, he who gains men first gains them in himself; he who loses men first loses himself. There has never been one who gained himself yet lost others, or lost himself yet gained others.

Opuzi says: The enlightened ruler personally wields authority and favor, never entrusting another with the instruments of power; the benighted ruler inverts shield and spear, though he calls it honor, his power slips away. Thus, to control rewards and punishments and thereby win the multitude was how Tian Chang usurped Qi; to monopolize authority and dominate the court was how Wang Mang overthrew Han.

Opuzi says: Fixed regulations cannot meet changing circumstances; a single path cannot respond to boundless directions. Carving a mark on a boat cannot retrieve a lost sword; gluing the zither’s pegs cannot harmonize clear tones. Thus, emerald canopies are not raised in fair weather, nor vermilion wheels used in crossing streams. If the flavor is bland, add salt; if the pot boils over, add water and reduce the fire.

Opuzi says: Cinnabar writs and iron tallies, the slitting of animals and smearing of blood cannot remedy the harm of broken promises—then even knotted cords would suffice for governance. The full might of the Five Punishments, the Nine Campaigns, and the extermination of clans cannot halt the treachery of covetous men—then even the shield-dance would suffice for transformation. Thus, the *Book of Documents* contains texts revered through the ages, and the *Book of Changes* adapts to the needs of the time.

Opuzi says: One who possesses the clarity to recognize truth cannot be deceived by falsehood; one who possesses the wisdom to fathom depths cannot be deluded by shallowness. Otherwise, one might mistake vipers for winged dragons and foxes for qilin and phoenixes.

Opuzi says: The world may praise in unison, yet the praised may not be worthy; custom may condemn with clamor, yet the condemned may not be evil. Thus, to welcome and approve is not as good as examining their deeds and testing their use; to reject and oppose is not as good as hearing their words and assessing their results. Then flatterers will not advance through empty talk, nor will true ability be set aside for its solitude. If the slow nag ceases to aspire to the grand chariot, and the warhorse gallops like lightning, how great the achievements that can be built, how distant the Dao that can be reached!

Opuzi says: Rotten wood cannot withstand the wind of a collapsing mountain; a cliff with fissures cannot withstand the waves of a deluge. Thus, the seven hundred years of Zhou’s mandate and the thirty reigns of its kings were not solely due to the victory at Muye or the defeat of the inverted spears; the disaster of Lutai did not begin on the morning of *jiazi*. Its strength had long waned, and its fall was long assured.

Opuzi says: To value the distant and despise the near is the common sentiment of men; to trust the ear and doubt the eye is the affliction of all ages. Thus, the King of Qin sighed over Han Fei’s writings and imagined his person, and Emperor Wu of Han was moved by Sima Xiangru’s prose and regretted not living in the same era. Yet when they finally obtained them, they could not elevate them—some accepted slander and executed them, others relegated them to idle posts. This is like Lord Ye, who loved the false form but paled at the sight of the true dragon.

Opuzi says: A pearl does not shine at night and is no different from desert gravel; a kun fish that does not soar to the heavens is no different from a peach pit. A swift steed that swallows its voice mingles with the mute; a talent of surpassing ability, if suppressed, joins the ranks of the mediocre. Thus, the eel and loach disdain the crimson dragon in the muddy depths, and the slow nag defiles the fine steed in the field—unaware that when still, they are the same, but when moving, they differ.

Opuzi says: Cast gold and jade upon the road, and passersby will halt; abandon brocade and fine silk in the mire, and onlookers will gasp in shock. Yet when men of lofty virtue are relegated to the company of the vulgar, or instruments of statecraft are discarded in straitened circumstances, critics do not protest their humiliation, nor do the successful rescue them from poverty. Some praise their writings yet stifle their persons; others employ their strategies yet forget their merit. This malady has persisted for ages.

Opuzi says: If the source is not deep, its flow cannot embrace the mountains; if the peak does not soar to the heavens, its clouds cannot blanket the sky. If wealth is not abundant, its generosity cannot be vast; if talent is not far-reaching, its words cannot be rich. Thus, seeing tusks a yard long, one knows they did not grow from a mouth an inch wide; seeing branches a hundred fathoms long, one knows they did not sprout from the tip of a hair.

Opuzi says: The divine phoenix rises at dawn in the cinnabar caves and gathers at dusk on the terraces of Xuanqiu; before the sun has shifted its shadow, it has traversed the nine realms, riding the wind and treading the clouds without hindrance—all because its six pinions are light and strong. The great talent and profound wisdom of men are likewise the six pinions of the state.

Opuzi says: The waters of the Qi and Wei cannot flow back, yet their distant currents cannot be without ripples; the stirring of dust cannot be without sound, yet its mournful tones cannot be without cause. The virtue that surpasses the common and stands above the crowd cannot establish merit in times that have not yet arrived.

Opuzi says: Vermilion and green adornments do not bloom on withered branches; the torrents of a collapsing mountain do not rise from a dried-up spring. The radiant flame of a night’s glow cannot reveal the forms of all things; the exhaustion of ambition and profit cannot make fragrant winds endure through the ages.

Opuzi says: A deep abyss that does not penetrate the earth cannot harbor leviathans that swallow boats; a towering mountain that does not reach the heavens cannot conceal jade and pearls or summon clouds and rain. One whose virtue does not transcend the common cannot gather a resounding reputation or establish profound substance. One whose resolve does not stand apart from the crowd cannot attain success or inscribe illustrious merit. Yet the mediocre man performs a cicada’s-wing good deed in the morning and expects the benefit of hills and mountains by evening—like planting millet and sorghum and sitting to demand a bountiful harvest.

Opuzi says: One who bears no conduct surpassing the vulgar yet seeks the reputation of a lofty gentleman, who lacks the foundation of the Dao and its arts yet schemes for cliques and factions, desires to gather purity and nobility in this life and spread a virtuous name to posterity—this is like hiking up one’s robes to cross the vast sea or standing on tiptoe to leap the Nine Heavens.

Opuzi says: A clay dragon, though painted in brilliant hues, cannot answer the summoning of auspicious clouds; a carved bird, though adorned with azure and gold, cannot rise on the wind’s ascent. A straw dog, though bedecked with gold and jade, cannot tread the light and halt in flight. Mediocre talent, though laden with favor and emolument, cannot make the heavens clear and the earth level.

Opuzi says: When poisoned porridge is set out, rats with rotting guts gather beside it; when a bright torch is raised at night, moths cluster below to die. Where fine meats and grains abound, the cauldron and cutting board are ever in use; where small talent bears great responsibility, tears of blood flow like mulberry and huo leaves serve as warning. How grave the lesson of Fan Li, how clear the mirror of Fan Sui!

Opuzi says: The vast sea, though it churns waves a thousand miles wide, cannot sweep the dust from mountain peaks; the howling wind, though it snaps trees of a thousand blades, cannot uproot the tender roots of grass. The roar of a tiger cannot intimidate a mosquito; towering talent cannot harmonize with the vulgar crowd.

Opuzi says: Unyielding resolve is the master of merit and fame; unflagging diligence is the teacher of all virtues. One who does not halt before perilous heights when climbing a mountain will surely reach its loftiest ridges; one who does not complain of hardship and adversity in poverty will surely perpetuate a noble reputation.

Opuzi says: The magpie, though not long-lived, cannot be denied its use as an instrument to save lives; scholars, though often poor and lowly, cannot be denied their ancient canons as tools to cultivate virtue. There are times when sowing yields no harvest, yet farming cannot be abandoned; there are times when practicing benevolence and righteousness brings misfortune, yet conduct and endeavor cannot be neglected.

Opuzi says: Overloading without cease is what sinks my boat; advancing blindly and forgetting retreat is what endangers my person. Though a swarm of scorpions gathers on the body, its root may seem secure, yet it is surely a sign of impending collapse.

Opuzi says: Auspicious clouds arise for the dragon’s ascent—not something a lizard or skink can summon; the whirlwind heralds the tiger’s charge—not something a fox or raccoon dog can bring forth. Thus, when a great man receives his mandate, men of surpassing talent gather like jade and silk; when hidden worthies are sought in secluded groves, the eminent rise from their rustic retreats.

Opuzi says: Metal, by its hardness, breaks; water, by its softness, endures; mountains, by their height, shift; valleys, by their lowness, remain secure. Thus, one who holds to the feminine principle avoids the calamity of contending for dominance; one who exalts others too much invites the misfortune of resentment.

Opuzi says: Han Xin concealed his valor beneath the crotch yet did not diminish his dragon’s leap and tiger’s gaze; Fan Ju concealed his brilliance in a soaking mat yet did not hinder his phoenix’s flight and pheasant’s rise. One became a lord facing south, the other governed the imperial court—thus, now humbled, now exalted, is how the light swan soars the void; now bent, now stretched, is how true talent awaits its time.

Opuzi says: The lowly firefly, though humble in its dwelling, refuses to lodge in the rat’s den; the pure celestial cicada, though starving, disdains the dung beetle’s feast. Thus, Yu Rang rejected the gifts of Zheng and Yang; Zeng Shen scorned the treasures of Jin and Chu.

Opuzi says: The whirlwind cannot stir the great sea’s waves; a hair’s tip cannot move a bell of ten thousand jun. Thus, Zhuangzi of Qiyuan sighed over casting away his gold; Bo Shi mourned Qi with tears of blood. A brief tune cannot summon refined harmony; power and profit cannot sway the heart of one who dwells in tranquility.

Opuzi says: The bear and brown bear do not contend in agility with the fox; the golden eagle does not compete in striking with the small sparrowhawk. Thus, Zhang Er concealed his strength as a gatekeeper; Zhu Hai hid his valor as a butcher.

Opuzi says: The fish is bewildered by fragrant bait; the tiger dies in the cage. The fox cannot be lured with a fishing line—that which comes must be a horned dragon; the unicorn cannot be enticed by snares and traps—that which is caught must be a qilin.

Opuzi says: The soaring cloud knows not the filth of the mud-dweller; the noble rarely consider the toil of those below. Yet when roots rot, even the finest timber cannot preserve its thousand-mile luxuriance; when the people resent, even Yao and Shun cannot rely on enduring blessings.

Opuzi says: Though a tree’s roots lie in sacred mountains, the master carpenter will still fell it with axe and adze; though a small fish dwells in the dragon’s pool, the fisherman will still cast his net. When mosquitoes gather on the eagle’s head, the osprey dares not peck; when mice nest beside the tiger, the wildcat dares not speak.

Opuzi says: The sacred tortoise is silent yet auspicious; its subtlety is manifest without form. The spring frog croaks endlessly yet utters ugly sounds; its clamor brings only harm to the ear. Thus, the rare voice must resound greatly; the few words must carry weight.

Opuzi says: The people of the eastern wilds despise the refined posture of sitting cross-legged; the tribes of the northern wastes abhor the scholar’s cap. Thus, the loyal and upright are cast out in an age of contention; the refined man finds no place in a world that hates the forthright.

Opuzi says: A ladle of water cannot quench the burning of eight marshes; a handful of earth cannot dam the boiling of Mount Dizhu; a single blade cannot fell the forests of a long isle. Alone, one cannot halt the wrongs of cliques and factions.

Opuzi says: A thousand sheep cannot defend against a single tiger; ten thousand sparrows cannot resist one hawk. A courtyard ablaze with torches does not match the last glow of the sun; a hundred drums beaten together do not equal the lingering echo of thunder. Thus, a court filled with mediocre men cannot set the constant norms in order; a single outstanding man is enough to root and sustain all affairs.

Opuzi says: Unwarranted success is like winter blossoms on trees and shrubs; enduring hardship in holding to the Dao is like bamboo and cypress standing firm in frost. Thus, one who discerns prosperity and adversity through solitary insight, though pressed with blades and halberds, will not lose the true path or change course—how could he deign to flatter and smile to conform to the vulgar? One who embodies square integrity and abides in uprightness, though tempted with fiefdoms, will not betray his nature to follow the times—how could he tread crooked paths to ingratiate himself?

Opuzi says: The crashing thunder cannot produce sound in the deaf man’s ear; the radiant sun, though it illumines the sky, cannot bend its light into a dark ravine. The bitter cold of solid ice cannot wither the winter-blooming flower; the scorching wind, though it melts stone, cannot topple the trees of Xiaoqiu. Thus, even the highest virtue has that which it cannot transform.

Opuzi says: The drawn crossbow, the perilous trigger, the keen arrowhead—these are indeed to be feared, yet the bold pheasant rushes toward them without suspicion. Dark governance and chaotic rule, malice toward the upright and envy of the capable—these are most difficult to fathom, yet the greedy vie for them without avoidance. Thus, flying blades assail them unawares, and sudden ruin overtakes them before they can rally. Therefore, what delights the foolish man is precisely what the enlightened man laments. What mediocre talent pursues is precisely what great wisdom shuns.

Opuzi says: When the wind does not cease, the fan is not needed; when the sun does not set, the candle is not lit. When blossoms do not fall, fruit does not form; when the bank does not erode, the valley does not fill. When the Nine Provinces are at peace, the merits of Han and Bai do not stand out; when a long-reigning lord succeeds the throne, the achievements of Yi and Huo do not arise. Thus, only in illness does one value the skilled physician; only in troubled times does one esteem loyalty and integrity.

Opuzi says: Those who covet glory delight in praise, while those who dread disgrace abhor slander. Yet for one who penetrates the primordial unity, who harmonizes with the transformations of creation, who blends fullness and emptiness into a single strand, who equalizes gain and loss with a single gesture—love and hatred never begin to bind him, and success or failure cannot disturb his harmony.

Opuzi says: One whose spirit is not muddied by gain or loss is utterly pure; one whose harmony is not adulterated by advantage or harm is supremely refined. Vast and boundless, it cannot be measured by ladle or cup; distant and unfathomable, it cannot be traced by half-step. Rare sound is the greatest tone; few harmonies exalt the self. How profound and remote is the Yellow Emperor’s Way—it does not share in the emptiness of death. How great is the matter of life and death—yet it does not alter one’s steadfastness. Why trouble oneself over trifling distinctions?

Opuzi says: Where trees grow dense, the carpenter enters; where pearls are fine, the oyster is split. Stones containing gold are smelted; herbs bearing medicine are plucked. Sharp blades dull first; mournful strings break soon. When used to suit oneself, they are the true treasures of the perfected. When talent aligns with worldly demands, it invites the calamities of skill.

Opuzi says: When the target is set, arrows fly toward it; when a fine reputation arises, slander and abuse assail it. Hoarding rare treasures does not invite resentment, yet resentment comes; when vessels are full and ambition proud, disaster is not summoned, yet disaster arrives.

Opuzi says: Treasures worth a thousand cities cannot be purchased by the poor and humble; vessels of towering stature cannot be recognized by the shallow and vulgar. Yet a foot-long gem does not dim its luster for lack of appreciation, and a man of surpassing virtue does not diminish his integrity for being obstructed. Rejoicing in Heaven and submitting to fate—what grievance, what blame?

Opuzi says: The great roc has no use for dawn’s vigilance; the giant elephant possesses no talent for swift pursuit. Thus, Jiang Wan failed in a hundred-li march yet became a paragon of the Three Excellencies; Chen Ping was exhausted in managing his household yet harbored the strategies of six ruses.

Opuzi says: Clarity and obscurity are matters of talent, arising naturally and beyond adornment. Success and failure are matters of timing, subject to constraints and beyond force. Lü Shang was not dull in youth nor wise in old age—yet he cast his plain silk and met his moment. Han Xin was not timid at first nor brave at last—yet he rose from dire straits to greatness.

Opuzi says: The galloping steed cannot retrieve past errors; a thousand gold pieces cannot redeem a single flaw in speech. Thus, those who broadly bestow would do better to guard their defenses; those who diligently seek would do better to temper their words.

Opuzi says: The stalwart man loves his state as his family and serves his lord as his kin—thus, disloyal acts do not arise, and his guilt is none. The benevolent man regards others as himself and treats the distant as the near—thus, unforgiving resentment does not arise, and his responsibility is none.

Opuzi says: If dark ice does not form and white snow does not accumulate, the luxuriance of the green pine is not revealed. If custom does not decay and moral instruction does not decline, the purity of unsullied conduct is not distinguished. Only in a perilous state does one sink into obscurity—thus, Zhuang Zun and Lai Xi upheld their lofty disdain for honor. Only in chaotic times does one suffer hunger and cold—thus, Zeng Shen and Lie Yukou spread their renown for forgetting wealth.

Opuzi says: Heaven dwells on high yet gazes below—thus, its net, though sparse, does not leak. The spirit is luminous and upright—thus, its Way rewards the true and punishes the false. Therefore, when kindness and harmony spread through the Nine Regions, the seven luminaries find their place in the boundless sky. When cruelty and harm appear among creatures, the two qi err in the four seasons.

Opuzi says: Heaven’s order holds boundless honor; human rank holds no nobility that violates virtue. Thus, though Confucius was but a commoner, he received sacrifices for a hundred generations. Though Xin and Gui were emperors, their servants would not deign to compare themselves to them. The older Shang Yang grew, the humbler his station, yet the greater his fame. The more illustrious Jie and Zhou’s reigns became, the more their slander grew. Thus, the life of the King of Qi does not match the tomb of Liu Xuan; the palace of the King of Qin does not equal the lane of Zheng Kangcheng.

Opuzi says: Echoes cannot exist without form and sound to manifest them; blessings cannot be summoned without virtue to attract them. Thus, when Yao governed for over seventy years, only then did auspicious stars radiate. When Yang Gong accumulated virtue into old age, only then did golden rain fall. The distant traveler arrives late; the belated benefactor’s recompense is slow.

Opuzi says: When principle is exhausted, one cannot demand excess; when a single attainment is reached, one cannot seek universal aid. Thus, the last ripples of a great wave cannot stir duckweed; the aftermath of a fierce wind cannot lift light dust. The residual force of a powerful crossbow cannot pierce mist; the fading glow of the setting sun cannot illuminate the eastern mountains.

Opuzi says: Though the celestial bodies may dim, they cannot be compared to the steadfast radiance of fireflies and candles. Though the Yellow River may be turbid, it cannot be likened to the clarity of ponds and streams. Though mountains may crumble, they remain loftier than mounds; though tigers may grow lean, they remain fiercer than jackals.

Opuzi says: Had Shennong not suffered nine illnesses, the Way of the Four Classics would not have been bequeathed. Had Yu not endured calloused hands and feet, the blessings of the dark jade tablet would not have gathered. Thus, long suffering is the root of profound joy; brief toil is the beginning of lasting ease.

Opuzi says: Though the golden hook and cassia bait be precious, they cannot restrain the depths of the nine abysses. Though illustrious favor and abundant emoluments be noble, they cannot attract the reclusive man of no desires. Thus, by the Lüliang cliffs dwells a man of lofty stance; by the river’s edge, people ceaselessly chant the *Fatan*. Jade and silk gather in vain at the lane of Master Ziling; empty carriages return in vain from the gate of Xu You.

Opuzi says: Perception and hearing differ; loves and hates are hard to share. Birds, seeing Xishi, take flight in alarm; fish and turtles, hearing the *Jiushao*, sink deep. Thus, the splendor of court robes cannot please the eyes of the naked land; the pure notes of water-chestnut songs cannot delight the ears of the Chu exile. The benevolence of Gugong cannot enlighten the land-greedy Di; the eloquence of Duanmu cannot free the horse-tethering servant.

Opuzi says: The rituals of refined conduct are detested in lands of naked squatting; the craftsman’s plumb line is envied in the workshop of crooked timber. The gluttonous and ravenous despise the unblemished whiteness of plain silk; the cliquish and scheming resent the solitary loftiness of high integrity—just as the merchant hates a rival in profit, and the homely woman resents the beauty of a kingdom’s fairest.

Opuzi says: When the noble man ascends, he advances the worthy and disperses emoluments; when the mediocre man gains power, he vaunts his rank and scorns the scholar. He bestows favors lavishly upon flatterers, employs talent only in petty kindnesses, shares no peace with the wise yet expects rescue in peril, partakes in no joy with the extraordinary yet desires solace in sorrow—like praying for rain from a famous mountain when wildfire already blazes across the sky, or felling trees in Min’s eastern forests when the floodwaters surge above the heavens. Is this not too late?

**Chapter Forty: On Diction and Meaning**

Some say: Heaven and earth’s roundness and squareness are not the work of compass and square; the Three Luminaries’ radiance is not the power of polish and ink. Spring blossoms’ brilliance is not the dye of gradual staining; orchid and angelica’s fragrance is not borrowed from external scent. Know then that the utmost truth values naturalness. Meaning is prized for its rarity of encounter; diction is beautified by its infrequency. Yet surveying past and present writers, few can bring forth untrammeled splendor at the tip of the brush. Most ladle from earlier words—why?

Opuzi says: Pure tones are prized for their refined resonance; harmonious compositions are treasured for their clear distinctions. Thus, the eight sounds take different forms yet share the same tuning; embroidered patterns vary yet the five colors are balanced. Only because the crude has its master and the beautiful its sequence, the grand compositions have no fixed melody, and the great kitchen no set flavor. Timber piles up on Mounts Zi and Yu, yet without the master carpenter, no craft emerges; books are boundless, yet without exceptional talent, no essence is gathered. Why must one seek a thousand trees to build a great hall, or divine words to compose a chapter?

Opuzi says: Talent has purity and turbidity; thought has length and brevity. Though all engage in writing, their levels differ by ten thousand grades. Some are vast and flowing yet lack depth; some grasp truth yet their words are dull. To speak against principle yet excel in craft is but a partial strength, not the talent of comprehensive mastery. Blind to self-assessment, forcing oneself to兼通, straying from one’s ability into alien tasks—how can one avoid ridicule?

Opuzi says: The five flavors clash yet all are sweet; colors diverge yet all are lovely. Human nature loves what aligns with itself and scorns what differs. The style of writing is especially hard to judge. If it merely pleases the ear or suits the heart, few know the profound delight of the nine movements’ savor or the elegance of the *Ya* and *Song*. This is like tasting salt and plum’s saltiness and sourness yet not knowing the great broth’s absence of seasoning; admiring the lightness of floating feathers yet being blinded to the profundity of the abyss’s depths.

The exceptional and grandly untrammeled soar beyond the bounds of the mysterious and yellow; the constrained and cramped are fettered within cages. Wings have strength and weakness, so flight and perching have high and low; tracks have swiftness and slowness, so progress has far and near. The dull and sharp cannot be tuned with fixed pegs.

Writing values richness and abundance—why must all praise be as one mouth? If it cannot rescue the world’s declining customs, stem the decay of the secluded path, clear the way for the doubtful, or relieve the poor’s want, it is no different from spring blossoms that do not serve as food, or orchids that do not save from icy cold. Ancient poems criticized faults, thus were beneficial and prized; modern poems are pure empty praise, thus harmful and cheap.

Opuzi says: Writers each have their flaws. The deeply afflicted suffer from excessive analogies, redundant expansions, earnest amplifications—wishing to discard them yet pitying to do so, unaware they become burdensome. The shallowly afflicted suffer from beauty without substance, insufficient evidence, glossy skin yet weak bones. If ornate and resplendent, they outshine the seven luminaries; if profound and subtle, they rival the unfathomable abyss. Human affairs, however minute, are thoroughly permeated; the kingly Way, however vast, is fully prepared. Thus, though the body be lowly, the words are noble—through a thousand years, their glory grows ever brighter.

**Chapter Forty-One: Following the Root**

Opuzi says: The mysterious, quiescent, and tranquil are the root of spirit and clarity. Yin and yang, softness and hardness are the root of the Two Modes. Towering peaks and deep valleys are the root of mountains and hills. Virtue, conduct, and learning are the root of the noble man. None can stand without a root. Thus, to reach the heights, one must strengthen the foundation; to flourish at the end, one must deepen the root. If village friends are not harmonious yet one busily seeks distant connections, if one’s official reputation is unestablished yet one craves extraordinary prominence—though one may briefly steal empty fame like frost-bitten flowers gleaming in the cold, they will wither before the morning ends. Though one may seize the great treasure unprepared, riding the dust like a flea on a galloping horse, few scales cling to soar on the waves—glancing back, one is already a withered stump on perilous land.

The sage and worthy diligently strive as if so, yet the shallow and careless scorn thus. With accumulated habit, one forgets the stench of the fish market; in the land of the naked, one does not notice the shame of exposed form. Unless one withdraws from the world without distress, equalizing success and failure, how can one abandon the near and easy to seek the distant and arduous?

To remedy this flaw, the method is no other: simply elevate the people from the cliffs and crags, employ talent without calculation.

**Chapter Forty-Two: Responding to Ridicule**

Opuzi says: A guest ridiculed me, saying: "Master, you embrace the One, conceal your brilliance in the abyss of spirit, turn your back on the world, and wander alone—so remote, so serene. Your resolve is firm, yet hesitation does not arise in your heart; your course is set, yet worldly entanglements do not bind your breast. Boyang took the Dao and virtue as his head; Zhuangzi crowned his work with ‘Free and Easy Wandering.’ Thus, they established towering stature in the nine skies, spread fragrant glory without end. Yet you, Master, hold lofty principles and do not serve, yet write books on the lord’s Way and minister’s duty; do not associate with the world, yet compose critiques of customs and discourses on saving lives. You cherish your own limbs yet compile methods of warfare and defense; do not pursue advancement yet have essays on examining appointments and the rise and fall of careers. I am truly perplexed."

Opuzi says: The great order of lord and minister is next to heaven and earth. To find joy in the Dao, whether appearing or withdrawing, is one in intent—what does it matter? The great man adapts to changing circumstances. Laozi practiced non-action, yet wrote his book; the Old Man of the Valley ultimately withdrew, yet composed his work—both discussed worldly affairs. Why must one hold office before speaking of its matters?

If the vessel is not fine jade or fragrant catalpa, it will not weep; if the essence is not a hidden dragon, wind and clouds will not gather. My talent is meager, my virtue slight, my abilities unfit for governance. Appearance and withdrawal share the same end; action and stillness are one in principle. Must one be a high official to discuss government affairs, or reside in office to discourse on order and chaos?

Ever lamenting Zhuangzi’s words and deeds as self-aggrandizing shackles upon the world’s work: though dwelling in the Lacquer Garden, he indulged in wild talk, fond of painting demons and spirits, despising depictions of dogs and horses as too narrow, disparaging loyalty and integrity, slandering benevolence and righteousness. Such carving of tigers and painting of dragons could hardly summon wind and clouds; empty ledgers by the millions could not save the penniless child’s horse from hoof-skinning, nor could a table piled high with dishes fill an empty stomach.

Others may say: "Your writings, sir, indicting customs and speaking bitter truths in forthright words, will only incur hatred from those in power and bring ostracism in your time—hardly the way to spread your name, gain renown, and be valued." I reply: A craftsman treasures a vessel for its usefulness in urgent need, not for adorning its outer form; a writer values words for their aid to teaching, not for currying favor and amassing praise. If one merely flatters, fawns, and conceals faults with empty praise, how can this correct errors, admonish transgressions, awaken the deluded, or remedy faults? Fearing few will harmonize, one abandons the "White Snow" melody; dreading hard sales, one cheapens the "Liancheng" gem. I seek nothing from this. Not that I cannot string together florid phrases to please, nor that I do not know forthright words often bring trouble—yet I cannot bear to betray my feelings, bend my brush, or mingle false with true. I wish only for heart and mouth to align, and to feel no shame before the wise who may come after. Prosperity and adversity are fated; success and obstruction are left to Heaven. Why must one’s conduct and words bring glory in one’s own time?

The noble man, when opening his mouth or moving his brush, must guard against delusion and obscurity, rectify the inclination to echo others’ errors, and grind away the darkness of evasive obscurantism. Yet those who write books merely adorn and play with florid phrases, flaunting abstruse and impractical words, stringing together unverifiable and useless expressions, crafting ornate and empty beauties—such works resemble the "hard and white" debates of Gongsun Long or the "punishments and names" discourses of the Legalists. Though they may encompass heaven and earth, penetrate the infinitesimal, untie linked rings, separate the inseparable, make bird shadows move not, give feet to chicken eggs, turn dogs into sheep, or speak of giant turtles and long snakes, they merely display cleverness and parade marvels to deceive the vulgar—what difference is there from painting granaries to stave off hunger, gazing at the Milky Way to quench thirst, praising the jade of Kun Mountain to relieve Yuan Xian’s poverty, or perusing a pharmacopeia to cure a critical illness? Mozi carved a wooden rooster to move Heaven, yet it was no match for a three-inch axle pin; Guan Qing cast a golden image of a fine steed, yet it was no match for a nag’s practical use. Lofty words that soar to autumn skies yet cannot be applied—Confucius would not exchange them for his own.

**Chapter 43: Obscuring Analogies**

I have always held Wang Zhongren’s *Lunheng*, with its eighty-odd chapters, to be the crown of extraordinary talent. A fellow student, Lu Sheng, challenged me, saying: "Jade is prized for its rarity, gravel despised for its abundance. Thus, Fu Xi’s trigrams numbered fewer than ten yet embraced the Two Modes; Laozi’s words did not exceed ten thousand yet fully expounded the Dao and virtue. Wang Chong’s writings fill boxes and pile up scrolls, now entering, now exiting, now Confucian, now Mohist, with words strung together and meanings not always fine—such are the weeds of the marshlands, not to be compared with the millet and sorghum of measured steps."

I replied: Those who create are called sages; those who transmit are called worthies. You see only the ranks of transmission and creation but have not heard of limits on their quantity. You, sir, are like one who hides in a burrow’s dimness, unaware of the boundlessness of the Eight Extremities; who clings to the glow of a lamp or candle, ignorant of the brilliance of the Three Lights; who swims in a shallow, stagnant pool, oblivious to the vastness of the Southern Sea; who is mired in the lowly mound of an anthill, unenlightened by the towering majesty of Mount Song. Heaven and Earth are called great because they embrace the Eight Wastes, vast and boundless; mountains and seas are called rich because they encompass vast expanses, containing all manner of things. If, as you elegantly argue, fewness is prized and abundance despised, then the vault of heaven, though vast and all-embracing, would not be valued for its thick bearing.

In a foot of water, there are no leviathans; on a handspan’s branch, no wings that brush the sky; atop an anthill, no forests of Fusang; in a stagnant puddle, no floods that rise above hills. The giant turtle’s head crowns Yingzhou; flying waves soar above Fangzhang; the great peach rests upon a measuring rope; the Jianmu tree towers over Duguang; the giant kun swims across the heavenly pond; the roc soars to the clouds. Moreover, thunder’s roar cannot refine its sound; the Yellow River’s surge cannot confine its flow; the swiftest steed cannot catch its tracks; the great swan cannot lower its flight. Thick clouds bring fierce rain; a strong bow sends arrows far. Wang Sheng, learned and talented, how could you not grasp this?

You say jade is prized for its scarcity and stone despised for its abundance. Yet beneath the dark gardens, atop the thorny peaks, in the marshes of the Nine [ ], in the abysses of Zhefang, jade and pearls pile into mountains, night-luminous gems blaze to the heavens—are these not fine? You cite Fu Xi’s few writings, yet the Duke of Zhou, following the *Great Change*, added rites and music; Confucius, composing the *Spring and Autumn Annals*, appended ten chapters—should they be demoted for exceeding Fu Xi and Laozi? Few words fail to encompass the ultimate principle; sparse phrases cannot fully express the myriad affairs. Thus, one must accumulate chapters and volumes to raise the guiding threads.

The sun and moon ascend to illuminate day and night; the Five Materials arise with different uses; the hundred herbs flourish with varied effects; the four seasons cycle to complete the year’s work; the five colors gather to adorn brocade; the eight sounds harmonize to beautify the *Xiaoshao* melody; the myriad words converge to distinguish the arts and the Dao. Even with wealth piled like Taozhi’s, its use is scant—how is this different from Yuan Xian? To hold the measure of an uncarved block yet write in meager and crude terms—how does this differ from the petty and trivial?

Music is treasured by those who know it; books are transmitted by those who recognize them. The melodies of the blind musician Kuang may not be understood in his own time; lofty words and refined writings need not fear lack of appreciation or diminish their worth. Moreover, the depths of rivers and seas cannot be exhausted, yet this does not lessen their profundity; the crooked trees of the Five Peaks cannot be counted, yet this does not diminish their towering heights. Though the jade tablet of the Xia sovereign may have a flaw the size of a hair, its radiance and luster suffice to compensate. Though a work of tens of thousands of words may contain unadorned passages, its profound meanings and lofty principles suffice to cover them. Thus, the turbidity of the Four Seas does not compare to the clarity of a jar of water; the leanness of a giant elephant is not the same as the plumpness of a lamb.

You also criticize his writings for now entering, now exiting, now Confucian, now Mohist. Yet speech issues from the mouth, and writing is speech put to paper. If the brush should not record diverse matters, then discussions should always adhere to one thing. Formerly, when feudal lords sought counsel on governance or disciples asked about benevolence, Confucius answered each with different words, adapting his guidance to the matter at hand and the urgency of the time. This is like the myriad prescriptions for treating illness or the varying points for acupuncture and moxibustion—used to dispel cold with warmth, eliminate heat with cool, with the sole aim of saving lives and preserving bodies. How can one, like a traveler, follow a single road to Qi and Chu without changing course?

The wealth of Tao Zhu and Bai Gui encompassed not one single commodity—such was their abundance. The creatures of Yunmeng and Mengzhu were myriad and diverse—such was their vastness. Thus, the *Honglie* of Huainan begins with "Originating the Dao" and "Inaugurating Purity," yet also includes military strategy and the art of rulership. The writings of Zhuang Zhou treat life and death as one, yet also contain his awe for the sacrificial ox, his admiration for the phoenix, his plea for grain to relieve hunger. If one were to discard a text because its words are not uniform, it would be like treating a pearl in the eye by gouging out the eye, curing damp impediment by severing the foot, eradicating weeds by reaping the grain, or loathing withered branches by felling the tree.

**Chapter Forty-Four: The Hundred Schools**
Gezhi Zi said: Though the words of the Hundred Schools are not all pure, refined, ornate, or profound, they are, without exception, the lodging of the heart and the concentrated thought of the talented. The orthodox classics are the deep sea of the Dao and virtue; the masters’ writings are the flowing streams that deepen them. Looking up and comparing them, they are like the auspicious stars that assist the Three Luminaries; looking down and measuring them, they are like the thickets that complement Mount Song. Yet scholars cling to a single discipline, wandering a well and neglecting the sea, stumbling in the mire and stagnating in unchanging困境. The masters’ writings draw from the vast and distant, the subtle and boundless, the unfathomable source, and spread forth inexhaustible currents. Their transformations are not bound by the compass and square of rules; their lateral connections do not sink into the crooked paths of deviance. Their style is lofty and severe, their edge difficult to exhaust. Those who favor only sour or sweet cannot appreciate their flavor; those whose thoughts are limited cannot discern their spirit. The ancients sighed at the rarity of talent, and so for a hundred generations, scholars have followed in one another’s footsteps. They do not discard the luminous jewel of night because uncut jade does not arise from the hills of Ban and Shang, nor do they abandon words that aid teaching because they do not issue from the gates of Zhou or Confucius. It is like those who carry water: though their vessels differ, their aim of extinguishing fire is the same. It is like acupuncture and moxibustion: though their techniques vary, their goal of curing illness is equal. Yet those of narrow vision cling to one thing and reject breadth, confusing their thoughts. They do not recognize that accumulating the smallest bits can equal the weight of mountains, or that gathering hundreds and thousands can reach the number of billions and trillions. They are bewildered by the trivialities of poetry and rhapsodies, yet overlook the profound and beautiful words of the masters. They invert truth and falsehood, mix jade with stone, equate grand music with the tunes of Sangjian, and rank dragon patterns with plain silk. How lamentable! How deplorable! And not just in one respect.

**Chapter Forty-Five: Literature and Conduct**
Some say: Virtue and conduct are the root; literature is the branch. Thus, in the sequence of the Four Categories, literature does not occupy the highest place. Then writing on paper is but the dregs of lees, a thing to be transmitted no more than the straw dogs used in sacrifices or the distinctions of high and low—worthy of ridicule.

Gezhi Zi replied: One may discard the fish trap once the fish is caught, but before the fish is caught, one cannot do without the trap. One may abandon literature once virtue is realized, but before virtue is realized, one cannot do without literature. As for the elegance of brushstrokes, the resonance of rhythm, the skill in arranging words and comparing meanings, the beauty or ugliness of their sources and flows, the length or brevity of their reach, the depth or shallowness of their citations and allusions—their differences are so vast that even the space between heaven and a hair’s tip cannot illustrate their remoteness. Their mutual surpassing is so great that even the Three Luminaries shining together cannot compare to their magnitude or minuteness. The sharpness of Longyuan swords and the dullness of lead ingots cannot match their keenness or bluntness. The lightness of swan feathers and the weight of piled gold cannot compare to their lightness or heaviness. Yet vulgar scholars see only the ability to dip the brush and draw on paper, and so they lump them all together. This is why Bo Shi endlessly pondered Zhong Zi, and the craftsmen of Ying ceased wielding their axes. Those who carve and cut may stand shoulder to shoulder, yet Ban and Di alone hold the name of unmatched skill. Those who pluck the zither may be many, yet Kui and Xiang alone possess the title of pure sound. Among a thousand teams of horses in the stable, only Qiji has the price of surpassing the herd. Among ten thousand beauties, only Weishi has the allure that transcends the age. For they far surpass the multitude.

Moreover, to say that literature is but the surplus of virtue and conduct, like ten feet being the surplus of a丈, is something I have never heard before. The Eight Trigrams arose from the flight of the hawk and falcon; the Six Jia emerged from the burden of the divine turtle. Where literature exists, though it may be noble, the root is not necessarily shallow, nor is it necessarily thin where it is base. It is like brocade and embroidery relying on plain silk, or pearls and jade resting on clams and stone. Clouds and rain arise from the smallest measure; rivers and seas begin with the tiniest span. If the principle is truly thus, then the refined discourse is flawed.

It is also said: The ascending dragon rises slowly, glancing back as it soars into the clouds. The sweating blood horse walks leisurely, breathing deeply as it covers a thousand *li*.
