Thus, the ant marvels at its ascent without steps; the slow and lame are astonished that it surpasses them without gradual progress. If one gallops within the bounds of poetry and discourse, or circles within the confines of a single classic, viewing the vast and extraordinary with common sentiment, measuring the boundless with a footrule, from childhood to gray hair, one will never attain it.

Moreover,世俗 generally venerates antiquity and despises the present, respects what they have heard and disdains what they see. Though there may be a steed that chases the wind and leaves the dust behind, they still say it does not match the horses ridden by Bo Le. Though there may be uncut jade as bright as two cities at night, they still say it does not compare to the jade wept over by the jade appraiser of Chu. Though there may be a sword that can sever a horse’s leg or carve a cicada, they still say it does not match the swords forged by Ou Ye. Though there may be medicine that revives the withered and restores the rotten, they still say it does not compare to the remedies compounded by He and Que. Though there may be a scholar who surpasses the crowd and walks alone, they still say he does not measure up to the ancients.

**Chapter Forty-Six: Zheng and Guo**
Gezhi Zi said: Ji Sheng believed that Guo Linzhong of Taiyuan ultimately declined the command of the Three Dukes. His learning was all-encompassing, and his name was weighty in past ages. Moreover, he was skilled in discerning others—one who knows others is wise, and thus he was a vessel second only to the sages. Yet in a declining age, he was anxious and restless, never at ease in his seat, his ambition set on correcting disorder and practicing the Dao, much like Confucius.

I replied: Wisdom or its absence hinges on a single word. Is the flaw in the pivot of the mechanism not enough to cause chaos? Bai Gui foolishly granted him the evaluation of being second only to the sages—this is not easily conceded. What is meant by "second only to the sages" is one who embodies the complete model and guards against the world, peerless and unmatched, with no room for comparison between them and Zhou or Confucius. How could such a person be worthy of this rank? Linzhong was outstanding, surpassing his time, his discernment clear and penetrating. Compared to ordinary people, the discussion naturally elevates him, but in reality, he still falls short. This man had eloquence, bearing, and the ability to hold his own, and he was adept at seizing opportunities. Moreover, those fond of meddling acted as his wings, spreading his reputation in all directions. Thus, he was able to leverage this and be esteemed in a chaotic age, praised by those who listen without verifying. When he bestowed a single word of praise, it was weightier than a thousand gold pieces. Wherever he traveled, the worthy and the foolish alike were swept up in his wake, calling it the descent of dragons and phoenixes, the emergence of rare omens.

When he spoke, his lingering tones became models to emulate; when he moved, his footprints became patterns to imitate—one might call him skilled at striking the great drum and worthy to raise high the sun and moon. Yet this was no true reclusion. For when he wished to stand in court, the age was in great chaos; when he wished to lie low, he found confinement unbearable. If he leapt forth, he feared calamity; if he withdrew, he found no peace. Thus he wavered, now plump, now gaunt, while the world pursued his bloom and never probed its root, admired his form and never grasped his spirit. So when his bamboo hat rotted in the rain, others still took it as a model, blind to its flaws—all was illusion. The common folk chased his reputation to such an extent that though flaws were plain, none dared point them out.

Linzhong’s learning touched many fields, and he was not without insight into others. Yet he lacked the clarity to avoid error in reality and was blind in self-assessment. When urged to take office, Linzhong replied: “By day I observe human affairs; by night I guard against celestial signs. What Heaven has cast aside cannot be upheld. Now the fortune lies in the hexagram *Bright Obscurity*, in the position *Do Not Act*—a time for lingering in reclusion, not for the moment when Heaven favors public service. Even on dry land I fear the surging sea will overflow and I become its fish—how then could I dare face the gale and ride the rushing waves?”

Better the mountain caves where spirit finds repose and heart delights in ease—like Pengzu, carefree, drifting, passing the years in leisure. By his own words, Linzhong knew the Han could not be saved, nor was it within his power to remedy; this much he discerned clearly. He should have looked up to the heights of Shang and Luo, looked down to the expanse of the Five Lakes, pursued Master Chao to the towering peaks, sought the Fisherman on the blue-green waves. If he could not bind his steps to the mountain recluses and depart alone, then he should have veiled his light in the deep pools, sheathed his scales, sealed his words in silence. Yet instead, he journeyed from west to east, his mat never warmed, yearning to emulate the sorrowful deeds of Confucius and Mozi. The noble man who grieves for the world wanders the four quarters—yet even recluses mocked and censured him.

Linzhong’s talent was not of the moment, his vessel not peerless. In public service, he could not pacify the ruler or govern the people, transform customs or reform mores. In private, he could not exhaust his brush in writing or trace the Six Arts, yet he flaunted himself, overstepping all bounds. His fame blazed, his renown flourished, and he reaped no small reward. Yet in the end, he advanced without aiding order or chaos, and in retreat left no mark upon bamboo and silk. Observe his rise and fall, his melting like ice, his withering like grass—he differed not from the common man. For no reason, he plunged into the waves, cast himself into the dust, gathered in the capital, mingled with the noble and powerful. Wheels overturned, defenses crumbled, and he had no leisure to rest. Thus his reputation spread like wildfire, the people of Qin and Hu flocked to him, lanes filled with crimson-wheeled carriages, halls lined with scarlet-gowned guests. Chariots crowded the streets, memorials piled high—truly he was a knight-errant, not one who fit the measure of reclusion.

In an age of the Dao, one who reached such a state would still not be counted among the ranks of the pure and lofty, nor numbered among the hidden worthies. How much less, then, in this time of peril and chaos, is he worthy of praise? Who would not say he was blind to the rise and fall of Heaven and man, deluded about his own strengths and weaknesses? In vain he turned from the path of tranquility and silence, achieving no benefit, yet escaping disaster—this was mere luck.

To take such a course and claim concern for the world and devotion to the state, aspiring to the likeness of the Uncrowned King, is like a lame man chasing a dragon steed, a sparrow pursuing the swan, a scorched cicada comparing itself to the roc, a shrew to the great elephant. Thus Linzhong may be said to possess talent that dazzles the world, but not the substance of steadfast integrity. He saw all things clearly, and when nearly called to great use, his outward brilliance flared while his inner spirit was hollow, unable to bear the strain. His words and deeds clashed, his mouth spoke of quiet retreat while his heart craved glory and profit. He was no bird nesting in the mystic gardens, no hidden spirit in the ninefold abyss.

To praise oneself, to advertise one’s own worth—these are the shameful acts of scholars and women. Knowing the impossible, he still imitated the ancients, aspiring to the vessel of a near-sage—where was his stability? Though he spoke of knowing others, such discernment was difficult even for Yao and Shun, a vexation even to Confucius. One whose clarity rivals the sun and moon, who traces beginnings and sees ends, may still err and not always hit the mark—how much more so for Linzhong, whose light was but a flickering candle, his understanding half-right, half-wrong? Yet this was no small achievement.

Thus his name weighed heavily in his time, his praises resounded long after his death. What he gained, the world transmitted; what he lost, none recognized. Though he did somewhat sift the nameless scholars from the wilds, point to uncut jade in the hills, he could not advance the loyal and resolute to the court, stand against invaders on the frontier, relieve the desperate from their plight, or break the schemes of traitors in their pursuit. Like Baozi recommending Guan, like Zhong’s promotion of Rangju, Linzhong’s name shook the court, his reputation commanded respect in his day. The high ministers of the three and nine ranks all revered him, his strength could lift talent, his words could raise the stagnant. Yet he only nursed his illness in the capital, gathered guests, and made no effort to mend the crumbling state. He knew men but would not recommend them—what difference is there between this and recognizing fertile soil fit for fine crops, straight timber fit for beams and pillars, yet never plowing to sow good grain, never felling to build the frame? How does this relieve famine? How does it shelter the homeless?

His refusal of office was indeed a lofty principle; his ceaseless rushing about was also his affliction. Jisheng once said: “In life, Linzhong was a model for his age; in death, his fragrant legacy endures. No great scholar or fine gentleman has ever pointed out his flaws—why then do I alone criticize him? Will I not be ridiculed by future generations?” The Master of Embracing Simplicity replied: “Why should this be so? If my words are just, let them be passed to posterity. Why should I fear the lack of agreement from those who come after? Moreover, many former worthies have also criticized him—only the imperial edicts praised him excessively.”

Thus the Grand Tutor Zhuge Yuansun also said: “Linzhong concealed himself but did not cultivate true reclusion; his emergence did not benefit the times. In truth, he sought only to spread his name and nurture his reputation. Street gossip and alley chatter took his criticisms of the court as eloquence, his slander of governance as loftiness. The vulgar prized him, flocking to him like Guo Jie and Yuan She in former times.”

Later scholars, drawn by his fame, could not examine him by the classics of the sage kings or measure him against the conduct of the ancient worthies. Bewitched by his flowery reputation, they competed to take him as their standard, learning from him as if they could not keep up, speaking of him until their ears were full. Even men of middling ability did not perceive the truth—how could the young and ignorant know? The Prefect of Lingling, Yin Bojing, a man of towering talent and resolute judgment, also said: “Linzhong entered the company of generals and ministers, traveled among the feudal lords, elevated private discourse to sway the masses, and wielded influence in the court. What he desired spread like wind and rain, changing prices and altering postures; what he despised was crushed and sunk. Scholars disdained him, and he broke the reputation of the so-called worthy. In times of chaos, to hide and conceal one’s light is not far from the mark.”

The noble man practices the Dao to aid his ruler and rectify customs. At that time, the ruler could not be aided, customs could not be rectified. Linzhong circled in pure discourse among the lanes and alleys, yet this did nothing to halt the decline of the world or relieve the suffering of the people. The former Gentleman of the Secretariat, Zhou Gongyuan, a man of heroic stature and renowned learning, also said: “To encounter order and assist it is called delighting in the Dao; to meet chaos and save it is called grieving for the Dao; when chaos cannot be saved and one withdraws, this is called guarding the Dao. Yu Shun delighted in the Dao; Confucius grieved for the Dao; Boyi guarded the Dao.”

In the declining years of the Han, as the world teetered and affairs turned to socializing, Linzhong ought to have roused himself with an empty heart, seeking like-minded noble men to jointly correct and rectify the age. Yet he remained aloof, a lone hero—hardly the proper way to save the world. At that time, even the eunuchs treated their livestock as kin. Though men like Chen Fan and Dou Wu, who held the highest offices as ministers and governors, all revered Linzhong and trusted his judgments of right and wrong, could one not have hoped that he might aid the imperiled and reform customs? Yet Linzhong neither recommended men of action to bring the slightest benefit nor fled into reclusion. Thus, when compared to those like Xu You and Chao Fu, who abandoned their posts to await worthy guests, he was likened to the Duke of Zhou, who nurtured disciples; when avoiding service, he was equated with Confucius; when forsaking kin to rely on the powerful, he was matched with Ziyou and Zixia. Hence, the world confused name and reality, and great disorder grew ever worse.

If one claims Linzhong was ignorant, how could he be called wise? If he knew yet did not change, how could he be said to grieve for the Dao? In ancient times, the Four Greybeards resembled the Duke of Zhou but could not become him; now, Linzhong resembled Confucius but could not be him. Thereupon, one who questioned this sighed and said: “Then this man was merely one who fled chaos, not a lofty hermit of complete reclusion.”

**“Critique of Mi Heng” (Chapter 47)**
The *Baopuzi* states: At the end of the Han, there was a man named Mi Heng, twenty-three years of age. Kong Wenju, though past fifty, held a high rank among the Nine Ministers and was renowned for his literary talent, yet he befriended Heng while the latter was still a commoner. He even memorialized the Han court, recommending that Heng be appointed directly to the post of Gentleman of the Palace. He declared: “When mountains give birth to spirits, extraordinary men emerge together. Whatever I see once, I can recite from memory; whatever I hear in passing, I never forget. My nature aligns with the Dao, my thoughts are as if divine.” Such was his praise of Heng.

Heng traveled to Xuchang, where from the highest ministers and nobles to the worthies of the realm, none escaped his scorn. He never addressed them by their titles, calling them instead “Uncle So-and-so,” or by their surnames, as one would a child. He called Kong Rong “Big Boy” and Yang Xiu “Little Boy.” Only Xun Yu was deemed barely worth speaking to; beyond him, all were mere wooden puppets or clay effigies—lifeless things, no more than wine jars or rice sacks.

Once, at an assembly of high officials, Heng suddenly frowned, his expression mournful, sighing with sorrow and indignation. Someone mocked him, saying: “Here we have a gathering of heroes—why this lament?” Heng glanced around at the crowd and replied: “Amid these rows of corpses and coffins, how can a humane man not grieve?”

Cao Cao once gnashed his teeth, wishing to kill him, but Heng had committed no capital offense under the Eight Laws, and Cao feared the reputation of slaying a scholar. Thus, he demoted Heng to the post of drumming officer. Heng showed no remorse or shame; instead, he tied ox horns to a pillar and played them with his mouth, producing strange sounds while beating the drum. Those who heard could not tell it was the work of one man.

His criticisms grew even sharper, utterly without restraint. Soon after, he fled and sought refuge with Liu Biao, Governor of Jingzhou. Biao wished to draft a letter to Sun Quan, who had already consolidated power in the southeast with a million armored troops, proposing an alliance to resist the Central States. He had his scholars compose the letter, but none could capture his intent. Finally, he showed it to Heng. Heng read it and said: “This is fit only for Sun’s lackeys, the boys who hold his blades. If Zhang Zibu were to see it, he would be deeply shamed.” With that, he crumpled the paper and threw it to the ground. Biao, dismayed, looked at him strangely and said: “Is there nothing you can do?” He regretted not having Heng tend the fields instead.

Heng asked for paper and brush, then rewrote the letter. The others had produced over ten drafts, all of which Heng merely glanced at before memorizing them. When he finished writing, he returned the draft to Biao. Biao compared it to the originals—there was not a single error. All were astonished. Biao then asked Heng to compose another, and Heng did so without pause, his hand never stopping. Biao found it excellent and put it to use.

Heng’s arrogance grew ever more extreme. The gentry of the province all despised him, and even Biao could no longer endure him and wished to kill him. Someone admonished him: “Cao Cao is known for his severity, yet he could tolerate Heng, who has but a slight reputation. If you kill him now, no wandering scholar in the world will ever set foot in Jing and Chu again.” Thus, Biao sent him away.

Heng fled to Xiakou and took refuge with General Huang Zu, who treated him as an honored guest. Huang Zu’s eldest son, Huang She, traveled with Heng. Passing a tomb, they read the inscription on its stele. After a single glance, they moved on. Later, She said: “The stele inscription we saw earlier was excellent. It’s a pity I didn’t copy it down.” Heng replied: “You only remember the name. I recall the whole thing after one reading.” He then wrote it from memory. At the end, one character was missing due to damage to the stone. Heng supplied half the character and said: “I suspect this should be such-and-such a word, though I cannot be certain.”

Throughout his life, Heng spoke and acted with disdain for others, yet secretly longed for glory and renown. Thus, though he soared through the phoenix groves, he could not hide in the reeds and brambles. His self-cultivation was flawed, his critiques misguided, and he failed to recognize his errors. Hence, he opened his mouth only to be hated, took a step only to invite disaster. With such talents, how could he have found a place in the world or met a natural end? Like a crow’s caw or a fox’s howl, though men dislike the sound, the creatures do not change their cries—what good did it do? The sea of men in Xuchang, with Wenju as his patron and bearing the weight of his support, could not make him content—this much was clear. He was like a man with a fatal illness, beyond even the skill of the great physician Bian Que; like rotten wood or leaden darts, beyond the craft of Lu Ban or Ou Ye. Yet he fled again to the lands of Jing and Chu, where he met his doom—this was the consequence of Heng’s blindness. He sought what he could not attain, not that he could have attained it and failed to use it.

Alas! Let men of talent take warning!

**“The Disciple Asks”** said: “What perplexes me is Mi Heng’s empty reputation.”
**“You have spoken of his true flaws,”** I replied. “How could I not awaken to your guiding words, casting aside my staff to follow the right path?”

**“Refuting Bao” (Chapter 48)**
Bao Jingyan revered the books of Laozi and Zhuangzi and excelled in incisive debate. He believed that the ancients lived without rulers, a state superior to the present age. Thus, he wrote in his treatise: The Confucians say, “Heaven gave birth to the common people and set up rulers for them.” Could it be that august Heaven spoke so earnestly? Was this not merely an excuse for those who desired it?

When the strong oppress the weak, the weak submit. When the cunning deceive the foolish, the foolish serve them.

The subjugation of the weak gives rise to the Way of ruler and minister; the servitude of the foolish establishes control over the few in strength. Thus, the bonds of servitude and the toil of labor arise from contention over strength and the comparison of wit and folly. Yet august Heaven remains truly uninvolved.

In the primal chaos, namelessness was prized; all living things rejoiced in attaining their desires. Thus, to strip the bark from pillars and carve lacquer was not the wish of the wood; to pluck the kingfisher’s feathers and rend the halcyon’s plumes was not the desire of the bird. To tighten the reins and force the bit was not the nature of the horse; to bear the yoke and haul heavy loads was not the joy of the ox. The sprouts of deceit and cunning, the exertion of force contrary to truth, the felling of roots to adorn the useless, the capture of flying birds for ornate display—piercing the noses of creatures born whole, binding the feet of those meant to roam free—these were never the intent of the myriad things born together.

To toil the common people in order to nourish those in office, where the noble enjoy ample stipends while the people suffer hardship—if one could escape death and gain life, the joy would be boundless, yet this is not as good as never having faced death at all. To decline rank and refuse stipends in pursuit of empty fame is not as good as never having had to decline them. When the world is in rebellion and chaos, loyalty and righteousness become manifest; when the six relations are in discord, filial piety and kindness are extolled.

In the distant past, there were no rulers, no ministers. People drank from wells they dug, ate from fields they tilled, rose with the sun and rested with its setting. They drifted without attachment, content in their ease, neither competing nor scheming, knowing neither glory nor shame. Mountains had no paths, marshes no boats or bridges; rivers and valleys were uncrossed, so none encroached upon another. The multitudes did not gather, so none attacked one another. High nests were left unplundered, deep pools unscooped. Phoenixes and simurghs nested in courtyards; dragons and scaled creatures swam in garden ponds. One could tread upon hungry tigers, grasp vipers and adders, wade through marshes without startling gulls, or enter forests without alarming foxes. Ambition and profit did not arise; calamity and chaos did not occur. Weapons were unused; walls and moats were unbuilt. The myriad things were profoundly united, forgetting one another in the Dao. Plagues did not spread; the people lived out their years. Purity dwelled in their hearts; scheming minds did not stir. They fed at ease, drummed their bellies, and wandered freely. Their words were unadorned; their conduct unembellished.

How could they amass wealth to seize the people’s property? How could they impose harsh punishments to create pits and snares? Yet when the age descended into decline, wit and cunning arose, virtue and power waned, and hierarchy was established. The rites of ascent and descent, of increase and decrease, were elaborated; the robes of dark and yellow silk, the caps of office, were adorned. Towers soared into the clouds; vermilion and green were applied to rafters and beams. They dredged the steepest peaks for treasure, dived into the deepest pools for pearls, and amassed jade like forests—yet this was not enough to exhaust their extravagance. They piled gold into mountains—yet this was not enough to satisfy their expenses. Indulging in excess and dissipation, they betrayed the great origin of their beginnings. The farther they strayed from antiquity, the more they turned from simplicity, increasing their veneration of the worthy, so the people contended for fame; prizing wealth, so thieves and bandits arose. When desires became visible, the true and upright heart was disturbed; when power and profit were displayed, the path of robbery and plunder opened. The forging of sharp weapons prolonged the suffering of invasion and division. They feared their crossbows were not strong enough, their armor not sturdy enough, their spears not sharp enough, their shields not thick enough. If there were no oppression, all these could be discarded.

Thus it is said: If white jade is not shattered, how can it become a ritual scepter? If the Dao is not abandoned, why would one seek benevolence and righteousness? It was the likes of Jie and Zhou who roasted men alive, minced remonstrators into jerky, pickled feudal lords, split open hearts, and shattered shins, exhausting the evils of arrogance and excess, employing the cruelty of roasting and branding. Had these men remained commoners, their natures might have curbed their extravagance—how could they have inflicted such atrocities? Their ability to slaughter and carve up the world arose from their position as rulers, which allowed them to indulge their will.

Once ruler and minister were established, wickedness multiplied daily—yet one still wishes to raise one’s arms in defiance? In shackles and fetters, one suffers amidst the bitterness of the road; the lord trembles in fear within the ancestral temple; the common people are tormented in their hardship. To restrain them with ritual and measure, to order them with punishments—this is like trying to dam a source that floods the heavens or stem an unfathomable current by plugging it with a handful of earth or blocking it with one’s palm.

**The Master Who Embraces Simplicity** replied: I have heard that when the primordial obscurity parted, the turbid descended and the pure ascended; the vault of Heaven arched above, the expanse of Earth settled below. Heaven and Earth took their places, and from their forms, the distant was drawn: thus, Heaven is honored and Earth is lowly, making manifest the structure of human relations. From the near, drawn from the body: the head and limbs thus illustrate the order of ruler and minister. The pattern of hierarchy has its origins here.

As for the Great Ultimate in its undifferentiated chaos, before the Two Modes had form—this was not as good as when the dark and yellow were sundered, the Seven Luminaries hung in the sky, yin and yang shaped the myriad things, and all were distinct. From this, we know that birds gather and beasts scatter, nesting in trees and burrowing in dens, eating flesh and blood, wearing grass and straw. Within, there were no distinctions of the six relations; without, no ranks of hierarchy. This was not as good as sheltering in spacious halls, enjoying fine grains and delicacies, wearing embroidered robes and silken garments, facing winter and summer with ease, governing the world with wise rulers, and employing skilled artisans. Offices were established, duties divided, and the cosmos was harmonious.

When noble and base were distinguished, people yearned for reward and feared punishment; when power was equal and strength balanced, contention and plunder knew no fear. Thus, sages arose, receiving their mandate from Heaven. Some wove nets to hunt and fish; some observed the stars to drill fire; some tasted plants to select grains; some built shelters to shield from the elements. They prepared things for use, removed harm, and brought forth benefit. The people rejoiced and followed them, honoring them as rulers—thus the Way of ruler and minister was born.

How could there be deceit of the foolish or oppression of the weak? The Three August Ones and Five Emperors succeeded one another, and the teachings of the Dao flourished. They distinguished right from wrong, encouraged virtue and discouraged vice. Virtue was abundant, punishments were clear, and songs of praise arose. The great transformation was achieved; the steps of the celestial ladder were level; the Seven Governors followed their courses. The phoenix sang in the morning sun; the qilin and unicorn appeared bearing auspicious signs. Dragons emerged from the rivers, spouting patterns; the sun’s elder displayed its radiance in the sky. The imperial wind spread across the nine regions; weapons were stored away in armories. Thus, ritual systems ensured the ruler’s peace, and punishments were set aside.

As for extravagance, dissipation, madness, and violence—do these necessarily arise from having rulers?

Yet Master Bao alone cites the crimes of a declining age while ignoring the principles of perfect governance—why is this? Moreover, in remote antiquity, simplicity and plainness prevailed, for the people had not yet changed; their minds remained as unformed as those of children, free from scheming. This was not because they knew restraint but chose not to act, nor because they desired and yet endured. If person contended with person over the profits of wild grasses, if household strove with household over the lands of nests and dens, if above there were no officials to redress wrongs and below there were only factions bound by heavy oaths, then private strife would surpass public warfare, wooden clubs and stones would outdo halberds and shields, corpses would litter the fields, and blood would dye the roads crimson. Long without rulers, all living things would perish.

As for the taming of dragons and the domestication of phoenixes, the *Hetu* and *Luoshu* diagrams, the qilin bearing inscribed tablets on its back, the yellow fish surging in the waves, the red birds soaring to bestow teachings, or the triple convergence of whirlwinds—all these occurred in eras with rulers, not in times without kings. Such auspicious omens, pointing to the profoundest mysteries, either manifest the signs of dynastic revolution or reveal the flourishing of perfect governance. If rulers were not aligned with the Will of Heaven, who would summon these propitious responses?

If you deem chaos and defense as beauty, then Heaven and Earth ought not to be divided.

If you esteem namelessness as the highest, then the Eight Trigrams ought not to have been drawn. Could Creation have erred, and Fuxi been benighted? The refined discourse of the ancients revered only spontaneity. Pray tell: to recognize the mother yet forget the father is the nature of all living things; prostration in reverence is but a late adornment of the world. Yet nature cannot be left to itself—one must honor the father; adornment cannot be abolished—one must perform obeisance. If you abandon both, how can a child find peace?

In ancient times, the living had no houses of timber, the dead no coffins for burial. Rivers lacked boats and oars, land lacked carriages and horses. People swallowed poison and perished, fell ill without medicine, and died unjustly without end. Later sages reformed these ways and bequeathed them to the people, whose descendants to this day rely on their beneficence. The benefits of ingenuity and craft are not easily undone. Now, if you were to dwell in the crudeness of nests and caves, cast the dead into the wilds, cross rivers by swimming, traverse mountains on foot bearing loads, discard cauldrons and tripods to eat raw and rank food, abandon needles and stone to suffer natural illness, clothe yourself in nakedness, take a woman as mate without matchmakers—would you not say this is impossible? How much more so, then, without a ruler?

If the people of high antiquity were like wood or stone, or like ice so pure it does not chill, or could go without food and not hunger, then perhaps it would suffice. But if the desires for clothing and food reside in their hearts, then what they contend over need not be gold or jade, nor need their competition be for glory or rank. Acorns and taro can provoke lawsuits; coarse greens and pulse suffice to incite plunder. The nature of desire sprouts at the moment of receiving qi; the inclination to favor oneself takes root at the formation of the body. Usurpation, murder, and annexation arise from nature itself. If this is so, how could order be maintained?

When a wise king sits above, the feudal lords all follow his model, rising at dawn and retiring late, extending the hours of audience, welcoming criticism to correct faults, holding close advisors to account, heeding street gossip for self-examination, reflecting on missteps with evening trepidation. He spreads a cleansing wind to sweep away corruption, sharpens autumn’s severity to discipline all things. His laws are strict, his nets tightly woven: those who transgress are not spared. Executions punish minor crimes; the Nine Punishments chastise great rebels. Yet still he fears wolves blocking the path, laments the disorder of human relations, worries that the arrogation of power will bring ruin to families, and dreads treachery harming the state. Thus he deploys hawk-like officials to impeach wrongdoers, and tiger-like ministers to wield the rod at the four quarters. Were he to cease banishing and exiling the reckless and cunning, allowing them no fear, then Robber Zhi would rampage and plunder, while the good and innocent fold their hands and await disaster. With no lord to defend them, no borders to rely on, how could one hope that all families become Boyis and Liuxias? It would be like carrying a pig and wishing to remain unscented, or leaning on a river and hoping not to get wet; like driving a galloping horse without reins, or sailing a light boat without rudder or oar—how could it be possible?

Master Bao further objects: Heaven and Earth take their positions, the two qi shape all things. Delighting in yang, clouds take flight; favoring yin, rivers settle. Following softness and hardness, they complete their natures; adapting to the four seasons and eight nodes, they transform and generate, each attaching to what suits it—originally without distinction of high or low. Once ruler and minister are established, however, transformations multiply. When otters abound, fish are disturbed; when hawks proliferate, birds are thrown into chaos. When offices are set up, the common people are burdened; when offerings to superiors are lavish, the people below grow poor. Hoarding treasures, adorning palaces, feasting on lavish spreads, dressing in dragon-patterned robes, amassing idle women within and leaving many men widowed without—seeking rare jewels, prizing strange objects, crafting useless implements, indulging insatiable desires—neither ghosts nor spirits, whence comes the wealth to sustain this?

When grain and silk accumulate, the people suffer cold and hunger; when the bureaucracy is fully staffed, resources are squandered on their upkeep. The palace guard consumes without labor; the people support idle hands. The masses lack clothing and food to sustain themselves—how much worse when taxes and corvées are heaped upon them? The people below cannot bear their fate, freezing and starving, violating the law in desperation. The king above labors in anxiety, his ministers below furrow their brows. Fearing calamity’s approach, dreading the failure of wisdom and courage, he offers generous ranks and heavy emoluments to entice them, fortifies walls and deepens moats to guard against treachery. Yet he does not realize that lavish rewards impoverish the people and make ministers arrogant, while fortified walls increase labor and sharpen the art of attack. Thus, scattering the gold of Lutai and distributing the grain of Juqiao brought universal joy—how much more so if one had never amassed gold or hoarded grain in the first place? Releasing oxen in Taolin, freeing horses on Huashan, sheathing shields and spears, storing bows and arrows—this was still deemed excessive. How much more so if one had never raised armies or waged war?

Thatched roofs and earthen steps, abandoning weaving to pluck mallows, using sacks as curtains, washing furs for bedding, concubines not wearing silk, horses not fed grain—frugality was taken as a model, a fine tale to tell. This is what Robber Zhi meant when he divided spoils and took the lesser share as deference, or what fish in a drying pool mean when they moisten one another with foam. If one’s person bears no public service, one’s family no tax burden, if one is content with one’s land and delights in one’s lot, if within one has enough for clothing and food, and without no strife for power or profit—then wielding a staff to rob and plunder is not human nature.

The teaching of symbolic punishments left the people with no crimes; yet the more laws were promulgated, the more thieves and bandits arose. Was it that they lacked avaricious natures while only the rulers were rapacious? No—when the state is tranquil, the people naturally align; when the people are weary and resentful, cunning and deceit arise. To leave things to nature is still to fear violence and oppression; to labor without rest, to seize without end, fields grow fallow, granaries empty, looms idle, food insufficient for the mouth, clothing inadequate for the body—how could one expect order to prevail?

Thus, efforts to avert disaster only deepen it; strict prohibitions fail to halt transgressions. Checkpoints and bridges are meant to prevent wrongdoing, yet crafty officials exploit them for mischief. Weights and measures are meant to detect fraud, yet the wicked use them to deceive. Great ministers are meant to shore up peril, yet treacherous ministers fear their lord’s safety is not imperiled. Arms and armor are meant to quell strife, yet bandits and rebels use them to foment it. All these are the consequences of having rulers.

When the people see advantage, they contend for it. The wealthy and noble have much to gain. Even petty disputes among commoners are trivial, and the strength of individuals has its limits. Without territory to covet, cities to desire, gold or jewels to crave, or power to compete for, they cannot gather followers or command others. How can this compare to a king’s wrathful display, mustering armies and deploying troops, driving innocent people to attack blameless states, where corpses lie by the tens of thousands, blood flows to float the oars, and the land turns crimson? Wicked rulers have existed in every age, venting their cruelty and disordering the world. Within, no loyal and good men are spared; without, the common people lie exposed in death. Is this merely the harm of petty strife?

As for shifting filial piety to serve the lord, or replacing devotion to parents with loyalty—even without rulers, such distortions would still arise.

In ancient times, houses sufficed to shelter from wind and rain, yet now they are adorned with vermilion and purple, embellished with gold and jade. In ancient times, clothing sufficed to cover the body, yet now it is woven with dark silks, embroidered with intricate patterns, and draped in brocades, fine silks, and gauze. In ancient times, music sufficed to settle the human heart, yet now it overflows with frantic melodies and wanton sounds that startle the soul and disrupt harmony. In ancient times, food and drink sufficed to sate hunger and nourish the body, yet now forests are burned, rivers drained, and all living creatures slaughtered. How can one, because affairs have gone too far, entirely abolish them?

If the sage-kings Yao and Shun were on the throne, with Hou Ji and Yu assisting in governance—living in humble palaces, levying light taxes, and employing the people in due season—if they exalted the pure winds of frugality and strictly enforced prohibitions against extravagant feasts, honoring and elevating those who embraced simplicity and restraint while demoting and punishing those who disrupted customs and encroached upon the people, then songs of praise would arise and the common folk would live in peace. Why must one fear fire and demolish houses, or dread storms and fill great rivers?

The Master of Embracing Simplicity said: "Master Bao valued the ancient age without rulers," a claim I have already refuted. The subsequent responses in my writings are too numerous to record in full; I have briefly excerpted his arguments and appended my refutations. Master Bao said: "Rulers seek rare treasures, gather strange objects, adorn useless things, and indulge insatiable desires." The Master of Embracing Simplicity refuted this, saying: "May I ask—have all emperors and kings throughout history sought rare treasures and gathered strange objects? Were there those who did not? Now, I have heard that when Yao ruled as emperor..."

The casting of gold into the mountains marked Yu’s abdication, just as the discarding of jade into the valleys, coarse food, and plain garments marked Shun’s vigil at the gatekeeper’s post—from this it may be known that they neither drained the deeps to carve pearls, nor split the crags to quarry jade, nor smelted cinnabar and lead ores, nor crossed the seas to rend kingfishers’ plumes, nor netted hawksbill in distant lands, nor dug pigments from the Min and Han rivers. When robes and insignia show no distinction, majesty and weight cannot be manifest; when names and ranks are not differentiated, rites and emblems lose their proper gradations. Thus the Duke of Zhou established the canons for distinguishing noble from base and high from low: in palaces and dwellings, the height was limited by the span of pounded-earth walls; in caps, canopies, banners, and flags, ornamentation followed regulated patterns; in carriages, robes, vessels, and utensils, quantities were fixed by statute; in kitchens and banquets, dishes adhered to prescribed standards; in years of famine or disaster, reductions and withdrawals were duly made. Insatiable desires do not belong to the discourse of the Daoist adept; to cite them as proof of Jie and Zhou’s crimes is insufficient to settle the judgment of refined debate.

Master Bao said: “When the ruler’s rear palace holds three thousand consorts, can this all be Heaven’s will? When grain and silk accumulate, the common people suffer cold and hunger.”

The Master of Embracing Simplicity refuted this, saying: “The number of a true king’s consorts is fixed by the sage. The sage is one whose virtue joins with that of Heaven and Earth. Since his virtue joins with Heaven and Earth, how could this be mere whim? How could it be merely to gratify passions and sate desires?”

Thus it serves to assist the six palaces in regulating yin and yang, to instruct and solemnify the ancestral temples, to serve the divine spirits, to uphold the great sacrifices, to don the dark-tasseled robes, and to broaden the paths of the royal lineage. Moreover, according to the *Records of the Nine Lands* in the Zhou canon and the geographical texts of the Han, the number of women in the realm exceeds that of men—how could the king’s consorts possibly crowd out those who ought to marry?

Duke Ji of Zhou pondered this and seemed to have considered it carefully. The emperor leads the hundred officials in the *jie-tian* ritual; the empress and consorts of noble rank oversee weaving. Extending to the common folk, farming duties are limited, tillage is rewarded, and idleness is punished. A tithe is levied to supply public needs, households maintain reserves for defense, and the state stores nine years’ worth of provisions. Each follows the natural division of labor, none is deprived of their season, taxes are light, corvée labor is sparse, and the people suffer neither hunger nor cold. When clothing and food are sufficient, rites and deference arise.

In the eras of Emperors Wen and Jing, the people devoted themselves to farming, households were well-provided, and granaries overflowed with grain—so much that the red husks rotted beyond counting. Yet even then, scholars and commoners still dined on fine meats and rode in carriages drawn by oxen and horses. This was because taxation was moderate and did not deplete the people. In later ages, officials abandoned the system of tillage quotas, and private pursuits turned to frivolous trades. The way of growing grain narrowed while idle mouths multiplied, so that both high and low alike transgressed, and offenders abounded.

Master Bao thus blamed the ruler, but it would be better to criticize the excesses of conscription and selection, to censure the unreliability of farming quotas, to rebuke the three thousand who drink like oxen, and to reduce the land tax to a third or half. If only the rear palace adhered to the Zhou rites and taxes were not levied arbitrarily, this would suffice. Must there truly be no ruler?

If the ruler rises late even once, affairs will fall into disorder. When entering the forest without a forester, even the deer will elude you—how can this be sustained indefinitely? Without a guiding authority, the noble lose their model, the people achieve their desires, and even a loose net still catches some fish. Can there be no net at all?

Master Bao said: “For people to live, clothing and food are already burdensome. If one further imposes heavy taxes and labor services, hunger and cold will converge, and the people will be unable to endure their fate, leading them to defy the law and commit crimes.”

The Master of Embracing Simplicity refuted this, saying: “The spider spins its web, yet fleas and lice do not starve. Human ingenuity harnesses the myriad things to feed mouths and clothe bodies—what hardship is there in this? The problem lies in the rich having no limit to their desires and the noble having boundless expenditures. How can one, for the sake of a single precaution, abandon governance entirely, fearing the cruelty of Jie and Zhou? To say there is taxation and labor service is to invite chaos—then I ask: in the age of peace under Yao and Shun, in the virtuous eras of the Three Dynasties, were there no taxes or labor services to support the ruler and ministers? Did the Son of Heaven and his officials plow the fields to sustain themselves?”

Master Bao then said: “The king treads as if over deep abysses and tiger tails, his peril beyond comparison. He sleeps lightly, rising before dawn, and eats only after the sun has declined—what cause has he to fear calamity?”

The Master of Embracing Simplicity countered: “If a ruler truly acts thus, he is a sage-king. The true affliction of kings lies in arrogance and extravagance, in failing to employ the worthy and employing the unworthy. King Jie of Xia pointed to the sun and swore he would not perish; the First Emperor of Qin feared sharing a posthumous name with others, yet both brought ruin upon themselves and became laughingstocks for posterity. If a ruler fears danger, remains vigilant at dusk, broadly accepts admonitions, consults even the humble, nurtures the aged, and seeks counsel from the venerable—what worry is there of misjudgment? What peril is there that the hundred affairs will not prosper? Diligence orders the constant norms; laxity breeds villainy. How much worse, then, if there is no ruler at all—could there be no chaos?”

Master Bao said: “The king covets rare omens and lures distant lands, seeking to exalt his virtue and magnify his might, dazzling the un-subdued. What benefit is there to the common people in white pheasants or jade rings?”

The Master of Embracing Simplicity refuted this, saying: “When the king’s virtue reaches Heaven, there are celestial portents; when it reaches Earth, there are terrestrial responses. The radiant star scatters its light to aid the moon’s glow; the solar halo displays its hues to mark the sun’s path. Divine birds sing in the palace towers; golden images gleam upon the clear pools—were these brought about by humble words or lavish gifts? Wang Mang, treacherous and harboring malice, fabricated signs of peace to deceive the court and the realm, even sending envoys to foreign lands to present auspicious omens. Can this be taken as proof that all ancient omens were the same?

Seeing a tail a fathom long, one knows the beast is not a handspan in size; observing fangs a cubit long, one knows the mouth is not an inch wide. Thus, the Queen Mother of the West sent envoys, proving her numinous transformation and boundless influence. The distant state of Yuechang, through repeated translations, came to offer tribute, showing that benevolence had reached even the remotest lands. Remote domains cannot be subdued by force; barbarians cannot be cowed by might. Without perfect governance, how could this be achieved?”

Why, then, did Master Bao claim such things were of no use? The House of Zhou was not lacking in jade and thus needed the Queen Mother of the West’s ring to be deemed wealthy; nor was it sparing in its meals and so exhausted the pheasants of Yuechang to fill its kitchens. What made these gifts precious was that they signified the attainment of Great Peace—whereby the ruler above was free of harsh and oppressive governance. What could be more abundant than this? To say they were of no use or benefit to the people is to fail to see their profound source and vast import. Was not Master Bao’s remark fitting?

Master Bao said: “The sovereign fears unforeseen treachery and so fortifies the city walls in preparation.” The *Baopuzi* retorted: “The lords and kings set up barriers—what the *Great Change* values. Why, then, should fortified walls be criticized? When the Two Modes first unfolded and the myriad things were transformed and born, both deviance and rectitude came to be. The sage, knowing that guarding against villainy is natural and that the benighted masses are as hard to change as withered wood is to flourish under spring’s warmth or metal and stone to melt beneath the summer sun, took his model from the *Xikan* and prepared for what had not yet sprouted. With layered gates came the watchman’s clapper as a warning; with military governance came the suppression of violent intruders. Yet to seek their eradication—what principle justifies this? The rhinoceros’s horn, the phoenix’s spur—Heaven indeed bestows them, but must they be used daily?”

The wasp and scorpion bear venom to defend themselves; the clever bird carries reeds to shield against nets; the badger bends its burrow to guard against sudden assault; the water buffalo forms ranks to repel the ferocity of tigers and leopards. Yet Master Bao would cast aside armor and helmets to advance sharp blades, raze city walls to halt charging forces. If armor and helmets are discarded and sharp blades do not cease, if city walls are destroyed and charges still gather—even Gongshu Ban and Mo Di could not preserve themselves. How, then, shall our lives be secured?

Some say: “If there are no coveted goods, even without fortified walls, enemies will not come.” Master Baopu replies: “Must coveted goods be gold or jade, the point of an awl or knife? The foolish masses contend over them. The great war of the Yue people arose from the unequal division of a python’s flesh; the rebellion of Wu and Chu was sparked by a single mulberry leaf. In times of famine, men devour one another. Yet in the past, bare-handed and barefoot, from afar like Fu Hou and Zigao, from near like Yu Gong, they probed the truth, weighed punishments, split hairs and shaved grains. Those condemned to execution swallowed their voices and sang of virtue; those mutilated by amputation or nose-cutting bore no grudge to their dying day—all this occurred not in a time without rulers.

Long ago, when a widower dwelt in lowliness, the Four Peaks did not conceal him; when obscure worthies were sought, the finest talents were all raised. Some cast aside their butcher’s knives to enter the golden gates; some left their trowels and bricks to ascend jade halls; some abandoned their fodder and hay to become ministers and chancellors; some, fleeing for their lives, became supreme generals. Bo Liu avenged his foe; Xie Hu recommended his enemy; Fang Hui knocked his head to attract scholars; Qin Xi shattered his skull to advance the worthy. I ask: were these times without rulers?

It is further said: ‘Fields lie fallow, granaries stand empty—all because of rulers.’ But rulers are not the creeping weeds that choke the fields, nor are ministers the sparrows and rats that deplete the granaries. That fields lie fallow and granaries stand empty is ultimately due to calamitous fate—floods and droughts, epidemics and plagues—leading to famine and want. How can this be blamed on the levies and demands of governance?

Among the Eight Regulations, food is foremost, called the people’s Heaven. Hou Ji tilled the fields himself; the Yu sovereign plowed in person. In years of plenty, millet and rice abound; our granaries brim with grain. Canals are dug to drain alkaline soils, turning them into fertile fields. Shao Fu built the embankments of Yangling, piling grain like mountains; Shu Ao created the irrigation of Qisi, leaving households with rotting millet. Zhao Guo devised the triple-plow technique, enriching the lands west of the Pass; Ren Yan taught the people of Jiuzhen to farm, filling the bellies of the black-haired masses. Were these times without rulers?

*Knowing When to Stop*, Forty-Ninth
Master Baopu says: “No misfortune is greater than insatiability; no blessing is richer than—”

To know when to stop, to embrace sufficiency, and to dwell in emptiness—these are the calculations of preservation. Feasting in ease and abounding in fullness are the perils of what is hard to safeguard. Consider the likes of Shan Juan, Chao Fu, Guan Yin, and Hu Gong: all trod upon clouds and mist to soar aloft, relied on dragons and phoenixes to elevate their traces, discerned the sheathed blade within fragrant bait, and awakened to the overturned cart upon the road ahead. Shunning treacherous paths to avoid crossing, they evaded the hooks of Zhan He—truly adept at anticipating shadows beyond form and glimpsing solid ice in the first frost. They moved firewood before the blaze raged, furled sails and stilled oars before the gale struck, gazed at the nine oxen and sank deep, beheld the dense thicket and swiftly departed. They did not nest at the tip of the reed, nor lie supine beneath the crumbling cliff. Were these not men of vast capacity, profound insight, and keen perception, who surpassed their peers and walked alone, unbound by convention? Their desires aspired to merge with the Dao of creation, their hearts transcended the myriad things. Covetousness could not sting their purity, nor could turmoil disrupt their sincerity. If one lacks such innate integrity, how can it be attained? This is a path beyond the trodden track.

Yet those who know success often withdraw, for those who toil greatly are seldom rewarded. When the cunning hare is caught, the hunting hound is cast aside; when the high bird is felled, the fine bow is set to be discarded. Beholding the mirror of Peng and Han, one recalls the art of withdrawing the hairpin; witnessing the folly of the Yue lineage, one recognizes the value of gold and ivory. Like Lord Fan, who sailed away to escape the limelight, or Master Xue, who fled chaos to preserve his integrity; like the Two Shus, who resigned their seals at the height of abundance, or Tian Yu, who relinquished his sash as the candle waned—advancing avoids the calamity of overreaching, retreating spares the regret of trailing wet. Their pure wind is enough to scatter the dust of a thousand years, their virtuous resonance enough to dispel the delusions of ages to come. Compared to Chen and Dou, how distant they seem!

Yet some, though wise, fail in grand schemes; some, though strong, break under heavy burdens. Some, though uniquely right, are ensnared by the many; some, though wholly loyal, are impeached by those guarding all fronts. Some, advocating lofty plans, suffer the fate of Chao Cuo; some, exhausting heart and strength, meet the doom of Wu Qi. Thus, even those who tread cautiously and act with restraint may not escape. The Duke of Zhou was exiled, Confucius departed, Jia Yi was banished to the hinterlands, Sima Qian was branded innocent yet punished, Le Yi pacified Qi, Wu Zixu shattered Chu, Bai Qi expanded the realm with a hundred victories, Wenzi dominated Yue with nine strategies, Han Xin’s merits towered over the world, Qing Bu destroyed his family to aid the mandate—yet glory did not last a day before humiliation followed, nor did they shrink from its disaster. Were these truly wise men?

To serve as a minister is no easy task; in short, the crux lies in choosing one’s lord. I am not worthy to rely upon—such is the proof. To resign, yield to the worthy, preserve purity, and secure peace; to gain a fine name and ample reward—what greater fortune could there be? To cultivate this art is to grasp one in ten thousand chances. As fortune and misfortune are shaped by man, can one afford not to ponder this?

Those words that grate upon the ear are seldom met with joy; offerings of counsel bring honor, yet may invite ruin or disaster. When slander arises, how can one find the leisure to verify what to accept as true? When snares and nets fill the skies, even the fledgling mandarin duck hesitates to spread its wings; when pits and traps line every path, the unicorn and the sacred tiger retreat into hiding.

The passions cannot be indulged without limit, nor can desires be sated without end. The enlightened master their emotions through the Dao and dispel desires through strategy—how much more should those who scheme for others ensure loyalty, let alone fail to deliberate when plotting for themselves?

For the contented, there is ever sufficiency; the insatiable know no sufficiency. Constant sufficiency draws fortune, while insatiability invites calamity. To nourish life excessively is to hasten its end. The Song clan uprooted their seedlings, and the people of Ying exulted in Zhang Hua’s rise—yet their haste brought only withering and ruin. To know advance but forget retreat is to court destruction.

Few who race without restraint avoid stumbling; rare are those who tread waves without end who escape drowning. To wield blades without cease invites wounds; to strike without pause courts breakage. That fullness must yield to loss is the principle of nature—does the vessel of Zhou’s ancestral temple deceive us? Thus, when Yang You shot his arrows, passersby warned him to guard against the wind; when Dongye drove his chariot, Yanzi foresaw his downfall. Success and failure are not easily long endured.

The drinker need not always descend into chaos, yet most who do are drunk; wealth and nobility do not invariably lead to peril, yet peril often lurks within them. The wise foresee events in their earliest stirrings, fell trees at the first sign of a bud, refrain from boasting when blocks are stacked precariously, and discuss mending furs only after gathering all the fox pelts. The ancients feigned madness or folly—was this their delight? The times demanded it; they had no choice.

Some flee deep into the wilds only to meet waves, or hide in seclusion only to face fire—like Gong Sheng, who starved himself to death, or Li Ye, who swallowed poison in despair. Such fates arise from traces left behind, shadows that cannot be erased. Had their conduct been as cautious as treading ice, their bodies as still as dwelling in shade, their movements leaving no track to follow, and their quietude merging with non-action—would such misfortunes have befallen them? How much less for those who brandish the sun and moon to conceal their forms or beat war drums to seize weapons!

In an age of clarity, one may excel in aiding all under heaven; in an age of peril, one may barely preserve a single life. This lament is not unique to one scholar.

I have heard that no blaze is unquenched, no excess unchecked; brilliance bears the seeds of ash, and spring’s blossoms foretell autumn’s decay. The sun reaches its zenith and then declines; the moon waxes full and then wanes. The sequence of the four seasons ensures that all who achieve success must retreat. Observe the world: the loftiest structures, once they reach their limit, crumble. Turn to oneself: even fine meals and restrained drink, if immoderate, breed illness and harm one’s nature. How much more for those whose ambitions soar to the clouds without cease, whose power shakes rulers and whose honors mount without end?

The gnat guards against falling from a mountain only to soar freely, while the rhinoceros and tiger, if they plummet, shatter like scallions—this illustrates that great things cannot lose their proper place.

Moreover, when one in upright authority rebukes transgressions and follows the straight Way, when one strikes down those who violate discipline without considering enmity or grudges, resentment deepens and hatred accumulates. Yet if one abandons the law to indulge wrongdoing, if one heeds private pleas like an echo, if one spits out the hard and swallows the soft, bending the rule like a carpenter’s line—does not dwelling in such a position become wearisome?

Thus, those who preserve both body and reputation are exceedingly rare, while those who stumble and overturn their cauldrons are not few. Yet within, they recline in ornate chambers with graceful maidens, crimson curtains, and patterned canopies, their couches spread in lavish halls. Radiant beauties dazzle at their sides, their lithe forms and soft voices weaving songs of exquisite artistry—melodies like those of Song Yu and Cai Yong, the grace of *Yang’e*, their lips singing *Cai Ling* and *Yan Lu*, their feet tracing the steps of *Lü Shui* and the *Seven Disks*. Harmonious notes delight the ear, enchanting postures gladden the heart. Secret feasts follow one another, fine wine never withdrawn. Looking up, they ascend carved pavilions; gazing down, they reflect upon clear pools. They wander among fruit groves of crimson and emerald, sport in orchid gardens fragrant with blossoms. Patterned fish glide through the waves, red-feathered birds soar and wheel, arrows bring down swans from the clouds, chariot wheels drag bream and carp from afar. Rare treasures gather without seeking, luxuries arrive unbidden.

Abroad, their vermilion wheels gleam on the roads, lofty canopies press wheel to wheel, crimson banners billow like clouds, insignia and batons command awe, golden drums clamor, halberds and armor glitter. Proudly they ride in the rear carriage, their fine escorts filling the ranks. Their retinues exhaust the pleasures of sightseeing, their hunts and fishing reach the height of joy. Praise for their sagely brilliance fills their ears, while flattering words, spoken with one voice, dismiss the ancients—claiming Yi, Lü, Guan, and Yan are not their equals. How could they perceive the rise and fall as head and tail, joy and sorrow as morning and night? How could they willingly relinquish their exalted glory, beg to return their bones to the earth, turn their backs on vermilion gates, and return to the hills and gardens?

Now, if a sagely enlightened ruler sits above, great worthies assist in governance, the hundred offices are ordered only through them, and the myriad people are saved only through them—those who stand high without fearing peril, who are full without fearing excess—these are not the ones under discussion.

**Chapter Fifty: On Obscurity and Eminence**

Someone asked: *"Men of the same talent may yet meet with obscurity or eminence—what is the reason? Does this bind the swift and free, leaving them with regret?"*

The Master Embracing Simplicity replied: *"When abilities are no different, yet some are suppressed and others exalted, it is because they lack a true patron—thus, adversity and prosperity depend on the times. Success and failure are matters of fate. Those who discern the times—why lament their depths? Those who understand fate—why resent their lowly toil?*

*The *Zhanlu* and *Chungou* swords are of the finest steel, yet if not wielded, they cannot even sever morning mushrooms. The *Yaohua* and *Lilü* are treasures beyond price, yet if cast into the mire, tiles and pebbles pile upon them. Thus, things may be precious yet not necessarily prized, may be useful yet not necessarily employed.*

*The vulgar, blind to distinctions, cannot tell red from purple, nor beans from wheat. They deem the eminent worthy merely because they have risen, unaware that what they seek is but fleeting success. They scorn the obscure as inferior, yet do not examine those who uphold the Dao and remain in obscurity.*

*Moreover, the celestial bodies, if not fixed in the heavens, cannot radiate their great light to illumine all without exception. Mount Song and Mount Tai, if not rooted in the earth, cannot tower to the clouds. The hare’s swiftness depends on level ground to gallop, the dragon’s boat rides the rushing currents to display its speed. Fire’s brilliance did not blaze until Suiren, the sharpness of Chu steel was not honed until Ou Ye. Luxuriant blossoms await spring to display their splendor, soaring swans rely on the gale to glide lightly.*

*If the Four Mountains do not commend the worthy, then even the most capable remain unrecognized. If Ya did not recommend Guan Zhong, then Yi Wu would not have been employed. Rangju owed his rise to Pingzhong, Han Xin’s soaring began with Xiao He. Junsheng’s eloquence came from Shengzhi’s counsel, Qu Ni’s advancement stemmed from Wuzhi’s recommendation. Kongming emerged from Longzhong, Gongjin’s fame arose from Gongwo’s support. Thus, their illustrious names were recorded in the imperial annals, their meritorious deeds inscribed in their era.*

*But in the late years of the Han and the final days of Wu, it was not so. In their selection of scholars..."*

They invariably favored those who aligned with themselves, making such attachment the foremost criterion for selection. Those with many factions were invariably chosen, yet those who aligned with them were not necessarily vessels fit for advancement. Those who echoed their own views could not be cast aside, yet those with many factions were not necessarily talents surpassing the common herd. Trusting the clamor of the crowd, they deemed such men worthy; or, believing these to be mediocre and base, they could not relinquish their attachment to the familiar and the near. Or, recognizing those others as exceptional and outstanding, they could not maintain impartiality in utmost fairness. Thus, they cast aside the scales of assessment and took frequency of association as the measure of weight; they abandoned standards of measure and took the density of connections as the gauge of quantity. At such times, those so-called refined men of lofty bearing who wielded the state’s power of promotion and demotion—whose decisions of praise and censure were dictated by mere words—were rare indeed to escape this entanglement. How much more so when, in their hearts, they suddenly harbored hatred, stood alone in resentment, envied those not of their faction, or despised the independent and superior, or scorned the poor and unconnected?

Alas! The scholars of distant custom and the men of unparalleled distinction—those who, generation after generation, failed to meet with recognition—cannot be numbered. Some were suppressed and languished in marshes and wilds; others stood in court only to be cast out. Some cultivated virtue yet their Way did not prevail; some stored their talents yet the times did not guard them. Some waited for the Yellow River to run clear until their teeth had fallen out; some exhausted their loyalty and diligence yet were not known or employed. Their far-reaching influence did not extend to their age; their beneficent deeds did not reach the people. Treasures fit for the banquet mat lay mired in mud; talents for aiding the world ended without application. Like the man who played the zither yet did not meet King Wu Ding, or the one who held the fishing pole yet did not encounter King Wen—from ancient times to the present, how many have there been? Yet to lament this alone is indeed narrow-minded.

To gaze upon the distant path and be ashamed to follow it, to know the great Dao is obstructed yet not change course, to equate success and failure on a single road, to consign honor and disgrace to nature—how could one harbor melancholy in the face of scarce understanding, or sigh forever at the passing of rivers? To suspect resentment in this is to fail to comprehend the mind of the perfected person.

The ignorance of short-lived creatures regarding the long-lived, the frog in the well’s incomprehension of the vast sea—these have always been so.

**Chapter Fifty-One**
The *Baopuzi* states: There was a man called Master Tranquil and Profound. Though his years were but those of one who studies the will, he had already exhaustively perused the Six Classics and broadly synthesized the *He* and *Luo* texts. By day, he contended with the last rays of Xi He; by night, he illuminated the lingering glow of Wang Shu. No Dao was too distant for him to fathom; no word was too obscure for him to plumb. He took Confucianism and Daoism as his city walls, his spiritual acuity as his weapons. Thus, those who discoursed could not but trail dust and proffer jade in his presence; scholars, upon setting eyes on him, laid down their brushes. Soon, they awoke to the wisdom of silence and realized the blamelessness of guarding the One. When his intent was fulfilled, he deemed the fish-trap and rabbit-snare discardable; when the Dao diverged, he perceived that lofty singing finds few harmonies.

Thereupon, he embraced Laozi’s caution against frequent defeat and pondered the admonition of the golden man’s triple-sealed lips. He sheathed his blade’s edge as if encasing it, polished his brush on the vermilion tube, held gold and jade within, and restrained his florid eloquence. From dawn to dusk, he might utter not a single word. His disciples approached and said: “Master, you remain silent—how can your disciples expound? You differ not from the common man.”

I privately thought: If the great bell does not sound, it is no different from a heap of bronze; if the floating chime ceases its tone, it is indistinguishable from a pile of stones.

Master Tranquil and Profound replied: “I seek only to gather a distant name across ten thousand generations and find a true friend in the ages to come. How could I vie for recognition in this day or set my standard in this moment? A vessel for brewing fine wine, though excellent, does not itself become drunk. One who speaks humbly yet loftily, though correct, is not believed. To curl the tongue and exhaust the voice—what remedy is there for fleeing and evasion? The ancients laughed at fifty-nine when they reached sixty; only then did they realize the depth of their past delusion.

For jade is hard, and gold is strong.”

The cold of ice and the heat of fire—do they require self-proclamation to be known? Moreover, the eight tones and nine suites cannot avoid flaws of length or brevity; even Yang You’s hundred shots cannot prevent an occasional miss. Those who cross rivers without boats often drown in water; those fond of heated debate frequently slip in speech. Bo Ya was cautious with his strings, thus avoiding the burden of tangled fingers; the scholar guards his words, thus escaping the shame of careless slips. The shallow and vulgar act otherwise: they dispute the urgency of emptiness and nothingness, squander words on trivial matters, and expound at length on the useless doctrines of "hard and white." They litigate against the masters, condemn the sages, and fault the upright, calling such misguidance "profound." They cast aside the true classics to vie in heterodox studies, or match wits with the ignorant in contests of lip and tongue, spouting lofty discourse to those who cannot comprehend. Before those who cannot contend, with no discerning audience at hand, how is this different from playing refined music beside wooden effigies or displaying alchemical elixirs before clay idols?

In vain their mouths grow parched, their qi exhausted, their bold gestures like axes dulled and splintered—yet the knot remains unbroken. Their faces flush with agitation, their discord grows ever more distant, until anger distorts their countenance, shameful words escape their lips, and the shift toward pettiness arises. Once such flaws are stained, they cannot be mended. Far better to embrace silence to preserve the great tone, to restrain speech and await those who truly understand.

*Self-Narration, Fifty-First*

The Master of Embracing Simplicity, surnamed Ge, named Hong, styled Zhichuan, was a native of Jurong in Danyang. His ancestors descended from the Ge Tian clan, who once ruled all under heaven in ancient times. Later, they were demoted to the ranks of feudal lords and took Ge as their surname. Hong’s great-grandfather served as Inspector of Jingzhou. When Wang Mang usurped the throne, he scorned to serve a traitorous regime, resigned his post, and returned home. Together with Zhai Yi, Governor of Dongjun, he raised troops to punish Wang Mang but was defeated. Pardoned and spared from disaster, he feigned illness and withdrew from the world. Fearing that the Ge clan’s strength might eventually provoke unrest, Wang Mang relocated them to Langye. Hong’s grandfather, Pu, raised an army to aid Emperor Guangwu, rendering great service. After Guangwu ascended the throne, Pu was appointed General of Chariots and Cavalry, later promoted to General of Agile Cavalry, and enfeoffed as Marquis of Xiapei and Tongxian, with a fief of five thousand households. At the founding of the dynasty, Pu’s younger brother, Wen, followed the marquis on campaigns and achieved repeated victories. The marquis repeatedly memorialized the throne to commend Wen’s merits, but the court, citing Wen’s private service under his elder cousin without official military rank, refused to acknowledge his contributions. The marquis declared, “My brother and I together faced arrows and stones, our bodies covered in wounds, my right eye lost—yet he has received no reward, while I am laden with gold and purple. How can my heart be at ease?” He then petitioned to transfer his own enfeoffment to his brother. The emperor, wishing to honor the marquis’s noble integrity, granted the request. Wen, though reluctant, accepted the title and immediately ceded his residence to become the encampment of the General of Agile Cavalry, establishing his estate in Bowang Ward. To this day, its foundations and stone pillars remain. He also divided his stipends to provide for his officers and soldiers, treating them as generously as the two former marquises. The General of Agile Cavalry repeatedly urged him to desist, but Wen refused, saying, “This only burdens the people further. How can I accept?” Feigning other business, he crossed the Yangtze southward and settled in Jurong, where his descendants tilled the land and found solace in books. Though Wen repeatedly sent envoys to welcome the General of Agile Cavalry, the latter never returned. Instead, he stationed guards to maintain the estate in Bowang, hoping for the general’s eventual return—yet for generations, no one resided there.

Hong’s grandfather was a scholar of unparalleled breadth, mastering all fields of learning with profound precision. His literary and artistic attainments stood peerless in his time, and he possessed the talent to govern a state. Serving the Kingdom of Wu, he held successive posts as Magistrate of Haiyan, Lin’an, and Shanyin, then entered the capital as Gentleman of the Ministry of Personnel, Palace Aide to the Censor-in-Chief, Governor of Luling, Minister of Personnel, Junior Preceptor to the Crown Prince, President of the Imperial Secretariat, Grand Master of Ceremonies, Palace Attendant, and Intendant of the Palace Guards. He was also appointed General of Assisting Wu and enfeoffed as Marquis of Wushou. Hong’s father was renowned for his filial piety and fraternal devotion, a model for scholars. His conduct and learning were exhaustively recorded in official histories. He served Wu as Gentleman of the Five Offices, Prefect of Jiancheng and Nanchang, Gentleman of the Imperial Secretariat, and Minister of Justice, then as General of the Guards. He was appointed Governor of Kuaiji but had not yet assumed office when the Jin army advanced downstream, and the western borders collapsed. Recognizing his literary and martial talents, the court and populace unanimously recommended him. He was thus transferred to serve as Commander-in-Chief of Five Commanderies, charged with defending the frontier, granted five thousand personal troops, and placed in command of all expeditionary forces. Yet when heaven decrees ruin, human effort cannot prevail. As the former lord submitted to the Nine Provinces’ shared fate, Hong’s father, retaining his former rank, was summoned to serve as Gentleman of the Palace, gradually rising to the position of Grand Master of Splendid Happiness. He later served as Magistrate of Feixiang, a county of twenty thousand households, where his governance was deemed the finest in the province. His moral transformation was exceptional, his benevolence pervasive, his punishments just, and his domain peaceful, earning him widespread praise. Roads were free of bandits, and no one tilled public fields or crossed boundaries to trade. Even the smallest gifts were refused at his gate, while paper and brushes were supplied from his private funds. Through punishments, order was maintained; without words, transformation was achieved. He resigned due to illness, but an imperial edict summoned him to serve as Prefect of Wu, where he upheld strict integrity, correcting errors, promoting the worthy, and exposing the corrupt. The state and military were governed with solemn harmony. Later, he was appointed Governor of Shaoling and died in office.

Hong was his father’s third son. Born late in his parents’ lives, he was doted upon and not strictly disciplined in books and history. At the age of thirteen, he lost his kind father, prematurely deprived of paternal instruction. Struggling with hunger and cold, he personally tilled the fields, laboring beneath the stars and treading upon frost, diligently following the plow. Repeatedly ravaged by war and fire, his family’s books were utterly destroyed. During rare moments of respite from farming, with nothing to read, he carried his satchel and walked to borrow books, only to find that few households possessed complete collections. Thus, he redoubled his efforts, cutting and selling firewood to purchase paper and brushes, writing books by the light of kindling in his thatched hut. For this reason, he could not early immerse himself in the arts and literature. Often lacking paper, he wrote on the same sheets repeatedly, rendering his texts barely legible. At sixteen, he began studying the *Classic of Filial Piety*, the *Analects*, the *Book of Songs*, and the *Book of Changes*. Yet poverty prevented him from seeking distant teachers and friends, leaving him isolated and poorly informed, his thoughts shallow and his understanding limited. Many great principles remained beyond his grasp. Eager to read widely, he devoured all manner of books, memorizing whatever he encountered. Yet his nature was dull and forgetful, his will not steadfast, and his retention weak. Though he sometimes drew upon his readings in his writings, he never achieved the depth of a true Confucian scholar and was unfit to serve as a teacher. The *River Chart* and *Luo Writing*, along with apocryphal texts, he merely glanced at without deep engagement. He disliked astrological manuals, calculations, the Nine Palaces, three-player chess, *Taiyi* divination, and flying talismans, finding no affinity for such arts. Later, he dabbled in wind angles, qi observation, the Three Origins, *Dunjia*, *Liuren*, and *Taiyi* methods, gaining only a rough understanding without deep study. He concluded that such practices were tools for others’ use, mere distractions from self-cultivation. Believing that reducing desires was more beneficial, he abandoned them. According to the *Separate Record of Arts and Letters*, there were 13,299 volumes of books, and since the Wei dynasty, the corpus had doubled. Thus, he realized how much he had yet to see.
