Books from the south of the Yangtze were incomplete, and he once journeyed to the capital in search of rare and extraordinary texts, only to be thwarted by great chaos, forcing him to turn back midway. Each time, he sighed with regret. Now nearing forty, his youthful aspirations have waned. He thinks only of diminishing desires further, practicing non-action, and perhaps retiring to the marshes to preserve his life. His pursuit of broad learning has thus daily declined.

Ge Hong was a man of simple and rustic bearing, dull by nature, slow of speech, and plain in appearance, yet he never sought to adorn or refine himself. His hats and shoes were filthy and worn, his robes often tattered, and he felt no shame in this. As worldly fashions swiftly changed—now broad collars and wide sashes, now tight sleeves and long hems, now trailing skirts, now garments too short to cover the feet—he remained steadfast in his ways, refusing to follow the shifts of the age. His words were always plain and truthful, free of jest or mockery. When no suitable companion was at hand, he would pass the entire day in silence. Thus, all who knew him called him a man who "embraced simplicity," and so he took this as the title for his writings.

By nature, Ge Hong was frail and sickly, and poverty left him without carriage or horse. He could not bear to walk, nor did he enjoy it, and he despised the degenerate customs of his time—how men abandoned fundamentals to chase trivialities, how they overindulged in socializing. Thus, he took up his brush in seclusion, dwelling in stillness behind a wattle gate, with no place to rush to or follow after. Even those in power, though near at hand, scarcely knew him. His clothes could not ward off the cold, his roof could not keep out the rain, and his food could not fill his stomach. Yet fame never crossed his threshold, and he could not bring himself to care. Poor, with no servants, his fences collapsed, brambles choked his courtyard, and weeds overran his steps. When he ventured out, he pushed through thickets; when he returned, he brushed past overgrowth. Observers thought him lofty of mind, neglectful of the mundane, and never resentful of his hardships.

Unskilled in formal visits, he never paid respects to officials. Yet when it came to mourning the dead or tending to the sick, he would force himself to go, sparing no effort. But his frailty and frequent illnesses often left him unable to fulfill these duties, and critics would reproach him. Ge Hong accepted the blame without concern. He thought: if his intentions were pure and illness alone prevented him, why should he feel shame? What reason had others not to understand? Only those of clear discernment would forgive him, recognizing that his embrace of simplicity was not a ploy to cultivate loftiness.

Most men cherished the warmth of close bonds and prized the intimacy of those who came to them in ignorance. Ge Hong, however, believed that truly knowing another was no easy task—even the sages found it difficult. Superficial friendships, where words aligned but minds clashed, brought no benefit, only harm. Though he could not sever ties as abruptly as Zhuangzi’s friend did, he insisted on clarity and thorough understanding before committing to a relationship. For this, he was deeply resented, yet he never changed.

Those who chased success and stood obsequiously at the gates of the powerful despised Ge Hong for his difference, slandering him as arrogant and dismissive of custom. But Ge Hong was a man of unwavering conviction; praise and blame alike fell on deaf ears. He loathed those who, relying on their own strengths, belittled the weaknesses of others. As one of the least among Confucians, when he spoke with others, he always gauged their understanding and discussed only what they could grasp, never forcing them toward what they could not comprehend.

When debating with scholars, he would lay out the essentials. If he encountered someone of limited ability, he would offer only a rough sketch of his ideas, just enough to spark insight, without pressing the finer points to the point of confusion. Those who listened with a calm mind and pondered deeply often found their own realizations. When he judged someone unfit for discussion, even if questioned, he would plead ignorance to avoid wasting words.

By nature, Ge Hong deeply disliked imposing on officials or superiors. From youth to old age, he had secretly aided several friends in distress, yet none knew it was he who had helped them. He could not bear to see others wronged and would quietly intervene on their behalf. But for all others—even close relatives—if they held power or influence and were strangers to him, he would not trouble them with so much as a word or half a character.

When his supplies ran low and he urgently needed medicine, he would ask friends for help, and if they provided, he accepted without refusal. Any assistance he received, he repaid gradually over time, ensuring the giver never noticed. If the aid did not come from a friend, he would not accept it. When his own provisions lasted ten days, he would share them to relieve others’ needs. But if his own stores were insufficient, he would not deprive himself.

He did not strive for petty displays of virtue or meticulous scruples. Among villagers, those deemed "good and kind" would sometimes bring wine and dishes to visit him. Though he did not consider them his equals, he did not refuse them. If he later repaid the gesture, he did not do so immediately.

Ge Hong once remarked on the historian’s account of a man who refused to eat with his brothers but kept his table spotless for guests: such was the false conduct of those seeking fame, not the magnanimity of a statesman. He particularly despised those without principle—men who neglected the fundamentals of farming and weaving to pursue illicit gains. Some sold official appointments for bribes; others exploited their influence to extort wealth. Some took bribes from criminals, others wronged the innocent. Some harbored fugitives and sheltered outlaws; others conscripted laborers to obstruct public works. Some hoarded goods to inflate prices; others monopolized markets to seize profits from the people. Some seized fields and robbed the weak of their livelihoods. They cowered before officials, seeking opportunities to exploit, then flaunted their gains before their wives and concubines or used them to buy fame and rank. With such men, Ge Hong refused all association.

Thus, the worldly despised him, and naturally, they kept their distance. No carriage tracks marred his lane, no guests of differing minds crossed his hall. His courtyard could have been set with bird nets, and his tables gathered dust. From the time he first gained understanding until his later years, his lips never uttered another’s faults, nor did he delight in others’ secrets—this came naturally to him. Even his servants, if they had shortcomings or shameful acts, he never mocked. He never judged or ranked people’s strengths and weaknesses, nor did he enjoy scolding others. If pressed by superiors to speak of someone’s likes or dislikes, he would only mention their finest qualities. When discussing writing, he would highlight its best aspects, never pointing out its flaws. Thus, he incurred no resentment for praise or blame.

When powerful men occasionally asked him about officials or commoners—who was pure, who was capable—he would extol the virtues of the upright and talented. For the greedy, violent, or ignorant, he would claim to know nothing of them. For this, he was often criticized, accused of being overly cautious, unable to clearly distinguish right from wrong. Yet Ge Hong never altered his ways.

He often saw how people, when discussing others, would compare and rank them, their praise and criticism often misplaced. Those who were praised would take it as their due, though their virtue might not be genuine. Those who were slighted would hate their detractors to the bone, as if they were blood enemies. Ge Hong took this as a warning and thereafter never spoke of others.

When engaging with scholars, he would grasp the main points. If he encountered someone of limited understanding, he would offer only a rough outline of his ideas, just enough to inspire insight, without pressing the finer points to the point of confusion. Those who listened with a calm mind and pondered deeply often found their own realizations. If he judged someone unfit for discussion, even if questioned, he would plead ignorance to avoid wasting words. His nature deeply disliked imposing on officials or superiors. From youth to old age, he had secretly aided several friends in distress, yet none knew it was he who had helped them. He could not bear to see others wronged and would quietly intervene. But for others—even close relatives—if they held power and were strangers to him, he would not trouble them with so much as a word.

When his supplies ran low and he urgently needed medicine, he would ask friends for help, accepting without refusal. Any assistance he received, he repaid gradually, ensuring the giver never noticed. If the aid did not come from a friend, he would not accept it. When his provisions lasted ten days, he would share them to relieve others’ needs, but if his own stores were insufficient, he would not deprive himself. He did not strive for petty displays of virtue or meticulous scruples. Among villagers, those deemed "good and kind" would sometimes bring wine and dishes to visit him. Though he did not consider them his equals, he did not refuse them. If he later repaid the gesture, he did not do so immediately.

Though his clan’s disciples and younger kin might deem such a stance too lenient, he would assign them both praise and blame without distinction. Some mocked him for this, to which he replied: “My person belongs to me. The principle should be easy to grasp. Suppose someone asked me to compare myself to the ancients or to my contemporaries, forcing me to rank myself—truly, I could not know with whom I might be matched. How much less should I presume to judge those who are not myself?

In the late Han, the corruption of custom gave rise to factions and cliques. Men like Xu Shao and his ilk wielded their tongues to issue warnings and engage in disputes, turning debates into feuds. Thus, the scholars of Runan had no fixed reputations, only the monthly evaluations of character. Even Emperor Wu of Wei deeply despised this and sought to behead them, driving them to flee in panic, nearly to the point of annihilation. The lesson lies close at hand—one may well take it as a teacher.

Moreover, people are not easily known. Even fathers and elder brothers may not fully understand their sons and younger brothers. Is what aligns with me necessarily right? Is what differs from me necessarily wrong? Some begin well but end poorly. Yao, the Duke of Zhou, Confucius, and Jizha all lamented their own incompleteness. How can those of us with narrow, flickering candlelight vision presume to judge others and assess their worth? Do we surpass those sagely and virtuous ancients?

During the Da’an era, Shi Bing rebelled, ravaging six provinces. Villages and towns were laid waste, and the righteous cause was overturned. The Grand Marshal of the Righteous Army summoned Hong to serve as a general and captain. Repeatedly pressed, and fearing deep calamity would befall his homeland, he recalled the ancient principle of aiding those in dire peril. Yet he also dreaded military discipline and dared not act on his own will. Thus, he recruited several hundred men and joined the army in its advance. Once, they attacked a separate rebel force and routed them. On that day, coins and silks piled like mountains, and treasures covered the ground. The other armies all released their soldiers to gather spoils, their carts and packs stretching end to end. Hong alone ordered his troops not to stray from formation. If any soldier was found looting, Hong immediately executed him as an example. Thus, none dared abandon their posts. Later, when hundreds of ambushers emerged to strike the other armies, those forces had scattered, their men and horses burdened with plunder, their fighting spirit gone. They fell into chaos, suffering heavy casualties, nearly beyond recovery. Only Hong’s troops remained orderly, their carts and tents undamaged, saving the other armies from total collapse. Hong’s merit was great in this.

In a later battle, he slew a minor rebel chieftain and captured many suits of armor and heads, presenting them as trophies to headquarters. The Grand Marshal then bestowed upon Hong the title of General Fubo, along with a gift of a hundred bolts of cloth, following standard practice. Many officers sealed their rewards or sent them home, but Hong distributed his among his officers and soldiers, as well as to impoverished acquaintances. Of the hundred bolts, he kept only ten for himself, then immediately used them to buy meat and wine to feast his officers and men. For a time, this became the talk of the day.

When the conflict ended, Hong cast aside his weapons and armor and headed straight for Luoyang, seeking to expand his collection of rare books. He made no mention of his military achievements, secretly admiring the principle of Lu Lian, who refused gold from Liaocheng, and Wu Zixu, who declined rewards for preserving Chu—embracing the virtue of not dwelling on success.

Just then, the capital fell into great turmoil, and the northern roads were blocked. Chen Min rebelled in Jiangdong, cutting off the return route. An old friend, Dong Ju, who had been appointed Governor of Guangzhou, submitted a memorial requesting Hong as his adjutant. Though the post was not to his liking, the south offered a refuge, so he reluctantly accepted. Sent ahead to muster troops, he waited for Dong Ju to follow, but Dong was killed en route. Hong thus remained in Guangzhou, frequently invited by regional commanders to serve, but he declined every offer.

He reflected: wealth and honor may be gradually attained but cannot be seized all at once. The pursuit of such things is wearisome and enough to exhaust a person. Moreover, glory and power are like lodgers—neither permanent nor something one can cling to. What rises high will inevitably fall; what blazes bright will fade. Like spring blossoms, they must wither. To rejoice in gaining them or grieve in losing them is to invite endless regret, sorrow, and fear—struggles too numerous to name. They are not worth the trouble.

Assessing his own nature, he found it deeply indolent, his talents severely limited. Even if he were to bow and scrape, rushing about in the dust, he would surely fail to achieve great fame or avoid entanglements—how much more so given his inability? Better to cultivate the Way of Master Red Pine and Wang Qiao, which lies within oneself and does not depend on others.

He aspired to ascend famous mountains, practice dietary arts, and nurture his nature—not out of neglect for worldly affairs, but because one cannot serve both at once. If he did not abandon worldly duties, how could he cultivate tranquility and the profound?

Moreover, true understanding is difficult and cannot be borrowed through inquiry or discussion. Thus, his carriage and horses never entered the domains of the powerful, and not a single word of his writing was exchanged with those in office. Though he could not entirely withdraw from the scholar’s circle, he refused to receive visitors. When others’ works infringed upon his own, he could not maintain his focus. He sighed: “There is no Dao in the mountains and forests.” Yet the ancients who cultivated the Dao invariably entered the mountains and forests, truly seeking to distance themselves from clamor and prevent their minds from being disturbed.

Now, he would fulfill his original aspiration: leaving his homeland, he would journey to Mount Song to seek the path of Fangping and Liang Gong. His earlier work, the *Master’s Book*, in both inner and outer chapters, had fortunately already been completed. He would now compile and arrange it to show to future generations.

At fifteen or sixteen, he had written poems, rhapsodies, and miscellaneous essays, which he then believed were passable. By the time he reached twenty, he reviewed them again and found much unsatisfactory. His talent had not necessarily improved—only his reading had broadened, and he now discerned the difference between the refined and the coarse. Thus, he discarded most of his earlier work, keeping less than one-tenth. Excluding the *Master’s Book*, he still had over a hundred scrolls of miscellaneous writings, though many remained unrevised, filled with sorrow and indignation, and he lacked the leisure to refine them further.

Others wrote swiftly, finding satisfaction in their work as soon as it was done. But his own talent was dull, his thoughts slow, and he could not produce polished prose on demand. Each time he revised a character, the text improved, but his laziness and the volume of his work prevented frequent review.

In his twenties, he had calculated that writing minor, trivial pieces wasted time better spent establishing a coherent philosophy. Thus, he began drafting the *Master’s Book*. However, war and upheaval scattered him, and some works were lost. For over a decade on the road, he did not take up his brush. Only in the Jianwu era did he finally settle, completing twenty inner chapters and fifty outer chapters, along with a hundred scrolls of stele inscriptions, eulogies, poems, and rhapsodies. He also wrote thirty scrolls of military documents, proclamations, letters, and memorials. Additionally, he compiled works not included in common catalogs: the *Biographies of Divine Immortals* in ten scrolls, the *Biographies of Recluses* in ten scrolls, and an anthology of essential passages from the Five Classics, the Seven Histories, the Hundred Schools, military affairs, techniques, and miscellaneous wonders, totaling three hundred and ten scrolls, each with its own catalog.

The inner chapters discuss matters of Daoist concern: divine immortals, elixirs, strange transformations, life-nourishing practices, longevity techniques, and methods to ward off misfortune. The outer chapters address Confucian themes: human successes and failures, and the right and wrong of worldly affairs.

Hong noted that Emperor Wen of Wei’s *Canon of Classics* omitted mention of games like tanqi and swordplay. He intended to briefly record what he knew, but in truth, his skills were few and minor, and he could not boast falsely. Now, he would fully describe what he had not yet mastered.

Ge Hong’s nature was dull and his talents mediocre. From the time his hair was first tied up in childhood, he lagged behind other boys in tossing tiles or wrestling. He never fought cocks, raced dogs or horses, and when he saw others gambling, he paid no heed. Even when dragged along to watch, he remained utterly disengaged, as if dozing in broad daylight. To this day, he does not know how many lines are on a chessboard or the names of the dice in *chupu*. He considers such pastimes trivial arts that scatter the mind and waste time. For officials, they harm governance; for scholars, they disrupt recitation; for commoners, they cause neglect of farming; for merchants, they lead to loss of goods. Before the outcome is decided, players grow heated in dispute, their faces flushed with anger, calling this "amusement" when in truth it is torment. It erodes integrity, sparks contention, invites heavy wagers, and breeds hidden grudges. In the past, Duke Min of Song and the Crown Prince of Wu met with fatal disasters, while the rebellion of the Seven States nearly toppled the imperial court—lessons bright as mirrors for a hundred generations. Whenever he observed players, he saw them flushed with shame and rage, striking out with hands and feet, hurling insults, severing friendships, and breaking ties—all too common. Resentment does not depend on scale; whether great or small, it often brings regret. Such pursuits are not worth the trouble.

Confucius warned against dozing in the daytime, but compared to Ge Hong, even that seems preferable. Daytime dozing is merely unproductive, whereas it does not risk resentment or litigation. The sage still wore out bamboo strips three times in his devotion to the classics—how could an ordinary man of modest talent manage both? All such games are inferior to even a foot of books. Thus, he naturally disliked them and never engaged, though such pastimes are dear to the common crowd.

In his youth, he once studied archery, but his strength was insufficient to draw a strong bow—only one like Yan Gao’s. He believed archery was among the Six Arts and could be used to repel bandits, resist raids, and hunt birds and beasts, so he practiced it. Once, while in the military, he shot at pursuing cavalry, striking down two enemies and a horse, thereby escaping death. He also learned the use of the shield and single- and double-edged swords, all with oral formulas and essential techniques for subduing opponents. There were even secret methods so skillful they seemed divine. When facing the untrained, he could single-handedly prevail, advancing without hindrance.

Later, he learned the seven-foot staff technique, which could be used to disarm opponents or break through defenses. Yet this, too, was a non-essential skill—knowing it was like possessing the horn of a qilin or the spur of a phoenix: what need was there for it? Few in his time understood such things.

From a young age, Ge Hong had a steadfast resolve never to enter officialdom. Whenever he read the biographies of Chao Fu, Xu You, the northern recluse of Zizhou, the two Jiangs of Shihu, the two Xiaos, Fazhen, and Zilong, he would set aside his books and lean forward in admiration. He aspired to master the Five Classics and compose a work of his own, so that later generations would know him as a literary scholar. Later, when the prefecture and the General of Chariots and Cavalry summoned him, he declined. Though recommended to the prime minister’s office of the Prince of Langye, when the uprising began and the rebels were pacified, he did not seek recognition or visit the office to claim merit. The officials in charge never rewarded his contributions.

He hoped that the King of Jin, who answered Heaven’s call and followed the people’s will, would restore order, revive the fading imperial lineage, repair the neglected ancestral temples, and honor the unrecognized merits of the previous dynasty to encourage future service. Ge Hong followed the general amnesty and, in the year *gengyin*, received an edict granting him the title of Marquis of Guanzhong, with a fief of two hundred households in Jurong. He had secretly led troops to suppress rebels and save his homeland, but his efforts were deemed insufficient for record. The honor of golden and purple seals was not his original aspiration. He had wished to emulate Lu Lian from afar and Tian Chou more closely, submitting petitions to decline and fulfill his reclusive ambitions. However, a general amnesty was in place, and his request was not granted.

In the past, Zhong You declined a reward he was due, yet this discouraged others from doing good. The barbarians had not yet been pacified, the realm was troubled, and the state sought to clarify rewards and punishments to uphold the law. How could a humble man like himself stubbornly cling to his timid resolve and resist the grand, all-encompassing system? Thus, he set aside his personal inclinations and respectfully accepted the imperial decree.

After Ge Hong wrote his autobiographical essay, someone challenged him, saying: "In his later years, Wang Chong feared his name and deeds would perish, so he wrote an account of himself. You, however, are in the prime of life, living in an era of sagely rule. You should be untying the silk bundles of Shen Gong and mounting the rush mats of Mei Sheng, illuminating the Nine Five with your brilliance and surpassing the fame of Kunwu. Why instead pursue the path of an old scholar, wasting your fragrance?"

Ge Hong replied: "Heaven and Earth stretch vast, yet man dwells as if in a borrowed abode. The morning mushroom’s brief bloom withers before the day is done; the spring flower’s glory fades within ten days. Even the fleeting flight of the whirlwind or the sudden flash of lightning are not necessarily swifter. The span of a hundred years is like a shooting star’s swift passage; gray hair flies by like an arrow through a gap. How much more so for those who wither in summer’s heat or perish before autumn’s chill? Thus, Xiangzi lamented the unharvested grain, and Yang Xiong mourned his early death.

"Looking back through the ages, extraordinary men have risen like dragons through literary arts or crouched like tigers through martial deeds. Their great merits are recorded in the bright halls, their virtuous reputations echoing through the bamboo and silk. Though their forms may decay in the earth, their fine reputations soar with the days. Even after a thousand or ten thousand generations, they remain revered.

"I, however, am mediocre and obscure, my steps out of sync with the times, my voice discordant with the world. My steps clash with the crowd, and within, I lack the support of the Jin or Zhang families; without, I have no friends to dust off their hats for me. Though the path may be smooth, my feet are not those of a *qilin*; though the skies may be vast, my wings are not those of the great peng. Above, I cannot soar like a hawk to aid the state; below, I cannot glorify my parents or leave behind a name. My virtues are not entrusted to good historians, nor is my fame inscribed on bells and tripods. Thus, in the margins of my writings, I have composed this autobiographical essay. Though it may not alter my fate, I rely on future generations to carry on the record."

*Baopuzi, Outer Chapters, Scroll Four*
